To reach him she'd have to dive the passage, find the dog-handles by feel, and crack a door that the sea is holding shut from the wrong side — air on his side, water on hers, and the second the seal breaks the level finds its own answer and the answer drowns the smaller room. Not yet. Maybe not at all. She files it next to everything else she cannot do this minute, and the filing costs her something it usually doesn't.

She knocks once more, slow, a promise she has no right to make and makes anyway: *we know you're there.* One rap comes back. *I know you do.*

She turns her face up toward the sound and reaches with her senses the way she reached for the surface in the first minute — not to see, there is nothing to see, but to build the thing in her head where her eyes can't go. The racks run fore and aft along what was the deck overhead, maybe four feet above the apex of the dome, close enough that when she stands tall on the grating her fingers can graze the lowest of the pots, the cold wet steel tube of a cage, the bite of the net stretched over it. She walks her hand along it. Finds the place where it's hung — a lashing of doubled line led up into the dark to a padeye she can't see, the line gone hard as cable under the strain. She follows it to the next pot, and the next, building the wall in her mind. Eight pots in this run. One block of hydraulics, heavier than all of them, hung off a single eyebolt at the end. A thousand pounds of gear over the heads of four people who have nowhere else to stand.

She does the arithmetic and the arithmetic is simple and ugly. The rack is shedding. When it goes, it goes onto the only ground they have. They have two choices and both of them cost.

She can move everyone off the shelf-line, out from under the run, into the lower water where the dome closes down — gets the bodies clear of the guillotine, surrenders the only dry high ground they own, puts Sal's broken leg and Marcus's failing core back into thirty-four-degree water that will finish what the air has started. Saves them from the rack by handing them to the cold. The cold is slower. The cold is also certain.

Or she can go up and re-lash the load. Put a strap back on the worst of it, take the strain off the parting line, buy the rack another few hours of holding its breath. Keep the dry ground. The cost of that is exposure — somebody up there in the wet and the cold with their hands above their head working line they can't see — and the risk that the working itself, the pulling and the hauling, is what tips the balance and brings it all down on the head of whoever's doing it.

Right call by the numbers is the re-lash. She knows it before she's finished thinking it. The dry shelf is the thing keeping Sal alive at all and the thing slowing the kid's slide, and you do not give up the only warm ground you have to a danger that *might* fall when you can spend an hour reducing the chance it does. You triage the certain against the possible and you defend the certain. It's the cleanest call she's made all night.

It also means putting herself directly under the thing she's trying to keep from falling — and the one set of hands she'd have wanted beside her up there is sealed behind a bulkhead, knocking.

"Cole." She keeps her voice down, pitched for him and not the room. "The run over the shelf is shedding. I want it re-lashed before it sheds the rest of the way. I'm going up."

A pause. In it she hears him doing his own arithmetic, and she'd give a good deal to read which numbers he's running. "You want me up there," he says. "I've got the reach and the hands for it."

"I want you down here." It comes out faster than she means it to, and harder, and she covers it with reasons because the reasons are good. "Sal needs holding when the hull moves — if it shifts while I'm up there and he slides, that leg goes through the muscle and he bleeds out internal and we've got nothing for it. Marcus is half-gone and panicking and somebody steady has to keep him from doing something that drowns him. That's you. You're the one who can hold this room together at the floor while somebody works the ceiling. I need the strong hands *down here.*"

Every word of it is true. She listens to herself say it and hears that it's true and notes, the way she notes a clean strong pulse on a patient who's about to crash anyway, that the true thing and the thing she wants are the same thing, and that she's relieved they are, and that the relief is suspicious. She files it. She does not have time to read it.

"Then who's up there with me," she says, and the answer is already in the dark with them, and she doesn't like it.

Nobody. That's the answer. The second pair of hands on the rack would have been Pavel's — fifteen seasons, the man who rigged half that gear and knows which eyebolt's good and which one's been weeping rust since October, the one she'd want above her own head over anyone on the boat. And Pavel is sealed in a steel closet aft, knocking, dry and whole and forty feet of flooded boat away, and there is no getting him to the rope and no getting the rope to him. She has the kid, who is half-gone. She has the cook, who can't stand on the leg. And she has Cole, who can do two things from the floor and not three — hold the broken men when the hull moves, and tend her standing part as far up as his reach goes without leaving them. That's the most he can be.

So it's her. Alone, up in it, belayed by a man who can't take his hands off Sal and Marcus for more than a grab. Not the rig she wants. The rig she has.

"I work it," she says. "You belay what I send you and you don't leave the floor. If the hull moves you've got Sal and the kid before you've got me. That's the order. Say it back."

"You first if I can reach you," Cole says, which is not saying it back, which is the warm thing and the wrong thing, and she lets it stand because arguing it spends air she needs for the climb.

She walks it through out loud anyway, the way she'd walk a junior through a line they were about to put in someone's chest — slow, exact, every step named before she takes it, because in the dark a plan you haven't said out loud is a plan you don't have. "Worst of the run is the middle. The third and fourth pots — that's where the parted strand was. I get a strap around that pair, lead it to a good eye, take the strain off the old line so it stops sawing itself apart. I don't touch the block at the end, it's too heavy to manage by hand and if I wake it up I can't put it back to sleep. In and out. Two straps if the first holds. One if my hands quit. I don't get greedy. Greedy gets you killed." Pavel's first skipper told him that; Pavel told her, her first week; she gives it back to the dark now and somewhere aft, if he can hear it through the steel, she hopes it lands.

She has line. The torch is Cole's to ration and she won't burn it for this — she'd rather have hands that know the dark than eyes that need a battery — so she works blind, which is the only way left to work. She runs a length of three-strand through her fist until she hits a splice she trusts. She finds the padeye overhead Pavel swore was sound the day he set it and decides to believe a dead-of-night memory of a man knocking behind a wall. She gets her boots set on the highest part of the grating, the water at her shins, and reaches up into the cold and the wet and the close steel smell of the pots, and the work begins.

It is bad work. It is the worst kind — fine work for fingers that are going, done above your own head, in a position that drains the blood out of your arms inside a minute, on a load that could drop while you're tied into it. She gets the bight of the strap over the third pot's frame by feel, hunting the gap between the net and the steel, her knuckles meeting cold tube and colder net and the slick of diesel that's gotten everywhere. The first time she misses the gap and the line slides off and she has to start again, and the second time her right hand won't quite close on the bitter end and she has to pin it against her palm with her thumb and feed it through that way, clumsy, a half-second behind where she's telling it to be. *The shunt's on,* she thinks, clinical, watching her own hand the way she'd watch a stranger's. *Ten minutes of grip and I've spent six of it.* She files the number. She keeps working.

"Talk to me," Cole says from the floor, stretched up off the grating as far as the two broken men will let him go, his fingers on the standing part below her, taking the weight as she frees it. "You go quiet up there I start making things up."

"I'm threading the bight around the third frame. It's around. I'm bringing the end down to you."

"I have it. Good. That's good. Now the fourth."

She works the bitter end down to him hand over hand, and below her she hears him take a turn around something solid and snub it, the old motion, a man making a line fast in the dark by the feel of his own fingers. There is a comfort in it she has no business taking and takes anyway. And somewhere under the comfort is the thing she's really leaning on, which is not Cole and not his easy voice — it's the memory of a man behind a wall, the boat learned by its bones across fifteen seasons. *Third pot's eye is good, fourth one's the rust.* She is rigging by Pavel's knowing even with Pavel sealed off where she can't reach him, the way you can stand on a floor someone else built and never see. This is the thing about a boat with a man like that on it: the dark gets smaller. It stops being an enemy with no edges and becomes a room with corners somebody once put a hand on. She has spent two seasons learning the *Adeline Marsh* by her sounds; Pavel spent fifteen learning her by her bones, and right now his bones are upside down and underwater and he still has them memorized behind that bulkhead, and she lets herself believe, for one breath, that competence is a kind of floor you can stand on.

It is the most dangerous thing she believes all night, and she won't understand that until later.

She moves down the run. The hull picks that moment to remind them what it is — a long low groan comes up through the grating into her boots, the deep iron note of a hundred tons of steel resettling, and the dome tips a degree under her reaching hand and the whole rack overhead shifts in its lashings and grumbles, and she freezes with her arm up inside it, one hand on the fourth pot's frame and the cage moving, actually moving, two inches toward her face and back, a thing the size of a refrigerator swinging slow on rotten rope a foot above her head.

"Ren." Cole, from the floor, sharp now, the warmth gone off it for the first time all night. "Get down. That's enough, that one's enough, get down off there."

"Not yet." Her voice comes out steady because she makes it. "I've got a hand on the fourth. Cole — take a strain on what we've got, slow, just enough — let me feel if the third holds when the fourth moves."

"Strain on," Cole says, and she feels the strap come tight under her hand, feels the third pot settle back against the new line, the load redistributing, a fraction of the weight coming off the old parted lashing and onto the strap she set. It holds. It actually holds. Something in her chest she didn't know was clenched lets go a quarter-turn. *That's it,* she thinks. *That's the buy. One more strap and we've got hours.*

"Marcus, sit *down,*" Cole says, off to the side, and under it Marcus is making a thin sound, a boy who has been told for an hour that breathing can be used up and is now using it up, and Sal is saying *easy, kid, easy, she's got it, the woman's got it* through his teeth, and the room is a held breath and Ren is at the top of it with her arms above her head and the cold three-quarters of the way into her hands.

She gets the bight around the fourth frame on the first try this time, fast, because she has the rhythm of it now, and she's bringing the end down to Cole's waiting hands and thinking *one more, then off, then dry ground for everybody, then I go to Sal* —

The second strand parts.

It's the same sound as the first, that high clean tick, and it comes from the old lashing on the third pot, the one she just took the strain off — except taking the strain off a load doesn't only relieve the old line, it *changes* the load, redistributes it, and somewhere up in the dark a strand that was holding one pot's worth is suddenly holding two, and it announces itself the only way it can.

"Off," she says. "Cole, hold what you've got and stay on the floor — that just shifted, the load just shifted —"

The third strand goes before she finishes the word.

It's not a tick this time. It's a run — *tick-tick-tick* in a fast falling line, three lashings letting go in under a second like buttons popping off a coat, and the whole rack over the shelf drops. Not all of it. A foot. A thousand pounds of gear falls a foot in the black and *catches* — catches on one line, one fouled length that snags and holds and brings the whole swinging mass up short with a jolt that comes down through the air itself, a pressure, a wind of displaced cold, and then the pots are swinging. Eight crab cages and a hydraulic block hung on a single strand, a wall of steel three thousand pounds heavy swinging slow through the dark over the only ground they have, and Ren can hear exactly where it is by the sound of it parting the air an arm's length from her face.

And she is under it.

She knows it the way she'd know it on a patient — the geometry, cold and instant. She walked herself to the third and fourth pots and that put her directly beneath the worst of the run, arms up inside the load, boots set on the high grating with nowhere fast to go. The swing of it crosses back toward her face and she feels the wind of it on her cheek and does the arithmetic she's spent all night doing for other people, and the number it gives her is *not enough room and not enough time,* and she has a half-second to spend, and she spends it the way she'd want it spent — flat and useful, no fear in it, just the body deciding which way to throw itself.

"Ren —"

It's Cole, off the floor now, breaking his own order, coming up off the grating toward the sound of her with both arms reaching — there is no stopping three thousand pounds with two hands that won't close and he reaches anyway, hunting her collar, the canvas of her bibs, anything, *get a fist in her and pull, that's all, pull her a foot, a foot is the whole difference* — and his hand finds the collar of her jacket, the wet heavy canvas of it, gets two fingers and then a fist into it, and he hauls with everything he has, throwing his own weight backward off the shelf, and she feels herself start to come.

She feels herself start to come.

And above them, in the dark, the one fouled strand that is holding three thousand pounds of swinging steel lets go.


---


# Chapter Six

## The Man Behind the Wall

*POV: Cole — T+4:00*

The sound the rack makes when it finally lets go is not the sound Cole has been bracing for. He's been waiting for a crash, for the big honest noise of a thing falling, and instead what comes is a kind of suck — a wet inrushing *whoomp* up there in the dark of the bilge-sky, the sound of a thousand pounds of pots dropping the last foot into the water all at once and shoving the water out of the way to make room for themselves.

And then the water is on him before he understands it's moving.

He has both arms around Sal and the kid, has had them there since the lashings started going, his back to the rack and his body between the load and the two men he's decided are his to keep — and the wave the falling gear pushes ahead of itself comes across the shelf at chest height and takes all three of them off their feet like a hand sweeping a table. Sal screams, the leg, the leg, and the scream goes underwater and turns to bubbles against Cole's chin. The kid claws. Cole gets a boot down on something, loses it, gets it down again on the grating, and *stands,* hauling the two of them up with him out of the black churn into the half a foot of foul air that's left between the new water and the steel, and he holds them there and counts in his head the way he counts a deck before a launch — what's where, what's loose, what's about to kill somebody.

He counts wrong, the first time, because the count he gets is *two.*

Sal, coughing, draped over his right arm, a dead weight that howls. The kid, Marcus, both hands fisted in Cole's collar, breathing in those high stabbing gulps. Two. He had four a minute ago. The fourth is Ren, who'd gone up on the high side under the run to work the lashings and to work the line through the gap in the bulkhead to the man on the far side of it — and now he has two in his arms and the dome is a foot lower than it was and there is a smell in the air that wasn't there before, a flat coppery undertone under the diesel that he knows and won't name yet.

"Ren," he says. Not loud. He doesn't have the air to be loud. "Ren, sound off."

A choke, off to his left, low, right at the new waterline. A wet tearing cough and then a voice with no words in it yet, just the machinery of someone trying to be a person again after drowning a little. It's her. He knows the sound. He's spent two seasons learning who the people on a boat are by the noises they make when they think no one's listening, and that ugly determined cough is Ren coming back up.

"That's it," he tells her, "that's the way, get it up, you're alright" — and it's the voice, the low easy one, the one for spooked greenhorns and green seas, and it works on her too, he can hear it work, the cough finding a rhythm, the rhythm finding the edge of words. *Four.* He has his four. The relief that goes through him is real and physical, a loosening behind the sternum, and he lets himself have it for exactly as long as it takes to ask the next question, because the next question is the one with the bad answer — and the bad answer isn't in this pocket. It's behind the wall.

"Pavel," he says, turned toward the gap in the flooded bulkhead, the throat of black water through which they'd been passing line and words to the fifth of them all night. "Pavel. Sound off."

Nothing.

"Pavel. You there?"

The hull drips. The water sloshes against itself in the close dark, finding its new level, lapping at his ribs. Sal has stopped screaming and started a low continuous groan that is somehow worse. The kid is saying *oh god oh god* in a small fast loop. And under all of it, the place across the bulkhead where Pavel's voice should be, where it had been an hour ago, dry and amused, talking them through which eyebolt was good — that place is just *flat.* A hole in the sound of the room. Cole knows that hole. He's stood on enough decks in enough weather to know the exact shape of the silence a man leaves when he's stopped being able to answer.

He thinks: *the run came down on the high side, by the bulkhead, and whatever it did over here it did worse over there — the gear-shed's a smaller gap and the shed's air had less to lose.* He builds it without wanting to, the way you can't not see a thing once you've started seeing it. The pots came down and the water shoved out and the surge drove through the gap into Pavel's little drowning pocket on the far side, the one they'd reached with line and a voice and could never reach with hands, and took the last of his air, or shifted the steel between them and pinned the gap shut, and either way the gap is silent now.

He could be wrong. He tells himself he could be wrong, that Pavel could be holding in some six-inch pocket of air that didn't go under, waiting, and the not-knowing is a real not-knowing — but the floor of him, the cold floor under the warm part, has already done the arithmetic and come out where it comes out, and he hates that the number is so clean.

"Cole." Ren, finding words. Closer now — she's hauled herself back toward the shelf, toward the sound of him, the way they all home on his voice now, which is a thing he's noticed and tried not to need. "Cole, the gap — I had the line to him. He had it on his end. The strand went up here and I let go to grab the rack and the line went *slack* —"

"I know," he says. "I know you had it."

"He's right *there,* he's right behind the steel —" and there's a splash, a hard committed splash, her going for the bulkhead gap, going for the black throat of it, and that's the moment Cole has to make the call that the whole rest of the night is going to be built on, and he makes it the way he makes all of them — fast, certain, and with a reason ready before anyone asks.

"Ren. *Ren.* Stop. Listen to my voice and stop."

She stops. He can hear her stop, treading, gasping, right at the edge of the churn where the gear came down.

"You go through that gap with no light, in this, you don't come back up," he says. "That's a fact and you know it's a fact. You've got maybe a minute of useful hands left and so do I and we are not spending them dying in a hole in a wall. Tell me where the gap is. Tell me the way through. I'll go."

It's true. Every word of it is true. That's the thing about Cole that even Cole half-admires in the worst moments — he never has to lie, not really, not about the load-bearing things; the truth is almost always already shaped like the thing he needs. She has a minute of hands. He has a minute of hands, and his are better in the water, salvage-diver's hands, breath he's kept since he was a boy under the docks in Anacortes pulling rich men's outboards off the bottom. He is the right man to go. He is, genuinely, the only man who *should* go.

And he is not going to find Pavel. He knows that too, knows it in the same breath as the offer, and he makes the offer anyway, and he goes under anyway, because going under is the thing a good man does and a good man is exactly what he needs to be seen to be.

"Low corner of the bulkhead, hard right," Ren says, fast, clinical, the panic shoved down where she keeps it. "The gap's maybe a foot and a half, down at the waterline, then it opens into the shed on the far side — eight, ten feet of flooded passage and you're in his pocket. The line runs straight to it. Follow the line. Cole —" and here her voice does something, snags on something — "don't. If you can't, *don't.* I'm not losing two."

"I'll be quick," he says, and takes the breath.

---

The taking of the breath is the part he's good at and the part nobody sees. You don't gulp it. A gulp is fear and fear burns air. You let the lungs fill from the bottom like a tank filling, slow, to the very top, past where it's comfortable, into the place where your body starts to argue, and then you hold a half-second more, and *then* you go, calm, with the whole tank, while the panickers are already half-empty from the gasping. Forty years he's known this. It's the one thing the sea ever taught him that he's sure is purely good.

The water closes over his head and the cold takes his face like a wet palm and the dark is total — not dim, total, the black of a flooded steel box ninety miles from a light. He goes down and finds the line where Ren said it would be, the slack line running out of their pocket into the wall, and he hauls himself along it to the gap — a foot and a half of jagged steel throat where the bulkhead never quite met the hull — and he turns his shoulders square and works through, the frame raking his slicker, into the gear-shed on the far side, and at the second handhold past the gap his knuckles find the first pot.

It's wrong, the way it sits. A crab pot is a seven-foot steel cube of frame and net and it has a logic to it, a way it lives on a deck, and this one is upside down and canted and jammed against three others in a pile that has no logic at all, a collapsed stack settling and ticking as the water finishes pulling it where it wants to go. The shed's whole load has come down through here, where it shouldn't have been able to reach him, and that tells Cole more than he wants to know about what the surge did on this side of the wall. He gets a mitt — his bare hand, already going to a mitt of meat that won't quite close — around a frame tube and pulls himself in along the pile, feeling, *feeling,* because there's nothing else to do but feel, and the cold is climbing his arms now and the breath in his chest has started its countdown, the slow squeeze, the number ticking down behind his eyes.

He finds Pavel by the boot.

It's the boot first — the deck boot, the steel toe, jammed up at an angle a leg doesn't make on its own, the foot pointing back over the shin — and Cole's hand runs up from the boot along the leg and the leg goes into the pile and the pile does not move. He braces and hauls. A thousand pounds of pots and net and the ice still riming the cages, all of it nested and locked, and Pavel somewhere in the middle of it, and Cole pulls until the dark goes grainy at the edges and the burn at the bottom of his lungs becomes a voice, and the pile shifts maybe an inch and settles back exactly where it was.

He finds the hand. He doesn't go looking for it; it finds him, drifting, the way a hand drifts in water when there's nothing left telling it where to be. He takes it. It's a thing he does without deciding to — takes Pavel's hand in the dark, the big square competent hand that knew where every shackle on the boat lived, and squeezes it, once, the only thing he has left to give, a thing nobody will ever see him do and that buys nothing and that he does anyway. There is a part of Cole that is real and good and entirely useless and it comes out exactly here, eight feet down in the black, holding a dead man's hand because the dead man was his and there was nothing else.

The hand doesn't squeeze back.

His lungs are screaming now and the dark is closing from the sides and somewhere back through the gap there is air, and his whole body has turned into a single demand to *go,* and he stays one more second, two, holding the hand, because leaving is a thing you should at least make yourself feel — and then he lets go, and turns, and works back along the line toward the gap and the foul little breath of the world, and the last thing the floor of him does, down there where he can't pretend he didn't, is feel the relief.

Not relief that Pavel is dead. He would never. He'd have given a year to free that pile.

Relief that of all the souls this hull is holding tonight, the one taken is the one whose eyes he never had to manage — the one on the far side of a wall, where Cole's voice couldn't reach to steady him and couldn't reach to shape what he saw. Pavel, fifteen seasons, the man who knew the load, the man who'd been on deck through the season same as Cole, who'd helped stack the pots high and grumbled about how high, who had a deckhand's whole quiet body of knowing about how heavy the *Adeline Marsh* rode and how late they ran the gear. Pavel, who if anyone off this boat ever sat in a Coast Guard chair and got asked *was she stable, was she loaded right, did the skipper hold course when he shouldn't have* — Pavel is the one who could have answered with the weight of fifteen seasons behind it.

The thought arrives whole and fast and obscene and Cole slams a door on it so hard he almost convinces himself the door was always shut, that he never thought it, that what he felt going up was just the body's animal *I'm not dead.* He almost convinces himself. He's good at almost.

His head breaks into the air on the near side of the gap and he hauls in a breath that tastes of diesel and copper and the inside of his own fear, and he says the first thing, which is the true thing, before anyone can ask:

"He's gone. The shed's load came down on him and the gap's shut and he's gone. I couldn't move it. Nobody could've moved it."

---

Nobody says anything for a long moment, and into that nothing the hull groans, low, settling another inch, and the water laps up his chest and Cole understands that the box has changed shape around them while he was under. The dome is lower. He can feel it without seeing it — the steel that was a comfortable arm's reach over his head when he stood is brushing the crown of his skull now, and the slope of it has come in on both sides, so that the standing room, the part where a man could be a man with his head up, has shrunk to a strip down the middle. The shed let go on the far side and killed Pavel, and the same shed dropped its run on this side too, and the pots that came down here didn't just miss them. They displaced their own volume of water up into the room, and they bled some unmeasured share of the air out through whatever seam the settle opened, and the result is the same either way: the box is smaller, and lower, and the water that was at their shins an hour ago is at their chests now, and there is less air over it, and the air over it has gone sour with the copper smell and with the close used reek of four sets of lungs working hard in a shrinking space.

He does the arithmetic he's good at — not the moral one, the loading one, the one a skipper runs without thinking, weight and space and what stays and what goes over the side. The room lost no one out of its own four and yet the night took a man anyway, the one behind the wall, and there's a corpse jammed in a flooded shed a foot and a half through the steel and no way to be in here without knowing it's there. The room lost a foot of air. The room got colder, because the wave soaked all of them to the neck and a soaked body in this air sheds heat like a hot pan in a sink, and every one of them has just had their cold-clock reset to zero at the worst possible moment.

And the count holds at four. Four living — Ren, the cook, the kid, himself — and one dead behind the bulkhead, and one of the four with a leg that's already a corpse from the hip down, and Cole runs that too, can't not run it, and what comes out is a thing he has no business being comforted by and is comforted by anyway, down in the sealed loading-deck floor of himself where the cold keeps its own books.

*The witness is the one the night took, and not one of mine.*

He hears Ren get herself up onto the shelf — the wet effort of it, the slosh, the way she gasps and then makes the gasp stop because she's decided it's not allowed. He hears her go to Sal in the dark, hears her hands start their work, the murmur, *don't move it, I've got you, talk to me,* the medicine she does instead of feeling things. He hears the kid crying now, properly, the dam gone, *Pavel, Pavel was just, he was right there, he was just talking to me,* and Cole moves to him through the chest-deep water and gets an arm around the boy and pulls him in against his own soaked cold body, which has no warmth to give and gives the shape of warmth anyway, the idea of it, and that turns out to be most of what the kid needs.

"I know," Cole says into the wet dark over the boy's head. "I know. He was a good hand. Best on the boat." True. All of it true.

"He didn't —" Marcus is shaking against him, the deep teeth-rattling shake that Cole notes with one cold corner of his attention is the shake of a body whose furnace is failing, *file that, deal with it next.* "Did he — was it fast? Did it hurt?"

And here is the thing Cole does, the thing he is for, the small mercy that is also the small management and he can no longer tell where one stops and the other starts because he stopped being able to tell years ago and that not-being-able-to-tell is the whole disease of him:

"It was fast," he says. Low. Certain. The voice he keeps level on purpose, because a level voice is a thing a frightened boy can lean his whole weight on. "The water and the cold, that fast, you don't even — it's like a light going off. He didn't suffer. I'd tell you if he did."

He doesn't know if it's true. He has no way on earth to know if it's true; he held a hand in the dark that didn't hold back and that's the entire sum of what he knows. But the boy needs it to be true, needs it the way you need a cleat in a blow, and Sal across the shelf has gone quiet to listen and needs it, and even Ren — he can feel her listening, the medic who knows exactly how a man drowns under steel in cold water and knows it is not a light going off, knows it is the body fighting every second of the way down — even Ren says nothing, lets it stand, because the lie is load-bearing and they are all of them hanging their weight on it.

"He didn't suffer," Cole says again, softer, and feels it catch in the room and hold, the way Ren's *nobody dies in the next hour* caught and held, and he thinks, in the part of him that's always taking the reading, *that's the trick, that's the only real tool there is in a bad night — give them the true thing they can stand on and don't make them stand on the true thing they can't.* He's proud of it, a little. He's always been a little proud of it. It's the gift Ferro saw in him green and made him for, the gift that's kept crews calm through weather that should've broken them, and it is the exact same gift, the precise identical muscle, that is right now keeping three frightened people from looking too hard at the man holding them.

He doesn't think that last part. He's careful, even here, even now, not to think that last part all the way to the end of the sentence.

---

The cold makes them count, after. It's Ren who starts it, because of course it's Ren, because counting heads is the thing she does the way he does the steadying — her hand on Sal and her face turned out to the dark, doing her census by breath.

"Sound off," she says. "I want to hear everyone. Sal."

"Here." Around his teeth, ragged, but here.

"Marcus."

"Here." Small. Wet. But here.

"Cole."

"Here," he says.

She doesn't call a fifth name. Nobody does. But the silence where Pavel's voice used to come through the gap sits in the middle of the room with them like a person who isn't breathing, and Cole listens to Ren *not* say *Pavel* and understands that she's stopped the roll at the four who answered on purpose, that she's the kind who'd rather leave the empty space empty than paper it over, and he files that about her too. She won't say a comfortable thing she doesn't mean. He's spent the night noticing she's the one person in the box he can't fully manage with a warm true-enough sentence, and the way she leaves Pavel's name lying unspoken in the dark is the proof, and it's a small cold problem he sets aside to think about later, when the immediate work of keeping four people alive isn't eating all the air there is.

"Four," Ren says, at last, flat, to the dark, the way you'd write a number on a chart. "We're four. In here, we're four."

"Four," Cole agrees, gentle, and means it as comfort, and means it as fact, and underneath, in the floor where the doors don't catch, means the other thing, the loading thing — not how many he has, but *who* he's lost. A man's gone tonight and it's the one man aboard who could have stood in a Coast Guard chair and named how she rode and how late they ran her, and that man died behind a wall where Cole's voice never had to manage his eyes. He hates how fast that came, that accounting. He has hated it the whole way up from the gap and he is hating it now and he is keeping it anyway, the way you keep a tool you're ashamed to own because the night is long and you know you'll need it before the night is done.

The water comes up another finger's width while they're sitting in the four of it.

Somewhere through the steel, in the black, in the cold, the shed's load ticks and settles, and Pavel rides where it decided to put him, and the *Adeline Marsh* sighs her long iron sigh and takes herself one more degree down toward the only resting place she's ever going to get — and Cole sits in the chest-deep dark with a freezing boy under one arm and a dying cook within reach of his hand and a medic across the shelf who counts the truth and won't round it up, and he does the only thing he has ever really known how to do, which is to be the calmest, most useful, most necessary man in a room full of people who are going to need him to stay exactly that until the very last second they have left.

"Alright," he says, low and easy, the hand on the tiller, the warm over the cold. "We're alright for now. Let's get the boy warm. Let's get to work."

And it's true, every word of it, which is the only thing that's ever kept his hands so much steadier than his conscience: he never has to make the comfort up. The night keeps handing him true things shaped exactly like the things he needs people to believe, and he takes them, and he passes them on, and he doesn't look too hard at the man in the middle doing the passing.


---


# Chapter Seven

## Jobs

*POV: Ren — T+5:00*

The first light is not light. It's the dark going one shade less than total, a greyness with no source, and Ren notices it the way she'd notice a pupil starting to answer — not because she can see anything but because the steel over her head has stopped being the same black as the steel beside her.

She holds still and lets her eyes argue with it. Above her and to the left, where the dome closes down toward the water, there's a seam — a hairline of grey laid along a weld the way frost lays along a window edge — and she understands she's looking at the underside of the morning. Somewhere up there, past the hull, past the inch of trapped air and the foot of steel, the sky that used to be over the boat has come up grey and is leaking a thread of itself in through a crack that water hasn't found yet. A day is starting. Out there it's a Tuesday. Somebody on St. Paul is making coffee.

She files the grey two ways at once, because that's the only way she knows how to hold anything now. The good way: it means dawn, it means a search aircraft can see, it means the worst of the first night is behind them. The bad way: it means the second night is ahead of them, and she's already learned what the box does to people in the dark, and now they'll all know exactly what's coming because they've felt the first one. Hope, in a place like this, is a thing you have to ration the same as the flares. Spend it wrong and the body that was holding on lets go.

She does her census before she does anything else. She's done it every time she's surfaced from the half-sleep that isn't sleep, the cold doze that the cold keeps interrupting, and she does it now: not by sight, still, by breath, because the grey is a rumor and not a working light.

Sal, to her right, against the high steel — the slow labored draw of a man whose body is spending everything it has on a leg it can't feel and can't forget. Marcus, nearer, the shake gone shallow and fast in his breathing, a thing she doesn't like and parks for the next ten minutes. Cole, beyond Marcus, that same even metronome she clocked in the first minute of all this, in, hold, out, a man asleep or a man pretending to sleep so the others can. Three breaths that aren't hers. She counts them the way she's counted them since the rack came down — three she's keeping, and her own underneath, and the cold flat arithmetic that the room used to be more than this.

Three. She lets the word be the word. She's stopped reaching for the fourth.

*The water,* she thinks, before anything else, because the water is the clock that doesn't lie. She'd marked it before she let herself doze — a rivet, a proud little dome of steel on a frame within reach of her hand, the water just under it. She finds the rivet now with two fingers that take a second too long to report back.

The water is over the rivet. Not by much. By a knuckle, maybe less, hard to say with fingers this slow. But over.

She does the math she doesn't want to do. Through the night the water held at their chests, give or take the slop, and she'd told herself it was holding because the tide had been going out when they rolled and an outgoing tide was the one mercy the sea had handed them. But a tide that goes out comes back. It's coming back now. The grey at the weld and the water over the rivet are the same fact wearing two faces: the world is turning, and the turning brings the sea up.

She doesn't say it yet. A figure spoken in the dark to frightened people is a weight, not a tool. She learned that the first hour and she hasn't unlearned it.

She makes her hands work instead.

---

There's a smell she's been trying not to name and now, fully awake, she names it: the air has gone thick. Not just the diesel and the copper and the herring stink that have been there since the start — those have a bottom to them, a fixed badness she's adjusted to. This is on top. A flatness. A used quality, like the inside of a sleeping bag in the morning, and with it, behind her eyes, the first dull pressure of a headache that has nothing to do with the cold.

She knows this one too. She knows it the way she knows the math of cold and the math of blood loss. Four people — three, three and her — breathing hard in a sealed steel box, and every breath they take is a breath that comes back at them a little worse, the air filling slowly with the thing they exhale, the sweet slow poison of themselves. It's the part of this she's least able to fight and most afraid of, because it doesn't announce itself like cold does, with a place on the body you can point to. It comes in as a headache and a heaviness and bad decisions. People stop making sense and don't know they've stopped. The box poisons you and convinces you, while it does it, that everything is fine.

There's a thing you can do about it. It surfaces out of some training she half-remembers, some cave-rescue refresher in a fluorescent room a thousand years and a thousand miles ago: the water will take the bad air out of the room, if you give it the chance. Cold water, especially. The gas wants to go into the water and the water wants to take it, and all that's standing between them is the flat unbroken surface. Break the surface and you give them more skin to trade across. Agitate the water and the room breathes a little easier.

It cuts exactly against the other thing she knows, which is to stay still. Still bodies burn less air. Still bodies make less heat-loss. Every motion is a withdrawal from accounts that are already overdrawn. And here's a task that says move, splash, work — spend heat and oxygen now to scrub out the poison that's building, on a bet that the trade comes out ahead.

She sits with the cost-benefit the way she'd sit with a drug dose. Too little and the box keeps poisoning them slow. Too much and she runs three failing furnaces into the ground chasing an enemy she can't see. The grey light helps her decide, a little — daylight means she can afford to spend, because if a plane is going to find them it'll be in daylight, and a clear head matters more than a conserved one if there's a chance of rescue to be clear-headed for.

"Marcus." She keeps it low. The kid's breathing changes — he wasn't asleep either, none of them really sleep. "You with me?"

"Yeah." A beat. "Yeah, I'm here. I keep — I keep thinking I hear an engine and then it's just the water."

"That's the boat settling, not an engine. You'll know an engine. An engine doesn't stop." She finds his shoulder in the grey-dark, the suit-bulk of him, and is glad of the suit, glad she put him in it, even as some back room of her keeps a tally she won't open. "I've got a job for you. A real one this time, not bailing."

"Bailing wasn't real?"

"Bailing was for your hands." She says it flat, no apology in it, because the kid is sharp under the green and a lie he can see through costs more than the truth. "This one's for all of us. The air's going bad — you feel the headache?" A pause; she feels him feel for it, find it. "That's the air. We breathe it out, it builds up, it makes us stupid, and stupid in here is how people die. But the water'll take it back out if we keep the top of it broken. So I need somebody slapping the water. Open hand, flat, regular, not hard — you'll wear out hard. Like this." She does it once, lays her palm flat and brings it down on the black surface, and the slap comes back at her instant and close off the steel, and the water she throws up lands cold on her own face. "Slow and steady. A stroke every couple seconds. When your arm tires, you switch arms. When both tire, you tell me and somebody spells you. You keep that surface moving, and you'll be the reason we can still think straight in three hours. That's the job. It's the most important one in the room."

It isn't, quite. The most important one is the dive she hasn't told them about yet. But it's important, and it's true enough, and the kid needs a thing that's his, needs it the way the shaking body needs the idea of warmth — and she watches the grey shape of him sit up a little, take the weight of being useful, and start to slap the water in a slow patient rhythm, *splash ... splash ... splash,* and the sound is awful and the sound is exactly right, the metronome of the only thing they can do to the air.

"Good," she says. "That's it. You're working now."

It's a small lie and a smaller mercy and it works like a rope, and she hates a little how good she's getting at this again, the thing she came to the Bering Sea specifically to stop doing — handing frightened people a job-shaped reason to keep their hands moving so their minds don't have time to do the math she's already done.

---

Sal is awake and has been listening, and when she moves to him his voice comes out of the grey dry as a cracker, the way it does when he's deciding to be funny so nobody has to deal with how much it hurts.

"He's gonna scrub the air," Sal says. "Twenty years I cook for crews and nobody once asks me to slap water for my supper. Should've been a scientist."

"You'd have been a terrible scientist."

"I'd have been a *great* scientist. I'd have invented a soup you can't capsize." The joke runs out of him at the end, the way they all do now, thinning into a long careful breath as the boat rocks and the leg answers the rock. She gets a hand to his thigh, light, just to read it, and what she reads she doesn't like — the swelling has gone past tight into something drum-hard, the cold slowing the bleed the way cold does and the slowed bleed packing the muscle into its own casing until the casing has no give left. Compartment. She knows the word and knows there's nothing in this box she can do about the word, and she files it with the other things she can't fix, the growing list of them, and keeps her voice level.

"I'm going to splint that," she says. "Properly. Traction. It'll hurt like hell and it'll stop it grinding every time the boat moves, and it'll buy the leg some time."

"How much time?"

"More than no splint." Which is the truest answer and the one he wanted and the one that tells him nothing, and he grunts, accepting the trade.

"You'll need Cole," Sal says. Not a question. There's something under it she doesn't have the spare attention to dig out — a flatness, the same flatness as the air. "For the pulling. You can't pull and set it both. You'll need his hands."

"I'll need his hands," she agrees, and doesn't hear, yet, that Sal has just told her something. That'll be later. Right now it's only a fact about hands. There are exactly two pairs of strong cold-working hands in this room, hers and Cole's, and everything she needs to do that's worth doing needs two pairs, and that means everything she needs to do needs him. She's known it since the first hour. She files it again anyway, the way you re-read a number to make sure it hasn't changed. It hasn't.

---

Cole comes awake — or stops pretending to be asleep, she still can't tell which and notes that she can't — when she says his name, and he comes awake the way he does everything, smooth, no startle, already a sentence into being helpful.

"What do you need," he says. Not *what's wrong.* *What do you need.* A man who's been listening with his eyes shut, building the morning's to-do list out of the sounds the room made while he rested.

She lays it out for him in the grey, low, the two of them with their heads close so the kid and the cook don't have to carry the planning on top of everything else. The air, the headache, the kid on the water. The tide — she tells him the tide, because Cole is a skipper and Cole already knows it's coming back and there's no point managing a man who can read the same water you can. He takes it without flinching, just a slow nod she feels more than sees.

"She'll come up a hand's worth before she turns again," Cole says. "Maybe more. Then back down. We've got room for that. Barely."

"Barely's the whole word."

"Barely's most words out here." He says it easy and it's the kind of thing he says, the folksy old-hand wisdom that goes down like something true, and she lets it, because it is mostly true and because she needs his head clear for the part that matters and you don't pick at a man right before you ask him for the hardest thing.

She asks him for the hardest thing.

"The suits," she says. "We need the locker. We've got the kid in the torn-up neoprene I cut and that's keeping his core off the worst of it, but it's not a suit, it's a blanket, and Sal's got nothing, and you and I have got nothing, and the water's coming up. There are suits in that locker across the passage. There's a strobe. There's flares we haven't got. We need somebody to go get them."

The grey is just enough now that she can almost see his face — the suggestion of it, the dark plane of beard, the place his eyes are. She watches the place his eyes are, the way she's been watching it since the first count.

"It's a free-dive," Cole says. Not arguing. Walking the ground. "Twenty-some feet, the dog-leg around the reefer trunk, no light, in this water. You'd want it on slack — minimum current, the hull's not pumping water through the passage every roll. When's slack?"

"You tell me. You're the skipper."

A breath. She files the breath. "Tide turned to flood not long ago, by the feel. So we're an hour, ninety minutes off the bottom of the next slack. Maybe less." He's quiet a second, and she can hear him doing the same arithmetic she's already done, and she waits to see if he comes out where she came out. "Sooner's better," he says. "Not for the slack. For us. We dive that passage now, while there's still a diver in the room who can hold his breath and close his hands. We wait six hours for some perfect window, the water's higher, the diver's colder, the diver's stupider from the bad air. The window doesn't get better. We do."

He's come out exactly where she came out. That's the thing about Cole — she keeps setting traps of competence for him, little tests where the right answer and the self-serving answer might point different ways, and they never do, he just keeps being right, and the relief of it is a problem she hasn't named.

"That's what I had," she says. "Go on the next slack. Don't wait for better, there isn't better."

"There isn't better," Cole agrees, soft, and then, because he is who he is, he reaches past her for the thing before she can hand it to anyone: "Let me take it."

---

She doesn't answer right away.

It's the right call and she knows it's the right call and that's exactly what makes her sit on it a second. Cole is the stronger diver — he came up with that admission himself, days ago it feels like, last night, when he went under for Pavel and came up with nothing but the truth that nobody could've moved the pile. Salvage-diver's lungs, he'd said. A boy under the docks in Anacortes. She'd believed it then and believes it now and it's the believing that's the burr under everything: he is, genuinely, the only one who *should* go. Her own hands are the second-best hands in the box for almost every job and the second-best lungs by a wide margin, and the box has exactly enough margin for the best tool on the worst task and not a hair more.

And the room exhales when he offers. She hears it — Sal's careful breath letting go a notch, the kid's slapping rhythm steadying — the sound of frightened people hearing the strong calm man step up to the dark water, take it off their hands, make it his problem instead of theirs. It's a beautiful sound. It's the sound of a load coming off a deck. She feels it pull at her own chest too, the same animal slackening she felt in the first hour when his voice took the wheel of the black, and that's the part she makes herself look at, because looking at things is the only tool she trusts.

She names it for herself, clinical, the way she'd name a vital sign she didn't like the look of. *Relief.* The most expensive thing a body can spend in a place like this. Relief is the emotion that tells you to stop working — it's the unclench, the let-down, the *somebody else has it,* and somebody-else-has-it is how a triage misses the patient still bleeding under the calm. She's spent two seasons not feeling relief and now she's feeling it, here, in the worst room she's ever stood in, and she's feeling it *at Cole,* aiming it at the one man whose breathing was already managing itself in the first minute, the one steady reading on a chart she never did learn to read.

She wonders, in the grey, with the water over the rivet and the air going thick and the second night out there waiting past the thin grey thread of the morning, why she's spending her most expensive feeling on him. She doesn't have an answer. She has a note, a small wrongness filed next to the others, and a dive to plan, and a leg to set, and a kid slapping black water in the dark to keep them all able to think.

"Alright," Ren says. "You take it. Next slack. We'll rig you a tether — I'm not having you go into that passage on nothing." She turns her face to the grey seam, to the rumor of the morning leaking in through a crack the water hasn't reached yet. "Everybody's got a job now. Marcus has the air. Sal's got the leg coming. Cole's got the dive."

"And you?" Cole says, easy, warm, the question a man asks who already knows the answer is going to be a thing he can lean on.

"I'll hold the rope," she says.

She finds the tether line in the tangle by her knee — slick, taut, cold enough to take the heat out of her slow fingers — and starts, by feel, to make it into something that will hold a man's weight against the dark. *Splash ... splash ... splash,* the kid keeps the surface moving. Sal breathes his careful broken breath. Cole settles back to wait, that even metronome already gone quiet again, a man banking what he'll need.

She works the line and lets her body do its other arithmetic, the kind it does without asking. The water is over the rivet by a knuckle and a half now, and rising, and it will not stop rising until the tide decides to turn, and the tide turns when it turns and not for them. Somewhere behind her ribs the cold has gotten past the wrists and into the long bones, and she clocks it the way she clocks everything, a reading on a chart, *core's going,* and parks it, because there is nothing on the list before the dive and the dive is an hour out.

An hour. She lets the number sit. In an hour the water will be higher and the diver colder and the air thicker, and the window will not have gotten better — it never gets better, Cole said it himself and he was right, he is always right — it will only have gotten later. The grey at the weld is wider already. A day is starting out there, a Tuesday, somebody on St. Paul pouring coffee, and in here the only thing that is starting is the count to the one dive that buys them the suits, the strobe, the flares, the chance — the dive that spends the best diver in the box on the worst job in it, on a tether she is knotting now with fingers that take a second too long to report.

She finishes the knot. She tests it against her own weight, twice, the way you re-read a number to be sure it hasn't changed.

It holds. The water comes up another knuckle.

And she sits in the thin grey morning with the rope in her ruined hands and waits for the slack, for the clock at the bottom of the tide to come round, for the hour to spend itself down to the minute she sends a man she is starting to lean on into the dark to hold his breath against a door that does not care whether his hands close.


---


# Chapter Eight

## The Passage

*POV: Cole — T+6:30*

Slack water is a thing you have to take on faith down here, because there's no horizon to read it off, no buoy line going slack and then standing up again, no way to watch the sea quit pushing. You take it off the boat. Cole has had his hand flat against the steel rib by the mouth of the passage for the last twenty minutes, feeling the current pulse against it with each long roll of the hull, the water surging up the throat of the companionway and falling back, surging and falling, and a little while ago the surges started coming softer and farther apart. That's the tide turning its weight over in its sleep. That's as close to slack as the dark is going to tell him, and it won't last long, and he's the one who has to spend it.

"Now," he says. "It's flattening. We go now or we wait six hours for the next one, and in six hours the water's higher and we're colder. Now's the cheap one."

Nobody argues. Ren doesn't argue because she did the same arithmetic an hour ago and came out where he came out, and the other two don't argue because they have nothing to put in the scale. He hears Ren shift on the shelf, hears her hands moving — checking the tether knot at his waist for the third time, a flat bowline she tied herself and tested with her whole weight, because she doesn't trust a knot she didn't tie and he respects that, he'd be the same.

"Twenty-two feet," she says. Quiet, close to his ear, the briefing voice, all the panic filed away somewhere she keeps it. "One dog-leg. The reefer trunk's the corner — you'll feel it, big round steel, you go left around it, down and left because we're upside down. Locker's on the far wall past the trunk. Dogs on the door, four of them, you said."

"Four. Quarter-turn each. They'll be stiff."

"You feel the door won't come in two pulls, you come back. You hear me? Two pulls and you come back and we figure it out. I'm not pulling a drowned man up this rope."

"You won't," he says, and gives her the easy voice, the one that's a hand on the shoulder, and feels it do its work even on her, even now — feels her let out a breath she'd been keeping. *Two pulls and back* is good sense and he has no intention of obeying it and he doesn't tell her that. A man who gives himself an honest two pulls on a stuck door in a place like this comes home with nothing, and they need the suits more than they need his caution. He'll know the difference between *stuck* and *done* when his hands are on it. He always has.

He doesn't say the other thing either, the thing about where the passage goes. Because the passage doesn't only go to the suit locker. Past the reefer trunk, before the locker, the dog-leg opens off toward the wheelhouse ladder and the chart flat and the dark drowned spaces where the boat keeps her paperwork, and a man who knows this boat knows that, and Cole knows this boat. He's careful not to know it out loud. He files the geography the way you file a card you don't intend to play — face down, where you don't have to look at it.

"Tugs," Ren says. "One tug, I'm at the door. Two, I've got it open. Three, I'm coming back loaded, haul me. Anything fast and hard and you pull me the hell out, no questions. Say it back."

"One at the door. Two, it's open. Three, haul. Fast-and-hard, you pull." He flexes his hands in the black, opens and closes them against the cold to chase what blood he can down into the fingers while they still answer. They answer slow. Six and a half hours in this air has done to his hands what the season's first frosts do to a hose left out — stiff, half a beat behind his wanting them. He has maybe a minute of useful fingers in that water. Maybe less. He's not going to get a second dive out of these hands today and he knows it, so this one has to be the whole grocery list.

"Kid," he says. "Marcus. Where's the strobe staging — you got the line ready to feed me?"

"Yeah." The boy's voice is a wrecked small thing but it's holding, and giving him a job to hold it with was the right call. Marcus is sitting at the shelf edge with the tether coiled across his knees, his hands on it, ready to pay it out so it doesn't snag or drag. A useless-enough job — Ren's the anchor, Ren's the one with the strength to haul — but the boy's hands need to be on the rope. Busy hands don't shake as bad. Cole learned that off Ferro thirty years ago and it's never once been wrong.

"Sal."

"Go get my Gumby suit, you cheap bastard," Sal says from the dark, "and don't you come back without it," and it's meant to be a joke and three-quarters of it is and the last quarter is a man whose leg is dying asking for the only thing that might keep the rest of him. Cole gives him the laugh he's asking for, short and warm, because the laugh costs nothing and it's worth something.

"On the list," Cole says. "Top of the list. Alright." He gets a hand on the cold lip of steel at the mouth of the passage, where the dome closes down into the black water and the throat begins. "Going down. Count me. Somebody count me out loud so I've got a clock when I come back up."

"I've got you," Ren says. "I'll count. Go on your own breath, not mine."

He takes the breath.

---

The taking of the breath is the part nobody sees and the part he's better at than anything else he's ever done.

You don't gulp. A gulp is fear and fear is a fire you light in your own chest that burns the very air you're trying to bank. You stand still and you let the lungs fill from the bottom the way a tank fills, slow, no hurry, up past the easy fullness and into the place where the body starts to file its first small complaints, and then a half-breath past *that,* into the part that aches a little, and you hold it there and let the ache settle and become ordinary. Forty years of this. Salvage water off Anacortes as a kid, pulling rich men's lower units off the mud for ten dollars apiece, learning that the boys who came up first were the boys who went down scared. He went down calm. He has always gone down calm. It's the one clean thing the sea ever gave him and he's never been sure he deserved it and he's never once turned it down.

The water closes over his head and the cold takes his face like a wet hand laid flat across it, and the dark goes from the dark of the bubble — which has edges, which has the men breathing in it — to the dark of the drowned passage, which has none. This is the true black. Ninety miles from a light and twenty-two feet of flooded steel between him and the next air, and his eyes are open and giving him nothing, just the faint red churn his own brain paints on the inside of the lids because it can't stand to send him pure nothing.

He goes down and forward along the throat, hand over hand, counting the handholds the way Ren counts heads. A frame. A pipe run. The lip of a doorway gone sideways. The cold is in his wrists before the third handhold and that's the clock starting, that's the body shunting the warm in toward the heart and leaving the hands to fend, and behind his eyes a number ticks down that he doesn't let himself look at directly, he only lets himself feel it — the slow squeeze beginning low in the chest, the lungs starting their long quiet argument for the surface.

The reefer trunk finds him before he finds it — a great round wall of insulated steel where the passage doglegs, and he gets both hands on the curve of it and works around to the left and down, *down and left because we're upside down,* and there's a half-second where the inverted geometry tries to throw him, where his body insists the trunk should curve the other way and the locker should be up not down, and he kills the insistence the way he kills everything inconvenient, fast, without arguing, and trusts the chart in his head over the lie in his gut.

And the chart in his head, here, against his will, lays the whole flat out for him. Around the trunk to the left: the suits. Around the trunk to the *right*, twelve feet on, down a short ladder gone wrong: the chart flat, the radio nook, the float-free EPIRB's bracket on the wheelhouse skin, all of it drowned and black and reachable, by a man with the lungs for it, if a man wanted to reach it.

He goes left. He goes left because the suits are left and the suits are the job and three people need the suits, and that is true, that is so completely true that he can hold it up in front of the other thing like a hand in front of a candle and not see the candle at all. He's good at that. He's better at that than he is at diving and he's very good at diving.

His knuckles find the far wall and walk it and find the locker door.

It's where she said. Where *he* said, really — he's the one who knew the locker was here, knew it the way you know your own kitchen in the dark, and he let Ren say it back to him in the briefing so it would be her knowledge in the room and not his, a small free trade, the kind he makes without deciding to anymore. The door's gone sideways with the boat, the four dogs ranged down what used to be its edge, and he gets his bad cold meat of a hand around the first dog and it won't turn.

It won't turn, and the chest-squeeze has graduated from a complaint to a demand, and somewhere up a rope a woman is counting him and a boy is feeding him line and a cook is waiting for the thing that might save his leg, and Cole does the cost-benefit the way he does all of them, fast and certain — the dog's just stiff, it's not seized, stiff is a *yes* you have to spend on, seized is a *no* you have to respect — and he braces his frozen fingers and his failing breath against the one quarter-turn and *spends.*

It gives. Grinds a quarter-turn and gives. Second dog, the same — brace, burn, give. The lungs are loud now. The number behind his eyes is small. Third dog he can't get a grip on at all with the right hand, the fingers have quit closing, so he switches, works it with the left and the heel of his palm, and it gives, three of four, and the door shifts in its frame, a slack give that means the seal's broken and the last dog is just along for the ride. He gets a hand into the gap and hauls and the door swings out into the water slow and heavy as a thing that doesn't want to be opened, and the locker breathes its trapped cold out at him, and Cole reaches in.

His hands know the suits before his mind names them. The neoprene, the heft, the lumpy folded weight of a Gumby in its bag — he gets a fist into the first one and drags it free and there's a second behind it and he gets that one too, both bags by their straps in his left fist now, and his right goes deeper for the third and finds it and the third is wrong. Slack where it should be packed, a long gone-soft tear down the side of the bag and the suit inside it slumped and waterlogged, the neoprene split. Torn. He knows it by feel the way Ren would know a cold pulse, *this one's dead,* and he takes it anyway because torn neoprene is still neoprene and a cold man will take what he can get, and now he's got three suit-bags in two hands that barely close and the breath is nearly gone.

Deeper still, the locker holds its other small treasures and he sweeps for them blind — the hard plastic tube of the flare canister, he gets that, jams it under his arm; the squared-off shape of the strobe, that too, into the crook of his elbow; and his sweeping hand brushes something that isn't gear, something with give to it, something that turns slowly toward his touch in the water.

He knows what it is before he lets himself know.

Soft. Small. The wet weight of fur gone heavy. A collar with a tag on it that ticks once against his frozen knuckle, a little brass tick in the black, the loudest small sound he's heard all night.

Biscuit.

The dog must have been thrown in here in the roll, in through the open locker or chased in by the water, the way a small terrified thing runs toward the last dark hole when the world ends — and got shut in, or trapped behind the door, and drowned in the cold while four people up a flooded passage were learning each other's names. Cole's lungs are screaming and the black is coming in from the sides the way it does when the breath is spent, the grainy closing-down, and a smart man, a man doing only the arithmetic, leaves the dog. The dog buys nothing. The dog is weight, and he is at the bottom of his breath with three suits and a flare canister and a strobe and twenty-two feet of black passage between him and the air.

He takes the dog.

He gets his ruined right hand into the wet fur and the collar and he takes the dog, tucks the small drowned body against his chest under the arm that's already full, and turns, and that turn costs him a second he doesn't have, and starts back up the passage with his hands full of every reason these people might live and one small dead thing that's no reason at all.

It is the only part of the dive he does for free.

---

The way back is worse than the way down, the way it always is, because the body has spent its calm on the going and has nothing left for the coming but want. The squeeze is a fist now. The dark behind his eyes has stopped being dark and started being a kind of loud — a high singing pressure, the brain beginning to gripe in earnest about the air it isn't getting — and the geometry tries to lie to him again at the reefer trunk, tries to send him right, *down and right,* toward the chart flat, and for one bad swimming half-second the lie and the truth and the dwindling air all tangle and he could go the wrong way, he could go toward the thing he isn't reaching for, and the body in this state will follow the strongest pull and the strongest pull is *anywhere, just go.*

He gets a hand on the trunk and stops. One second. Burns it. Sets the chart in his head straight — *the air is back the way you came, left around the trunk going down means right around it coming up, the candle's behind the hand, don't look at the candle* — and he goes right around the trunk and up the throat toward the place his own starved bubbles are crawling, and he does not, on the way past, reach for anything in the dark to his left that a Coast Guard man might one day ask him about. He's so far past thinking it that it isn't even a refusal anymore. It's just the shape his swimming has, the channel his whole night's been cut into, and the water carries him up it like it agrees with him.

Three tugs he never sends — there's no hand free to send them, both fists full, and anyway the rope's gone slack and snaking because Ren's already feeling the change in him, the way the line stopped paying out and started coming back, and he feels the first pull of her hauling, taking up his weight, and he kicks into it.

His head breaks the surface into the foul little breath of the world and he hauls in air that tastes of diesel and copper and three people's fear, and it is the best thing he has ever tasted, every single time, the air at the top of a dive, and he hates a little how good it is because a thing shouldn't feel that good in a place this bad.

"Got 'em," he gets out, around the heaving. "Got the suits."

Hands take the bags off him — Ren's hands, fast and sure, stripping his arms of their load before he's even fully up, and he hears her count them by feel as they come, *one, two,* and then the small catch in her when the third one comes soft and slack in her grip, *and torn,* she says, flat, filing it, and he hears her set the flare canister and the strobe up onto the dry of the shelf with the careful click of someone who knows exactly what each thing is worth.

And then there's the dog.

He's still got the dog. He surfaces with the dog tucked to his chest and his arms empty of everything else, and for a second nobody understands the shape of what he's holding, and then Marcus does — Marcus, nineteen, who fed the little mongrel galley scraps all season against the rules — Marcus makes a sound Cole won't forget, a short broken-off *no* with nothing behind it, just the air leaving him.

"He was in the locker," Cole says. Low. Easy. The hand on the tiller, even here. "He must've run in when she went over. He didn't — it was quick, in the cold, it's quick." He doesn't know if it's quick. He held a dog's drowned weight in the dark and he doesn't know one true thing about how it went and he says *it's quick* anyway, the load-bearing lie, the one tool he's sure of, and he lifts the small body up out of the water and lays it on the dry shelf as gently as he's ever laid anything down in his life.

He arranges the little legs. He doesn't have to. Nobody asked him to and nobody can see him do it and it buys not one degree of warmth or one minute of air, and he does it anyway — straightens the wet fur, lays the head right, closes the locker on his own ruined hands long enough to give a drowned dog the dignity of looking asleep instead of looking like what it is. There's a part of Cole that's real and good and entirely useless, and it has come out exactly twice tonight, both times in the dark, both times over the dead, both times for nothing. He doesn't examine why it only ever comes out for the dead. He's careful, even now, not to follow that thought to the end of its sentence.

"Okay," he says, and his voice has found its level again, the warm laid back down over the cold. "Okay. Biscuit's out of the water. He's not in the cold anymore." Which is the kind thing to say and is even true and is also, he knows, the thing that gets the boy past the dog and back to the living, and he can no longer tell which of those three reasons he's saying it for, and he stopped being able to tell years ago, and that not-being-able-to-tell is the whole long quiet disease of him.

---

They get the dog covered with a scrap of net. Ren works fast in the dark, the way she does her grieving — with her hands, on a task, never on the feeling. He hears her go down the manifest of what he brought up like she's reading off a chart: two suits, intact, sealed dry in their bags, *good, those are good;* one suit torn, which she's already cutting open by feel, talking half to herself, *neoprene's still neoprene, I can wrap the worst-off in this, it's not nothing;* a flare canister, count unknown till there's light to count by; a strobe, battery unknown. She stages the strobe up at the high point of the passage mouth where she says a swimmer coming up the throat from outside might see it, and the *if* in that, the enormous *if* of anyone ever coming up the throat from outside, sits in the dark and nobody touches it.

And while she's doing all that, in the few feet of cold between the shelf and the passage, Cole does the arithmetic she hasn't done yet. Or she's done it and won't say it, which would be like her — she'd hold the bad figure as a shape and not a number, the way she held *how long* on the first night. But Cole's already past the shape and into the number, because the number is the thing he can't not see, the loading-arithmetic, the skipper's reflex that runs whether he wants it to or not: what's aboard, what's the weight, what stays, what goes over the side.

Two suits. Three people.

He lays it out in the dark behind his eyes, clean and cold, the way he'd lay out a deck before a launch. Two intact immersion suits — six hours of life apiece in this water, more if a man's lucky, the warmest thing that's going to exist in this box. Three living bodies to put them on. And the torn one, the third one, the one he carried up that won't seal and won't hold heat, isn't a suit, it's a blanket, and a blanket in this is a kindness, not a save.

So the suits go on two of three. That's the math and there's no soft way to hold it. And his mind does the next thing without being asked, ranks the three the way the cold's going to rank them whether anyone votes on it or not. The kid — young, thin, no fat on him, furnace already failing, the body that'll lose heat fastest; the kid needs a suit or the kid is the first to go, and a boy you saved is a good thing to have when there are questions later, and Cole notes that he had that second thought right alongside the first and slams the door on it and keeps the first. The cook — can't move, can't make his own heat, a leg that's a corpse from the hip down already pumping cold dead blood back toward a heart that doesn't need the help; Sal in a suit might last to rescue, Sal without one almost surely won't.

Which is two. The kid and the cook. The two who can't help themselves. The two who, it does not escape the cold floor of him, are also the two whose survival he most needs to be standing next to when the boat finally gives somebody up to a clean dry Coast Guard chair and a list of questions about how heavy she ran and how late they held the gear — a grateful rescued boy and a cook in a borrowed suit, both of them alive because Cole's hands went down the black passage. He has that thought and he is ashamed of it and he keeps it, because the night is long and a man keeps his tools.

And that leaves the third body. The two strong divers, him and Ren, are the ones who can do without — *have* to do without, because somebody has to be able to go back in the water, and the suited bodies can't dive in a Gumby and the cook can't dive at all. So it's him and Ren in their wet clothes for the rest of it, sharing the torn-suit blanket and what heat their bodies still make, and that's correct, that's the only allocation that works, and he'll be the first to say it when Ren gets to the question, he'll say it warm and easy and like it was obvious, *give 'em to Sal and the kid, we're the ones who can take it.* It'll sound like generosity. It is generosity, mostly. It's also a man making sure the two suits go exactly where his story needs them to go, and Cole has long since stopped being able to find the seam between those two things, and tonight, in the chest-deep cold with a drowned dog under a scrap of net and a passage at his back that he can never close again — because that door is open now, that throat is open, and he can feel it already, the cold coming up out of it stronger than before, the box's last warmth starting to bleed down the open passage into the sea — tonight he doesn't even look for the seam.

He just does the arithmetic, and gets the answer everyone's going to get eventually, and gets it first, and sits with it in the dark a while before anyone else arrives.

"Ren," he says, when he hears her finish staging the strobe. Gentle. The hand on the tiller, laid down soft over a thing that isn't soft at all. "When you've got a second. We need to talk about the suits. There's only the two."

"I know," she says, out of the black, flat as the weld seam, not rounding it up. "Two suits, three of us. I've been doing the math since you went down."

"Yeah," Cole says.

"And one of us with a leg that's already dead," she says, and there it is, she's gone straight to the bottom of it, she went under the shape and got the number same as he did, and he should have known she would, the medic who counts the truth and won't paper it. "So really it's two suits and two and a half people, and we both know which half doesn't get one."
