The water comes up another finger's width while they sit in that, the open passage breathing cold up out of the dark at their backs, and somewhere under the scrap of net a small dead thing keeps the dignity Cole gave it, and Cole listens to Ren say the thing he carried up the passage in his chest, the thing he had first and kept to himself, and understands that the only person in this box who does the arithmetic as cold and as fast as he does is the one person in it he can't manage with a warm true-enough sentence — and that she just did the suit math out loud, in the open, the way he never would, and put the dead half of it right there on the shelf next to the dog where everyone could see it.

"Two and a half," Cole agrees, low and easy, the warm laid carefully over the cold. "Let's see what we can do about the half."

It's not a lie. He means to do everything he can for Sal's leg, with his own two hands, all the way to the end. That's the terrible part, the part that even Cole, in the cold floor of himself where the doors don't catch, can't quite get comfortable with: he means it, and it's still the most useful thing he could possibly be seen to do, and he can't tell anymore which is why he's going to do it.

He flexes his dead hands in the dark and waits for the blood to come back into them, because there's a leg to splint and a boy to warm and a long night still to be the most necessary man in, and the passage at his back breathes its new cold, and won't ever close again, and that's the price of the suits, already coming due, right on schedule.


---


# Chapter Nine

## Two Suits, Three People

*POV: Ren — T+7:30*

The suits come up off the shelf orange even in the dark, or Ren's eyes invent the orange, because she knows the color and the mind hands you the color you expect when it has nothing real to go on. They are heavier than they should be. That's the first true thing her hands tell her: a survival suit is a buoyant skin, a thing built to want the surface, and these two have come back from the passage soaked through the seams and along the long zips, and a wet suit is a slow suit, and a slow suit is a problem she'll have to solve in the dark with fingers that quit on her ten minutes after she starts.

Cole laid them out before he went under for Sal's traction setup — two whole ones, one torn, the flares in their little waxed tube, the strobe, and the dog. He laid Biscuit on the high end of the shelf the way you'd lay a sleeping thing you didn't want to wake, and nobody has moved her, and Ren is not going to be the one to move her yet. The dead get to keep their place a while. It's the one luxury the box still affords.

She runs her hands over the first suit, learning it by feel the way she learned the air pocket — by reaching, by the slope and the give of the thing. Neoprene, the lumpy mitt at the end of each arm with its single split for the thumb, the hood folded back into the collar, the long diagonal zip that's the whole game. She finds the zip-pull. Drags it a few inches. It catches, grinds, gives. Salt and grit in the teeth already. *Don the suit dry and sealed and it'll hold heat a day or better. Don it wet, half-zipped, and it's a wetsuit you bail out of.* The research lives in her hands from a safety course in another life, a classroom with a dummy and a kiddie pool and an instructor with a stopwatch saying *two minutes, people, two minutes or you're a statistic,* and she had laughed about it on the bus home and she is not laughing now.

"Two," Cole says. He's surfaced from Sal's leg, breathing it back, the warm easy voice with the work under it. "Two good ones and the one that's no good as a suit. I make it two."

"Two," Ren says.

Neither of them says the rest of the sentence, which is *and three of us*, because the sentence finishes itself in the dark and finishes the same for both of them and there's no profit in saying out loud a thing the cold has already said.

---

She does the arithmetic the way she was trained to, which is fast and out loud, because a number you keep in your head you can flinch from and a number you say in front of people you have to stand behind.

"Talk it through with me," she says, to the room, to Cole, to whoever's still got the wits. "Two suits. Three bodies that need them and one of those bodies is going back in the water before this is over." She doesn't name the fourth thing, which is that there are four of them up here and only three need debating, because she already knows where she sits in her own arithmetic and she's seated herself last on purpose, the way the cook serves himself last, and she'll examine why she did that some other hour.

"Sal can't make heat," she says. Her hand is on his shin, the good one, and even the good one is cold as a railing. "He can't move, he can't shiver hard enough to matter, the leg's eating his blood and his core both. He's the one losing fastest and he's the one who can do the least about it. A suit on Sal is the highest return there is." She says it flat, a chart entry, and across the shelf Sal makes a sound that isn't quite a laugh.

"Don't talk about me like I'm the math problem," Sal says.

"You're the math problem," Ren says. "You're a good one, though. You score high."

"Christ," Sal says, and lets his head go back against the steel, and there's a wet thread of something in his voice that might be relief and might just be the leg. "Put it on the kid. Marcus. Put it on Marcus."

She'd already moved there in her head and hearing Sal say it does a small ugly thing to her, because Sal saying *put it on the kid, not me* is Sal voting himself off, Sal doing the cook's-portion thing, the last-served thing, and a man who starts giving away his survival in the dark is a man who's started to let go, and she clocks that the way she'd clock a dropping pressure — not a crisis yet, a direction. People quit out loud first and quiet last. Sal's quitting out loud, generous, which means there's still some fight in him to spend.

"You don't get to give yours away," she tells him. "That's not on offer. You don't have the heat to be a hero with."

She turns to Marcus.

He's against Cole's side where Cole put him an hour ago, wedged into the strip of standing air, and Ren gets her hands on him to read him and the reading is the thing she's been not-reading since the rack came down, because she has been busy, because there has been a body to recover she couldn't recover and a dive to plan and a leg to keep, and Marcus has been the corner of the room she kept telling herself she'd get to.

She gets to him now.

He's shaking. That's good. She holds onto that the way she's been taught to: shivering is the furnace lit, shivering is the body still arguing its case, and a kid who shakes is a kid who's still in the fight. But she has hands that have spent thirty-one years and six of them in the back of an ambulance learning the grammar of shaking, and this shaking has changed since she last had her hands on him. It's coarser. Slower. It comes in bouts now instead of the steady high rattle of two hours ago, a long shudder and then a lull that's almost worse, the lull where the body rests between rounds like a fighter who's started to lose them. His hands, when she finds them, are wooden. His speech, when he answers her, has a slur in it, the *umbles*, the stumble in the mouth that means the cold's gotten up past the hands and the feet and into the wiring.

*Mild going moderate,* she thinks. *He's at the top of the slope and the slope only goes one way.* And she does not say it, because saying it is a verdict and she swore off verdicts, and because the suit is the intervention and she has the suit in her hands.

"Marcus. Hey. Stay with me, talk to me. What's my name."

"R-Ren." The R goes on too long. "You're R-Ren. You're the — you're the medic." A pause, the shaking taking him, riding it out. "Am I — is it my t-turn for a job? I can do a job."

"Your job," she says, "is to get into this suit and let it work. That's the whole job. Best one on the boat."

"That's wh-what Cole said about Pavel," Marcus says, and the name drops into the dark and sits there, and nobody picks it up.

---

Getting a hypothermic nineteen-year-old into a wet survival suit in the black with hands that don't work is not a heroic act and there is no part of it that looks like one. It is a wrestling match with a wet octopus conducted by two people who can't see and a third who can't help and won't hold still.

She does it by feel and by system. Boots first — the suit's integrated feet, she has to get his deck boots off first, and his boots are half-frozen to his socks and the laces are ice and her fingers have started the lag, the half-second delay between wanting and doing that means the ten minutes are spending. She gets a knife. Cuts the laces. Peels the boots. His feet come out white and waxy and she does not let herself think the word for white-and-waxy because the word is a verdict.

"Cole. I need your hands on the zip side. I'm going to feed him in legs first and you're going to keep the suit from twisting, and when I say *up* we sit him up together and shoot the arms through, fast, both at once, because every second the suit's open he's losing more than the suit's giving."

"Tell me when," Cole says, and his hands are there, and they are good hands, and she hates a little how glad she is of them. They are the second pair of competent hands in the box and they belong to the calmest man in it and right now, with a dying boy's legs in her grip, she does not have the bandwidth to keep wondering why the calmest man in the box was so calm so fast, and she lets the wondering go the way you let go of a handhold to reach a better one, and the letting-go is itself a small surrender she'll account for later.

"Legs in," she says. She feeds them. The wet neoprene grips the wet skin and fights her the whole way and she works it up over the white feet, the wooden calves, the knees that won't bend right because the kid's gone too stiff to help. "Now — Marcus, on three we sit you up, it's going to hurt and you're going to do it anyway. One. Two. *Up.*"

They sit him up. Cole takes the weight, all of it, like it's nothing, and Ren shoots the right arm through and the mitt comes out the cuff and she does the left and Marcus cries out, a thin broken sound, because the cold makes everything hurt and the moving makes it hurt more, and she talks over it, *good, good, almost, hood now,* and gets the hood up over his head and reaches across him and finds the zip-pull and drags it.

It goes a third of the way and jams.

Salt. Grit. The wet teeth. She works it, backs it off, works it again, and it grinds up another hand's width and stops dead against something in the track she can't see and can't clear with fingers that have started to be hooks. The suit is open from his sternum down. An open suit is a bucket. The thing that was going to save his core heat is going to fill with the next slosh of thirty-four-degree water and become a cold-water enema instead, and she is kneeling in the dark feeling the difference between a saved boy and a drowned one come down to four inches of a zipper she can't make move.

"Give me the strobe," she says. "I need light. Two seconds of light, I have to *see* the track."

A pause. She feels the pause. The strobe is staged, it's the rescue beacon, it's the thing they don't spend, and she's asking to spend it on a zipper.

"It's a strobe, not a torch," Cole says, gentle, reasonable. "It'll flash, it won't hold. You'll get a picture every half-second and your eyes won't keep up." Which is true. Which is exactly true. And under the true thing she hears the other thing, the thing she's started hearing under all of Cole's true things, which is *and it's mine to ration, and I'd rather not spend it,* and she does not have time to resent it.

"Then I do it blind. Hold him up. Don't let the water in."

She does it blind. She gets her thumbnail — the one nail that's grown back wrong since the galley burn, the hard ridged one — into the track ahead of the slider and rakes it, picking the grit out of the teeth by feel, working backward from the jam, and it takes four passes and the burn climbing her forearm and a long string of the worst words she knows said quietly into the dark, and then the slider grinds, catches, and *runs,* all the way up to his throat, and the suit seals, and she gets the storm flap down over it and feels it lock.

She sits back on her heels in the water and her own breath is sawing and her hands are done, properly done now, two clubs at the ends of her arms, and the boy is sealed into a wet suit that will give him hours he didn't have a minute ago, and that is the whole of the win and it is a real one and she lets herself have it for exactly as long as it takes to do the next reading.

She puts a club of a hand against the boy's chest, over the suit, where she can't feel anything, and then she gets two fingers bare against his throat under the sealed hood, in the warm-wet of the only skin she can reach, and finds the carotid and counts it against the slow roll of the hull.

Slow. Slower than it was. But there. She counts it against the hull's roll for a full fifteen seconds the way she was taught, no shortcuts, because a rate you guess at is a rate you flatter, and the count comes in low — low enough that she runs it twice to be sure she isn't reading her own pulse in her own fingertips, the way frozen hands will lie to you and hand you back your own heart and call it the patient's. The second count agrees with the first. It's his. It's low. It's there.

---

The second suit is the easy one and the hard one.

Easy because there are only two candidates left in the arithmetic — herself and Sal — and Sal scores high, Sal can't make heat, Sal is the chart's clearest call. Hard because the suit on Sal is the suit not on the diver, and there's going to be a diver, there's already been one and there'll be another, because the passage is open now and the box is bleeding heat through it and the next time they need something from the far side of that flooded throat somebody goes into the water with what they've got on their skin, and what's on Ren's skin is two seasons of soaked deck gear and a paramedic's understanding of exactly how fast that's worth nothing.

"Suit goes on Sal," she says.

"Suit goes on Sal," Cole agrees, instantly, warmly, like it was never a question, and she clocks the instantly. She clocks how fast and how easy he gives away the second suit, the way she clocked Sal giving away his, except Sal gave away his own and Cole is giving away hers — the suit that, by the other arithmetic, the diver's arithmetic, might have gone on the person most likely to be in the water next, which is her, or him. Cole hands both suits to the two people who can't dive and does it like a generous man and she cannot for the life of her tell, kneeling here with her hands gone to clubs, whether it's the generosity of a good man or the bookkeeping of a man making sure the two bodies that survive in plain sight are the cook he can be seen keeping and the boy he can be seen saving. The two whose living is a credit to him. She files it. She's filing a lot about Cole and the file is getting heavy and she has nowhere to put it down.

They suit Sal. It's worse than the kid, because Sal can't move the leg and the suit's foot has to go on over a fracture that screams at a breath, and they do it in pieces, and Sal swears at them and through them and finally just breathes in the high whistle that's all the pain will let out, and when it's done he's sealed too, the leg splinted underneath stiff and wrong but sealed, and there are two orange skins holding heat in the dark and one of them is on a boy who's losing and one's on a man who's losing slower, and the math is served, and Ren has served herself last, and her last is nothing.

She is the diver with no suit. She made herself that. She'll do it again next time, too, because the arithmetic comes out that way and she trusts the arithmetic, and somewhere under the trusting is the thing she doesn't look at, which is that serving yourself last is also a way of never having to wonder whether you'd have served yourself first.

---

There are the flares, after, and the strobe, and one more small ration to spend or hoard.

"Burn one?" Cole says. He turns the waxed tube in his hands; she hears it. "Check for a ship. There's the off chance, the long off chance, one of the other boats in the fleet's close enough to see a light come up through a hull seam."

She thinks about it for real, because that's the discipline — you don't dismiss the long shot out of fear of looking foolish, you price it. A flare burns ninety seconds. There are two of them and a strobe. The odds another opilio boat is inside line-of-sight of a light leaking up through six inches of inverted hull, in February, at night, in a sea that's still working — the odds are a rounding error. And a flare burned now is a flare not burned the minute they hear a rotor, if they ever hear a rotor.

"We hoard," she says. "Both flares, the strobe, we hoard. We don't spend a single one until we've got something real to spend it on. The strobe gets staged at the high point of the passage where a swimmer might find it if a swimmer ever comes through. The flares stay dry and stay ours." She hears Cole's small nod in the dark, the agreement that costs him nothing because it's what he'd have said, and she notes that too — that the one time she'd have welcomed an argument, to push against and be sure, he gives her none.

She does the staging herself. Crawls to the high lip where the passage opens its black throat into the room, the throat that's been breathing cold into the box since Cole opened it, and she can feel the difference there, a draft of colder where the warm-rotten pocket air slides down into the flooded passage and the passage's chill rises to meet it — the heat going out the door they had to open to save anyone at all. Every save costs the next one. She wedges the strobe up where the steel will hold it and where, if a hand ever comes up through that water, it's the first thing the hand will find.

Then she crawls back to Marcus, because Marcus is the corner of the room she's promised herself she won't leave again, and she puts her ruined hand on the sealed orange chest and feels nothing through it, which is the suit working and the cold in her own hand both, and she leans her ear down to the boy's hood instead, to listen, because listening is the one instrument she has left that the cold can't take.

She listens for his breath the way she listened for four breaths in the first black minute of the night. She finds it. It's there, and under her ear it still carries the rattle of the shaking — coarser than two hours ago, slower, bunched into bouts now with a long lull between, but it's still the body arguing, still the furnace lit, and a kid who still shakes is a kid still in the fight. She holds onto the shaking the way she's been taught to. While it's there she hasn't lost him.

"You're alright," she tells him, into the hood, low and certain, the splint-it-before-you-carry-it voice. "You're in the suit. The suit does the work now. You just breathe and let it work, and I'm right here. I'm going to thaw these hands a few minutes so they're worth a damn to you again, and then I'm going to come back and work you head to foot. They're no good to you frozen. You understand me? I'm not leaving. I'm getting sharp."

"M'kay," Marcus says, the shudder taking the word and shaking it. "C-can do a job."

"Your job's the suit." She means it as the logistics it is — hands she can't feel are hands that can do him harm, and a medic with two clubs for hands is a medic who waits for the clubs to come back, and there is nothing in the thought yet of the other thing, the quiet thing, the file that hasn't been opened. She is not writing him off. She is triaging her own ruined hands and her own ruined attention across a room with four bodies in it and one of her, and Marcus, right now, in her honest reckoning, is the one who is held — sealed, shaking, breathing — and the leg and the diver and the dead girl on the high shelf are the ones still pulling at her, and so she does what the night has taught her to do, which is set down for a minute the thing that's holding and reach for the thing that's slipping. It is the right call. She is almost sure it is the right call.

She tucks her dead hands up into her own armpits, into the last banked coal of her own core, to wake them, and she keeps her ear at the hood while she does it, because the ear costs nothing and the count is the one instrument the cold can't take from her. The shaking goes through him. She lets the count of it rock her toward the few minutes' rest she's allowed herself.

Across the room the staged strobe holds its dark vigil over the black throat of the passage, unlit, waiting for a hand that hasn't come.

She does not yet know how long a few minutes is, in the dark, to hands that have already spent more than they're saying. She does not yet know that the next time she has anything left to give the boy, the shaking will have stopped — and that the stopping is the one reading she has spent her whole life knowing how to take, and the only one she will refuse, tonight, to take in time.


---


# Chapter Ten

## Up Out of the Tide

*POV: Cole — T+9:00*

The tide announces itself the way it always does, which is by lying. It doesn't surge. It doesn't break against the shelf and say *here I am.* It just stops being where it was. Cole has his back against the cold steel of the dome's low edge with the kid wedged in beside him and Sal laid out along the high strip, and the water's been at his chest for hours, at his chest when he sits and climbing his collarbone when he leans, and somewhere in the long grey nothing of waiting he leans to brace a hand on the steel and the water touches his throat where it didn't an hour ago. A cold mouth at the hollow of his neck. Just for a second, on the lean. Then gone again when he straightens.

He doesn't say anything yet. You don't announce water. Announcing water is how you turn three people who are managing into three people who are drowning in their heads before the sea ever gets the chance. He just holds still and runs the number the way he runs all of them — quiet, behind the warm.

The seam. There's a weld seam Cole found on the perimeter walk hours ago, an old repair welt running level around the frame, and he's been reading the water against it all night the way you'd read a tide-board nailed to a piling — it's been up around chest height since before the dive, and he's watched it crawl. Last he had two fingers on it the water was a palm's width below the welt. He reaches over now, slow, and the welt is *under.* The water's a hand's width over the top of it and rising on the tide. Not a finger, not the slow inch-an-hour creep of a settling hull finding its level. The tide's come in under them, lifting the sea inside the box the same as it lifts the sea outside, and a hand's width of water across a room this size is more water than a man can think about without going somewhere bad, so Cole doesn't think about the volume. He thinks about the move.

"Alright," he says, low and easy, to the dark. "We're going to shift up the strip a little. Tide's making. Nothing wrong, nothing new — she breathes in and out same as us, and right now she's breathing in. We just give her the low ground and take the high. Sal, you don't lift a thing, you let me and Ren do the lifting. Marcus, you're going to help me move the totes. Slow. We've got time."

They don't have time, exactly, but a man telling you you've got time is a thing you can put your weight on, and Cole has learned that the having of time and the being told you have it are close enough in the dark that nobody checks the difference. He gets a boot under himself and stands chest-deep into the strip of standing room they've got left — and there isn't much, the dome dropped a foot when the rack came down on Pavel and it hasn't given the foot back — and he starts the slow careful business of moving a survival situation up out of a rising tide eight inches at a time without anyone noticing how little there is left to move it to.

The work is real, and it's the only kind of work left in the box. He wants to be clear with himself about that much, in the cold floor of him where he keeps the honest accounting he never shows anyone: the tide is making, the water is at his chest and the high strip is a galley-table's width of grating that's getting narrower by the hour, and everything that has to stay dry has to go up, and he's the one to do the moving. The bait totes are buoyant and they ride up against his chest in the black and clog the strip a man needs to lie in; he wrestles them down the slope into the deep water and jams them where they'll float without crowding the bodies, and the cold climbs his arms to the shoulder every time he reaches down into it and he counts the reach the way he counts everything — what it spends against what it buys. The few dry rags they've hoarded, the cut-down hide of the torn third suit they butchered into wraps an hour ago — he moves all of it up, hand over hand, sorting by feel, dry from wet, useful from ballast, racing the line up the welt. He gets Sal shifted, which is the bad part, which is the part where the cook makes the high thin sound through his teeth that Cole has come to hate, and Cole gets behind him in the chest-deep water and takes the cook's weight off the wet grating and holds the broken leg steady against his own chest while Ren works the splint up the slope, the two of them moving the man up out of the rising water an inch at a time like they're walking a piano up a stair, and the whole time Cole keeps the voice going — *that's it, that's the worst of it, you're past the worst of it, Sal, almost there* — because the voice is a thing the cook can grip with the part of him that isn't screaming.

And it works. It always works. That's the thing about the gift Ferro saw in him green and made him for: it never doesn't work. Cole has stood on the deck of a boat taking green water over the rail with the radar gone and the autopilot fighting him and four men who wanted to panic, and he has talked the panic down out of them with nothing but a low voice and a sentence that was true enough, and they have lived, every time, every single time, and he has come to believe — not in words, never in words, but in the deep wordless place where a man keeps the things he can't afford to look at — that as long as the voice keeps working, as long as the hands keep working, the ledger stays balanced. That a man who saves enough is square with the saving. He could not say this sentence out loud. He has never let himself finish it even silently. But it runs under everything he does in the dark tonight, the way a keel runs under a boat — unseen, load-bearing, the reason the whole thing rides the way it rides.

"How's the leg," Ren says, when Sal's settled. Flat. Clinical. She's somewhere off to his right, a voice and a set of hands; he hasn't seen her face in hours, none of them have seen anything in hours, and he's found that the dark makes Ren *more* of what she is, not less — strips the small social softness off her and leaves just the count, the assessment, the next true thing.

"It's a leg with no opinions," Sal says, around his teeth, and there's a thread of the old galley dryness in it, thinning out, but there. "Numb's better than what it was. Ask me to dance, see what happens."

"I'll pass."

"Smart girl."

Cole lets that little exchange happen and doesn't step on it, because the joke is doing work — Sal making himself a person again, Ren letting him, the two of them building a small dry place out of nothing — and a skipper who knows when to shut up is half a skipper's whole value. He moves on to the kid.

---

The kid is the thing Cole's been not-looking-at the way he doesn't look at the chart locker, and for a different reason, and for the same one.

The kid stopped shivering an hour back — Ren had her hands on him for that, read it off him while she was sealing him into the suit, and the not-shivering is an old fact now, a fact Cole's been carrying since before the tide started making. He isn't going to be the one who panics at a thing they already know. What he's reading for now is the next thing down. He helped with the totes — but *helped* is generous; he got a hand on one and pushed it the wrong way and let it go, and when Cole said *up the strip, bud, up toward Sal,* the kid moved the wrong direction, down the slope into the deeper water, like a man following a current he can't feel, and Cole had to turn him by the shoulders and point him and the kid went where he was pointed without seeming to know why. That's the new thing. That's the thing under the not-shivering. Cole gets a hand on him, gets him wedged up onto the high strip, and the kid lets himself be moved like cargo, no help in it and no fight in it either.

That's the part Cole knows, in the place he knows things he isn't a medic for. He's pulled enough cold men out of enough water in forty years to have watched this stretch of it more than once: the shivering goes first — that's the furnace quitting — and then for a while a man can still talk, still answer, still tell you he's fine in a slurred easy voice, and then that goes too, in stages, the way a battery doesn't die all at once but drops a bar and drops a bar. The talking gets stupider. The body stops minding where it is. A man will wade *toward* the cold because the part of him that knew which way was warm has gone dark, and he'll start fumbling at his own collar like the suit's too tight when the truth is the cold has reached the switch that tells him hot from cold and flipped it. Cole has seen a man on a deck try to take his coat off in a forty-knot wind, smiling, and that man did not make the dock. The kid hasn't gone for his zip yet. But he's wading the wrong way, and he's stopped minding, and Cole knows what the next bar down looks like.

"Marcus," Cole says. "Hey. Talk to me."

"M'good." Thick. Slow. The words come unstuck from each other, a little wrong in the spacing. "M'too warm, s'the thing. S'too warm in the — " and Cole feels the kid's wooden hand come up and paw at the suit's collar, weak, fretting at the zip-pull, trying to get it down. There it is. The next bar. Cole takes the hand, gentle, and moves it off the zip and folds it back down against the boy's chest.

"Leave that," he says, easy, like it's nothing. "Leave the zip be. That's the suit working — it's *supposed* to feel warm, that's it doing its job, you let it keep doing its job and you keep your hands in. Hands in, bud. Tuck 'em." The kid's in one of the two intact immersion suits they pulled on the dive, zipped to the chin, and that suit is the only reason the boy's voice is still making words at all, and the cold is in the boy's wiring now telling him the suit's the enemy, telling him to open it to the water, and Cole has seen exactly where that road goes. "But I need you talking. You don't get to go quiet on me, that's the one rule. Tell me — tell me what you're studying. School. The fish thing."

"Marine bio." A little life in it, surfacing. "Was gonna do — there's these worms. Riftia. They live on the vents, the hot ones, no light, no — they don't even eat, they got bacteria inside 'em make the food, it's —" The thread frays. "S'cold down there too. The deep. People think it's — but the vents are hot and around 'em it's like, two degrees. Cold and hot right next to each other."

"Cold and hot right next to each other," Cole says. "Tell me more about that."

And the kid does, in pieces, fading and surging, and Cole gets himself down into the high strip beside him and does the thing the situation needs, which is also — and Cole is too honest in the cold floor of him to pretend otherwise — the thing that looks the best of anything a man could do in here. He gets his own body around the boy. He's soaked and freezing and he hasn't got much heat to give, but he's bigger, he's got more mass, more furnace even guttering, and a body against a body is worth more than a body alone; that's not sentiment, that's physics, the cold sheds slower off two than off one. He wraps the kid in against his chest the way he wrapped him in after the rack went, and he pulls one of the cut neoprene strips up over the both of them, and he keeps the voice going into the wet dark over the boy's head.

"That's it. Keep talking. Riftia. The worms. I'm listening."

He is doing the right thing. He wants that on the record, the internal one, the only one that's ever fully honest: he is doing the exact right thing, the thing Ren would tell him to do if she weren't elbow-deep in Sal's leg, the thing that buys the kid heat and time and maybe his life. And the floor of him, the cold loading-deck floor where the doors don't catch, runs the other accounting alongside it the way it always does, the way he can't make it stop:

It isn't about what the boy saw. The boy saw nothing — green as spring, the owner's nephew, lied his way onto the berth and doesn't know a deck-load cert from a grocery list, never stood a watch where it mattered, couldn't tell a soul how heavy she rode or how late they ran the gear if you sat him down and begged him. That's not the thread Cole's fingers find in the dark. It's simpler and worse than that. It's that a body wrapped around a freezing boy is the kind of thing that *gets told.* It's a picture. A man hears, after a wreck, all the small ugly true things — who rode her hard, who skipped the cert, who held a course he had no business holding — and then he hears one warm thing, the skipper who climbed down into the cold and gave a dying kid the heat off his own chest, and the warm thing is the one that sticks, the one that gets repeated, the one that sits in front of every cold true thing like a man's body between you and the wind. Cole isn't building a witness. He's building the picture that goes on top of the file. The boy doesn't have to remember anything. The boy just has to have been warmed.

Cole feels the thought arrive and he does not slam the door on it this time, the way he slammed it on the other one going up from Pavel's body, because this thought is allowed — this thought doesn't cost the boy anything, this thought rides right alongside the warming and the talking and doesn't slow either one — and that's exactly what makes it the worst kind. He can hold the boy and want the boy to live with his whole real heart, and underneath, in the same beat, in the same breath, take the reading on what the warming will look like later, and never once feel the two things contradict, because in Cole the two things have never once contradicted. The horror of him, if he could ever see it, is that there is no seam between the good and the use. They are the same muscle. He warms the kid because warming the kid is right and warming the kid is useful and Cole Vantine has spent forty-six years being unable to tell those two apart and has called the not-being-able-to-tell *leadership.*

"Cole." Ren's voice, low, across the dark. Not for Sal. For him. "Is he answering you."

She knows. Of course she knows; she'd have heard it in the boy's voice, the thickness, the warm easy slur, the words coming unstuck. She's asking because she wants the reading from where her own hands can't reach, and she's asking quiet, off the kid's frequency, so the boy doesn't hear his own death being weighed.

"He's in the suit," Cole says, which is an answer and isn't.

"Cole. Is he answering. Does he know where he is."

A beat. The hull drips. The water laps the new line, a hand's width over the welt, patient. Beside Cole the kid's hand stirs and starts to drift back up toward the zip-pull, and Cole catches it again and tucks it down without breaking the count of his own voice.

"He's slurry," Cole says. "He went for his zip. Wanted it open. And he's been wading the wrong way — down, toward the deep, when I let go of him." He keeps it low, off the kid's frequency, the way she pitched hers. "He's a bar lower than he was, Ren. Maybe two."

He hears Ren go still. He can't see her, but he knows the stillness — the particular stillness of the medic doing the math, the cold honest arithmetic she runs the way he runs the loading sums, and he knows, because he's spent the night learning her, that the going-for-the-zip is a word in her grammar he doesn't have, that it means something specific and bad to a paramedic, that the number she's getting off it is not a good number. She won't soften it and she won't say it in front of the kid. There's a long quiet where the two of them, the medic and the skipper, sit in the dark on either side of a boy who's gone warm and easy, and do the same arithmetic from opposite sides of the room, and arrive, Cole would bet his boat, at the same ugly place.

"Don't let him open that suit," Ren says, fast, low, and there's a thing in it he hasn't heard off her before, not fear exactly but the edge a number gets when it's worse than she expected. "Whatever he does, his hands stay off the zip. He opens it, the water gets in and that's the end of him, faster than the cold alone. Keep his hands pinned if you have to. Keep him talking, keep him moving if he'll move, don't let him sleep. I'll come when Sal's traction's set — I can't leave this leg right now or he loses it for sure." A pause. "I'm trusting you with the kid, Cole. I need both my hands on this."

"I've got him," Cole says.

And he does. He's got him. The boy talks, fading, about the worms in the dark that make their own food out of poison, about the cold and the hot living right next to each other on the floor of a sea, and Cole keeps his body around him and his voice going, and across the room Ren sets a broken man's leg in the black by feel, and Sal makes his thin sound through his teeth, and the water comes up the welt a finger's width while Cole isn't watching, and the whole sealed box of them tilts a half-degree further into the only rest it's ever going to find.

---

He checks the strobe himself. That's the part he doesn't have to do, and does anyway, and tells himself it's diligence.

They'd staged it earlier — a battery strobe out of the suit locker, the kind that clips to a survival suit's shoulder and flashes white, and Ren had wanted it set at the high point of the flooded passage, up where the throat of the companionway nearly breaks the surface, so that if anything ever came down that passage from the outside sea — a diver, a swimmer, a light — it would meet the flash and know there was breath beyond it. A reasonable place for it. Cole agreed. He agrees with reasonable things; agreeing with reasonable things is most of how a man stays the one in charge.

But sometime in the long grey waiting, with the boy talking smaller and smaller against his chest, Cole eases out from under the neoprene, settles the kid against a tote, tells him *thirty seconds, keep talking, I'm right here,* and he wades down the slope into the deeper water at the mouth of the passage to put his own eyes on the strobe — and there's the seam in him, right there, the thing he can't say even silently. A man who believed help was coming wouldn't need to check the strobe. A man who'd keyed the bridge EPIRB before the roll, who'd sent a 406 megahertz cry for help screaming up off the masthead with the boat's name and number stitched into it, who *knew* the Coast Guard had a position and a vessel and was already turning a cutter ninety miles their way — that man would sit in the dark and wait, and not go wading down a flooding passage in the worst cold of the night to make sure a four-dollar strobe was still flashing into the empty sea.

Cole goes and checks the strobe.

He finds it where they set it, the white flash stuttering against the black water of the passage throat, steady, the little light doing its small dumb hopeful work, and he reaches up and confirms its mounting with a hand gone half to meat, and the relief he feels at finding it lit is the relief of a man who is not sure anyone is coming and needs the strobe to be working because the strobe might be all there is. He stands there in the chest-deep cold at the mouth of the throat and he does not let himself finish the thought, the thought about the EPIRB, the thought about whether the green confirm light he told them all about was a thing he saw or a thing he needs to have seen, the thought that the only honest cry for help that ever left this boat is a battery strobe flashing into twenty-two feet of flooded passage where nobody is looking. He does not finish it. He has gotten very good, over a long career and one long night, at the not-finishing. The sentences that would damn him all run out into the same dark, and he's learned to let go of the end of them the way you let go of a line you don't want to be holding when somebody checks who's holding it.

He wades back up the slope toward the high strip, the water dropping off his chest to his waist as he climbs, and even up here it's higher than it was four minutes ago, the tide still making, the box still shrinking, and he gets back down into the strip and pulls the boy against him and the boy says, drowsy, content, warm as a child in a bed, "You came back."

"I never went anywhere," Cole says. "Checking a light. Keep talking."

"S'nice," Marcus says. "I was cold for so long and now it's nice. Thank you. For the — for getting in. You didn't have to."

"Course I did."

"No." The kid's hand finds Cole's forearm in the dark and rests there, light, grateful, a boy's hand, and Cole feels the lack of strength in it, the way it just lies there with no grip in it at all. "Pavel said — he said you were the best skipper he ever — that you'd get us through. He said it before. I'm glad it's you."

Cole holds very still in the dark with the boy's warm thank-you settling over him like the false heat settling over the boy, and across the room Ren's hands work a broken leg by feel and her voice murmurs the medicine she does instead of feeling, and the strobe stutters its small white nothing into the empty throat of the passage, and the water comes up the welt one more finger's width, unhurried, patient, doing its arithmetic on all of them at once.

"Rest a minute," Cole tells the boy, low, the hand on the tiller, the warm laid down careful over the cold. "Not sleep. Just rest. I've got you. We're alright for now."

It's even true. That's the useful kind. And underneath, in the loading-deck floor where the doors don't catch and the honest sums get run, Cole already knows the arithmetic he isn't saying — knows it whole, knows it clean, hates how clean it is: two suits came up off that dive, and one of them is sealed around a boy who has stopped minding where he is, who paws at the zip that's keeping him alive like it's a coat in a warm room, who wades toward the deep when you let go of his arm. Cole has watched men cross that particular line on open decks in his forty years, and he has learned the one thing the line tells you, which is which side of it a man is going to be found on. They don't come back from the wrong-way wandering. They get warm and easy and stupid and quiet, in that order, and then they get still.

He keeps the thought. He keeps it the way you keep a tool you're ashamed to own. He keeps the boy's hand pinned soft and warm against his chest, off the zip, the way Ren told him, and the hand fights him a little — weak, fretful, wanting the water — and then forgets it was fighting and lies still, grateful, no grip in it at all. The water comes up the welt another finger while Cole holds it down. The tide is still making. There is more box above them than below them, but not by much now, and not for long, and the high strip they fought everything up onto tonight is just the next thing the sea is coming for. Cole holds the boy, and holds the line off the zip, and waits for the medic to finish the leg, and does not let himself wonder whose body the suit will be keeping warm by morning.


---


# Chapter Eleven

## Traction

*POV: Ren — T+10:00*

The leg is going to kill Sal before the cold does, and Ren can feel it under her hands the way she can feel a tide turning — not all at once, just a direction, just the thing the water has decided.

She has both palms flat on his right thigh in the dark, above the break, where the muscle should be slack and warm and instead is a drum somebody has been tuning all night, the skin stretched so tight over the swelling that her thumb leaves no dent when she presses. She knows this drum. Closed mid-shaft femur, the broken ends bleeding into the meat of the leg where the bleed has nowhere to go, the thigh filling like a wineskin, and the cold that's been her ally everywhere else turning traitor here — slowing the bleed, yes, buying him hours she didn't think he had, but slowing the blood out of the tissue too, so the pressure builds and the muscle drowns inside its own sheath with no water in it at all. Compartment. She has the word and she hates the word. The word means *now,* and *now* is the one resource the box has never once given her on credit.

"Sal," she says. "I'm going to ask you a thing and I want the truth, not the brave version."

"My specialty's the brave version." His voice has thinned out over the hours, the Brooklyn gone reedy, the jokes coming slower and landing softer like a man throwing food to a fish he can't quite see. "What."

"The foot. Can you feel me." She finds his boot by feel — they got it off him two hours ago, or six, she's stopped trusting the count of hours, the dark has eaten the hours the way it eats everything — and she works her fingers down through the wet wool sock to the toes, which are the temperature of the steel, which is to say the temperature of the sea. She pinches the big one. "That. Can you feel that."

A pause she doesn't like.

"...Little bit," Sal says. "Pins."

It's a lie, the brave version, and they both let it be one because the truth is a thing she can read off him without his help: the toes are dusky, the cold has them, the swelling above is squeezing the artery that feeds them the way you'd kink a hose, and below the kink the foot is starving. She has maybe a window. She doesn't know the size of the window because she has no monitor and no light and no way to measure a pulse she can barely find, and a window you can't measure is just a wall you haven't hit yet.

"Okay," she says, flat, the voice she keeps for the worst of it, the one with no weather in it. "Here's what's true. The leg's swelling shut. If I leave it, the foot dies first and then it works its way up, and that's a slower worse death than the one outside that hull. So I'm going to splint it and I'm going to pull it long while I do — straighten it, give the ends room, take some of the squeeze off — and Sal, I'm not going to lie to you about the pulling part."

"Don't," he says. "I been waiting all night for you to do the thing you keep not doing. Do the thing."

She would, if she had the hands. That's the whole arithmetic of the next ten minutes and she runs it the way she runs all of them, fast and cold and out loud only in her own head. A traction splint is two people. One pair of hands to hold the body still and seat the splint, one pair to pull the leg out long against the pull of the muscle that wants to ride the broken ends back over each other — and the muscle is the strongest in the body, the femoral mass, a thigh built across forty years of standing in a galley on a heaving deck, and it will not give for a tired woman whose fingers stopped fully closing somewhere around the fourth hour. She has the splint half-built already, did it in the slack water before the tide came up — two gaff handles from the gear tangle, a bait-knife to cut the line, the torn suit's neoprene cut into a sleeve to pad the rail of it. What she does not have, what she has never once had in this box, is enough of herself to do a two-person job alone.

So she does the thing she's been not-doing, which is the thing she least wants to do, which is to need him.

"Cole."

He's been a shape and a sound off to her left for the last hour — the low murmur of him keeping the kid company, the wet shift of his big body in the water, the small administrations of a man who's appointed himself the warmth of the room because there's no other warmth to appoint. The tide came up a hand's width while the sky outside was doing whatever the sky does, lifting the water off her chest and up toward her collarbone, putting the passage's open throat under and turning it into a mouth that breathes cold into the room without stopping, and Cole has spent that whole rising hour with his own body wrapped around Marcus, feeding the kid the last heat he's got, doing the most visible good a man can do in a place where everyone is the audience. She heard him do it. She filed it. She's been filing him all night and she doesn't fully know what file he's in.

"Here," he says, and is, the water moving as he comes, the warm easy *here* of a man who never makes you ask twice. "What do you need."

"Your hands. Both. For about four minutes that are going to feel like an hour to him and longer to you." She finds his wrist in the dark, guides it down to the ankle, sets his grip where she needs it — one hand cupping the heel, one locked above the malleoli, the boot off, the bare cold foot in his bare cold hands. "You pull when I say pull. Steady, not a yank — a yank tears something. You pull like you're easing a boat off a bar, slow and even and you don't stop till I say. He's going to scream and you're going to want to stop. You don't stop. The stopping is what hurts him, not the pulling. You understand me."

"I've reduced a dislocated shoulder on a pitching deck off a man who was fighting me the whole time," Cole says, and there's a thing in his voice she'd notice if she had a hand free to notice it with — a small reach for standing, for the record of his own competence laid down quick and easy, the way he lays everything down — but she doesn't have a hand free. "Slow and even. I've got him. Sal — you hear that, you old bastard? She's going to fix your leg and I'm going to hold your foot, and you're going to cuss us both, and in the morning you'll be telling it like it was nothing."

"In the morning," Sal says, and the way he says it is the first thing all night that frightens Ren about him more than the leg — because it isn't quite a question and it isn't quite a hope, it's a man trying the word *morning* in his mouth to see if it still means anything, and finding out it almost doesn't.

She gets her own hands set. The splint frame along the outside of the leg, the padded gaff handles, the ankle hitch of cut line she's tied a dozen times in daylight and is now tying blind by the feel of the bait-knife's grooves and the wet rope and her own slow fingers that answer half a second behind her wanting them to. She seats the proximal end against the ischial tuberosity, the hard knob of the seat bone, the anchor point — finds it by pressing up into the groin until Sal hisses, *there, that's the spot, that's the brace* — and she thinks, in the cold clinical back room where she keeps the things that keep her, *this is the only part of medicine I ever liked. The part that's just engineering. The part where you don't have to decide who.*

"Okay," she says. "Sal, breathe out. All the way out, empty, and when you're empty I start, because the scream's smaller on empty. Cole — on my word."

"On your word."

"Sal. Breathe out."

He breathes out. The reedy rush of it in the dark, the last of the brave version leaving him.

"Pull," Ren says.

---

The scream is the kind that comes up out of a place under speech, the place the body keeps for exactly this, and it fills the little reeking dome and slaps off the steel and comes back doubled, and Marcus somewhere behind her makes a small broken echo of it, *don't, stop, you're hurting him,* and Sal's whole bulk bucks against the grating, and Ren leans her weight into the splint and into the seat bone and feels through her palms the slow terrible give as the leg comes long — the broken ends she can feel grinding past each other and then, blessedly, separating, the femur lengthening back toward the shape a femur is supposed to be, the thigh's drum-tight pressure finding one half-inch of somewhere to go.

"Don't stop," she says to Cole, low, under the scream. "Steady. You're doing it. Don't stop."

"I'm not stopping." His voice is tight now, the easy gone out of it, the work in it instead, and his big hands on the foot are pulling exactly the way she told him to — slow, even, a boat off a bar, the salvage-diver's patience that knows you don't win against the sea by yanking — and some cold professional part of Ren, the part that ran calls with strangers for six years and learned to love the ones whose hands were good, registers it with a kind of helpless gratitude she resents even as it's happening. *His hands are right. His hands are exactly right.* She would want these hands. On any call, in any wreck, with any patient she'd ever had — the steady ones, the ones that don't flinch when the patient screams, the ones you can hand the hard half of a two-person job and not look back — she would want exactly these. The thought sits in her wrong and she has no time to look at why, so she files it, on top of the rest of the file, the file on the man whose foot is in his hands beside the foot of the man whose leg is in hers, the four of them braided into a single creature doing one thing in the dark.

"That's it," she says. "Hold it long. Hold it right there. I'm hitching it off. Don't let it back an inch."

She ties the traction hitch to the foot of the frame with the leg held out at length, takes a wrap, takes a second, feeds the line through and snubs it, and the splint takes the load off Cole's hands and holds the leg long by itself the way it's built to — the gaff handles bowing slightly under the tension, the cut line creaking, the whole improvised rig humming with the pull of a thigh muscle it has out-stubborned by sheer mechanical advantage. She gets a hand on the thigh again. The drum is still a drum. But it's a half-inch looser, and a half-inch is a window, and a window is more than she had a minute ago.

"You can let go," she tells Cole. "Slow. Let the splint take it. Slow."

He eases off the foot finger by finger, testing whether the rig will hold what he's been holding, and it holds, and the leg stays long, and Sal's scream has fallen back down into the groan, the low continuous one that's worse to listen to and means the worst is past.

"There," Ren says. To Sal. To the leg. To the dark. "There. That's the worst of it. You did the worst of it."

"That," says Sal, when he can find the words again, "was not the brave version."

"No."

"That was the other one."

"Yeah," Ren says. "Sorry."

"Don't be sorry." He's panting. The pain's still in him — it'll be in him till the leg comes off or he does, and she knows which she'd bet on if she let herself bet — but the panic's gone out of the leg, the animal alarm of the dying foot, the body's red light. "Don't ever be sorry for the thing that works. Cole. You still got my foot?"

"I got your foot," Cole says, and there's the easy back in his voice now, the warm flooding back the second the hard part's done, the relief of a man stepping off the part of the deck that was on fire. "It's an ugly foot. But I got it."

"It's a beautiful foot," Sal says. "It's a Michelangelo. You don't know feet."

And it's almost a joke, almost the old Sal, and Ren lets it go by because the room needs it to go by — and that's when Sal says the other thing, the thing he's been holding longer than she's been holding his leg, and he says it the way men say the true thing when the pain has worn the lid off them and there's no light to see whose face changes.

"You held her long," Sal says. "She rode heavy and you held her long, didn't you, Cole."

---

Ren is wrist-deep in the splint, snubbing a second wrap on the traction line because the first is creeping, and the words go past her at the level of weather — not into the file, just over it. She hears *held her long* and *rode heavy* and they sort themselves automatically into the only grammar she's got the attention to give them, which is the grammar of the leg: *hold the traction long, the swelling rode heavy on the artery.* She's got Sal's voice slotted as a man talking about his own leg, his own pain, the long bad ride of the night, because that is what the front of her mind has room for and the front of her mind is the only part of her that's awake.

But somewhere under the weather, in the cold back room where she keeps the strange vital signs, a small thing turns over. Not alarm. Less than alarm. The faint snag of a phrase that didn't quite fit its slot — *she rode heavy* — a *she* that isn't the leg, a *she* the size and weight of a boat. It catches on nothing and lets go and is gone, filed under the wrong heading, in the wrong drawer, in a cabinet she is too cold and too busy and too far inside another man's thigh to open.

And in the half-second of dark where she might have looked up, where on any other night her whole instrument would have swung toward the wrongness the way it swung toward Cole's too-steady breathing in the first minute of the wreck, Cole answers.

"Long as I could, Sal," he says.

That's all. *Long as I could.* Warm, rueful, a tired man owning a hard night — and it is the answer of a skipper who stood by his boat to the last, and it is also, exactly and identically, the answer of a man who held a course an hour too long with a deck stacked too high because turning would have logged what he'd let her run all season, and the two answers are the same words in the same warm voice and there is no light in the box to tell them apart and no free hand in the box to reach for the difference. The seam is right there. It is right there in the open between them, *long as I could,* a sentence with a fault line down the middle of it, and the one person in the box built to find fault lines has both her hands inside a broken leg and her whole mind on a window that's closing in a foot she can't save.

Ren ties off the second wrap. The line holds. She does not look up. The thing in the back room settles again, unexamined, the way silt settles when you stop stirring the water, and she will not know — not for hours, not until the dive, not until the cold has already taken the thing she should be turning toward right now — that the most important sentence anyone has said all night just went past her at the level of weather while she was saving a leg that is, the back room has already quietly calculated and not told the front, probably going to come off anyway.

"Good," Cole says, into the quiet, to all of them, the room's pulse-taker laying his hand on the room. "Leg's set. That's a real thing done. Sal, you rest that. Ren —" and here the warmth turns, gentle, toward her, toward the work she does instead of feeling — "the boy. Go see to the boy. I've got Sal."

---

She'd forgotten Marcus.

That's the truth of it, the truth she'll have to carry, and it arrives now with the small private horror of a thing you find out about yourself in the dark: that for the length of the splint — four minutes that were an hour to Sal and longer to Cole and to her were no time at all, were a single held breath of pure engineering, the part of medicine she loved because it's just physics — she had set the kid down. Not coldly. Not on purpose. The way you set down a thing you're carrying when you need both hands for a door, fully meaning to pick it up again the second you're through.

She turns from Sal's leg, finds the kid by feel along the shelf, gets her two cold fingers to the side of his throat.

He's where she left him. That's the first thing she clocks, and she clocks it with the small false ease of a woman counting a thing she has not actually counted in an hour: *he's where I left him, the suit's on him, the suit's working, he's holding.* The carotid is there under her fingers — she finds it, slow, and gives it a count against the boat's roll and gets a number she rounds without deciding to, the way she rounded it the last time, because the front of her mind is still half inside the splint and a number you round is a number you don't have to act on yet.

"Marcus." She rubs her knuckles on his sternum, the grind that brings a man up from the deep place. "Marcus. Talk to me. Where are you."

A long swimming pause. And then, drowsy, almost content, the voice of a boy warm in a bed who's been woken too early: "'M here. Y'said an hour. 'S not been an hour." A small fond complaint, the kind a body makes when it doesn't yet know it's stopped keeping time. "Tha's okay. 'M fine. Go do Sal."

*Go do Sal.* He's giving her permission. He's giving her the out and he doesn't know it's an out, and she takes it before she's looked at the taking, the way you take a handhold in the dark — not a decision, a reflex, the body solving the room faster than the mind can vote.

"I'll be right back to you," she says. "Few more minutes. You hold."

"I'm holdin'," he says, content. "Always do me right, Ren."

And here is the place — though she won't be able to find it again later, no matter how many times she walks back to it in a warm room weeks from now and tries to put her finger on the exact dark second it happened. It does not happen in words. There are no words for it; that's why it's so easy, that's why it never once feels like the thing it is. It is not a verdict. It is smaller and worse than a verdict: it is a verdict beginning to form, a tag beginning to write itself before she has read him well enough to earn it. The leg is a window she can work — pressure she can read, traction she can hold, a problem with an engineering shape. The boy is the warm calm corner she has already, twice now, told herself she'll get to. And some cold sorting room at the base of her, the room that cleared her in London and never cleared her at all, starts — only starts — to move him. Not all the way. Not to the drawer she'll put him in later, the one with the worst label on it. Just a hand's-breadth toward it. Just far enough that her two ruined hands turn, on their own, back toward the leg, and the turning feels like nothing, feels like efficiency, feels like the correct allocation of the only medic in a box with two hands that don't fully close — and that is exactly how the worst thing she will ever do is going to feel while she's doing it: like nothing. Like good triage. Like the numbers, which are the only floor she has ever found to stand on, and which are also, she has never once let herself feel, the wall she builds between herself and the ones she sets down.

She does not finish the thought. The cold won't let her hold a long one tonight and the leg's line is creeping again behind her and the front of her mind is grateful, in a way it will be ashamed of for the rest of her life, to be called away before the tag finishes writing itself. She files him half-moved. She does not run the one number that matters — how much margin the boy actually has — because the back room has half-filed him already, and a half-filed patient doesn't get a fresh number, and a fresh number is the only thing that would have told her the truth, which is that he is not as far down as the warm voice makes him sound, that there is a window in him too, a window she could still be hauling on with everything she's got, and is not.

"You rest," she hears herself say. "I've got the leg to finish. I'll come back and work you properly when my hands thaw — they're no good to you cold, you understand? Give me a few minutes to get warm and I'll do you right."

"'Kay," says Marcus, content. "Y'always do me right."

*A few minutes.* She hands him the phrase and turns away from it, and she does not let herself price it, and the not-pricing is the whole of the sin — because pricing is the thing she is for, pricing is the one instrument the cold hasn't taken yet, and she has just chosen, without choosing, not to run the number on her own promise. A few minutes is a currency she taught a roomful of frightened people, in the first black hour, that nobody in this box gets to spend on credit. She knows that. She is the one who knows that. And she sets it down next to the boy and walks her two hands back to the leg.

She tucks the kid's hood tighter — and her wrist, dropping, brushes the seam of the passage's open throat, which the rising water has lifted closer to him than it was an hour ago, so that the cold draft sliding off it now passes directly over the sealed orange shoulder she's just settled, a thin steady exhalation of the sea breathing onto the one body in the box with the least heat left to lose, and she registers the draft on her own wrist and not on him, and moves on. The passage takes a little more from him every minute it's open, and it will not close, and she opened the arithmetic that opened it.

She turns back toward Sal's leg, toward the line that's creeping again, toward the work that has a shape she can hold.

"Three," she says to the dark, soft, the census, the coin in the fist. "Still three."

The dark doesn't answer. The hull groans somewhere forward, the long iron complaint of a boat settling deeper on its back, and the passage breathes its cold breath across the room, and behind her, very quietly, content, warm, a boy who is not as far gone as she has half-decided he is stops talking — not because he's gone, but because he's resting, the way she told him to.


---


# Chapter Twelve

## The Seam

*POV: Cole — T+11:00*

The torch has about a finger of light left in it, and Cole has been spending it the way you spend the last of your water on a long pull — a sip at a time, telling everyone it's plenty.

He holds it now low against the slope of the steel, the way he's learned to, so the beam doesn't throw out into the dark and waste itself on water there's no use looking at. It puts a coin of weak yellow on the new high ground — a strip of grating no wider than a galley table, jammed against the steel where the dome comes down, the only place left a man can sit with his head clear of the wet. Eleven hours under and this is what the box has shrunk to. He runs the light along it once, slow, taking the reading: Sal propped in the corner with the leg out straight in its splint, the gaff handles lashed alongside the thigh and the cut line running taut down to the cleat Cole drove into a frame two hours back, holding the traction so the broken ends don't ride up over each other and start the bleed again. The kid next to him, zipped to the chin in the orange suit, his head fallen sideways onto Sal's shoulder, not moving. Ren on the far end with her back to the steel and her hands tucked into her armpits, asleep or close to it, her chin dropping and catching, dropping and catching.

Four of them. He counts them by the light the way Ren counts them by breath, and the count comes out four, and the fourth one — the boy — he has to look at twice to be sure he's still in it.

"Marcus," Cole says, easy, not loud. "Hey. You with me, bud?"

A long beat. Then the kid's mouth moves, and a sound comes out that isn't a word but is a sound, which is what Cole wanted. Still in it. He lets the light leave the boy's face before the boy can wake all the way and see how little there is of it.

He kills the torch.

He does it without ceremony, thumb on the slide, and the dark comes down so total that for a second his eyes hunt for a shape and find nothing, not his own hand, not the water a foot below him, nothing. He's been waiting for the right moment to do this — the torch is the last light they have and there's no charging it, no spare cell, and once it's gone it's gone, so a man wants to spend it for something and not on a long cold night of just sitting. Sitting they can do in the dark. They can sit in the dark cheaper than they can sit in the light, and the battery keeps best cold, and there's going to be a worse minute than this one coming, and when it comes he wants a light for it.

That's the reason he gives, and it's true, and it's the reason he says out loud, and it carries the room the way a true reason does.

"Lights out for a while," he says into the black. "Saving it for when we need it. Everybody's where they're meant to be — Sal corner, Marcus by Sal, Ren end. Reach out and you'll find your neighbor. Nobody move from your spot."

"Aye," Sal says, around the teeth. The cook's voice has gone thin over the hours, the fat boiled out of it by the pain, but it's still got the Brooklyn in it, still got the shape of a man who fed people for forty years and resents not being able to. "You always did like the lights low, Vantine. Mood man."

It's a joke. It's Sal being Sal, throwing the old needle to keep the room from going all the way to grief, and Cole takes it the way you take a thrown line, laughs the small laugh that costs nothing and buys a little. "Mood man," he says. "That's me." And there's a reason to laugh, and the laughing uses air, and he clocks even the air it uses, because he clocks everything now, he can't help it, the part of him that loaded a deck for thirty years is loading this room too, weighing what each thing costs against what it buys, and a laugh that keeps the cook from crying is worth the breath.

The dark is better. He won't say that. But the dark is better, because in the dark a face is just a voice, and a voice you can manage, and a face you sometimes can't.

---

The cold has a different math at eleven hours than it had at one.

At one hour it was a thing that came at you, a hand reaching in, and you fought it, you stamped your feet and rubbed your arms and made heat against it. At eleven it's just the temperature of the world and you're the same temperature as the world, or near it, and the fighting's gone out of you not because you quit but because the body learns there's nothing on the other side of the fighting worth the fuel. Cole sits in the chest-deep cold with his arms folded into himself and feels the slow, even, patient leak of himself going out into the steel and the water, and he doesn't fight it, he rations it, which is a different thing. He keeps his hands in. He keeps still. Still is cheap. He's spent thirty years learning what's cheap.

The water's at his waist when he sits and at his chest when he leans, and somewhere in the last hour it came up another finger, the high tide working its slow way in through the open throat of the passage they cut to get the suits. He thinks about that, distantly, the way you'd think about a debt — that the dive bought them the suits and the suits are keeping Sal's core warm and the kid's, and the price of the dive was the open passage, and the open passage is where the cold's coming in now, the warm-ish pocket bleeding its little heat out the door they had to leave open. Every good thing in here cost a thing. He's noticed that. He doesn't make a sentence of it. The sentence would have an edge he doesn't want to run his thumb along, not in front of himself.

He reaches out in the dark and finds the cut line, the traction line, and lays two fingers on it the way Ren showed him — not to pull, just to feel the tension, to make sure the leg's still being held and the cleat hasn't crept. The line's tight. The leg's held. Good. That's his job now, one of them — he's the counter-traction in this thing, the four-handed tool the room runs on, his strength and Ren's hands, and somewhere in the last hours he's stopped being able to tell whether he's glad of the job because it keeps a man alive or because a man who's the counter-traction is a man nobody can do without. Both. It's always both with him. That's the disease, the not being able to tell, and he stopped trying to cure it around the time the boat went over.

"Cole." Sal's voice, low, pitched for him, pitched under the room. "You holding that line, or you sleeping on me?"

"Holding it. It's tight."

"It's tight 'cause you set it tight. Kid's got the hands of a — " Sal stops, breathes, the breath catching on the leg. "Of a fish-cook. You got skipper hands. Always did."

It's nothing. It's an old man making talk in the dark to outrun the pain. But there's something in the way Sal lands on *always did* — the second time he's said it tonight, *you always did* — that makes a small thing turn over in the floor of Cole, the cold floor under the warm part, and Cole lays the warm part over it fast, the way he lays the warm part over everything.

"Twenty years you been telling me I got good hands," he says, easy. "Twenty years you been letting me reach the top shelf for you."

"Twenty years," Sal agrees. And then, after a while, after a long enough while that Cole thinks it's done and lets the line go a little slack in his attention: "She went over fast, didn't she."

Cole keeps his hand on the line. Keeps the tension reading. The room is dark and the water laps and the kid breathes his slow shallow breaths against Sal's shoulder and Ren's chin drops and catches, and Cole says, level, "Forty seconds. About. Fast as I've seen."

"Fast as you've seen." Sal turns the words over. "I been on her nine seasons, Vantine. She heels and she stands up. That's her whole personality, that boat. She's a stander-upper. She heeled like that a hundred times this season alone, riding heavy as she rode, and she always — " the breath catches again, and this time Cole can't tell if it's the leg or the sentence " — she always stood back up. So what I keep coming back to, lying here, is. She didn't heel. She just laid down. She laid down like she was tired. Like there was something already over her she couldn't carry."

There it is.

Cole has been waiting for it for eleven hours, since the first hour, since he hauled Sal up onto the shelf and felt the cook's eyes find his in the last of the early dark before the torch was even rationed, and he has had eleven hours to build the answer, and the answer is built, and it is smooth and true-enough and ready, and he does not use it.

He doesn't use it because Sal isn't asking.

That's the thing he understands, lying his hand on the traction line in the black — Sal isn't asking a question. A question wants an answer. Sal is telling him something. Sal, who has been on the *Adeline Marsh* nine seasons, who has stood in the galley with his hands in the dough and felt through the soles of his ruined feet exactly how the boat rode and how high they stacked the pots and how late they ran the gear when the weather was building — Sal is telling him, in the dark, gently, with a broken leg and a thread of life that runs straight through Cole's two good hands, that he knows. Not what, maybe. Not the whole of it. But enough. Enough to know the boat that laid down tired had a reason to be tired, and that the reason had a man's name on it.

"She rode heavy," Cole says. Quiet. He gives that much, because that much is fleet-wide, that much every boat in the Bering does, that much hides a thing in plain sight. "We all rode heavy. Short season. You don't make the season light, you don't make the boat payment, none of us." He hears his own voice do the thing it does, the warm even thing, the hand on the tiller, and he hears, underneath it, in the place he tries not to listen, the cold thing checking whether the warm thing landed. "She was a good boat. The weather came up wrong. That's the sea, Sal. That's just the sea."

The water laps. Sal is quiet for a long time.

"That's just the sea," Sal says, at last. And there's no fight in it. There's something worse than fight in it — there's a kind of letting it go, a kind of agreeing not to say the next thing, and Cole feels the shape of the trade arrive in the dark between them, unspoken and total and clean.

Sal will not say the next thing. Because Cole's hand is on the line that's holding his leg, and Cole's lungs are the only lungs in this box that can run the passage if the passage needs running again, and Cole is the difference between Sal seeing his daughter and Sal riding the pile out past the shelf the way Pavel's riding it now. Sal needs him. Sal needs him too much to say the thing he knows, and Cole needs Sal to not say it, and so the two of them have made, in the dark, without a word that names it, the only kind of peace this box has on offer: the peace of a man who needs you not looking too hard at what you are.

And Cole — and this is the part he'll carry, this is the part that gets into the cold floor under the warm part and stays — Cole is grateful. He is grateful to Sal for needing him. He is grateful the way a guilty man is grateful for a locked door, and he is ashamed of the gratitude in the same breath, and the shame and the gratitude don't cancel, they just sit in him together, two true things at the same temperature, the way everything in here is the same temperature now.

---

He has one more job to do before the long dark sets in, and it's a real one, which is good, because a real job is a place to put your hands and your hands are the only part of you that ever stops lying.

The kid is wrong.

He's been wrong for an hour, and Cole's been not-saying it the way they all not-say things now, but the kid is wrong and there's no torch to throw on it so Cole does it by hand, in the dark, the way you check a sleeping man you're afraid is dead. He reaches across Sal — "easy, just reaching past you" — and lays the back of his fingers against the kid's cheek above the suit's collar. The cheek is cold the way the steel is cold, and that's expected, everything's cold, but it's the wrongness under the cold he's checking for, and he finds it: the kid's stopped shivering.

An hour ago the boy was shaking so hard his teeth made a sound, the deep teeth-rattling shake of a body still arguing with the cold, throwing everything it had at the fire trying to keep the fire lit. Now nothing. Now the boy is still and quiet and his breathing's gone slow and shallow and his cheek is cold and slack, and Cole knows — he's hunted enough, been on deck through enough bad nights, lost enough men to know — that the still quiet ones are not the ones doing better. The shaking is the body fighting. When the shaking stops, the fight's gone out of the body the way it went out of Cole's an hour ago, only the body's got a lot less margin than Cole's got, the body's young and thin and never built for this, the body lied about its experience to get this berth and the cold doesn't care what the body told the owner.

"Ren," he says. Not loud. But not easy now either. "Ren, wake up. The kid."

He hears her come awake all at once, the way she does, no slow part to it — one breath she's asleep and the next she's a paramedic, the change is total and a little frightening, like a light he didn't know was off coming on. "What," she says. "What's wrong."

"He's stopped shaking. He's gone cold and quiet and he's not — he's barely answering."

She's across the grating in the dark before he finishes, her hands finding the kid by feel, and Cole feels her go to work beside him in the black, two fingers to the throat for the pulse, the back of a hand to the cheek, the ear down close to the slow shallow breath. He can't see any of it.
