He can only hear it — the small efficient sounds of her hands, the held quality of her own breathing while she counts the boy's, the way she goes utterly silent for the count and you could hear a drip fall.

And then he hears the thing he'll think about later. He hears her stop.

Not stop working — she keeps working, she's tucking the kid's hands up under his arms, she's pulling Sal's good arm over to make a second warm body against the boy's other side, she's doing the things — but something in her *stops*, some forward pressure in her hands eases off, the way a man eases off a winch when the line's not going to come no matter how he hauls. Cole knows that easing. He's done it himself, on deck, on a pot that's fouled past saving, on a line you finally let go because hauling it is just spending yourself on a thing that's already decided. The hands keep moving but the wanting's gone out of them.

He doesn't know what it means about her. He can't know — he doesn't know about London, doesn't know about the child on the board, doesn't know there's a thing in Ren that does exactly this, that reads a body and *decides* and once it's decided stops spending itself, the cold efficient mercy that is also the cruelest thing she carries. He doesn't know any of that. He only knows the sound of a hand letting go of a fouled line, and he knows it on her now, and some cold instrument in him takes the reading and files it without his permission: *the medic's calling the kid.*

And he should say something. There's a thought under the not-saying, and it comes up the way the worst ones do, fast and from the cold floor, and it isn't about the boy living — that ledger's closed, the medic's hands closed it — it's about what's leaving with him. *That's a quiet voice going out the door.* A boy who couldn't read a load line or a stability cert, who saw nothing and would have asked nothing, the one body in this box that was never going to cost Cole a question — and the dark is taking it, and some instrument in him registers the going as a kind of loss in its own column, separate from the boy, separate from grief, a thing subtracted from his own safety while a child dies six inches from his hand. The recognizing of it is so cold that for once Cole doesn't slam the door, he flinches from it — flinches the way you'd flinch from your own hand on a hot stove, fast, before the burn even lands — because there are sums even Cole won't run, and this is one, this is the one, weighing a dying boy by what his silence was worth. The boy's not a column. The boy's a boy. The boy's nineteen and lied to get here and called Cole *sir* the first morning and Cole told him not to, and the boy's cold and quiet against the steel and the medic's hands have let go of him.

"Ren," Cole says, careful, low, the warm laid very thin over a thing he doesn't have a name for. "You good? What're we — what do you need from me. Tell me what to do for him."

A pause. In the dark. The water laps. Sal has gone still and silent, listening, the way the whole room goes still when the medic's hands are on the youngest one.

"Keep the line tight on Sal," Ren says. Flat. Even. The same flat even she uses for a number she's writing on a chart. "And start bailing the low corner. Tide's still coming. We can't let the suits go under."

It's a good order. It's the right order. The tide is coming and the suits keeping Sal and the kid warm are only worth anything dry, and there is real work in the low corner and a man should do real work. Cole takes the order. He takes it the way the room takes his orders, because she's the medical voice the way he's the steady voice, and you stand on the voice that's yours to stand on.

But he hears what's under it, or he thinks he does — he hears her turn away from the boy to the tide, he hears her spend the room's hands on the thing that can still be saved instead of the thing she's decided can't, and there is a cold piece of him, the piece he hates, the piece that's kept him alive and ashamed in equal measure his whole life, that recognizes it. He knows that move. He made the same move at the bottom of the dive, when he let go of Pavel's hand and went up. You let go of the one that's gone and you go to the one that isn't. It's the right move. It's always the right move. It's the move that keeps you the most useful man in the room and lets a death go uncharged at the same time, and the terrible thing — the thing Cole understands in the dark, holding the line, while Ren bails the corner and the kid goes quiet against the steel — the terrible thing is that he and the medic might be made of the same metal after all.

He doesn't think that all the way to the end of the sentence either. He's careful, even here. Especially here.

---

The dark is full when it's done. The bailing slows and stops — there's only so much you can move with a bait tote against a tide that doesn't care — and the room settles into the long cold sit, four bodies wedged on a galley-table of grating with the water at their waists and the steel sweating a foot over their heads and the air gone close and used and faintly copper under the diesel. Cole keeps his hand on the traction line. He keeps the leg held. He keeps still, rationing himself out into the cold an even thread at a time.

He thinks: *Sal knows.* And: *Sal needs me.* And the two thoughts don't fight, they fit, they lock together the way two good thoughts lock when a man's spent his whole life loading a deck to make the weight come out right. Sal knows and Sal needs him, and need is the only court that has ever acquitted anybody Cole ever watched. He's seen it on deck a hundred times — the deckhand everyone half-suspects of skimming the share, the engineer everyone knows drinks, the skipper everyone whispers about — and they stay, they all stay, season after season, because they're useful, because the boat needs the hands more than the boat needs the truth about the hands. A drowning room doesn't audit its rescuers. It just counts whether the hands work. His hands work. His hands are working right now, holding a broken man's leg together in the dark, and a man whose hands are working that hard is not a man anyone in this box can afford to look at too long.

He can work with that. He's always been able to work with that. It's not even a comfort, exactly — it's colder than a comfort, it's a tool, it's the tool he reached for in the dark when he understood that Sal had stopped asking and started knowing — but it holds his weight, and right now holding his weight is the whole of the job.

*Sal knew, and Sal needed him, and need was the only court that had ever acquitted anybody.* He turns it over once more in the black, the way Sal turned over *she laid down like she was tired*, and finds it solid, and rests his cold weight on it the way the whole room rests its weight on his hands.

He can work with need.

The water comes up another finger while he's resting on it, and the kid against the steel does not shiver, and Cole holds the line, and does not reach for the torch, because there's nothing in the dark he wants to see.


---


# Chapter Thirteen

## Her Turn in the Water

*POV: Ren — T+12:00*

Twelve hours under, and the dark has stopped being a thing that happened to her and become the place she lives.

She has learned to do without her eyes the way you learn to do without a hand you've lost — not by getting it back but by re-routing every job through what's left. She knows the room by its temperatures now. The cold strip of grating under her thighs. The colder steel a foot above her crown, weeping its slow film down the back of her neck. The slightly-less-cold patch on her left that is Sal's bulk, radiating the last of itself through the orange suit. The flat dead cold straight ahead, off the high ground, where the water sits black and patient and full of two men who used to breathe. She maps it all without a single photon, the way she used to read a darkened room by the sound of a patient's breath and the smell coming off the skin.

She wakes — she must have slept, her chin's been doing the drop-and-catch — to the sound of the water against the steel having changed.

That's the thing she came up out of sleep for. Not a voice, not a groan of gear. The lap of the water against the hull has gone slack. For hours it has had a push in it, a small insistent shoulder leaning the level up a finger at a time, the flood tide working in through the open throat of the passage they cut. Now the push is gone. The water just slops, aimless, the way water does when it's between minds about which way to go.

Slack. The tide has crested and is sitting at the top of its swing, deciding, and in a few minutes it will start to ebb, and the current that pulses through the flooded companionway with the hull's roll will be at its weakest it's going to be for the next six hours.

She does the arithmetic before she's all the way awake. That is the part of her that never sleeps, the part London couldn't kill, the part that lies there in the freezing dark and runs numbers because numbers are a place to put your weight. *Slack water now. Strongest the divers will ever be is already behind us, twelve hours behind us. Coldest the water will ever be is still ahead. If the second dive is going to happen, it happens on a slack, and the slacks are running out faster than the bodies are.*

The second dive. She has been not-deciding it for hours, the way you don't decide a thing you've already decided.

---

"Cole." She pitches it low, under the room, the way they've all learned to talk now — the dark has taught them to speak the way you speak in a sick-room. "You awake."

"I'm awake." His voice comes from the corner where the traction line runs, steady, unsurprised, a man who sleeps the way a watch-stander sleeps, half of him always on the wheel. "You alright?"

"Tide's gone slack."

A pause. She hears him hear it — hears the small shift as he tilts his head to the water, confirming what she's already confirmed, and she knows he's arrived where she's arrived because his breathing changes, goes a notch deeper, a man squaring up to a thing.

"It has," he says.

"We're going for the beacon."

She lays it down flat, no question in it, because a question is a thing he can manage and a statement is a thing he has to argue against in the open. She's learned that about him in twelve hours, learned it the way you learn the tells of someone you're trapped with — Cole is easiest to read when you give him nothing soft to push on. He's a man who steers. Give him a course and he'll find the gentle reasons to alter it. Give him a fixed mark and he has to come at it head-on.

"The beacon," he says. Careful. "Ren — "

"The float-free unit. The one that's been knocking down there since the second hour." She keeps her own voice level, clinical, the chart-voice. "It never floated free. It's fouled in the gear in the passage. Which means it never got an antenna up, which means it never keyed, which means we don't know if anybody on this earth knows we're upside down ninety miles off nowhere." She lets that sit one beat. "We've been telling each other a rescue's coming for twelve hours. I want to stop telling each other and find out. If it's fouled we free it. If it's dead we know we're on our own clock, and we ration accordingly. Either way we stop guessing."

The room is quiet. Sal, who she'd have sworn was unconscious, says nothing, but his breathing has gone shallow and held, the breathing of a man listening hard with his eyes shut.

"It's a long swim," Cole says.

"Twenty-two feet. One dog-leg." She gives him his own numbers back, the ones he reeled off in the first hours with that strange exactness, the length of a passage he claimed never to have walked. "You did it on a breath. So can I."

"That's the suits I went for. The suits were ten feet in. The beacon's fouled in the gear *past* the dog-leg, down toward the chart locker, that's — " a breath " — that's another eight, ten feet, and it's down, you'd be diving down into the engine flat where Dee — " He stops. Doesn't say it. *Where Dee is.* "It's a different swim, Ren. It's a worse swim. It's colder now and the water's deeper in there and it's a death trap of loose gear in the black. I've made it once and I wouldn't make it twice for the suits, let alone for a beacon that might already be dead."

"I know," she says.

"You're not the diver I am."

"I know that too."

And here it is, the place she's been steering toward since she woke up, and she gives him the operational truth because the operational truth is real and load-bearing and she can stand on it the whole way down: "That's exactly why it's me. If you go and it goes wrong, the room loses its only diver *and* the only counter-traction strong enough to hold Sal's leg. We can't lose both halves of the tool, Cole. You hold the line, I go in the water. The room can survive me drowning. It can't survive you drowning. It's not brave, it's just the only allocation that doesn't kill everybody if it fails."

She hears him take that in. She hears it land, because it's true, and because it's the kind of true he respects — the deck-load kind, the weighing of what goes over the side. For a second she thinks he'll take it.

He doesn't.

---

"Let me go," he says, and there's a warmth comes into his voice now, the one he uses on the kid, the one he used on the whole room in the first hour. "I know that passage. I've got the lungs. I'll go down, I'll see if she's foulin' on something I can clear, and if she's dead she's dead and I'll come up and tell you and we'll know. You don't need to get wet for this. You're the one keepin' people alive up here — you're worth more dry."

It's a good argument. It's a *kind* argument, on its face, the kind that makes him the man who spends himself so she doesn't have to. And she lies there in the dark listening to it and feels, under the warmth, the thing she has started to feel under everything Cole says, a cold seam she can't yet name, like running your thumb along a weld and finding a place where the bead skipped.

Because here is what she does not say.

She does not say that she wants to see the chart locker. The chart locker that Cole moved Marcus off of in the first hour, easy, plausible — *nothing back there but glass and a drowned radio, kid* — the chart table he routed all of them away from with that managing gentleness. She does not say that when she asked him in the fourth hour which switch he keyed for the manual beacon, what color the confirm light was, he answered too smooth and too fast and she filed it without knowing she was filing it, and the file has been sitting in her ever since, getting heavier. She does not say that a man who's so sure he keyed a beacon and got a green light would not plan, the way Cole has been planning, for a thirty-hour float instead of a six-hour rescue. She does not say that the float-free EPIRB is fouled in the *un-dumped deck gear* — the gear that should have been let go, in a roll, by a man at the wheel who didn't let it go — and that she has started, lying here in the dark with nothing to do but think, to wonder *why* it didn't get dumped, and that the wondering has a shape, and the shape has been getting clearer the colder she gets.

She does not say: *I want to go down there with my own hands and not yours, because I have started to be afraid of what your hands have already touched.*

She'd call it confirming their rescue status. That's the file label. That's the comfortable name for it, and she knows, the way she knows the math of cold, that it's a name she's choosing because the true name is one she's not ready to carry up onto the high ground where she has to keep him alive. She is triaging her own attention, pointing it at the survival task and away from the thing underneath, the way she's pointed it away from harder things her whole life. *You can find the truth or save the bodies. Not both, not at once.* For now she's calling it bodies.

But she's going in the water herself. That part she's not negotiating.

"You're worth more dry too," she tells him, and lets it sound like a return of his kindness, which it is, and is also her closing the door. "You're the line. I need the strongest hands on this boat on the other end of a rope I'm tied to, not down in the water with me where there's nobody to pull either of us out. You hold me, I'll do the swimming. That's the call."

The water laps, slack and mindless. Somewhere off the shelf, Pavel rides his slow circuit in the cold.

"Sal," Cole says, not to her. "Talk sense to her."

And Sal — Sal, who has been listening with his eyes shut, Sal who knows things in this dark that Ren is only starting to know — Sal says, after a long breath that catches halfway on the leg, "Leave her be, Vantine. The girl wants to put her own hand on the thing." And there is something in how he says it, some weight under the wheeze, that makes Cole go very quiet, quiet the way a man goes quiet when another man has said more than the words. Ren files that too. She has a lot of files now.

"Alright," Cole says, at last. Soft. "Alright. We'll rig you a tether."

---

The task is the thing. The task is always the thing, the rope you hold instead.

She works it in the dark by feel, and the working steadies her the way work always steadies her, sorts the room back into problems with solutions. They have line — they have nothing *but* line, coils of it salvaged off the gear and off the splint rig, half-inch poly, slick and stiff with cold. She runs a length through her hands to find its breaks and frays, foot by foot, the rough fibers catching on the numb pads of her fingers where the sensation has gone grainy, like reading Braille through a glove. Her hands have maybe — she does the math without wanting to — maybe four of ten good minutes left in them up here in the air, and the second she's in that thirty-four-degree water the cold-incapacitation clock starts eating them down from the other end, fast. *Ten minutes of working hands in the water if she were fresh and warm. She is neither. Call it three. Three minutes of fingers that close.*

So the rig has to be a thing her hands can manage half-dead and blind. She ties a bowline around her own waist — a loop that won't slip and crush her ribs, that she could throw off one-handed if she had to — and tests it with her whole weight against the cleat. She measures out the line in arm-spans, counting, so Cole on the other end will know by the marks how deep she is: a figure-eight knot every fathom, six feet, her fingers fumbling the tucks, having to do two of them twice because the cold's made her clumsy and a sloppy knot in a tether is a way to die. She works a bight into the bitter end for Cole's hands to hold, big enough that even his cold fists can't lose it.

"Signals," she says, because this is the part that keeps people alive and she's run it a hundred times in another life, on ropes and on radios and on the held hands of people going under anesthesia. "One pull, I'm fine, paying out. Two pulls, give me slack. Three pulls — " she puts her hand flat on the line so he'll feel it through the tension while she says it " — three pulls is haul me up, hard, don't wait, don't ask, just haul. If I go three you don't get to decide it's not really three. You haul."

"I haul," Cole says.

"If the line goes slack and stays slack and there's no signal, that's me caught or me out. You count ten and you haul anyway. Better you drag me out hung up on something than leave me down there being polite about it."

"Count of ten, then haul." His voice has changed. It's gone flat and operational, the warmth banked, and she trusts this version of him more than the other one, the way you trust a man more when he's stopped trying to handle you and started just doing the work. *That,* she thinks, *is the difference between care and competence, and I am betting my life on the competence and trying not to think about the care.*

She strips down to what she'll dive in — not naked, that's a way to die of the cold-shock gasp before you ever reach the beacon, but down to the base layers, peeling off the soaked outer bibs that'll only drag and snag, her fingers shaking so hard on the zip that Cole has to find it for her in the dark and run it down, his big hands gentle and sure, and for a second their hands are tangled at her waist and his are so much warmer than hers that she has to fight the animal urge to just hold on to the warmth, to not go anywhere, to stay up here in the bad air where at least she's breathing.

That urge is the body. The body will let you not go. The body is full of good reasons to stay exactly where the dying is slow.

"You don't have to do this," Cole says, low, just for her, his hands still warm at her waist. One more try, and it doesn't sound like managing now, it sounds like a man afraid for her, and that's worse, because she can't tell anymore which of his kindnesses are real and which are the kind that do work he needs done, and the not-being-able-to-tell is the whole shape of the thing she's going down there to find out.

"Hold the line tight," she says, and takes his hands off her waist, and gives him the bight.

---

She gets herself to the lip of the high ground where the grating drops off into the black water, and sits with her legs in it to the knee, and the cold comes up her shins like a closing door and she makes herself breathe through it, slow, fighting the gasp before she's even under, because the gasp underwater is the killer, the gasp underwater is how Marcus's body will die if the cold gets that far into him, the gasp is the first lie the cold tells you and you have to call it before it's spoken.

She fixes the dive in her mind the way she fixed the roll in the first minute, re-soldering the directions: *Down is the sky. The passage mouth is below and to the left, where the cold's worst, where the draft comes up from the open throat. Follow the cold. The dog-leg is to the right around the reefer trunk. Past it, down, the gear, the beacon, Dee.* She fixes the count — the breath she'll spend, the kicks, the one return she gets and no second one.

Behind her in the dark, Sal has started, very quietly, to pray — not the flat hopeless string from the first hour but something slower, an old man saying words over a young woman going into the water, and she doesn't believe in it and is glad of it anyway, the way she was glad of the prayer in the first minute, a sound to count by.

She finds the bitter end of the line one more time, feels along it to where it leaves her hands and runs through the dark to his, and pulls it taut between them — testing, the way you test any line before you trust your weight to it — and feels Cole take up the slack on the far end, feels his grip come on solid and even, a thousand pounds of pots couldn't drag that grip loose, the best and strongest pair of hands in the box closing around the rope that is the only thing between her and a black ninety miles of water.

And she understands, with a clarity she does not like, sitting on the lip of the cold with her own life knotted around her waist, that she is about to go looking for the truth about what Cole did — and that the rope that will bring her back up, the rope she is trusting with the last of her breath, is held at the other end by the man she has gone down there to look into.

She breathes the foul air all the way down to the bottom of her lungs, one last time, holds it, and lets the cold take her under.


---


# Chapter Fourteen

## Three Good Reasons

*POV: Cole — T+13:00*

The line is half-inch poly, wet and stiff with the cold, and Cole has maybe sixty feet of it coiled against his chest, and the far end of it is tied around Ren's waist with a bowline he checked twice while she pretended not to let him.

He checked it because a man checks a knot. That's the reason he'd give. It's a true reason. It's also that a bowline is a thing his hands can do in the dark without his head, and his head is busy doing the other thing — running the arithmetic of how much line he has to give before she reaches the place he doesn't want her to reach, and whether he can make the giving of it look like nothing.

"You don't have to do it this way," he says. Easy. The level voice, the hand on the tiller. He's said it three ways now and he's about to say it a fourth, and he knows the fourth time is one time too many and he says it anyway because the cold has thinned out the part of him that knows when to stop. "I've got the lungs, Ren. I made the passage twice. You send me, you keep your hands here for Sal, and if it goes wrong I'm the one in the water, not the medic. That's the right load. You put the strongest swimmer on the worst dive and you keep the doctor on the dock. Anybody'd tell you that."

In the dark he can hear her not answering. He can hear her checking her own gear by feel — the slap of her palm finding the strobe she's clipped to her chest, the small wet sounds of a person making themselves ready to leave air. He can hear Sal breathing through his teeth in the corner, and the kid not making any sound at all, which is a sound of its own now, a hole in the room where the kid's shivering used to be.

"You hold the line," Ren says. Flat. "That's your job. You feel me stop, you wait. Two tugs means I'm down still working — you give me line. Three tugs means haul, haul like the boat's on fire. You don't haul on your own clock. You haul on mine."

"On yours."

"Say it back."

"Two tugs, give you line. Three tugs, I haul. You stop, I wait." He keeps his voice warm over it. Underneath, the cold thing is already at work, because *you stop, I wait* is exactly the instruction he wanted and could not have asked for, and she's just handed it to him as if it were hers. *If she stops, he waits.* And if she stops at the chart locker — if she stops at the one place in this drowned boat a man would stop, the place he's steered every set of eyes away from since the first dark hour — then the rule says he waits, and waiting is the same shape as letting, and a man can let a thing happen and call it following orders.

He doesn't follow that thought to the floor. He's careful. He lays the warm part over it.

"Then there's the cold," he says instead. Reason number two, or three, he's lost the count of his own reasons and that's bad, that's the cold in him too. "You go in unsuited. The sea's the same thirty-four it was at the roll but you're not the same. None of us are. Twelve hours in this and your gas reflex is going to cut your breath in half the second the water takes your face. You'll think you've got forty seconds and you'll have twenty. I had a salvage diver's lungs once and even I came up gray last time. You're a good diver, Ren. You're not a salvage diver. And the engine flat — the engine flat's a death trap, it's gear and cable and Dee's body somewhere in it, and a person who panics down there one time doesn't get a second one. I'm not — " he hears the warm crack, just slightly, and smooths it " — I'm just telling you what's down there. You go to the suit locker side, fine, you've watched me run it. You start working aft toward the flat, that's a different dive. That's a worse dive."

It's all true. That's the thing he keeps arriving at, in the dark, with the line cold in his hands: every word of it is true. The cold will cut her breath. The engine flat is a trap. Dee is down there in the dark with her bracketed tablet and her drowned hair, and a person could die finding her. He's not lying to Ren. He has not had to lie to Ren once tonight, and that is the part that should comfort him and doesn't, because he's known for thirty years that the most dangerous true thing a man can say is the true thing he's saying for the wrong reason.

He doesn't make the sentence about the wrong reason. He stops just short of it. He always stops just short of it.

---

"I know what's down there," Ren says.

She says it quiet, and there's something in the quiet that makes the cold floor of him tilt. Because she doesn't say it the way you say a thing you've been told. She says it the way you say a thing you've worked out.

"You know what's down there," Cole says. Carefully. Keeping the line slack and even in his hands so his hands don't tell her anything his voice doesn't.

"I know the EPIRB's down there. The float-free. The one that's supposed to pop loose and key the satellite and tell somebody a position." A pause. The water laps against the steel; somewhere a finger's width higher than it was an hour ago. "We've been listening to it knock for half a day, Cole. It's been knocking against the hull since the first night. It didn't float free. So either it's broke, or it's fouled, and either way the thing that's supposed to be calling for us isn't calling for us, and I'd like to know which, because the answer's the difference between a six-hour wait and a thirty-hour one, and we are past hour thirteen of the thirty."

She's laid it out like a chart. Closed mid-shaft, thigh tight, the cold starting the compartment clock — that's how she talks about a leg, and this is how she talks about a beacon, and the precision of it is a thing he's admired all night the way you admire a tool that fits the hand, and it is the precise thing in her that he has been most afraid of since the wheelhouse, since *she was froze through, send her below.* A detail-keeper. A woman who counts. He took her off the bridge so no one would count the deck-load and the heading and how late he held, and now she's down here counting anyway, counting the one thing left to count, and the line that tethers her to him runs straight from her counting to his ruin.

"You keyed the manual one," Ren says. Not a question. She's giving him the version he gave her, holding it up in the dark to see if it still holds water. "The bridge EPIRB. You said you keyed it before the roll. Green confirm light."

"I keyed it." He says it the way he's said it eight times, smooth, and for the first time in his life the smoothness sounds to his own ear like exactly what it is, a thing worn smooth by handling, and he wonders if it sounds that way to her. He reaches, the way he's reached eight times, for the memory of his own thumb on the switch and the green light coming up, and the memory is there, and it is exactly as thin as it was the first time, a picture of a thing rather than the thing, and he can't tell anymore — he genuinely cannot tell, in the cold, at hour thirteen — whether he keyed it or only wanted to have keyed it badly enough that the wanting laid down a memory in the shape of the doing. *He keyed it.* He needs to have keyed it. The room needs him to have keyed it. Need has always been enough for him. It's enough now.

"Then we've got a position out there already," Ren says. "If you keyed it. So tell me why we're rationing a torch like nobody's coming, and you're checking that strobe every two hours like a man who's not sure."

The cold floor under him drops a clear inch.

She's not asking it cruel. That's the worst of it. She's asking it the way she'd ask which switch, what color light — clean, level, a clinician's question, the kind that wants a number and not a fight. And he has the answer built, he's had it built since Ferro went into the water, *a man checks his gear, you'd check yours too, hope's not a thing you spend, you bank it* — and it is a good answer and it is even true and he opens his mouth to spend it.

And from the corner, in the dark, Sal says: "Ren."

Just her name. Low, pitched under the room, the cook's thin pain-boiled voice. And in it Cole hears the thing Sal isn't saying, the thing the two of them signed in the dark two hours back — *let it lie, girl, the man's hand is on my leg* — and he understands that Sal has heard exactly where this is going and is trying, in his old tired complicit way, to put a hand on it.

It almost works.

"I'm going down," Ren says. "We're losing the slack. Hold the line."

---

She goes in like a person who's decided. No splash, no gasp she can't help — she's braced for the gasp, he can hear her brace for it, the long flat exhale and then the held breath and then the slip of her body off the grating shelf into the black water, smooth, controlled, a diver and not a drowning. The line begins to run.

It runs through his hands cold and steady and he lets it. He's good at this — counter-traction, anchor, the four-handed tool the room runs on, his strength on one end of a line and someone else's life on the other. He's held a man's leg together with this same grip. He's held a basket steady off a heaving deck. He pays out the poly a foot at a time, feeling for her through it, because a tether is a kind of nerve and a good anchor man reads his diver the way Ren reads a pulse — through the line, the small tugs and surges, the rhythm of a body working in the dark.

Twenty feet. She's at the passage mouth. The line drags left around the reefer trunk, the dog-leg, the way it dragged for him on the suit dive, and he feeds it through and around and clocks the reefer trunk by the change in the drag the way he clocks everything. Thirty feet. She's into the throat now, past the open door they cut and can't close, down where the cold comes up to bleed the room. He gives her the line.

He could pull her now.

The thought arrives whole, fully built, the way the bad ones always arrive fastest and most complete. Three tugs is haul — but tugs run both ways, and a man's hands can give the signal as easily as read it. A smart haul, a *caution* haul, a man feeling his diver linger and bringing her up early for her own good — who would ever fault him. *She was twelve hours cold and unsuited and her breath was going to cut and I made the call to bring her up, I'd make it again.* It's the move he'd praise in another anchor man. It is the safe loving prudent move and it would, very cleanly, with no fingerprints on it at all, keep her from reaching the bottom of the passage where the float-free EPIRB hangs strangled in the un-dumped gear — strangled, he knows, in the exact pots that should have gone over the side, the pots his hand never released — and if she never reaches it she never sees how it's fouled, and if she never sees how it's fouled then the thing that didn't call for help stays a thing the sea did and not a thing a man did.

He has the line. His hand closes on it. The rule is her clock — two tugs to give, three to haul, and she's signaled neither. He could pull on his own clock and call it weather.

Forty feet. The line stops paying out.

She's stopped.

He stands in the chest-deep cold with the poly gone still in his fists and he reads her through it the way he reads everything, and what he reads is this: she's not in trouble. There's no thrash in the line, no surge, no fight — a diver in trouble fills the tether with panic and he's felt that and this isn't that. This is a diver who has reached a thing and is working it. He can feel her hands going, faint and far, transmitted up forty feet of wet line as the smallest tremor — the rhythm of someone working with their fingers at the edge of where their fingers still close. She's found something. She's stopped at the depth of the chart locker, the depth of the engine flat, the depth of every place he's spent thirteen hours steering the room's eyes away from, and she is down there in the killing dark *searching*, and the line in his hands has gone the slack of a woman who's stopped swimming and started reaching.

His grip tightens. His whole arm wants to haul. No signal from her — not the three tugs that mean haul, not the two that mean give, no tug at all — just a man's animal want to reel the danger back up into the air where he can manage it with his warm voice and his good hands. *Bring her up. She's lingered. Bring her up, it's the cautious call, no one will ever —*

He makes himself wait.

He makes himself open his hand back to slack, one finger at a time, the way Ren eases off a winch on a line that's not going to come, and it's the hardest physical thing he does all night, harder than the suit dive, harder than holding Sal's leg through the screaming — to make his own hand *not work*, to make the most useful hands in the box do nothing, to stand still and pay out line to the woman diving toward the bottom of him.

Because here is the cold true thing, and at hour thirteen the cold has thinned him out enough that he lets himself see it for one whole second before he covers it back over: if he hauls her now, she'll know. Not the way she half-knows now, in questions, in the seam in his beacon story she can't quite hear under the wear. She'll *know* — because a diver doesn't forget the feel of a tether yanking her off a thing the instant she reaches it, and a woman who counts will count that too, will add the early haul to the steered-away chart locker to the too-exact passage to the green light that's only a picture of a green light, and the sum of all of it will have his name on it as plain as a deck-load on a manifest. The surest way to point at a thing is to be the man who pulled her off it. The surest way to bury it is to let her come up holding nothing, the way he came up holding nothing, the way the dead come up holding nothing.

So he gives her the line.

He stands in the dark and feeds the slack poly out into the cold throat of the boat he drowned, to the one set of eyes in the world that can read what's down there, and he holds his own hands still by main force, and it is the bravest thing he's done in his life and it is the most cowardly thing he's done in his life and he understands, standing there, that for him those were always the same thing, that he has never once been able to tell them apart, and that the not-being-able-to-tell is the whole of what he is.

"She's down a while," Sal says, from the corner. Careful. The old man can't see the line but he can hear Cole's breathing change, the way a cook who's fed a man twenty years can hear it.

"She's working something," Cole says. Level. "She's alright. Line's quiet."

"You'd bring her up. If it went wrong. You'd —"

"I'd bring her up."

And that's true too. If the line filled with panic he'd haul her so fast he'd burn his palms on the poly, he'd have her in the air in five seconds, he'd breathe for her if he had to. He would save Ren Okafor's life with his whole body and not think twice — he's already saved it once, off the rack, the night Pavel's knock went quiet behind the gear-shed and the count of them held at four. His hands are honest about saving her. It's only his patience that's the lie. It's only the waiting that has a wrong reason in it. He can save her and ruin her in the same minute with the same two hands, the saving real and the ruin disguised as restraint, and a drowning room will only ever see the saving, because a drowning room counts whether the hands work and his hands work, his hands are working right now, working at the terrible labor of doing nothing.

The line, far down, gives one small surge. Not the three tugs that mean haul. Just a body shifting its grip on something heavy. Pulling something loose, or trying to. The tremor of her hands comes up the wet line into his, faint, slowing — slowing the way hands slow when the breath's running out and the cold's running in, and he feels her start to spend the last of it, the part of the breath you're not supposed to spend, the part the body lets you spend because the body's worst kindness is letting you push past where you should've turned.

His hand wants the line. He holds it slack.

*Come up,* he thinks, and means it, both ways at once, the saving way and the burying way braided together so tight he can't get a thumbnail between them. *Come up empty. Come up holding nothing. Come up alive and ignorant and let me keep being the man who pulled you out, and not the man you'll have to drown.*

The line goes still. Then it tugs once — sharp, deliberate, a person signaling *coming up* and not *help* — and then it begins, fast, to come back at him, the slack reversing, Ren rising hand over hand up her own tether toward the air, and Cole takes it in and coils it against his chest as fast as it comes, hauling now, hauling for real, his arms doing the honest work at last, and he can't tell — he genuinely cannot tell, and this is the cold truth he'll carry up into whatever's left of his life — whether his chest is tight with the fear of losing her or with the fear of what she's bringing up in her fist.

He gave her the line. He let her go all the way down. He didn't pull her off the truth, and he'll never be sure if that was the one decent thing he did in the dark or only the cleverest.

The line comes faster. She's close. And in the half-second before her head breaks the black water gasping, with whatever the cold kept for her held tight against her ribs, Cole understands that he gave her the line not because he was good, but because pulling her off it would have been the one mistake too obvious to outwork — and that this, the not being able to tell his courage from his cowardice, is the thing that's going to drown him long before the water does.

He gives her the line. It is the bravest cowardly thing he ever does.


---


# Chapter Fifteen

## What the Cold Keeps

*POV: Ren — T+14:00*

The last thing she does before she goes under is fix the count, because a count is a thing she can carry down into the black and bring back up, and almost nothing else is.

Four. Sal in the corner with the leg held long on its line, breathing the shallow upper breaths of a man who's learned that the deep ones cost. Marcus against him, quiet, the suit zipped to his chin, not shivering — she will not look at that number now, she has set that number down, she will pick it up when her hands are warm and not before. Cole behind her, the tether snubbed twice around his forearm and once around the cleat he drove for Sal's traction, his big feet braced on the grating, the anchor. And her. The fourth breath is her own, and she makes it slow on purpose, three down and four up, packing the lungs the way the salvage men taught Cole and Cole taught nobody, because Cole was supposed to be the one going down and she has overruled him, and now the breath is hers to spend and she is bad at it.

"You count me to forty," she says into the dark. Her voice slaps flat off the steel a foot over her head, no distance in it, a sound in a closed mouth. "Out loud, slow. At forty you pull whether I've tugged or not. Two tugs means down still working, give me line. Three tugs means haul, haul like the boat's on fire. No tugs at forty, you haul anyway. Say it back."

"Forty, then I haul." Warm, even, the hand on the tiller. "Two down, three up, no tugs I haul. Ren —" and there it is, the third time tonight, the warm reasonable pull of him — "let me go. My lungs are twice yours in this water. You hold Sal's line and I'll —"

"You can't hold Sal's line and pull me both." She has the answer built; she built it an hour ago and it is true and it is also not the whole of why. "If you're down and I'm up, and the rig creeps, I can't haul you and tend the leg with these hands. If I'm down and you're up, you can do both. It's the only way the room runs if it goes wrong. You're the anchor. I'm the diver. We don't have a second arrangement."

It is operationally sound. She hears it land on him as operationally sound, the way her flat true things land — there's no room in it to argue without arguing against the room. What she does not say, what stays down in the cold back drawer where she keeps the things she can't yet open, is the other half of the arithmetic: that the chart locker is down there, the one Cole has steered every body away from since the first hour, *nothing back there but glass and a drowned radio, kid, mind your hands;* that the float-free beacon has been knocking against the hull for thirteen hours, fouled in something, failing to rise; and that she wants her own hands on the truth of it and not his, wants to know whether the Coast Guard is coming or whether they have all spent the night holding a rope tied to nothing. She would call it *confirming our rescue status.* She is a paramedic. She knows how to give a clean clinical name to a thing she is afraid to call by its real one.

"Tie's good?" she says.

He checks it without being asked — she feels the line jump as his hand runs the knot at her waist, the bowline she tied and he re-dressed because he can't help re-dressing a knot, the salvage in him — and the small competence of it does the thing his competence always does, eases a quarter-turn in her she resents. *His hands are right.* She files it. The file is getting thick.

"Tie's good," Cole says. "Ren. Last thing. The engine flat's a death trap down there. Cable, sharp steel, the reefer trunk. You feel the dog-leg, you don't push past it. The suits are down the straight run; the chart side is the other way and there's nothing on it worth a breath. You hear me. *Nothing on it worth a breath.*"

"I hear you," she says.

And she does. She hears the three good reasons stacked up neat — cable, steel, the trunk — and she hears, the way she heard his breath manage itself in the first black minute and put a note beside it, that a man with nothing to hide does not build you three reasons to stay out of a room. She does not say that either. She breathes the breath. She lets the dark have her.

---

The water takes her face like a hand.

That is the first thing, every time, the thing no number prepares you for — the cold doesn't creep here, it *seizes,* it clamps the whole face at once and the gasp reflex fires up her throat and she clenches it down behind her teeth, because the gasp underwater is a lungful of the Bering Sea and the autopsies all read the same. She holds the air she packed. She gets a hand on the lip of the grating, the known edge, and pulls herself down and under, into the throat of the passage, and the steel slides past her shoulders into a black that is a different black than the dome's — heavier, total, a pressure on the eyes.

Four kicks to the dog-leg. She set the count before she went and she spends it now: one, the cold flooding the suit-less gaps of her, the collar of her jacket flushing ice down her spine; two, the current pulsing against her as the hull rolls its slow half-degree, shoving her toward the reefer trunk she can't see and then sucking back; three, her left hand reading the wall, the rivets, the weld she's memorized; four, and the wall falls away to the left where the passage bends, the dog-leg, the place Cole said don't push past.

The suits are the other way. Down the straight run, where the light of the strobe is staged at the mouth, where the known good thing is.

She pushes past.

She turns into the bend, into the part of the boat that is the engine flat, and the breath in her chest stops being air and becomes a number, and the number is counting down, and somewhere above her in a world she can no longer point to Cole is saying it out loud, slow — *eight, nine* — the only clock she has, a man's voice she half-trusts feeding her the seconds through a rope.

The engine flat is a drowned cathedral turned on its head. She knows the shape of it from a dry life — the long block of the main, the catwalk, Dee's little steel cubby with the screens — but it is all wrong now, all overhead become underfoot, and she navigates it the way she's learned to navigate everything in this hull, by reaching and by ruling out. Her right mitt finds cable, a swag of it loose in the water, and she remembers *cable, sharp steel* and works her hand along it palm-flat, not gripping, because a gripped hand in fouled cable in the dark is a hand you leave down here. The cold is in her wrists already. Of course it is. The shunt fired the instant the water took her; she has maybe ten minutes of hands in this water and she is not going to spend them, she is going to spend forty seconds and surface, that was the deal, *fourteen, fifteen,* she can hear it, faint, the rope carrying his count like a wire carries a far voice.

She is looking for two things and she does not let herself want them in any order, because wanting them in order is how you stay too long for the second one.

The beacon. The tablet. She finds the beacon first, because the beacon has been telling her where it is for thirteen hours.

---

It is the knocking.

She feels it before she finds it — a dull rhythmic *tock* coming up the water against her cheekbone, the float-free EPIRB tapping the hull on the hull's own slow roll, the sound that's been the room's bad lullaby since the first night, the thing Cole called *just physics, away or fouled, can't know.* She follows it with her ruled-out hand, the cable in her left, the dark in front, and her right mitt closes on the housing of it — the hard rounded shape of the canister, the antenna whip, the bracket — and she reads it the way she'd read a wound, by feel, fast, because the count is at *twenty* and the count is the only honest thing down here.

It did not fail.

That is what her hand tells her, all at once, the way a hand tells you a fracture is open before your eyes have caught up. The beacon is whole. The hydrostatic release has fired — she finds the popped collar of it, the bracket sprung, the thing has done its one job, it has let go to rise. It wants to rise. It is *trying* to rise, the buoyant canister straining up against its own tether of —

— of gear. Of line. She works her mitt up the strain and the strain runs into a snarl, a coiled wad of poly line and the steel rail of something her hand knows the shape of even drowned and upside down: the edge of a crab pot. A pot. A seven-hundred-pound steel cage that should be a hundred feet up in the open air on a deck, lashed in its stack, and instead is down here in the flooded guts of the hull with the beacon's float-line wrapped three times around its rail, holding the one thing that calls for help pinned to the one thing that should have been thrown away.

The deck didn't dump.

She has the sentence whole before she means to have it, and it is not a sentence about the leg, and there is no slot in the front of her mind to make it harmless. *The deck didn't dump.* The pots that should have gone over the side when the boat heeled — the pots a skipper releases to save a rolling boat, the lashings he trips, the weight he lets the sea have so the boat can stand back up — they are *here,* in the water with her, every one of them, undumped, and the beacon that would have called the Coast Guard fourteen hours ago is throttled in the one she happens to have her hand on, and she understands in a single cold drop, the same drop as the first minute of the wreck, *they have gone over, fully over,* that the boat did not just roll. The boat rolled *loaded.* The boat rolled with everything still lashed high that a man is supposed to let go.

*Twenty-five,* says the rope.

She could free it. Some animal part of her grabs at that, the medic's part, the part that catches falling numbers — get the line off the rail, let the beacon rise, let it find the sky, let it *call* — and her cold fingers go to the snarl and pull at it and the poly is swollen and frozen-stiff and three turns deep around a steel edge and her hands are mitts, are hooks, are the things the cold left her instead of fingers, and at *twenty-eight* she knows the truth of that the way she knows the truth of a leg that's going to come off: not in this breath. Not in five breaths. Not with these hands in this water. Freeing the beacon is a daylight job with two warm hands and a knife, and she has neither, and the count is running, and there is a second thing down here, the portable thing, the thing she *can* carry.

She lets the beacon go. She lets go of the one that calls for help because hauling on it is just spending herself on a thing that's already decided — and even as she does it, even as her cold hook of a hand opens and gives the strangled beacon back to the dark, some far room in her registers the shape of the choice and hates it, because it is *his* choice, it is the choice Cole made at the bottom of the dive when he let go of Pavel, it is the move that keeps you useful and lets a thing go uncharged, and she has just made it underwater with her own hand. You let go of the one that's gone. You go to the one you can carry.

She goes to Dee.

---

She knows where Dee's cubby is the way you know where a thing was in a house you grew up in. Two reaches to the left, into the little steel box of an engineer's station, and her mitt sweeps the surface that is now the underside of a shelf, sweeps it for the hard-cornered shape, and finds it.

The tablet is still in its bracket.

Dee mounted it there herself — Ren watched her do it one flat afternoon in port, ratcheting the cradle to the bulkhead, *so it logs even if I'm not babysitting it,* the ruggedized brick with the gasketed seams that Dee was proud of the way Sal is proud of a knife, and it is here, where Dee left it, in the drowned dark of the flat where Dee is also still — Ren's hand brushes something soft and yielding past the bracket and flinches off it and does not let her mind say the word, *thirty-one, thirty-two,* there is no breath to spend on the word — and Ren gets both mitts on the cradle and works the release with the heels of her hands because her fingers won't, presses and twists the way she'd pop a stuck quick-coupler, and the tablet comes free into her hands.

It is small. It is the size of the truth and the truth is the size of a dinner plate, a cold flat brick she clutches to her chest under her jacket, jams down inside the layers against her sternum where her last heat is, and the moment it's against her she stops being a diver looking for the beacon and becomes a diver who has stayed too long. *Thirty-four.* The count is in the bad country now. Her hands are gone — not numb, *gone,* she flexes them against the tablet's edge and feels nothing answer, the cold has taken the last of the fine grip and left her the hooks — and the air in her chest has turned from a held thing into a wanting thing, the air-hunger climbing her throat, the body starting to argue that this breath is the last breath and she should open her mouth and find more, which is the lie the body tells, the worst kindness, the thing that drowns the strong ones.

She tugs the line twice. *Down still, give me line* — no. She means three. She means haul. Her gone hand fumbles the count of her own tugs, two when she means three, and somewhere above Cole is at *thirty-six* and reading two tugs as *keep working* and feeding her slack, more rope, more time she does not have, and she feels the line go loose in the water beside her instead of tightening, and the cold drop of *he's giving me line* arrives, and under it, fast and ugly and she'll hate it later, the worse drop: *he's giving me line because I'm near the chart locker and he wants me to find nothing.*

She doesn't believe it. She doesn't *disbelieve* it. She has no breath to spend on believing anything. She gets a mitt on the tether and hauls on it herself, hand over ruined hand, dragging her own body back down the snarl of the count toward the dog-leg, *thirty-eight,* and she feels the rope finally come taut and bite as Cole — at *thirty-nine,* at the edge of his own promise — gives up reading the tugs and just hauls, the way she told him to, the way the deal always ended, and the two of them pull her at once, her own gone hands and his good ones, the line a single intention through the black, and she comes off the bottom of the flat and through the dog-leg with the cathedral falling away behind her and the strangled beacon knocking its slow tock at her back, *tock, tock,* the sound of a thing still trying to rise that she has left in the dark on purpose.

---

Her head breaks the surface inside the dome and the air is foul and close and it is the best thing she has ever breathed.

She gasps it — the surfacing gasp, the safe one, the lungs hauling in the diesel and the copper and the staleness like it's mountain morning — and Cole's hands are on the back of her jacket, on her collar, hauling her up the slope of the grating out of the water onto the strip of high ground, and she lets him, she has no choice, her own hands won't grip and her legs won't gather under her, she is a hundred and thirty pounds of soaked dead weight and his are the only hands in the box strong enough to land it. He lands her. He always lands her. He pulls her up against his own cold bulk and gets her clear of the water to the waist and holds her there while she heaves the breath back into a body that forgot it had one, and the tablet is a hard flat coldness against her sternum under the jacket, and her arms are crossed over it without her telling them to, holding it the way you hold the one thing.

The count comes back first, before the words. It's always the count. She runs it on the breathing in the dark — Sal in the corner, the upper-shallow rhythm. Marcus against him, the slow one, the one she set down. Cole at her back, close, his breath in her ear, and his is steady, his is always steady, the metronome somebody set and walked away from, *fourteen hours and his breath is still the calmest thing in this box,* and now she has her hand — her gone, hooked, useless hand — flat against the cold tablet that says why.

Four. Still four. And the four now includes a man whose breath she has been filing since the first minute, and a brick against her chest that is the answer to the file, and a beacon in the dark she left strangled in the gear he wouldn't dump.

She knows. She doesn't have the tablet's words yet — it's sealed, dead-cold, dripping, and there is no power in the box to wake it and no dry to crack it open and no light to read it by if she could. But she does not need the words. She has the beacon in her hands' memory, the popped release and the swollen line and the steel rail of the pot, *the deck didn't dump,* and she has fourteen hours of a man steering everyone off the one room where the proof of it lies, and she has *long as I could, Sal,* a sentence with a fault line down the middle that went past her at the level of weather while she saved a leg, and the silt at the bottom of her has stopped settling and started to clear, and through the clearing water she can see, finally, the shape of the thing she's been keeping alive.

It was not the sea. She's known that since the bend in the passage. The sea doesn't lash its pots to the rail. A man does that. A man lets it stay lashed.

"Ren." Cole's voice in her ear, warm, low, the hand on the tiller — and then it does the thing, the small thing, the thing that is the whole of him: it asks. Easy. Curious. A skipper checking what his diver found. "Hey. You're alright. You're up, you're breathing, that's the hard part. What'd you find down there? You reach it? The beacon — could you tell, was it away or fouled?"

And here is where she learns what she is.

Because there is a true answer, and the true answer is *it's fouled, it's fouled in the deck pots that never went over, the pots that should've been dumped, it never had a chance to rise, you've all been holding a rope tied to a thing that was throttled in the gear since the first second* — and the true answer is right there in her mouth, cold and whole, the way the truth always is once you've held it in your hands. And she feels herself, in the half-second the room leaves for it, do the thing. The reallocation. The hand coming off one rope to take another. She takes the reading off the moment the way she took it off Marcus — *what does saying it buy, what does it cost, who can I still keep alive* — and the sorting room at the base of her, the one that cleared her in London and never cleared her at all, does its quiet cold arithmetic on the truth itself: that a man whose hands she still needs to dive this passage, to hold this leg, to land her body when the water takes her again, is a man you do not turn into your enemy at hour fourteen with the tide rising and the count at four; that the truth keeps; that the truth, sealed and cold against her sternum, is a thing she can carry up into the daylight if there's ever daylight, and use then, with warm hands and a clear head and no broken leg depending on the accused.

She triages the truth.

"Couldn't reach it," she hears herself say. Her voice comes out flat and even, the voice she keeps for the worst of it, the one with no weather in it. "Too fouled. It's snarled in gear, deep — I got a hand on it but it's wrapped three deep around something I couldn't shift, not with these hands, not in one breath. It's not floating free. That's all I could tell." A breath. The diesel and the copper. "Sorry. I tried."

"You tried," Cole says. Warm. Easy. And is there a thing under it — a fraction of a held breath let go, a quarter-turn of his own slack — or is she filing him now the way the cold makes you file everything, reading tells into a man because she finally has the reason to? She can't tell. She has no light. She has a count and a brick and a lie in her mouth that tastes exactly, precisely, identically like every comfortable thing she has been told in the dark all night — *we can do an hour, she was froze through, away or fouled can't know, long as I could* — the warm true-enough thing you say to keep the room from coming apart, and she has just said one, with her own flat certain voice, and it worked like a rope.

She holds the tablet against her sternum where her last heat is, and she counts the heads by their breathing, and the count is four, and one of them is a man she may have to hang, and she has just started, in the dark, with her own two gone hands, to write him off the way she writes off the dying — quietly, wordlessly, deciding which truth she can save and which she sets down for later, telling herself *later,* telling herself *when my hands thaw.* It is the same promise she made to the boy an hour ago, and behind her in the corner the boy is resting the way she told him to, the deep slow breaths spaced out long, and she runs the count over him once more and the count holds. Triage held. She did the arithmetic clean and the arithmetic stood up, and she will not spend the heat she has left second-guessing a sum that came out right.

"Three," she says to the dark, soft, and then catches it, corrects it, because the kid's still breathing and the count is still four and she will not drop a head she hasn't lost yet. "Four. Still four."

The dark doesn't answer. The water comes up another finger's width while she's listening for it to, and against her chest the tablet keeps its cold, and somewhere down the throat of the passage the beacon knocks its slow patient tock against the hull, trying to rise, held down by the weight a man would not let go of. She has one truth pressed to her ribs to carry up to the daylight, and one truth she set down strangled in the dark because her hands couldn't hold it, and one head she has set down to rest because her hands couldn't help it either, and she has them sorted, all of them, the way she keeps everything sorted: the savable from the spent. The arithmetic is clean. She lies still in the cold and lets it hold her, and she believes the sum.


---


# Chapter Sixteen

## Expectant

*POV: Cole — T+15:30*

Ren came up wrong from the dive, and Cole has been watching her be wrong for half an hour now, the way you watch a sea you don't like the look of.

He hauled her back up the open throat of the passage himself — that was his job, the tether, the only hands in the box strong enough to bring her home against the current that pulses in time with the hull — and she came out of the water like a thing landed, half-drowned, retching the cold sea back up in long ugly heaves, and that part he understood. That part is just what the dive costs. A body that goes down into thirty-four degrees on a thread of breath and stays past its number comes back like that or doesn't come back, and she stayed past her number, he could feel it on the line, the long terrible slack where she stopped paying out and stopped coming back and he stood in the dark with the rope going dead in his hands and made himself wait three more seconds than he wanted to before he hauled. So the retching he understood. He held her while she did it, one hand flat between her shoulder blades, and told her she was alright, she was up, she was breathing, that was the hard part done — the same words Ren herself uses, he's noticed, the words have gone around the box so many times nobody knows whose they are anymore.

It's the after he doesn't understand.

Because the after isn't shock. He's seen shock. Shock shakes and babbles and grabs your wrist and won't let go and asks the same three questions in a loop. Ren is doing none of that. Ren has gone quiet and slow and somewhere else, wedged on the strip of grating with her knees drawn up and her arms crossed over her chest, holding herself the way the cold makes you hold yourself — except her hand, her right hand, is flat against her own ribs under the collar of her gear, pressed there, pressing something there, and it has been pressed there since he pulled her up, and a cold and unwelcome part of him that he keeps in a back room and tries not to consult has been quietly asking, the whole half hour, *what did she bring up.*

He doesn't ask. You don't ask a person who's just nearly drowned what they're holding. It would be unkind, and unkindness is a thing he can't afford, has never been able to afford, the whole shape of him built around the fact that the useful man is also the kind one, the steady one, the one you'd want — and so he doesn't ask, and tells himself he doesn't ask because it would be unkind, and lets the other reason stay in the back room where the light's off.

"Couldn't reach it," she said, when she could talk. That's what she gave him, surfacing, spitting seawater, when he asked her what was down there, what she found. *Couldn't reach it. Too fouled.* And he said, easy, *that's alright, you tried, that's more than the rest of us could,* and meant it, and also heard — in the back room, with the light off — that it was the kind of flat closed answer a person gives when the true answer is a thing they've decided to carry alone. He knows that answer. He has *been* that answer. He's been giving the box a version of *too fouled, couldn't reach it* for fifteen hours, and he knows exactly what it sounds like from the inside, and he is fairly sure, in the place he doesn't look, that he just heard it come out of her.

He files it. He doesn't make a sentence of it. The sentence would have her hand on her ribs in it, and the chart locker in it, and a thing she carried up out of the chart locker in it, and he is not going to build that sentence, not in the dark, not where he can't see what's on her face to manage it.

He keeps his own hand on the traction line instead. The leg's held. That's real. He holds the real thing.

---

Then the kid stops.

Cole isn't looking at the kid — there's nothing to look at, it's full dark, the torch dead since the small hours, the box run on breathing now the way Ren always ran it — but he's been listening to Marcus the way you listen to an engine you don't trust, half an ear on it under everything else, and the engine changes note. The boy's been breathing slow and shallow for hours, the long shallow draws of a body banked down to its pilot light, but there was a rhythm to it, a thing you could set your own breath against, and the rhythm comes apart.

A draw. A pause too long. Another draw, gulped, wrong, snatched out of order. Then a sound Cole has heard before and would give a great deal not to be hearing now — a low, jerking, rhythmic working of the boy's whole frame against the steel, his heels drumming faint on the grating, his breath sawing in catches. Not a seizure exactly. Cole doesn't know the word for it and doesn't need the word. He knows it from deck, from a man pulled blue out of the scuppers one bad November, the body's last argument with itself when the core's gone too cold and the machine starts firing wrong, the heart shaking instead of beating. He knows it's the bottom of the well. He knows you've got minutes, if you've got anything.

"Ren." He's already moving, already across the strip on his knees in the chest-deep cold, one hand out to find the boy. "Ren, the kid — he's gone bad, he's — *Ren.*"

She comes. He hears her come — not all at once this time, not the paramedic snapping on like a light the way she did in the dark of the eleventh hour. Slower. A half-beat slower than she should, a half-beat that he clocks and files and hates himself a little for clocking, because a man counting half-beats off a half-drowned woman while a boy dies is not a kind man, whatever else he is. But she comes, and her hands find Marcus past Cole's in the dark, and Cole does what he's good at, which is everything around the medic: he gets his big body behind the boy to take the weight off the steel, he reaches back and finds Sal's good arm and pulls it across to make a third wall of warmth, he says into the dark, low and even, *easy now, easy, we've got you, we've got you,* the hand-on-the-tiller voice, because the boy needs a voice to fall toward and because Sal needs the room to sound like it's being run.

He needs the kid to live.

He lets himself know that much, kneeling there with his arm around a drumming, dying body — he needs Marcus to live, and not only the clean way, not only the way you need a boy you've watched fail not to die in your arms. The other way too, the way he can't make a clean sentence of: a rescued kid is the best thing he could possibly carry up out of this. A nineteen-year-old who worshipped the calm skipper, who'll tell it ashore the way the young tell it, *Cole kept us alive, Cole held us together, the weather rolled us and Cole got us through* — that boy is worth more than gold when the questions come, and the questions are coming, the dive proved the questions are coming, Ren went down looking for something and her hand's been on her ribs ever since. He needs the boy. He needs the boy alive and grateful and telling the right story, and the ugliness of needing him that way, right now, with the boy's heels drumming faint against the grating, is a thing Cole feels arrive and slams the door on, hard, the way he slammed the door at the bottom of the dive, almost convincing himself the door was always shut.

The boy's not a witness. The boy's a boy. Cole holds him and does not let himself finish the other thought, and that's the most decent thing he's got left in him, the not-finishing, and he knows it isn't much.

"Talk to me," he tells Ren, under the boy's working breath. "What've we got. What do you need."

She doesn't answer right away. He feels her hands work — two fingers to the boy's throat, the back of a hand to the cheek, the small efficient movements he can hear but can't see — and he holds the boy still for her and waits for the orders, the way the room waits for orders, because that's the arrangement, she's the medical voice and he's the steady voice and you stand on the voice that's yours.

And he feels her stop.

He felt it once before, in the eleventh hour, and didn't have a name for it, and now he has the second look at it and still doesn't have a name but knows the shape of it cold. It's not that her hands quit. Her hands keep moving — she's tucking the boy's slack arms in, she's pulling the suit's hood tighter, she's doing the motions of working him. But the *pressure* goes out of them. The forward lean of a person hauling on a thing they think will come — that goes. Cole has felt that go out of his own arms a hundred times, on a fouled pot, on a snagged line, on a thing the sea has already decided, the exact moment the hauling becomes a waste of yourself and some animal accountant in you closes the ledger and tells the hands to ease. He knows that easing better than he knows almost anything. He's lived inside it. And he feels it pass through Ren's hands now, in the dark, with the boy still drumming faint against him, and the cold instrument in him takes the reading without his permission: *she's let go of him.*

He doesn't know about London. He'll think about that, after — that he didn't know, that there was no way for him to know, that the woman working the kid in the dark was carrying a dead child from another country into this one and there was nothing in the box to tell him so. He doesn't know there's a thing in Ren that does precisely this, that reads a body and *decides* and once it's decided stops spending the self it doesn't have, the cold efficient mercy that's also the cruelest freight she carries. He doesn't know she's tagged the boy the way she once tagged a child on a board. He only knows the sound of a hand coming off a fouled line, and he knows it on her now, the second time, and this time it's on the boy.

And Cole — God help him, this is the part that fits, this is the part that locks into the rest of him like a deck stowed to make the weight come out right — Cole *trusts the call.*

Of course he trusts it. She's the medic. She's been the medic the whole way down, the one who reads the body and says the number, the one whose verdict the room stands on the way the room stands on his hands. He's watched her be right about everything she's been right about — the leg, the suits, the order of who needs what — and right now her hands have eased off the boy and that means the medic has looked at the boy and seen what the medic sees, and who is Cole Vantine to second-guess the one true professional in the box. He's a fisherman with good lungs. She's the one with the training. When the medic's hands let go, the body's gone, and you turn your own hands toward the bodies that aren't, because that's the whole grim economy of a bad night, and he has run that economy himself, at the bottom of the dive, when he let go of Pavel's dead hand in the churn and went up to the two who were still kicking. You let go of the gone one. You go to the one that isn't. It's the right move. It's always the right move.

He trusts her the way the room trusts him. That's the rhyme of it, the thing he half-sees and won't look straight at: he banks on usefulness and she banks on triage and both of them, right now, are wrong about the boy, and neither of them can see it because the dark is doing for them what the dark does, which is let a man not see the thing he's deciding.

"You've got him?" he says. Careful. The warm laid thin. "Tell me what to do for him. Tell me and I'll do it."

A pause. The water laps. Sal has gone still and listening, the way the whole room goes still when the medic's hands are on the youngest one.

"Keep him out of the steel," Ren says. Flat. Even. The voice she keeps for numbers she's already written. "Body heat's the only heat there is and it's not — keep him off the cold steel and keep him breathing. That's what there is to do."

It isn't an order to fight. He hears that. He's a man who has stood at enough deathbeds in his own warm managing way to know the difference between *help me save him* and *help me lose him gently,* and what she just gave him is the second one, dressed in the first one's clothes. *Keep him breathing* is not *work him.* *That's what there is to do* is the thing you say when you've already decided there's nothing to do. He hears it. And the ugly accountant in him, before he can lock the door, runs the figure one more time — *a dead Marcus is a worse witness than a live one* — and then, right behind it, fast, almost faster, comes a thing that surprises him, a thing that isn't strategy at all: he's sorry. Plain sorry. Sorry for the boy who called him *sir* the first morning and got told not to, the boy who lied his way onto a boat to come north for a story and money for school and is going to die nineteen and content and drowsy-warm against the cold steel ninety miles off the islands because a deck rode heavy and a course held long. Cole is sorry, genuinely, in the one clean room left in him, and the sorrow and the calculation sit in him at the same time, the same temperature, neither one canceling the other, the way everything in here sits in him now.

He keeps the boy off the steel. That much he can do. He holds him.

---

It would be cleaner, Cole thinks, if Marcus had the decency to be gone already. He hates the thought and has it anyway. It would be cleaner if the boy slid under quiet, the way a man slides under in the water, the breathing just thinning out to nothing in the dark while the room held him. But the boy doesn't slide. The drumming eases, the wrong-firing of the cold heart settling back toward the slow shallow draws, and the boy comes up instead — not far, not back to himself, but up to that drowsy lit place the deep cold gives you near the end, the false warm landing where the body's stopped feeling the walls of the well.

"'M okay," the boy says, into Cole's arm. Thick. Slow. Almost happy. "'M not even cold."

"I know, bud," Cole says, and his voice does the thing it does, goes warm and even, the hand on the tiller, and underneath it the cold thing notes that the boy thinking he's warm is the worst sign there is and there's no light to confirm it and no need to, he can hear it. "I know. You rest. We've got you."

"Cole." A long swimming pause. "Cole, did I — did I do okay? Out there. On the deck. 'Fore." The words come unmoored, fifteen hours late, a boy reaching back past the wreck to some daylight watch nobody else remembers, some line of pots hauled clumsy in a building sea while a green kid tried not to be the weak one. "Was I — was I any use to you?"

And there it is, the question the boy's been carrying the way Cole carries his and Ren carries hers, the thing under everything, *was I any use,* because the boy learned the same lesson the whole deck learns, the lesson Cole built his life on and is dying inside of right now — that use is the only currency the boat respects, that a drowning room doesn't ask if you were good, only whether your hands worked.

Cole opens his mouth to tell him yes. To give the boy the one true useful thing left, *you were a good hand, kid, you pulled your weight, I'd ship with you again* — the warm lie that's barely a lie, the kind he's good at, the kind that costs nothing and buys a boy an easier ten feet of the dark.

But Ren answers first.

"You did fine, Marcus." Her voice out of the black beside him, low, gentle, kind — and flat. The flat of a number on a chart. "You did fine. Rest now."

*Rest now.* Cole hears it land. He hears it for what it is even if Ren can't, even if the boy can't — he hears the medic's hands come off the line and put it into words, *rest now,* the two softest most terrible words you can say to a body you've decided to set down, the white flag flown as comfort. He's said *rest now* himself, to dying men, in exactly that tone, meaning exactly that. He knows the words. He knows the tone. And he understands, holding the drowsy warm boy who just asked if he was any use, that the medic has flown the white flag, gently, in the dark, where no one can see whose verdict it is, and that the boy has taken it for kindness and gone easier into the cold for it.

"'Kay," Marcus says, content, the way he's said it all night. "'M resting. Tell my — tell my uncle I wasn't the worst one." A small pleased breath. "Wasn't the worst one, was I, Ren."

"You weren't," Ren says. "Rest."
