And then Cole feels her move — feels her turn, in the dark, away from the boy, the whole orientation of her shifting off him the way a vessel comes off a heading, and her voice changes register entirely, drops the gentle, picks up the work: "Cole. The tide's still making. Water's an inch off the suits. I need you on the low corner with me, we bail what we can or they go under, and a wet suit's worse than no suit." She's already up, already wading, already the other Ren, the one who turns her two cold hands toward the thing that can still be saved. "Keep Sal's line tight on your way. Come on. We're losing the corner."

She made the cold call. Cole hears it and, God help him, he agrees with it — the tide *is* making, the suits *are* about to go under, a wet suit *is* worse than no suit, there's real work in the low corner and a man should do real work and not kneel in the dark spending himself on a body the medic's already set down. It's the right order. It's the order he'd have given. It's the same move he made at the bottom of the dive and the same move she made over Sal's leg and the same move, he's beginning to understand in the dark with the boy gone slack and warm against his arm, that they are both made out of — the cold efficient turning of the hands toward the savable, the letting-go that keeps you the most useful body in the room while a death goes quietly uncharged behind you.

He eases the boy down off his arm, gently, onto Sal's good shoulder, off the cold steel, the way he was told. The boy doesn't stir. He's resting, the way she said. Cole checks the traction line on his way past — tight, the leg held, the one real thing — and wades to the low corner to bail beside the woman who just called it, and he does not let himself look back at the boy in the dark, partly because there's nothing to see in the dark and partly because the not-looking is a thing he's good at, the thing he's best at, the avoidance grammar he's been speaking all his life.

She made the call he'd have made. He tells himself that, bailing, the cold sea coming up over the suits and the two of them throwing it back a bait-tote at a time against a tide that doesn't care, the boy gone quiet and content against the cook's shoulder six feet behind them in the dark. *She made the cold call. She's the medic. The medic called it.* He rests his weight on it the way the whole room rests its weight on his hands.

She's the one with the training. When the medic's hands come off the line, the body's gone — that's the law of a bad night, and he has lived inside that law, and he turns his own hands toward the corner that can still be saved because the law says to and because the dark says nothing back. He only knows the medic's hands let go, and he trusted the letting-go the way the room trusts him, and he turned with her toward the work.

Behind them, very quietly, content, a boy breathes slow and shallow against the steel and says nothing more. Resting. The way they both told him to.

The water comes up another inch over the suits while they bail, and Cole throws it back, and does not look behind him, and tells the dark the same useful thing he's been telling it all night.

"We're alright for now," he says. And nobody answers, and he can't tell, in the dark, whether it's still true.


---


# Chapter Seventeen

## The Sat-Messenger

*POV: Ren — T+16:30*

The boy goes quiet the way the boat went quiet, which is to say in a long iron sigh that has no edges and then in nothing at all, and Ren is bailing the low corner with a bait tote when it happens, three feet away, in the dark, with her back to it.

She knows the exact texture of the silence because it is the texture she's been not-listening for. All night she has counted heads by their breathing — her own ragged saw, Sal's catch-and-grind around the pain, the kid's slow shallow shelf-deep draw — and somewhere in the last few tote-loads of seawater the kid's draw has thinned out under the slosh and the drip and her own dumb labor until it isn't there to count, and the not-being-there is a thing the back of her registers a full half-minute before the front of her turns around. That's the part she'll carry. The thirty seconds. She kept bailing for thirty seconds into a count that had already dropped to three because the front of her mind was busy spending the room's hands on the water that could still be moved, the way she'd been taught, the way she'd taught them, *spend the self you have on the thing you can change,* and the thing she'd decided she couldn't change had stopped breathing while she emptied a tote into the rising dark.

She sets the tote down. She doesn't splash. She turns.

"Marcus."

The cold has been her clock all night, the slow honest one, the one that gives you longer than it feels — and now she reaches for it and it's already done the sum and won't show her the working. She finds him by feel along the strip of grating, past Sal's good shoulder, her two cold fingers going to the side of the throat the way they've gone a thousand times in another country, in another life, on a hundred bodies that were and weren't there to feel.

No carotid. She presses harder, presses up under the jaw into the soft place, holds her own breath so it can't lie to her with its noise. The boat rolls its slow roll and she counts against it and gets nothing, no idle, no thread, no falling number — the number isn't falling, it's landed, it's at the floor, and a number at the floor is not a number anymore, it's a verdict.

She does not accept the verdict. That is the one mercy in her she has never been able to triage out of herself, the law she could recite in her sleep, *not dead until warm and dead,* and the kid is the temperature of the steel, the kid was never warm enough to be dead by the law, and so she goes to work.

"Cole. Light. Now."

---

The torch comes on and it is almost nothing, a finger of weak yellow, the last of the last, and Cole holds it low and close over the boy the way he's learned to hold it, and in its little coin of dirty light Ren sees Marcus's face for the first time in hours and wishes she hadn't.

It's a calm face. That's the obscenity of it. The cold takes the fear off them at the end the way it takes the warmth — the boy looks like a boy asleep in a bunk with the hood of his orange suit framing him, mouth slack, lashes down, the long apologetic gangle of him folded small against Sal, and there is nothing on the face of a sixteen-degree morning that would tell you the body inside the suit has stopped making the argument for staying alive. *Severe presents like sleep. Severe presents like death. You don't call it. You work it.*

She works it.

She gets him flat — *horizontal, you keep them horizontal,* afterdrop, the cold blood in the limbs that floods the core the instant you move them and stops the heart, she handles him like spun glass even now, even doing compressions she'll do them on a body laid flat and barely tilted — and she finds the hard plate of the sternum through the neoprene and she puts the heel of one hand on it and the other hand over and she leans her weight down into a chest that is cold and stiff and does not want to give, and counts.

"One. Two. Three." Out loud, flat, the metronome, the count that is the only prayer she ever learned to say and doesn't believe in and can't stop. "Four. Five."

"Ren." Cole's voice, low, the warm laid careful over something. "Ren, the water — "

"Hold the light." Thirty compressions. She knows it's thirty because she's counting and the counting is the rail. At the top of the thirty she tips the boy's head, pinches the cold nose, seals her mouth over the cold mouth and breathes into him — and she tastes the diesel and the copper and the staleness of sixteen hours of their own used air going into a body that can't use it, and she knows even as she does it the thing the research never lets her forget, the brutal arithmetic of a rescue breath in a poisoned box: *the air I'm giving him is the air I'm taking from the rest of us, and it's CO₂-thick already, and a breath of bad air into dead lungs is two breaths I won't get back.* She gives it anyway. Two breaths. She gives it because the law is *warm and dead* and the boy is not warm, and because there is a piece of her that is not running the arithmetic at all, a piece that is just a woman in the dark trying to make a chest rise that won't rise, and that piece does not care what the air costs.

She goes back to the sternum. "One. Two. Three."

The boat rolls. The water laps at the low corner she stopped bailing. Sal has gone utterly still beside the boy, his big ruined hand finding the kid's shoulder and resting there, and Ren can hear the old man's breath gone shallow and careful the way breath goes when a man is keeping very quiet so as not to be in the way of a thing he can't help with. Cole holds the dying light over the work and does not speak and does not move it and that, too, she'll think about later — that he held it steady, that the steady hand never wavered, that whatever else the man is, his hands did the right thing while she did the futile one.

She works him eight minutes. She knows it's eight minutes the way she knows everything, by the count, two hundred and forty compressions in cycles of thirty, and she will swear to it later, in a warm room, to a man with a clipboard, *I worked him eight minutes* — and the swearing will be true and will be the brave version both, because the eight minutes she gives him now, in the light, in front of Sal and Cole, are the eight minutes she did not give him in the dark an hour ago when he was not yet a body and there was margin in him she did not spend.

At the end of the eighth minute the chest under her hands has not changed and the cold has not changed and the boy's face in the failing torch is the same calm sleeping face it was at the start, and Ren stops with her palms flat on the dead sternum and her arms shaking and her breath sawing and she does the thing she has spent her whole adult life being able to do and has never once been able to live with: she calls it.

Not out loud. There are no words for it, that's why it's so easy, that's the horror of how easy it is. It's just a hand coming off a rope. The wanting goes out of her arms. She lifts her weight off the boy's chest and the chest stays where she leaves it and does not rise, and somewhere in the cold sorting room at the base of her a tag gets moved from one drawer to another, from *the one I can't change* to the only drawer past it, and there is no relief in it and there is no grief in it yet either, there is only the flat clean closing of a file, the sound a winch makes when you finally let the fouled line go because hauling it is just spending yourself on a thing the sea decided an hour ago.

"Okay," she says. To no one. To the boy. To the law she kept and the law she broke. "Okay. That's — okay."

"Ren." Cole again, and his voice has the thing in it she's heard all night, the steady warm pulse-take of a man laying his hand on the room. "You worked him. Everybody saw you work him. There wasn't — Ren, there was nothing in him to work. The cold had him hours back. You did everything."

And that is the moment she nearly takes it. That's the thing about the comfortable version — it's not a lie at the moment it's offered, it's a *rope,* it's the rope she's been throwing other people all night, *nobody dies in the next hour,* and Cole is throwing it to her now, the right words in the right warm voice, *you did everything, there was nothing to work,* and she feels it land and she feels the animal part of her reach for it the way the boy reached for *I'm not even cold.* It would hold her weight. It would hold her weight for years.

She doesn't take it. Not because she's strong. Because she's a paramedic, and a paramedic knows the difference between *there was nothing to work* and *I stopped working,* and she stood for thirty seconds in another country with her hands inside a salvageable adult while a child went cold on a board behind her, cleared by every number, ruined by every one, and she knows the exact shape of this particular rope and she knows it's the kind you hang on.

"He had margin," she says. Flat. To Cole. To the dark.

"Ren — "

"An hour ago. He had margin an hour ago and I didn't spend it." She hears her own voice do the thing Sal's did about *morning,* trying the words out to see if they still mean anything, and finding out they mean too much. "I tagged him and I went to the leg and the tide. I made him the one I couldn't change so I wouldn't have to spend myself on him, and then I made it true by not spending it." She gets her hands off the boy's chest and folds them into her armpits because they've started to shake in a way that isn't only cold. "Kill the light. It's done. We don't burn the last of it on a body."

A beat. Then the dark comes back down, total, and in the dark Cole says, gently, the most dangerous thing anyone has said to her all night: "You don't know that. You can't know the margin. Nobody can run that number after."

And the worst of it is that he's right, that nobody *can* run it, that she will never have the monitor and the core temp and the second chance that would tell her for certain — and that not being able to know is exactly the floor the comfortable version is built on, the soft uncertain place where you get to lay your worst act down and walk away. She knows that floor. She's been standing on it since London. She's not standing on it tonight.

"Three," she says to the dark, soft, the census, the coin in the fist. And then makes herself say the true number. "Two. Two and Sal."

---

The body cannot stay.

That is the thing about a body in a box this size, and Ren knows it the way she knows the rest of the brutal arithmetic — that the strip of grating they live on is a galley table's worth of high ground for the living, and a body takes a living person's space, and the water's coming up, and the cold work that has to be done now is the coldest work there is. The kid is wearing one of the two immersion suits the box has, and the other is sealed onto Sal, and the only body left in here without one is hers — the diver's, the one she served last on purpose hours ago and meant it — and the kid's suit is doing nothing for him now but keeping a dead boy a degree from the sea, and the living need it, and so the suit has to come off the dead and onto whatever furnace is left to fight for. Hers. The math hands her the suit she made sure she'd never take, and she takes it.

She knows all this before she lets herself know it. She holds it off the way you hold off a thing you're carrying when you need both hands for the door. And then there's no door left and she has to pick it up.

"Sal." Quiet. "I have to move him. And I have to take the suit. You understand what I'm telling you."

A long silence from the corner. Sal has known the boy three weeks. Sal has fed the boy, mothered him the way Sal mothers the whole crew with food he now can't cook, and Sal's big hand is still on the kid's shoulder in the dark, and Ren hears the old man's breath go and catch and go.

"...Yeah," Sal says, finally, around the teeth, around something worse than the teeth. "Yeah. Take it. He'd want — " The voice gives out. Comes back smaller. "Take it. Don't let him be cold for nothing."

"He won't be." It's a lie. The boy is past cold and past nothing and the suit is just neoprene and a dead law. But it's the lie that lets the old man's hand come off the boy's shoulder, and she takes the lie and uses it like a splint, the way she's used every lie all night.

She works in the dark because they will not burn the last finger of light on this. She finds the suit's zip at the throat by feel, the big toothed pull, and she works it down through the chest and the belly with her slow half-closing fingers, and she peels the cold stiff orange off the cold stiff boy the way you'd peel a thing off a thing, and the whole time the back room runs its quiet commentary, *strip the dead for the living, you've done this, this is just engineering, this is just physics, the part you love,* and the back room is lying, this is not the part she loves, there is no part of this anyone loves, and she does it anyway, with her hands, because her hands are the only part of her that ever stops lying and right now she needs them not to stop.

She gets one arm free. She gets the other. And it's working the boy's right arm out of the sleeve — folding the long apologetic limb of him in toward his body to slide the cold neoprene off the wrist — that her knuckles knock against something hard and flat zipped inside the bib pocket of the wet-weather gear he's still wearing under the suit, against the rules, the way he wore everything against the rules, the owner's nephew who lied to get the berth and thought the rules were for the rest of them.

Something hard and flat and the size of her palm. And — she goes still in the dark — *warm.* Warmer than the boy. Warmer than the steel. Warm the way only a thing with a battery in it is warm in a box this cold.

She works the bib zip down with fingers that won't quite close and gets her hand into the pocket and closes it around the warm flat thing and draws it out, and even before she has it clear she knows the shape of it, the rubberized housing, the little stub of antenna, the recessed face — she's seen a hundred of them, in another life, clipped to the packs of hikers and climbers and the people who go out past the edge of the maps — and her thumb finds the face and the face is *lit.* A dim grey-green glow, a screen, a small machine in the dark holding up its small lit face to a dead boy's pocket, and on the screen, when she gets it close to her eyes in the black, a line of pale text she has to read three times before it goes in:

*SOS SENT. POSITION ACQUIRED. CHECK-IN 14 OF 14.*

And under it, a clock. Counting. The little machine telling her, in the cold, in the dark, with no inflection at all, exactly how long it has been pinging her boy's position up to a satellite and down to a shore and out into the world of warm lit rooms where people decide who gets found.

Hours. It has been sending for hours.

---

She kneels in the chest-cold dark with the body half out of the suit and the warm lit thing in her hand and she does the math she cannot unmake.

He smuggled it aboard. Against the rules, in his bibs, the way a green kid keeps the thing his mother made him take — and he never told her, never told any of them, maybe because telling meant admitting he'd broken the owner's rule, maybe because by the time it would have mattered he was already too far down the well to find the words, *tell Ren I'm not even cold,* maybe — and this is the one that opens under her like the water opened under the boat — maybe he thumbed the SOS himself, early, in the first hours when his hands still worked, fourteen check-ins ago, and lay there quiet and content in the false warmth of the end *believing he had called for help, believing the cavalry was coming because of him, the weak one, the green one, the one who'd done the one true thing that would save them all* — and never said a word, because saying it was bragging, because he wanted it to be a surprise, because he wanted, more than anything, *to be useful.*

To be the one who saved them.

And she had knelt over him an hour ago with her two cold fingers on his idling heart and tagged him *the one I can't change* and gone to the leg.

She does not cry. She has never been able to cry on a call; it's a thing they cleared her of in London too, *appropriately composed,* *no evidence of impairment,* and she hates the composure the way she hates the numbers, because the composure is the wall and the wall is the thing that lets her keep doing the unbearable thing efficiently. So she does not cry. She holds the little machine up in the dark and reads its calm face again, *SOS SENT, POSITION ACQUIRED,* and she understands the whole cruel architecture of the night in one cold drop, the way she understood the inversion in the first minute, all of it arriving at once:

That help is coming. That help has been coming for hours. Not because of the manual beacon Cole said he keyed, the green confirm light he answered for too smoothly when she asked which switch — she knows now, she's known since the dive, the dive that ate her attention while a boy went quiet, *the beacon never spoke, the EPIRB never floated, the men who were supposed to call for help called for nothing.* Help is coming because of a contraband sat-messenger in a dead boy's pocket, a rule broken by the youngest, weakest, greenest person in the box, the one she wrote off, the one she let go cold while she bailed a corner. *Marcus saved them. Marcus is saving them right now, dead, with a machine she didn't know he had, while the useful men's hands and the useful men's lungs and the useful men's beacons called into a dead line all night.*

The useful did not save them. The boy she failed did.

She holds that. She makes herself hold it the way she'd hold a pressure she can't release, both hands, full weight, no flinching, because the one thing she has left, the one thing the night has not yet taken, is the refusal to set a true thing down because it's heavy. She set the boy down. She will not set this down too.

"Ren." Cole, in the dark, and there's a new thing in his voice now, careful, very careful, a man feeling the edge of something. "What've you got there. What's that light."

She closes her hand over the lit face. The glow leaks pink through her fingers.

"Nothing," she says. The same word she said to him at the top of the dive, *nothing, couldn't reach it, too fouled,* the word that tasted like all the comfortable lies she'd been told all book, and it tastes the same now, and she lets it. She has two small machines now. Dee's tablet, sealed and dark, pressed flat against her ribs under her own clothes since the dive — the truth about what the boat carried and how long he held her and the release he never tripped. And this, this warm lit thing, the truth about who actually called the cavalry while the useful men took the credit. Two small honest machines in a box full of large dishonest men, and herself, who has just added her own dishonesty to the pile, her own *the one I can't change,* her own thirty seconds with her back turned.

"It's the kid's," she says, which is true, and gives Cole nothing. "Help me with him. We have to put him in the water."

---

They do it the way you do the worst things, without ceremony, because ceremony is for people with warmth to spare.

She gets the suit the rest of the way off him in the dark and sets it aside — for herself, the suit will go to herself, the one she swore off when there were three to debate and now goes onto her because there is no one else left in the box to argue it onto — and then there is the boy, in his soaked under-layers, long and light and folded, and there is the water at the low corner where the grating shelves off into the flooded dark, where Pavel went in an avalanche eleven hours ago and rides the pile out past the shelf, and there is the only place for a body in a box this small.

Cole moves to help and she lets him, because his hands are strong and the boy must be eased in, not dropped — *handle them like glass even now, even dead, even past it,* the law doesn't switch off when the heart does, or hers doesn't — and the two of them, the medic and the man she may have to hang, take the dead boy between them in the dark and slide him off the shelf into the black water as gently as two cold people can manage, and the water takes him with a sound like the water takes everything, a small slosh, a closing, and then nothing.

"God keep you, kid," Sal says, low, from the corner, the closest thing the box has to a service, and Ren says nothing, because she doesn't believe in the keeping and the boy is past needing it and the only honest thing left to do is the next thing.

She gets the suit and works it onto herself in the dark — and it is the hardest thing she's tried to do with these hands all night, because the suit is wet and stiff and her fingers are two clubs and a survival suit is built to be donned by hands that work, fast, dry, and hers are none of those, and she fights the cold orange neoprene up over the soaked deck gear inch by inch, the same suit that an hour ago was keeping a dead boy a degree from the sea and now will keep her a degree from it instead, *the same currency, just reallocated, the diver can be worked and the boy could not,* and she hears the back room say it and she does not let the back room have it clean, not anymore. The boy could have been worked. An hour ago. She knows it now. She knew it then and built a wall against knowing. She gets one arm in and then the other and drags the long zip up to her throat with the ridged thumbnail that's the only finger she trusts, and the suit seals, and the wet cold of it is its own punishment and she takes that too, and does not call it justice, because justice is a warm-handed word and she's run out of those.

She lays her hand on Sal's traction line in the dark and feels it tight. She counts heads by their breathing, her own ragged saw, Sal's catch-and-grind, Cole's slow even metronome that has managed itself since the first minute of the wreck — and she gets two, and herself, and a silence where a third used to be, a boy-shaped silence she will hear for the rest of her life under every other sound.

"Two," she says to the dark. "And me. Three left to keep."

She does not say it the way she said *nobody dies in the next hour.* There's no rope in it. It's just the count, the true cold count, and she lets it be true.

And in her right fist, against the cold, the boy's small machine goes on holding up its calm lit face to the dark, *SOS SENT, POSITION ACQUIRED,* counting the minutes since the only person in the box who actually saved them called for help and told no one — counting them up, patient, indifferent, toward a rescue that is coming now, that is really coming, for the math of it is plain and the machine does not lie: help is on its way across the cold black miles because a boy broke a rule and died of it, and the useful men, and the woman who triaged him gone a little before he was, will be the ones it lifts out of the water.

She tucks the messenger in under the sealed suit, against the layers, next to where Dee's tablet rides flat under everything, two small true things between her and the orange skin a dead boy paid for, and she sits in the dark with her hand on a living man's broken leg and listens.

She listens the way she listened for four breaths in the first black minute, the way she listened for the boy's one breath an hour before she stopped getting it. There is the slow honest clock of the cold, still running, still giving them longer than it feels. There is the slosh at the low corner. There is Sal, and Cole, and the boy-shaped silence. And under all of it, too low for any ear to find, the thing she has decided to believe in instead of the keeping Sal asked for — a small machine on the far side of the cold, in a dead boy's hand once and her own now, throwing its line up through the black to a satellite to a shore to a warm lit room, *here, here, here,* fourteen times and counting, patient, indifferent, true.

The water comes up another finger's width. The count holds at three. And Ren sits in the dark she did not save them from, kept alive by the boy she did not save, and makes herself the one promise she still has the standing to make: that whoever the machine brings, whatever warm room it lifts them into, she will be the one who tells them whose hand called them. Not the useful men. The green one. The one who broke the rule and died of it and saved them all, and was tagged, by her, the one she couldn't change. She will not set that down. She has set enough down tonight to last her the rest of her life.


---


# Chapter Eighteen

## One Degree

*POV: Cole — T+18:00*

The boat tells him before it does it.

Cole has been on enough hulls long enough to know the sound a boat makes when it's about to change its mind — a low working in the steel under everything, not a groan you'd point to, just a difference in the way the dark sits, the way a man knows by the smell of the galley that the cook's about to drop something. He's been hearing it for an hour. He hasn't said it. There's no torch to throw on it and no use throwing one anyway; you can't fix a hull settling with light, and a thing you can't fix you don't announce, you wait for, with your hands ready.

So his hands are ready when it comes.

It comes as a long shudder that starts somewhere down in the sky beneath them — down where the wheelhouse hangs drowned, down where the weight of the flooding house has been hauling on the rest of her for eighteen hours — and travels up through the steel into the grating into the backs of Cole's thighs, and he has time, a half-second of time, to get one hand on the cleat that anchors Sal's traction line and the other flat on the cook's chest, and to say one word, *hold,* before the *Adeline Marsh* lays herself down another degree into the only position the sea is willing to give her.

It isn't the roll. The roll was forty seconds and a world turning over. This is one degree. One degree is nothing. One degree is what a boat does in a following sea and nobody spills their coffee. But this one degree comes with the whole drowned house pulling on it, and it doesn't come back, and that's the difference — a heel comes back and a settle stays, and Cole feels the staying in it before it's even finished, feels the dome over his head tilt and *keep* tilting and then stop tilted, the slope of the steel changed for good under his reaching hand.

The water moves with it. That's the part that nearly takes them. All the cold water in the box slides toward the new low side at once, a slack-tank slosh of it, and the strip of grating they've been wedged on — Sal corner, the kid's old place, Ren's end — tips its high edge down toward the wet, and for a second Cole is holding a broken man on a tilting table over black water with one hand on a cleat and the boat deciding whether to spill them all into it.

"Sal — *Sal,* don't fight it, let me — " The cook's weight comes against his arm, sliding, the splinted leg dragging, and Sal makes the high thin sound through his teeth that means the broken ends just rode against something, and Cole gets his shoulder under the man and drives him back up the slope toward the steel, toward the corner that's still a corner, and pins him there with his own body while the water finds its new level around them.

"Ren." He can't see her. He never can. "Ren, sound off."

"Here." Close. To his left and lower than she was — the settle has moved her, slid her down the grating toward the throat of the passage. Her voice has the flat hard edge it gets when she's already working a problem, no fear in it, no waste. "I'm in the water to my chest. The shelf's gone under on this end. Cole — the shelf's *under.*"

He reaches. His hand finds her shoulder, the wet orange of nothing — she's not in a suit, she's never been in a suit, she's the diver, she gave the suits away — and he gets a fist in the collar of her oilskin and hauls, and she comes up the new slope toward him hand over hand, and now there are three of them jammed into a corner that was meant for one, Sal half across Cole's lap with the bad leg out straight, Ren's back against Cole's chest, all of them above the water by a matter of inches and the inches running out.

"Everybody breathing," Cole says, into the dark, to the room and to himself. "Everybody's up. That's the hard part. Sal, you with me? Ren?"

"With you." Sal, around the teeth.

Ren doesn't answer with a word. She answers by going to work, which is her version of *with you,* and Cole feels her hands leave him and move down to the traction line, checking the tension, checking the cleat, reading the leg in the dark the way she reads everything, and he lets her, and keeps his arm a bar across the both of them so the next slosh doesn't peel them off, and takes the measure of what the boat just did to them.

---

The box is a different shape now. He learns it the way he learned the first one — by reaching, by the cold slope of the steel, by where the air gives out into water.

The dome's gone. That's the first thing his hand tells him. The high curve of the hull bottom that domed over them for eighteen hours, nine feet at the apex when they started, a hand less after the first settle, a foot less after the rack let go — it isn't a dome anymore. The degree of heel has rolled the high point off to one side, jammed it down into the angle where the hull bottom meets a frame, and what's left over their heads is a wedge: low on the passage side, lower still on the side that went down, pinching to a crack where the steel comes together. A man can't stand in it now. A man can barely sit upright in it. Cole reaches up and the steel is a foot from his crown when he's hunched, weeping, sweating its film of diesel and breath onto his scalp, and to his left it comes down to meet the black water at a slant you couldn't get your shoulders under.

The air's gone with the shape of it. He doesn't have a number — Ren's the one who carries numbers — but he doesn't need one. He can taste it. The air was souring before, close and used, a faint copper under the diesel that he'd been pretending was the diesel; now the settle has crushed the pocket down to two-thirds of what it was and packed three breathing bodies into the squeezed end of it, and the close used taste has a weight to it, a thickness, the first dull press of a headache coming on behind his eyes that he knows isn't only the cold. He's been in poorly-vented holds. He knows the feel of breathing a room that's started breathing you back.

"Air's worse," Ren says, beside him, reading the same thing off her own skull. "The settle dropped the volume. We're going to start getting stupid. Headaches first, then bad judgment, then — " she doesn't finish it. She doesn't have to. "Slow your breathing if you can. Don't talk if you don't have to. Every word's air."

"Copy that, mood man," Sal says, which is a word he didn't have to spend, and Cole hears Ren almost laugh and not, and is grateful to the cook for it, the small true theft of a breath to keep the room a room.

But she's right and Cole knows she's right and he files it the way he files everything, cold under the warm: *the air's the clock now, not just the cold.* He'd half-known it for hours and not made the sentence. Now the boat's made it for him. They had thirty hours of float and they've spent eighteen and the float doesn't matter anymore, because the air in this crushed wedge will quit before the hull does. He runs the arithmetic without wanting to, the deck-loading part of him that never stops weighing — three bodies, this much air, this much sour already — and the number it comes back with is a number with hours in it, single hours, and he puts a warm hand over the number the way he's put a warm hand over everything since the boat went over.

"We're alright for now," he says, low, even, the hand on the tiller. And it is even mostly true, the useful kind of true, which is the only kind he's had to work with in a long time.

---

There's a job to do and he's glad of it, because a job is a place to put his hands and his hands are the only part of him that's never learned to lie.

The strobe. The flares. The tablet — no, not the tablet, there's no tablet that's his to mind, there's the strobe and the flare canister and Ren's medical bag of cut neoprene, and in the settle the water's come up over the ledge where they'd staged the gear and it's all gone to the low side, gone toward the throat of the passage, going under. If the strobe floods they lose the one thing that might paint a swimmer to them in the dark. If the flares wet through they lose the only fire they have. Cole works it out in his head — what's worth the reach, what's worth the air the reach will cost, what's worth putting a hand down into thirty-four-degree black water for when the hands he has left are most of what's keeping all three of them off the floor of this thing.

"Strobe and flares went down the slope," he says. "I'm going to fish for them. Ren, hold Sal."

"I'll go," she says. "I'm already wet to the chest."

"You're the medic and you're not in a suit and your hands are worse off than mine." He says it gently, the way he says the things that are also true, and it is also true, all of it, and it is also the thing he wants — to be the one whose hands go down after the gear, to be the one the room can't spare, because a man who's fishing the strobe out of the dark with his bare arm to the shoulder in killing water is a man nobody in this box is going to look at too hard. He doesn't make that sentence. He's careful. He makes the first sentence, the true one, and lets the second one stay under the water with everything else. "Hold Sal. Keep his leg out of it. I won't be a minute."

He goes down the slope on his belly, one boot hooked in the grating, Ren's hand fisted in his belt the way his was fisted in her collar a minute ago — *the only hands strong enough,* he thinks, and doesn't finish that one either — and he puts his arm into the black water and the cold takes it the way it always takes it, like a hand closing on him, like the water's been waiting. He sweeps. He doesn't find the strobe. He finds the bait tote they bailed with, floating, useless; he finds a coil of line; he finds, with the back of his knuckles, soft and slow and final in the cold water, the kid.

Marcus.

He'd known the boy was there. They'd put him there — Ren had, an hour ago, two hours, the clock's gone soft — slid him off the shelf into the water on the low side because the dead don't get to keep a place on the grating when the living are running out of grating, and because the kid's suit, the orange suit they'd dived for and cut a third one apart to make, the kid's suit had to come off the dead boy to go on a living body that didn't have one. Cole had done that part. His hands had done it, in the dark, working the long zipper down and peeling the cold wet neoprene off cold wet limbs that didn't help him, the way you'd undress a thing, and he'd handed the suit up to Ren and she'd — he doesn't know what she'd done with it. Put it somewhere. Saved it for the next worst-off. The boy went into the water in his bibs and his long underwear and Cole's hands had let him go and gone back up to the living, the way his hands let Pavel go at the bottom of the dive and went back up, the way Ren's hands let the boy go an hour before he was all the way gone and went to the tide.

He'd known the boy was there. It's still a thing, finding him by hand in the dark. The knuckles meet the slack cheek above where the collar used to be and Cole's whole arm wants to pull back and he doesn't let it, because pulling back is a flinch and a flinch is wasted motion and there's a strobe down here somewhere that three living people need more than the dead boy needs Cole's arm not to touch him.

"Sorry, bud," he says, very quiet, under the room, the way he'd say it to a man overboard he couldn't reach. "Sorry. Just looking for the light."

He moves the boy's drifting arm aside — gently, the gentleness real and useless, the way the gentleness was real and useless when he laid the dog's body on the shelf ten hours ago — and reaches past him, and his fingers close on the hard plastic case of the strobe wedged against the kid's hip where the slosh had carried it, snagged on the dead boy like the boy had caught it for them, one last useful thing from a body that worked itself to death trying to be useful, and Cole takes it and does not think that sentence either, and comes up the slope spitting cold, the strobe in his fist.

"Got it." He's breathing hard and the breathing hard is air and the air is the clock and he makes himself slow it. "Strobe's good. Dry inside, I think — it's sealed. Didn't find the flares. They're down there, but they're down there under the boy and I'm not — " he stops. "I'm not going to keep fishing past him for them. We've got the strobe. The strobe's the one that matters if anything's coming."

He feels Ren go still beside him. Not the working still. The other one.

"Past the boy," she says. Flat.

"He drifted down to the low side. The gear's around him." Cole keeps his voice level because a level voice is a floor and right now somebody needs a floor. "I got the strobe off him. I left the flares." A beat. "You alright?"

"I'm fine." She isn't, and they both let her say it anyway, the way the room lets everybody lie the small ones now. And then, lower, almost not for him: "He caught the strobe for us."

"He did," Cole says.

And the cold piece of him, the piece he hates and can't put down, takes a reading on that — on Ren's voice doing the thing it's been doing since she came up off the second dive hours ago, gone strange and slowed and turned inward, a hairline crack running through the flat hard competent surface of her that wasn't there before she went in the water. He doesn't know what's in the crack. He's wondered. She went down for the EPIRB, to see had the beacon spoken, and she came up and said *nothing, couldn't reach it, too fouled,* and her voice when she said it had a thing in it he recognized and couldn't place, and he's a man who's spent his life reading the thing in voices, and the not-placing of it has sat in him for hours like the headache. Something happened down there. Something she's carrying and not saying.

He thinks about the chart locker, and makes himself not. The avoidance is old practice now, smooth as a worn handle. *Nothing down there but glass and a drowned radio.* He told the kid that on the first hour. He's told himself a version of it every hour since.

---

The settle gives him one mercy, and he hates how much he wants it.

Down on the low side, where the water's slid and deepened, the throat of the passage they cut for the suits — and past it, further down the drowned slope of the hull, the chart locker, the engine flat, the whole low forward end where the boat keeps her papers and her instruments and her drowned dead — that whole quarter of the box has gone under for good with this degree. He can hear it. The slosh comes from a deeper place now, an echo with more water under it, the sound a flooded compartment makes when it's finished flooding. The passage mouth is below the waterline. The chart locker is below the passage. A man couldn't dive it now if he wanted to; the dog-leg's underwater on both ends and the cold and the dark and the no-air at the bottom of it would have you before you reached anything worth reaching.

The boat's burying her own secrets. That's the sentence that comes, unbidden, warm and terrible, and Cole lets this one finish because there's nobody to manage it in front of, only the dark and the held breath and the cold piece of him that's been keeping the books all night. *The boat's burying her own secrets.* The chart table is down there, and the route log on it, the heading and the throttle and the long heavy season, all of it under the waterline now, under the dog-leg, under the cold, going where the sea takes things it's decided to keep. He doesn't walk all the way up to what's written on that log. He never does. He's built the not-walking-up-to-it so smooth and so old that it carries him past the place the way a worn channel carries water, and he's grateful for the channel, and he steers it.

He steers it the way he steered the kid off the chart table on the first hour — *nothing down there but glass and a drowned radio* — the way he's steered his own mind off the deck-load release every hour since. The release. The one lever, sitting right there under his hand when the gust came over the radio, the trip that would have dumped the rail and let her stand back up. He gets that close to it and no closer. He lets the sentence stop at the lever and not say what his hand did or didn't do, because a man can steer around a thing his whole life and never once have to look at it square, and Cole has had a long life of practice, and the water is helping him now.

The water's on his side. He has the grace not to say that one even to himself, all the way. But he feels it, the settle's cold mercy, the boat closing the door on the chart locker the way you'd pull a sheet over a face, and some part of him he is ashamed of unclenches a quarter-turn the way the whole room unclenched on the rope of Ren's lie the first hour, and he is grateful, and he is ashamed of the gratitude, and the two sit in him at the same temperature, which is the temperature of everything now.

The truth is underwater. He thinks the worst of it can't come up, because the water's taken it. He leans his back against Ren in the dark — her spine to his chest, one of her hands on Sal's traction line and the other tucked up under her oilskin against her ribs, holding something flat and hard he can't feel through the layers and doesn't reach for — and he rests on the cold mercy of a closed door, and does not know whose hand is on the other side of it.

"We're through the worst of it," he says, into the dark, to the room. And he almost believes it. He's built a whole life on almost believing the useful thing, and it's never failed him yet, not all the way, not where anyone could see. "Settle's done. She's found her seat. The air's bad and the water's high and it's going to be a long cold few hours, but she's not going anywhere fast now, and help comes or it doesn't, and either way we just hold. We've held this long. We hold."

"We hold," Sal says, thin.

Ren says nothing. He feels her breathe — slow, the way she told them all to breathe, every word air — and he feels the shape of her against his chest, the deckhand who came up off the second dive carrying something she won't name, and he counts the breaths in the dark the way she taught him, the way she's done since the first minute, *Sal, Ren, me,* three breaths in a crushed wedge of sour air, and the count comes out three and the number is the most precious thing in the box and he holds it the way she holds her sealed thing, the way Sal holds his silence, every soul in here holding the one thing it can't put down.

Two left to save, and he counts himself one of the savers, the way he's counted himself a saver all night, the most useful hands in a drowning room, and he rests his cold weight on that the way he rests it on the steel.

The water comes up another finger while he's resting on that, and the air goes one degree more used, and somewhere down on the low side the kid does not shiver and the chart locker fills the last of the way. Cole holds the line and the count and the warm thin lie over the cold, and waits for the worst minute he's saving the strobe for — and behind him, counted in the three, slow against his chest, breathes the one thing he's wrong about.


---


# Chapter Nineteen

## Hope's Cruelty

*POV: Ren — T+19:00*

The sound comes into the box the wrong way — not through the air, which is too thick now to carry anything clean, but up through the water and the steel, into the grating, into the bones of her wet seat, the way a dentist's drill comes up through your jaw before your ear catches it. A low beating drone. Felt before heard. She has her hand on Sal's traction line and her head against the cold wedge of the hull and she feels it in her teeth before she knows what it is, and the not-knowing lasts exactly one breath, because there is only one thing in the world that beats like that.

An engine. In the sky. The real sky, the one that's up where it belongs, ninety miles of black February water and forty feet of inverted steel away from her face.

"Quiet," she says. The word costs air and she spends it anyway. "Everybody quiet. *Listen.*"

Sal stops his thin teeth-noise mid-breath. Cole goes still against her back — she feels it, the held breath, the whole big slow body of him locking down to listen the way she's locked down. For a few seconds there is only the slosh and the drip and the drone, and then the drone resolves itself out of the steel into a thing with a shape, a heavy four-square grinding throb with a propeller-beat under it, and she knows it. Two seasons on a boat that worked under the Coast Guard's eye taught her this one. Not a helicopter. Bigger. Higher.

"Herc," she says. The word comes out of her like a thing she didn't decide to say. "It's a C-130. Cole — that's a Herc."

"I hear it." His voice is careful. Level. The tiller-hand laid on it. But she has her back against his chest and she can feel his heart, which is a thing she has never been able to feel before, a fast hard knock against her spine that his voice is keeping no part of, and she files it the way she files a pulse that doesn't match the face. *His mouth says level. His heart says forty.* She doesn't have time for what that means. She files it anyway. The filing is involuntary now, a reflex she can't switch off, the same reflex that counts heads and counts minutes and counted, two and a half hours ago, a boy's slowing pulse and rounded it down to nothing.

The drone swells. It's coming on a line that will pass somewhere off to the down side — to the right, to the sky-ward right, which her body still wants to read as the floor — and she does the only arithmetic that matters, fast, cold, the math of a search aircraft she's watched fly a hundred grid-legs over a hundred empty stretches of water looking for men who were already under.

"They're flying a search pattern," she says. "That's not a straight overflight, that's a leg. They've got a datum. Somebody told them where to look." She stops. Says the rest of it because the rest of it is the whole hinge of the next minute. "And they're looking at the *surface.* They're looking for a raft, or suits in the water, or a hull. They are not looking for four people sealed inside a steel bubble forty feet under their own keel, because there's no reason on God's earth for them to think anybody's *in* here."

"So we make a reason," Cole says.

"So we make a reason."

The drone is at its loudest now, off the down-side quarter, passing, and she can hear in the swell-and-fade of it the exact thing she's afraid of — that it's a wide leg, a fast leg, a marker pass, the plane stamping a position on a chart and grinding on to the next line of an enormous grid, and that in ninety seconds it will be a memory in the steel and they will be exactly where they were, three breaths in a sour wedge, except that now they'll know how close the world came and went.

She moves before she's finished deciding to. Her hands are already going for the gear-bag wedged under her arm against the hull, the dry-bag of cut neoprene and the spare line and the —

Her hands stop.

She knows what's in the bag and what isn't, and she knew it ten hours ago, and she has known it the whole time the engine was coming on, and her body went for the bag anyway because hope is stupid, hope reaches for the thing that isn't there, hope is the first lie the body tells you and the cruelest. The flares aren't in the bag. The flares went down the slope in the settle, down to the low side, down into the deep cold echoing water where the throat of the passage drowned for good — and they went down to a place she can name exactly, because she's the one who put the thing that's lying on top of them there.

The flares are under Marcus.

"The flares," she says, and her voice does something she doesn't let it do, a hairline, fast, gone. "Cole. Where are the flares."

A beat. She feels him not want to answer it.

"Down the low side." Careful. Floor-flat. "Under the — they went under the boy. I fished for the strobe and I left them. I told you. I wasn't going to keep reaching past him."

"I need them now."

"Ren —"

"That's a search plane, it has a datum, it is *looking,* and if a flare comes up through that passage and breaks the surface where it can see it, that's the whole game, that's found instead of foundered, and they are under the *boy,* Cole, they are eight feet down the slope under a nineteen-year-old who's been dead two and a half hours, and somebody has to go get them, and it has to be now, in the next minute, while the plane's still on this leg." She's already moving as she says it, sliding down off the high corner toward the wet, and Cole's hand is in her collar.

"You're not in a suit."

"I'm already wet to the chest. I've been wet to the chest for an hour." She pulls against his fist and he doesn't let go and she stops pulling, because pulling against Cole's hand is a losing trade and she knows the math of that one too. "Let me go down. You can't make the throat now, you said it yourself, it's underwater both ends, it's a dive, and you're holding Sal. If you go down there's no counter-traction and his leg lets go and he bleeds. *I* go. I dive the slope, I find them under the boy, I come up. Thirty seconds of hands. Let me go."

The drone is fading. She can hear it deciding to leave them.

"The strobe," Cole says. "We've got the strobe. The strobe's the one that —"

"The strobe doesn't *reach* the surface." She hears herself get loud and pulls it back down because loud is air. "The strobe's a light in a box forty feet under the water. It paints a swimmer who's already at the throat, that's it, that's all it's good for, and there's no swimmer, there's a plane, two thousand feet up, looking at the *top* of the sea. The only thing in this whole hull that can reach the top of the sea is a flare burning up through that passage in the half a second it takes the air to push it out. It's a one-in-a-hundred shot and the flares are under the boy and the plane is *leaving.*" She gets a hand flat on his chest, on the fast knock of his heart that his voice keeps lying about. "Cole. Let me go."

She feels him do the thing he does. The weighing. The big slow deck-load part of him that never stops figuring the trim of a thing before he lets it move. And then his fist opens out of her collar, finger by finger, the way a man pays out a line he wishes he could keep.

"Tether," he says. "You don't go down a flooded slope in the dark on a maybe without a line on you. Tie on."

She ties on. The line is the half-inch poly they rigged for the second dive — a fisherman's line that won't stretch and won't rot, and she's run her hands down it before in colder water than this. She takes a bight around her waist and through itself the way Cole showed her, the way that holds and lets go clean, and she puts the working end in his fist.

"Three tugs is haul me up," she says. "Two is give me line, I'm still working. Same as before."

"Three is haul. Two is give." He says it back to her flat and exact, the only kind of promise she'll trust, and she clocks that he gets it right this time, the code that went crooked between them on the last dive and nearly drowned her, and she files even that, even now, even with the plane fading — *he's careful with me, he's been careful with me all night, the most careful man in the box,* and she does not let herself finish what careful means in a man who has a reason to want her in the water.

"And Cole." She's at the waterline now, the cold of it already up her chest, her breath gone shallow and fast the way the cold makes it, the gasp-reflex she has to ride and not feed. "If I tug three and you don't feel me coming, you haul anyway. Don't wait for me to be done. You hauled me before. Haul me before I'm done."

"I hauled you before," he says, and there is something in it, low, that she doesn't have time for, and she takes one long breath of the bad sour air — air-hunger pulling at the bottom of it, the feeling she can't get the lungs full even when they're full, the CO₂ lying to her body the way hope lied to her hands — and she goes under.

---

The cold takes her face like the first night. It never stops being the first night. Her body goes for the gasp and she clamps it, hard, jaw shut, throat shut, and rides the spike of it with her eyes screwed against a black that her eyes can do nothing about anyway. Forty kicks of useful hands, the cold-math says — except she doesn't have forty, she's been wet for an hour, her core's been bleeding heat down the open throat of this hull all night, and what she has is some smaller, meaner number she won't know until her fingers stop answering.

She goes down the slope on her hands, the way you go down a roof in the dark, the grating cold and slick and tilting away under her, the new low side that the settle made. The water gets deeper and the deeper water is colder, a different cold, an older one. Her ears fill. The drone of the plane is gone now — under the water there's only her own pulse and the long iron mutter of the hull and the small mineral ticking of a steel thing dying slowly in the sea.

Eight feet. She counts the grating by the cross-members under her palms. One. Two. The slope steepens where the deck-frame turns. Her chest is a fist now, the held breath turning into a number, counting itself down whether she wants it to or not.

She finds the line first. The coil Cole found, drifting. Then the bait tote, hard plastic, nudging her shoulder, buoyant, useless. Then her hand goes out into the cold for the next thing and the next thing is cloth.

Cloth. Wet wool, the weave of it, long-underwear cloth gone heavy. An arm inside it.

She knows the arm. She dressed this arm, hours ago — no. She undressed it. She and Cole peeled the suit off it for Sal. She tagged this arm expectant with two fingers on its carotid while it still had a pulse in it, a small slow failing pulse with margin in it she didn't spend, and she stopped working it and turned to the water because the water was a problem she could solve and Marcus had become a problem she'd decided she couldn't, two and a half hours ago, in the dark, with the dive and the tablet and the whole drowned truth of Cole sitting in her chest eating her attention like cold eats fingers.

*Don't,* she tells herself. *Not now. The flares are under him. Find the flares.*

Her hand moves down the cloth arm to where the body lies against the low frame, drifting, anchored by nothing, a thing the water moves. The flares went down here. Under him. She has to reach past him — under him — into the deep angle where the gear collected, and she does it, she puts her arm down past the cold drifting weight of the boy she failed and into the black to feel for a hard plastic canister of the one fire in the world that could reach the surface, and her hand finds his hand.

His hand finds hers. That's the lie the dark tells her — that he reaches back. He doesn't reach back. The water moves his arm and his fingers brush her wrist, the cold dead curl of them, and her whole body wants to recoil and she does not let it, the way Cole didn't let it, she understands that now, she understands the exact size of what Cole did with the strobe, his arm in the dark past this same boy, and she keeps her hand going down past the touch of those fingers into the deep cold for the canister.

It isn't there.

She sweeps. Her fingers are going — she can feel them going, the lag coming into them, the half-second between wanting and answering that means the ten minutes are spent and she's into the meaner number now — and she sweeps the deep angle under the boy where she's sure the flares went, and her hand closes on the bait tote again, on the line again, on the grating, on the cold soft weight of the boy's hip, and not on the canister, not anywhere, and the breath in her chest is screaming now, the bottom of her lungs gone to fire, the air-hunger of the sour box still in her head telling her she's emptier than she is.

The water took the flares too. That's the answer her hands are giving her. The settle slid the boy down and the boy slid down further, into the throat, into the drowned dog-leg, and the flares went with him or went past him, down into the part of the hull that no diver alive can reach, the chart-locker dark, the place where the boat keeps the things it's decided to keep. She came down here past the dead boy on a one-in-a-hundred and the hundred came up empty, and the plane is gone, and her hands are gone, and she is forty feet under the sky with a coil of useless line and a dead boy's fingers against her wrist.

She tugs the tether. Three. Haul.

Nothing comes. A half-second of nothing, the line slack in the black, and the old animal terror climbs up her spine — *he's not hauling, he waited, he's going to let me* — and she hates the thought even as it comes because it's a hope-thought turned inside out, the body lying the other direction now, and then the line goes hard and her waist takes the bite of it and she's coming up the slope, Cole hauling, the only hands strong enough, hand over hand, dragging her wet and gone-handed up out of the deep cold past the boy and the gear and the drowned throat into the shallower black, and her face breaks the surface into the sour air and she gasps and the gasp is real and the air is bad and it is the best bad air she has ever breathed.

---

"Got them?" His voice, close, over her, his fist still in the tether hauling her the last of the way up the grating against his chest. "Ren. Talk to me. Did you get them."

"Gone." She's spitting cold water, her teeth going now, the deep shiver coming up out of her core in waves she can't ride. "Flares are gone. Down the throat. With — " she can't make the word, makes it. "With the boy. They went down with the boy. There's nothing down there. There's nothing to send up."

She feels him take it. The big slow body of him, the held breath, and then nothing — no speech, no floor laid down, none of the level tiller-voice, just his arm coming around her and Sal, a bar across them both against the next slosh, and his chest against her back where she can feel his heart still going forty under a voice that for once has nothing to say. She files that too. The one time all night Cole Vantine has run out of useful words is the time the world came and went over their heads and left them sealed in the dark with the fire drowned under the boy.

Above them, far off, in the sky that's up where it belongs, the drone is gone for good now. Pass complete. Position marked. A grease-pencil X on a chart in a warm dry plane grinding home to St. Paul to refuel, *hull located, no survivors visible, no signals received,* and ninety miles of black water between that sentence and the truth of three people breathing in a steel lung under their own keel.

Marcus did this. That's the thought that comes up through her like the drone came up through the grating, felt before understood, and once it's up she can't put it down. The plane came because of the boy. Not the EPIRB — the EPIRB never spoke, she held its strangled cord in her own hands. Not Cole's bridge beacon, the green confirm light she's stopped believing in. The plane came because somewhere in this hull, in a dead boy's bib pocket, a contraband sat-messenger no bigger than her palm has been throwing position-pings up through the steel and the water for sixteen hours, *SOS sent, position acquired,* counting up, the one machine on this whole drowned boat that told the truth and got it out — carried by the kid who broke the rules, the kid she wrote off, the kid whose fingers she just touched in the dark reaching past him for a fire that wasn't there.

*He's saving us,* she thinks. *Dead. He's been saving us the whole time, and I stopped working him to bail water, and now I'm alive because of a thing he did wrong, and he's not.* The math of it is unbearable and exact and there's no rounding it down. She's tried. It won't round.

"There'll be a helo," Cole says finally. The voice is back, low, careful, a man reaching for the floor again now that he's found his feet. "A Herc doesn't hoist. It locates and it marks and it calls the helo in. They found the hull. They marked it. That means there's a sixty inbound, Ren. A Jayhawk. It's coming. It might already be coming."

"I know." She does know. The cruelty of it is precisely that she knows. The plane wasn't the rescue. The plane was the beginning of the rescue, the worst possible beginning, because it means the rescue is *real* now, real and close and coming, and a thing she's been able to not-think for eighteen hours has come up the grating with the drone and is sitting in the box with all of them. If they come, she surfaces. If she surfaces, there is a report. If there is a report, there is a tablet flat and hard against her ribs that she carried up out of the cold, and there is a man with the most useful hands in the Bering Sea who has been keeping her alive all night, whose heart she can feel going forty against her spine, who went still and silent the one moment the world found them — *he heard it too,* she understands, *he heard exactly what I heard,* the same drone, the same arithmetic, the same door opening, and he is afraid of the rescue for the opposite reason she is, and for one cold clear second in the dark they are the two most frightened people in the box and it is the same plane that frightens them.

She files it, and this time she lets herself finish the sentence, because the cold has stripped the gloss off everything and there's no point lying to the dark.

*He doesn't want to be found either. He wants it less than I do.*

The water is at her chest. The air is one more degree used. Somewhere far under them, lower than the slosh, lower than the iron mutter, there is a new sound coming up through the sky-that-is-water, lower than the Herc and steadier, a beating that is not the plane and not the hull and not her own forty-knock pulse. A throb. Closer than the plane was. Coming on.

She knows that one too.

"That's the helo," Cole says, and his arm tightens across the three of them, and his voice has found its level, and his heart, against her back, finally slows to match it.

She has spent her last reach and her last hope in the same five minutes — gone down past the boy for a fire that drowned with him, come up empty-handed into the news that rescue is real — and the cold does the arithmetic for her the way it always does, patient, exact, indifferent to whether the answer is fair: help is coming, and it cannot come for everyone, and now she has to decide what coming-up costs.

The throb gets louder. Up where the sky belongs.

She holds the count in the dark. Three breaths. Hers, Sal's, Cole's. For now.


---


# Chapter Twenty

## I Can Take Two

*POV: Cole — T+21:00*

The knocking comes from the wrong place and that's how Cole knows it's a man.

The hull has been talking all night and he knows its whole vocabulary by now — the slosh, the iron sigh, the EPIRB tapping fouled in the passage gear, the deep echoing fill of the low side where the boat keeps her drowned. None of those knock. None of those knock *three,* a pause, *three,* the way a knuckle knocks on a door it can't see through, the way a man tells another man *I'm here, are you.* It comes up out of the flooded throat of the passage, down on the low side under the wedge of bad air, and Cole feels it before he understands it, the way he felt the settle before it came — a difference in the dark, a rhythm that isn't the boat's.

"Quiet," he says, and the room goes quiet, even Sal, even the long thin string of the cook's breathing through his teeth. "Listen."

*Three.* Pause. *Three.*

"That's a man," Ren says. Flat, but he hears the thing move under the flat, the thing that's been gone from her voice since the second dive, come back for a second. "That's somebody on the steel."

Cole's already moving. There's no thought in it he could name, just the body going where a job is, his belly down on the slope of the grating, one boot hooked, his face an inch off the black water at the lip of where the wedge gives out. He puts his fist on the steel and knocks back. *Three.* Pause. *Three.* And then, because a man drowning in here needs to be more than a rhythm, he does the thing he's done all his life, the thing his hands and his voice know how to do without him: he puts his mouth near the water and he *talks.*

"We hear you. Four — three. Three living, one's hurt. We're up in the air pocket, top of the hold, you're gonna come up into air. Come slow."

The water lights up.

That's the only way Cole has for it. He's been in the dark so long — full dark since the torch died, a dark with no edges, the inside of a closed fist — that the light coming up through the flooded passage doesn't read as light at first. It reads as the water turning a color it has no business being. A green-white glow blooming up from below the surface on the low side, swelling, wobbling, throwing the slosh into ribs of moving shadow on the steel over their heads, and Cole's eyes have forgotten how to do this and they sting and stream and he can't stop looking, none of them can, three people who've been blind for half a day turning their faces toward the first outside light in the world like plants.

Then the surface breaks and a head comes up into the wedge, and the head has a light strapped to it, and a man's voice says, around the regulator he spits out, loud and wrong and *alive,* a voice from the upright world:

"Coast Guard. Anybody home?"

---

He comes up out of the water the way Cole used to come up out of a salvage dive twenty years gone — easy, weight managed, a man who lives in cold water and isn't afraid of it. Black drysuit, fins, a mask shoved up on his forehead, the dive light on a band across his hood throwing its hard cone around the wedge so that for the first time since the roll Cole can *see* the room he's been reading by hand. He wishes, the instant he sees it, that he couldn't.

It's worse than his hands had told him. The wedge is a coffin of weeping steel a foot over their hunched heads, the diesel sheen swirling green-black on water that's risen to all their chests, Sal a grey ruin propped in the corner with the bad leg out straight in its lashed splint, Ren bare-headed and bare-handed and blue-lipped with her oilskin collar dark to the throat. Biscuit's net-wrapped shape half-floating off the drowned shelf. The light makes it all real in a way the dark had kept merciful, and Cole watches the swimmer's eyes go around it once, fast, professional, taking the same census Ren takes, and land on Sal, and land on Ren, and land on Cole, and do an arithmetic Cole already knows the answer to because he's been doing it all night.

"How many in the water you can't see?" the swimmer says. He's young — thirty, maybe — and his face under the light is already doing the thing Cole's seen on men who've been in this water too long: a tightness at the mouth, a slight wrongness in the eyes, the cold getting into the thinking. He's shaking. He's been swimming the wreck in thirty-four-degree water and he's a casualty starting, and he's grinning anyway, the bright fixed grin of a man working past his limit. "Anybody else alive in here?"

"No." Cole says it for the room because the room is his to speak for; that's a thing he's made true all night, a load he's stacked careful. "Three living. Pavel's gone — under the gear forward. The kid — " a half-beat, and he gives it the weight it's owed and not a grain more — "the kid didn't make it. Four, four and a half hours back now. He's in the water on the low side. Dee and the skipper never made the pocket. It's three. Him with the leg, her, me."

"Okay." The swimmer's already unclipping something, already working, his hands moving the way Cole's hands move, on their own, ahead of the thinking. "I'm Petty Officer Hartline. Rescue swimmer off the sixty. I'm gonna get you out of here." He says it the way you say a thing to keep a room from coming apart, and Cole knows the voice exactly, has used it all night, *I'm gonna get you out of here,* and something in him goes very still and very cold listening to a younger man use his own tool on his own room.

"You came up the passage," Cole says.

"Came up where the strobe was painting. You staged that?" Hartline's light swings to the strobe clipped at the high point of the wedge, still pulsing its small useless heartbeat into a dark that doesn't need it anymore. "Good. That's what walked me in. The hull's — " he stops, and Cole watches him not say it, watches him swallow the number he came up with. "How long's she been floating like this?"

"Twenty-one hours. Give or take." Cole says it level, even, and gives the man the thing he needs more than comfort, which is data. "She settled a degree about three hours ago. Dropped the dome, took the volume. Air's going bad — we've got headaches, the medic says we're into the CO₂. Water's come up to our chests since the settle and it's still making. There's a passage cut for the suit locker, low side, it's underwater now, both ends. The forward end's all flooded — chart locker, engine flat, all under. Where you came up is the only air left in her."

Hartline's light holds on Cole's face for a second longer than it needs to. Cole knows that look too. It's the look a man gives another man when he realizes the other man is the one who knows the boat, the one who can talk him through it, the one he's going to need — and Cole feels the old thing settle over him, the cold useful thing, the relief of being the indispensable one in a room that's drowning. He's the interface. He's the man who knows the inverted geography of this hull better than the medic does, better than the Coast Guard does, the man who can tell the swimmer where the steel comes together and where the water's deep enough to dive and where the basket can come and where it can't. He made himself that, over a season and a night. He didn't know all night what he was making himself *for.* Now the light's on it and he knows.

"You the skipper?" Hartline says.

"Relief skipper. Cole Vantine. Master's asleep in his bunk under sixty feet of water." He says it plain and lets it land plain, the master dead, the relief man left, and he watches Hartline file *skipper* next to his face, the senior man, the one who'll have the account, and Cole does not let himself feel how much he wanted that filed. "Tell me what you've got. Tell me the picture. I'll help you work it."

---

The picture is bad and Hartline gives it to him bad, the way one professional gives it to another, because the medic's listening and the cook's listening and you can't pretty a thing the people in it can already feel.

"I'm not gonna lie to you." Hartline's got a hand on the steel, steadying against the slosh, and his shaking's worse than it was a minute ago — the wedge is barely warmer than the water and he's spent his warmth swimming in. "We got your position off a sat-messenger ping — somebody aboard had a personal beacon going. C-130 prosecuted it, marked the datum, we came in behind off St. Paul. We are a long way out here. The bird's burning fuel just hanging over us, and we've got weather building back to the west — there's a window and it's closing and I can't tell you how wide it is, nobody can, you know how it is out here." He says *you know how it is* to Cole, skipper to skipper, and Cole nods like a man who knows how it is, and the cold piece of him notes the sat-messenger and files it and does not yet understand it. "Here's the part I hate. The hull's low. She's down to maybe a third of her float, the way she's sitting — that breach where your passage is, she's bleeding. I came up through it and I felt the surge. Every cycle I run a basket in and out of that water, I'm working that breach, and this much sea state, this much fuel —"

"How many can you take," Cole says. Quiet. He already knows. He's known the shape of it since the light came up. He just needs the man to say it so the room hears it from the Coast Guard and not from him.

Hartline looks at him. The grin's gone now; the cold's taken that too.

"Two." He says it flat. "I can rig and hoist two, this pass, clean, before the bird's gotta break for fuel and before that weather shuts the window. I get two up and the sixty turns for St. Paul to gas, and either it comes back and the hull's still here and the weather held and I get the rest, or — " he doesn't finish it, the way Ren doesn't finish things, the way Cole's spent all night not finishing things. "Two now is real. Two now I can promise you. Anything after two now is a coin I can't see both sides of. And I gotta tell you straight, the way she's floating, I wouldn't write a lot of insurance on a second pass."

The room takes it. Cole hears it take it — Sal's breath stops and starts, Ren goes the working-still she goes when a problem lands that has a person's life in it as a variable. And Cole, on his belly on the slope with the light finally on the world, feels the whole machine of him turn over once, smooth, oiled, the way it's turned over his whole life when a boat had more weight on her deck than she could carry and somebody had to choose what went over the side.

*Two now.* Three living, two seats. It's a stability problem. It's a loading problem. He's been solving loading problems his whole life; it's the one math he's never once gotten wrong, the math of what a hull will carry and what she'll dump and what has to go over the rail to keep her off her beam ends. He knows how to choose what goes over the side. He's so good at it he didn't trip the release the one night it would have saved them, because the release admitted the deck was loaded wrong, and a man who knows loading the way Cole knows loading would rather lay a boat down than admit out loud she's running heavy.

He puts the warm hand over the cold math the way he's put it over everything all night.

"Alright," he says, level, to Hartline, to the room. "Alright. Then we do it right. You tell me what you need from your end and I'll work mine. First thing — you're freezing. You came up cold and you're getting colder and we need you working, so come up here in the air and let me get you out of that water while we sort it."

He does it because it's true — the swimmer's degrading, anyone can see it, a hypothermic rescuer is a second casualty and a turned basket and a dead room — and he does it because it makes him the man who noticed, the steady one, the skipper minding his swimmer, and the two reasons are the same reason and the sameness is the most comfortable thing Cole knows. He gets a fist in the shoulder strap of Hartline's drysuit and hauls him up the slope into the wedge proper, up out of the worst of the water, and the swimmer comes shaking and grateful and lets himself be handled, and Cole handles him, and somewhere under the handling the books keep themselves the way they always do.

---

"There's a thing you're gonna want to know," Cole says, while Hartline gets his breath and his light and his radio sorted, while the man keys his mic and tells the bird overhead *three souls, two ambulatory one litter, requesting basket,* and the bird's voice crackles back tinny and far through the steel. "About how she went over."

He says it easy. He says it the way you offer a man a thing he hasn't asked for yet but will, sliding it across the table before the question, so the answer's already sitting there warm when the question comes cold.

"We were running the pots, working a building sea — you know February out here, you know what it does.
