We'd been icing all watch, spray freezing on every surface, and there's not a thing in the world you can do about that but knock it off the rigging and pray, and you can't knock it off a pot stack, it gets down inside the cages where you can't reach. We were heavy with ice topside and we knew it and we were working her careful." He keeps his voice in the channel it lives in, the warm even channel, and he watches Hartline take it the way a cold tired man takes a clear account from the senior man on scene — gratefully, as a thing he can write down later and not have to dig for. "And a williwaw came over us. Katabatic blast off the islands, no warning, you can't see one coming, it just falls out of the mountains onto the water. Hit us on the high side with the ice already up in the rigging and she went past her point and laid down. Forty seconds. Nothing anybody could've done. Weather rolled us."

*Weather rolled us.* He lays it down whole and clean and it is even mostly true, every plank of it — the ice was real, spray had been freezing on the rigging all watch, and the williwaw was real and forty seconds was real. The ice is a true plank and he sets it where a man's eye lands first. The thing wrong with the account is not any plank in it; it's the shape of the hole the planks are nailed around — the hole where the deck-load sits, the full pots stacked over her certified line all season and stacked over it again that night, the deck-load release he never tripped, the held course after the gust warning, the throttle he kept up to make the next pot-line. Every true sentence is laid so careful around that hole that a tired cold man can't see there's a hole there at all — can't see that a trimmed boat rides out a williwaw and a boat loaded the way Cole loaded her lays down. Cole has built it good. He's had all night and a season and he's a craftsman at it. *Weather rolled us.* It's the kind of true he's worked with his whole life, the useful kind, and he watches it go into the official record on scene through the one official ear in the box, and the cold piece of him is satisfied the way it's satisfied when a deck comes out trimmed.

And then, because he can't help it, because the books never close, his eye goes to Ren.

She's watching him. He can see her now — that's the new thing, the terrible gift of the light, he can *see* her face for the first time since the roll, the close-cropped hair plastered down, the blue at the lips, the burn-scar webbing on the wrist of the hand that's tucked up under her oilskin against her ribs the way it's been tucked up under there for hours, holding whatever she's been holding. And her face while he tells the swimmer *weather rolled us* is not doing what a face does when it hears a true thing it already lived. It's doing the working-still. It's reading him. It's the face he saw her put on a vital sign that didn't add up, the small wrongness she files and comes back to, and it's pointed at him now, full on, in the green-white light, and Cole understands with a drop in his belly like the boat going over that whatever she's been carrying since the second dive, whatever's tucked under her oilskin against her ribs, it is *about him,* and she has known it for hours, and the light has just shown him her knowing the way it showed him the wedge.

He doesn't let it move his face. Twenty-one hours, a season, a life — none of it moves his face. He's a craftsman at that too.

"Right, Ren?" he says. Easy. Warm. Sliding it to her the way he slid it to the swimmer, *help me lay this deck out trim,* the skipper and the medic with one account between them. "Weather. Fast. Nothing to be done."

A beat. The strobe pulses. Hartline, oblivious, cold, keys his mic again.

"Nothing to be done," Ren says.

She says it flat. She gives him the words and not one grain of weight under them, and Cole has spent his whole life under the weight in voices and there is no weight in this one, none, it is the flattest thing he's ever heard a person say, and that flatness is the loudest sound in the box. She has handed him his own sentence back with the life taken out of it, the way you hand a man back a tool you've decided you won't be borrowing. And in the green light he watches her hand move under her oilskin, just slightly, the way a hand moves to settle a thing it's protecting, and he knows — the cold piece of him knows, the part that's never once been wrong about loading — that she has something, and that she will decide what it weighs, and that the deck he's spent all night and a season trimming is not, after all, his to trim alone.

He covers it. He's already covering it. "Good," he says, to the room, to the swimmer, to her. "Good. Let's get people out of here."

---

Hartline's done with the bird. He pulls the mask down off his forehead and gets it half-seated and stops, one more time, the cold making him slow, the slowness making him honest.

"Basket's coming down to the water outside, port quarter of the hull where she's highest out — that's the only place the flight mech can keep it off the steel in this sea. Which means I'm taking people out the way I came in. Down through your passage, under, out the breach, and up to the basket in the water. It's a swim. Short, but it's a swim, in this." He looks at Sal, at the lashed-straight leg, at the grey. "Him I can't swim. Litter case, broke leg, can't go under on a breath. I'm gonna have to rig him a line and work him through and that's — that's most of my window right there, one of those. So." He looks up, and the cold's in his eyes but the question's clear and it's the only question and he asks it the way the procedure makes him ask it, fast and flat, because there's no kind way and no time. "Two. One of 'em's gotta be him or we leave him and I'm not leaving a litter case. So it's him and one. You're the skipper — " to Cole — "you decide who's the one, and you decide it now, 'cause every minute I'm in here breathing your bad air I'm closer to being a third litter case myself and then nobody decides anything."

The light swings between them. Sal — who can't be left, who isn't even a choice, who's the fixed weight the whole problem has to carry. Ren — the medic, bare-handed, blue-lipped, the one who went down to the chart locker and came up changed, the one with her hand pressed to whatever's under her oilskin, the one witness whose face won't take *weather rolled us.* And Cole — the skipper, the man who knows the passage, the man who can talk the swimmer through the dark, the indispensable hands, the most useful body in a drowning room.

Cole looks at the two of them in the first light he's had in twenty-one hours, and does the arithmetic of the basket like a man balancing a boat, weighing what the hull will carry and what has to go over the side, and finds — the way he always finds, the way he found it the night of the gust with his hand on the throttle and the release right there — that the math comes out the way the math has to come out to keep him afloat.

He has spent his whole life loading a deck to come out right.

He knows how to choose what goes over the side.

"Get the cook ready," Cole says, to Hartline, level, warm, the hand on the tiller, the skipper deciding. "He's the one that can't wait. We'll sort the second seat while you rig him."

And in the green light, with the strobe pulsing and the water at all their chests and the air gone sour and the bird burning fuel in the closing window overhead, he looks at Ren — at the medic, at the witness, at the hand against her ribs — and he lets her see him not say her name, the way all night he's let her see him not look at the chart locker, and he knows she sees it, and he knows she knows what it means, and he is already, even now, even here, choosing what goes over the side.

The water comes up another finger while he's deciding.

He doesn't feel it.

He's loading a deck.



---


# Chapter Twenty-One

## The Math of Two

*POV: Ren — T+22:00*

*Two now. Two later is a maybe.* The swimmer has laid it down and Ren is already inside it, doing the only thing she does when a new variable enters the field, which is count it. Three living bodies. Two seats this pass. The helo overhead is a sound she'd taken for the hull working until the light came up the passage — a deep beating up past the steel, a hover over a capsized boat in twenty-foot seas, burning fuel by the gallon-minute. The helo is the clock now, the way the cold was the clock, the way the air is the clock. Everything in this box has always been a clock, and the swimmer is just the newest one, and the kindest-looking, and Ren has learned not to trust the kind-looking clocks.

She watches the swimmer reading them in his green beam — *who's hurt, who's strong, who's going down fastest* — and she recognizes the work, the same triage she's run on bodies all her life, and she feels an old professional kinship with the man even as she understands he is about to become the worst problem in the box.

"What's the basket cycle," she says. "The hoist. Cycle time, in this sea." She says it flat, in the language he'll recognize, the language that tells him she is not a frightened deckhand but a person he can hand a hard number to. "I was medical, before this. Give me the real number, not the calm one."

A beat. The light holds on her. She watches him decide to do the thing he's been trained not to do, which is tell the truth in a hull.

"Hoist is fouling on the swells — they're flying it manual," he says. "Bird's tight on fuel; they came off St. Paul, got a tanker inbound but it's not on station." He works a numb hand under his lanyard, buying a second, and in the move the light wobbles and she sees his face clearly — young, younger than she expected, ice already glazing the rubber at his cheeks, his lips going the colorless color she's watched go colorless on three people in this box. Thirty-four-degree water, however long it took to swim a man down a flooded passage in the dark, and he is already on the clock she knows best. *He's got his own ten minutes,* she thinks. *He's already spending them.* "Two now is real," he says. "Two later is a maybe. That's the truth and that's the whole truth and I need you to help me pick the two."

And there it is, said twice now, said until it's a floor she has to stand on. The moral arithmetic she has been doing on bodies all her life, handed to her clean and cold in a green beam of light, *pick the two,* the exact verb that ran a child off the edge of the world in London, the verb she came to the Bering Sea to never have to use again.

---

She does the math because the math is the only floor.

She does it out loud, in the swimmer's light, because doing it out loud is how you keep it honest — a triage you can't say to the room is a triage you're hiding something inside.

"Sal goes," she says. "First, no argument. Broke femur, sixteen hours of cold on it, compartment's tight — he can't be left for a maybe. If the second cycle doesn't come, he's the one who dies of it, and he's the one who can't do a single thing to help himself in the wait. He's not a candidate. He's a given." She turns her head toward the dark where Sal is, his breath gone careful again. "You hear me, Sal? You're going. That part's done."

"Ren — " Sal starts.

"Done." She doesn't let him have it. She's seen this, the injured man who wants to be noble, who wants to wave the basket on to someone younger — and it's not nobility, it's the cold talking, it's the part of a dying man that has started to find dying restful, and she will not let the cold make Sal's choice for him. "You don't get a vote on whether you're worth the basket. That's mine. You're worth the basket. Next."

The swimmer is already moving, unclipping the pouch, reading the corner where Sal is, and she can hear in his economy of motion that he's agreed with her before she's finished — the broke leg is the obvious first lift, basket-only, can't take a sling. One slot gone. One slot left. And three living bodies, minus the given, makes two candidates for one chair, and the two candidates are her and Cole.

She runs it the way she'd run it on the boards. Out loud. Flat.

"That leaves one seat and two of us. Cole and me." She makes herself say his name level, a name on a chart. "So we run the same numbers we'd run on anyone. Who survives the wait if the second cycle doesn't come, and who's needed to manage the wait so the others survive it." She breathes; every word is air, but this is air worth spending. "I'm the medic. If Sal goes up first and Cole and I are still in the box, somebody has to keep Sal's traction in the basket on the way up or the lift kills him — afterdrop, the cold blood, you hoist a hypothermic man vertical too fast and you stop his heart at the top of the wire. That's real. That's not a thing I'm inventing to give myself a seat." She turns to the swimmer. "Tell him. Tell him I'm right about the afterdrop."

The light holds on her, then dips — a nod. "She's right," the swimmer says. "Circum-rescue collapse. We brief it. Keep 'em flat, keep 'em slow. The cook's a candidate to arrest on the wire."

"So the medic rides with the patient," Ren says. "That's the textbook. That's the call I'd make on any beach in the world. Sal and me go up, Sal in the basket and me on the next cycle or in the basket with him if the cage'll take two, and Cole — " she hears the math come out where it was always going to come out, the cold clean place the numbers always land, " — Cole's the strongest of the three of us and the one with the best odds of surviving the wait. So Cole waits."

She says it and it is correct. She has checked it three ways and it is correct, medically, operationally, every way the boards would grade it. Sal and Ren up; Cole waits for the maybe. The medic with the patient, the strongest man in the gamble. It is the call she'd make on anyone, and that is precisely why she trusts it, because the numbers don't know whose name is attached and the numbers came out Cole-waits.

And the thing about being right is that it is the most comfortable place in the world to stand, and Ren has spent her whole life learning that the most comfortable place to stand is exactly where she should check her own feet.

The box checks them for her. Somewhere under the wedge, down in the drowned half of the boat, the iron lets out a long low note she hasn't heard before — not the slosh, not the sigh, not the EPIRB tapping fouled in the gear, but a sound deeper than any of those, a sound with weight behind it, the sound a held thing makes the half-second before it stops holding. The water at her chest shivers with it. It is up another knuckle on her ribs since the swimmer cut his way in — she clocks the new cold line the way she'd clock a creeping fever, files it, the slow even rise that has not once gone the other way all night. The swimmer feels it too; his light jerks toward the black mouth of the passage and holds there a beat, asking the water a question, before he goes back to the strap. The box is not the same box it was when the light came up. It has begun, very quietly, to change its mind about how long it will be a box at all.

The swimmer is rigging Sal's corner, working fast, his cold hands fumbling a strap. "Okay," he says. "Cook first lift. Then — who's my second? Medic with the patient, you said. You. You're my second. That it? We doing that?"

"That's it," Ren says.

And Cole, who has not said a word through any of it, who has held the strobe steady and Sal's leg steady and let the medic run her numbers in the light the way he's let her run them all night, says, gently, in the warm even voice that is a floor people stand on:

"Ren. Before you lock that in. Can I say one thing."

---

She knows the thing before he says it. She knows it the way the back of her knew the boy had stopped breathing thirty seconds before the front of her turned around. She has known, somewhere under the numbers, since the swimmer's light came up the passage, what Cole would do with the one card the night has left in his hand, and she has been doing her clean correct arithmetic partly to keep from looking at it.

"Say it," she says.

"It's nothing against your call." His voice is for her, low, pitched under the rotor-beat and the swimmer's working, but not so low the swimmer can't catch it if he listens, and she understands that the not-so-low is the whole point, that he is saying it in front of the one official witness on scene on purpose, the way you'd put a thing on the record. "Your call's a good call. Medic with the patient, that's right, I'd make it too. I just — " and here it comes, warm, almost sorrowful, a man reluctant to raise a hard thing and raising it anyway because he cares too much not to — "I just want us all clear-eyed, before we send people up, about what's gonna happen after. Because there's gonna be an after now. There's a bird up there. We're getting out. And when we get out, there's gonna be questions. There's always questions, after. About who did what."

The cold in her wrists is an old cold, the first cold, the one from the first minute of the wreck. But this is a different cold, and it climbs faster.

"Cole."

"Marcus," he says. Just the name. Soft. Laid down between them like a card turned face up. "The kid. They're gonna ask about the kid. How a nineteen-year-old died in a box with a medic in it. And I was there, Ren — I held the light. I saw you work him eight minutes. I'll say that. I'll say you worked him till there was nothing to work, because that's what I saw." A pause, and the warmth in it is the worst part, the genuine concern laid over the cold thing underneath like a hand over a number. "But you and I both know what you said after. *He had margin. An hour ago. I tagged him and I went to the leg.* You said it. I'm not — Ren, I'm not throwing it at you. I'm telling you it's *in here* — " she can hear him touch his own chest, his own skull, where the witnessed thing lives — "and it's gonna be in there when the men with clipboards start asking. For both of us. And the cleanest thing for everybody — for you, for the kid's family, for all of us — is the simple true thing. The weather rolled us. The cold took the kid before anybody could've stopped it. We all did everything we could." The light from the swimmer's lamp catches the wet edge of his beard as he turns his face toward her in the dark. "Nobody's hands get looked at too close. That's the report that lets everybody go home."

He doesn't threaten her. That's the genius of it, the thing she'll turn over for years. He doesn't say *or else.* He doesn't have to. He's just made certain, gently, in front of the witness, that she knows that he knows — that her own thirty seconds are in his keeping, that the moment she opens her mouth to indict anyone's hands in this hull, her own go on the table beside them. He has not asked her for silence. He has shown her that silence is the chair they both have to sit in, and that the chair has her name carved next to his.

She understands, with a clarity that has no warmth in it at all, that she is being made complicit. Not in his crime — she doesn't even know yet, not all of it, only what the fouled beacon told her, only the deck that didn't dump. She is being made complicit in the *story.* In the weather-rolled-us, in the everybody-did-everything, in the comfortable version that buries the boy's margin and the held course in the same soft grave and lets everyone walk up into the warm with clean hands. He is offering her a rope. It is the same rope she threw the room the first hour, the same rope he threw her over the boy's body — *you did everything, there was nothing to work* — and it would hold her weight, and she knows exactly the kind of rope it is, because she has been hanging from a version of it since London.

And the cruelest part, the part Cole has built the whole thing on without ever saying it, is that the rope is partly *true.* The weather did roll them. The cold did take the boy. She did stand for thirty seconds with her back turned. Every plank in the comfortable version is a real plank. That's what makes it a floor you can stand on. That's what makes it so hard to refuse.

"Pick the two," the swimmer says, behind them, not hearing it, or hearing it as crew steadying crew. "I'm rigged for the cook. I need my second. Who's it gonna be."

---

She doesn't answer fast enough.

That's the thing she'll carry from this minute, the way she carries the thirty seconds with her back turned — that when the choice was forced, when the swimmer said *name the one,* she had her own name on her tongue, the medical-correct name, the name the numbers spat out three ways, and she could not get it past the thing Cole had laid across it. Because if she says *me* — if she goes up now with Sal, the medic with the patient, exactly right — she goes up owing Cole the silence. She goes up having let him put the rope in her hand and having held it. She goes up into the warm lit world of clipboards and questions as a woman who agreed, in a green beam of light, that nobody's hands would get looked at too close, hers least of all. And once she's up, once she's warm, once Cole is still down here being the indispensable man waiting on a maybe — she knows how that story finishes. She's watched usefulness write reports before. The skipper who stayed. The medic he sent up first. *He could work with need.* He's been working with it all night, and the last thing his need would buy is her silence, paid for in advance with her own thirty seconds.

And if she says *Cole* — if she puts him in the chair and stays in the box herself — then she's in the flooding wedge with a broke-legged man and a swimmer spending his own ten minutes, waiting on a hoist cycle the man himself called a maybe, having sent up the one person whose hands the report most needs to examine and the one person who'll make sure they never are. She'd be writing her own report into the water. She'd be triaging herself expectant.

Two doors and a wall between them, and behind her the swimmer is rigging a basket for a man who can't wait, and the helo is burning fuel it doesn't have over a sea that's closing, and Cole is breathing his slow even managed metronome eight inches away in the dark, the breath that has managed itself since the first minute of the wreck, the breath of a man who has already done his arithmetic and likes the answer.

"Sal goes," she says. It's the only word she's sure of. "Sal goes now. Rig him."

"Rigging him." The swimmer is already on it, threading the strap under the splint, and Ren moves to him in the cold water, her hands going to the traction line, to the leg, because the leg is a thing she can do while the impossible thing waits, because her hands are the only part of her that has never learned to lie and right now she needs them busy so they can't agree to anything. "Keep that leg straight," she tells the swimmer. "Straight as you can get it. He arrests on the wire, you bring him back down to me, you don't fight it up top, you hear?"

"I hear."

"And the second," the swimmer says, not looking up, his cold hands flying on the strap, "I need the second *now,* because once the cook's on the wire the bird's committed and I got maybe ninety seconds to put a second body in the cage before they have to break, so you tell me right now, the two of you, who is it, and you tell me without a fight, because a fight up here costs the cook his lift. *Name the one.*"

She opens her mouth. Her own name is there. The correct name. The clean name.

And it sits on her tongue like the ice on the swimmer's cheeks, like the cold in her wrists, like Cole's slow even breath in the dark, because saying it means taking the rope, and not saying it means staying in the water with the man whose hands she still needs to keep Sal alive on the wire, and she is, for the first time since the boat lay down, completely unable to do the arithmetic, because every answer it returns has her own name in the working and she has just spent a whole night learning what it costs to triage the inconvenient out of a sum.

She doesn't answer fast enough.

And the hull, which has been doing its own patient arithmetic under all of theirs since the first minute, which gives you longer than it feels and less than you hoped, finishes the thought it started under the wedge a few minutes ago and answers for her — that same deep note, grown now into a groan with the whole drowned sky behind it, the chart locker and the engine flat and all the dead pulling on the rest of her, and the swimmer's head snaps up and his light swings down to the black water where it's already, suddenly, *climbing,* faster than the tide, faster than anything it's done all night, the new cold mouth of the passage gulping where the swimmer cut his way in, and Cole says one word into the dark, the word he said before the settle, the word that means he heard the boat change its mind before anyone else:

*"Hold."*


---


# Chapter Twenty-Two

## The Breach

*POV: Cole — T+23:00*

The swimmer wants to cut the boat, and Cole is the only man in the dark who can tell him where.

He is a young one — Cole heard it the moment the voice came up the flooded throat ahead of the light, a voice with the dropped-flat cadence they train into them, *Coast Guard, I've got you, I've got you,* the same four words a man uses on a spooked horse, and under the training a young one's breath, fast, working hard against the cold he just swam through to reach them. The light came after the voice. A hand-lamp on the swimmer's helmet, the first outside light in the box in twenty-three hours, and when it broke the surface at the passage mouth and threw itself around the wedge, Cole had to shut his eyes against it like a man coming up out of a mine. Then he made himself open them, because a man who can see is worth more than a man who can't, and seeing is a thing he can give the room that nobody else can right now.

He sees the wedge for the first time since the settle. He wishes he hadn't. The hand-lamp lays it all out — the crushed slope of the steel coming down to meet black water a yard from his face, the diesel sheen working on the surface in slow oily wheels, Sal's grey face and the splint and the bad leg out straight on the grating, Ren beside him with her hood back and her cropped hair frozen into points and her eyes doing the fast flat sweep they do, taking the swimmer apart for parts the way she takes everything apart. And the water. The water everywhere, neck-high to a sitting man, climbing the last of the grating, and the swimmer's helmet riding it like a buoy in the throat with the whole cold weight of the sea pressing at his back to come in after him.

"I can't get the basket down this," the swimmer says. He's got an arm hooked over a frame, half in, half out, and he's looking at the passage the way Cole looked at it the first hour — measuring it, not liking the measure. "The dog-leg's too tight and it's under. I bring the litter in here, I can't turn it, and your cook can't swim out to me with that leg, he'll drown in the passage. I need a straight shot from the basket to him. I need to open this up."

"Open it how," Cole says, though he knows. He's known since the man said *cut.*

"There's a panel above the throat." The swimmer angles the lamp and the beam climbs the steel to a square of plate riveted into the hull bottom over the passage mouth — a sounding hatch, an access cover, Cole couldn't have named it from memory but his eye knows it the second the light's on it, knows it the way his eye knows everything on a boat. "Hydraulic shears off my belt, I cut the corners, I peel it, that gives me a hole straight up into your pocket I can run the basket and the litter through. Two minutes of cutting." He's breathing it out fast, the plan, getting it said before the cold takes the words. "But."

"But it lets the sea in," Cole says.

"It lets the sea in." The swimmer's eyes find his in the lamp-light, and they're young and they're steady and they've already done this arithmetic, and Cole respects him for not pretending. "That panel's below the outside waterline now — she's sitting that low. I cut it, water comes up through the new hole same as it'd come up a stove drain. How fast depends on your air. Right now you've got a bubble holding the sea down off that panel. I cut the panel, the bubble's got a new place to bleed, and she'll take water faster than she has all day." He says the next part plain, because plain is the only mercy he's got to give. "I open her up to get the cook out, and the clock changes. You go from hours to — less. I need to know your boat tells you that's worth it before I put a tool to her, because once I cut, there's no putting it back."

And there it is, laid on the grating between them, the whole of it: *to take the worst-hurt man out, breach the box that's keeping the rest alive.* Cole has been waiting for the sentence since the first hour, the one where saving somebody costs everybody, and here it is in a young man's flat trained voice, and the part of Cole that has spent all night being useful steps forward to answer it because answering it is the most useful thing left to do and the most useful man is the one nobody examines.

"It's worth it," Cole says.

He doesn't look at Ren when he says it. He's careful not to. He says it to the swimmer, skipper to rescuer, the senior man on a dead boat giving the call, and he lets the rank do what rank does, lets it close the question before anybody else can open it — because if the medic opens it, the medic starts doing math out loud, and the medic's math has a way of coming out *Sal and Ren, Cole waits,* and Cole has spent his whole life loading a deck to come out right and he is not going to let the load come out wrong now, in the last hour, with the answer this close.

"It's worth it," he says again. "He can't wait for a second cycle, and you said yourself there might not be one. You cut. I'll show you the panel and I'll brace the cook and I'll keep your high point above water as long as there's a high point. This boat's been telling me her business for twenty hours. She'll give us the time. Cut her."

---

The swimmer cuts her, and Cole does the bracing, because the bracing is the thing only he can do, and he holds onto that the way he's held onto every job all night.

He gets Sal up first — up the last of the slope, the cook's whole soft weight come against him, the splinted leg dragging, Sal making the high thin sound through his teeth that means the broken ends just rode against something and Cole driving him up anyway because there's no gentle way left, only the way that works. He wedges Sal into the angle where the wedge pinches highest, half across his own lap the way they've ridden for hours, the bad leg out straight along the grating, and he gets Ren's hands on the traction line and the strobe-bag and the medical neoprene, all of it up, all of it above the water that's coming, and then he turns to the swimmer and gives him the boat.

"Top corner of the panel, your two o'clock," Cole says, his arm up along the steel, finding the rivets by feel the way he's found everything in the dark, the lamp making his hand a thing he can watch from outside himself. "Cut that corner first, then the bottom outboard, then the bottom inboard. Leave the top inboard for last — that's your hinge. You cut three corners and peel her down on the fourth like a lid, and she'll fold flat against the frame out of your way. You cut her free all the way round, she drops, and she drops on you or on him." He nods at Sal. "Three corners. Peel her. I've stripped enough plate off enough hulls to know how she'll go."

He has. That part's true — twenty years of it, salvage in his youth and patch-work since, a man who knows how steel comes apart — and the swimmer hears the true thing in it and nods and sets the shears to the top corner, and Cole feels the small warm click of being believed, of being the one who knows, and he rests on it the way he rests his weight on the frame.

The shears bite. It's a sound Cole feels in his teeth — the hydraulic jaw closing on the plate, the steel giving with a flat tearing crack that the wedge throws back at all of them, close, no distance in it, the way every sound in here has had no distance in it since the lights went. The swimmer works fast and clean. Top corner. Bottom outboard. Each cut a crack and a shudder and a thread of the outside sea finding the new seam and starting to weep through it, a cold rope of it running down the steel and into the box, and Cole watches the water start to come and does the arithmetic he can't stop doing, the deck-loading part of him that never sleeps, weighing the inflow against the air against the float, and the number it gives back is small and getting smaller and he puts a warm hand over it the way he's put a warm hand over every number all night.

"How's she breathing," the swimmer says, not looking up, the shears at the bottom inboard corner now, the third cut.

"She'll hold for the cut," Cole says. He doesn't say what comes after the cut. After the cut isn't the swimmer's to manage; after the cut is Cole's, the way the boat has been Cole's since Ferro went down in his bunk and left him the senior man on a hull he'd already killed.

The third corner goes. The plate sags inboard on its one held corner exactly the way Cole said it would, folds down flat against the frame, and where it was there is a hole — a black ragged-edged square of nothing, a yard of it, open straight up into the wedge from the drowned throat below — and through it, the instant it opens, the sea comes.

Not weeping now. Coming. The bubble that held the sea down off that panel for twenty-three hours has its new place to bleed, and the cold black water boils up through the cut hole into the box the way water comes up a stove drain when the tide's wrong, fast and then faster, and the level in the wedge that was at a sitting man's neck climbs in a breath to his chin, and the air — Cole feels the air go with it, feels the pocket give up a third of itself into the new hole in a single shuddering exhale, the pressure dropping, his ears clicking with it, the headache behind his eyes blooming sudden and hard as the sour air crushes down smaller around three breathing skulls.

"There she is," the swimmer says, and Cole hears the change in his trained voice too, the fast under the flat. "She's taking it now. We move."

---

They move, and Cole is the floor they move on, and he knows it, and the knowing is the only warm thing in him.

The swimmer's got the litter up through the hole — a rigid basket-litter on the hoist line, dropped down through the cut panel from the hovering helo Cole can't see and can only feel now, a beat in the steel, a far pulse under the slosh — and the two of them work Sal into it in the rising water with the cold lamp swinging. It's bad. It's the worst kind of work, lifting a man with a broken thigh into a steel cage while the sea climbs your own chest, and Sal screams it out plain now, no teeth left to hold it behind, the splinted leg riding wrong no matter how they cradle it, and Cole keeps his voice the low even thing it's been all night — *easy, Sal, easy, you're going up, you're the first one up, you hear me* — and gets the man's bulk into the litter and the straps across him and his arms tucked and his face clear of the water that's at all their chests now and climbing.

"Cook's rigged," the swimmer says into the dark over his head, a hand to his helmet, and somewhere up the cut hole a flight mechanic Cole will never meet takes a strain on the line. The hoist comes tight. Sal's weight lifts off Cole's hands for the first time in twenty hours, the whole soft ruined bulk of him gone light against the wire, ready to go up out of the box — and Cole feels the old clean satisfaction of a thing about to come out right settle over him in the killing cold. He got Sal to the wire. His hands did that. A man can do a lot of good in a bad night.

The swimmer steadies the litter against the surge and turns in the rising water to the two that are left.

"Basket comes back down for the litter cycle in four minutes," the swimmer says. He's reading the water now, reading it the way Ren reads it, and Cole watches the young face do the math and not like the answer. "Four minutes, and this boat doesn't have four minutes the way she's taking it. I get one more up this cycle, clean, before she's too low to work. One. The other rides the next cycle if there is one." He looks at the two of them — the medic with her frozen points of hair and her flat sweeping eyes, the broad slow grey-bearded skipper with his hand on the frame — and he says the thing the way they're trained to say it, fast and flat and without flinching. "One now. The strong one waits. Medic, you're rigged for the swim. Skipper — can you make the passage on your own air if she comes to it?"

And there it is, the second time, the question under the question, and this time it's pointed straight at Cole, and Cole has the answer ready the way he's had every answer ready all night, because he's the one who knows the passage, the one who knows the boat, the one who can talk a swimmer in and brace a broken man and tell you which corner to cut first — the indispensable one, the one the room can't spare, and the one nobody examines, because you don't audit the man whose hands are keeping you off the floor.

"I can make the passage," Cole says. "I've made it twice. I'm the diver. She's not." He nods at Ren, and the warm reasonable voice does the warm reasonable work, the skipper allocating his crew like deck-load, putting the one who can't dive in the basket and keeping the one who can in the water, and every word of it is true and every word of it is the load coming out exactly the way he means it to come out. "Take her. I'll ride the cut down to the throat and free-dive out to your basket on the next cycle. I've got the lungs and I've got the route. Take her now. We're losing the boat."

He says *take her now* and he watches Ren's face when he says it, because he can't help it, because she's the one variable on the boat he's never fully been able to load, and her face does a thing he doesn't expect.

It doesn't argue. He'd braced for the argument — for the medic's math, for *Sal and me, Cole waits,* for the flat hard voice that's overruled him before. But she doesn't reach for the math. She looks at him in the swinging lamp-light with the water at her collarbones and her hand pressed flat to her ribs the way it's been pressed since the second dive, and Cole, who has spent his whole life reading the thing in a face, reads this one, and what's in it isn't argument. What's in it is the thing she's been carrying since she came up off that dive, the hairline crack run all the way open now, and behind the crack, looking out at him, is something that knows him.

He doesn't have the word for it yet. But his hands know it before his head does — the way his hands always know the weight before he does — and they go cold on the frame.

---

The water's at his chin and the swimmer's working Sal's litter clear of the gear toward the cut, and Ren is still beside Cole in the pinch with the sea at her collarbones, rigged for the swim and not yet moving, and in the last of the wedge above the climbing water Cole holds the high corner with one arm and does the final arithmetic, the one he's been keeping off the page all night, and lets it come out.

There's no second cycle. He knew it when the sea came up through the panel; he knows it now in his ears and his teeth and the float-number that's gone from hours to single hours to no hours at all. The swimmer's *four minutes* is a kindness, the way Ren's *nobody dies in the next hour* was a kindness — a rope thrown to people whose weight is over nothing. The boat is going. She'll take Sal up and take this last basket and then she'll fold the rest of the way, and the void Cole's been living in for twenty-three hours will fill the last of the way with the sea it's been holding off, and there is no version of the next minutes where four people come off this boat alive. Sal's on the wire. Ren goes next. That's two, and two is what the swimmer said, and two is what there ever was going to be.

Which leaves one in the water and the boat going, and a man who can choose to be that one.

The thought doesn't arrive cold. That's the part he didn't expect, kneeling in the dark all night waiting for it. It arrives warm, the warmest thing in the box — the old clean satisfaction, scaled up to the size of a life: the most useful man, the last man, the skipper last off his boat, the one who stayed in the water so the others could go up the wire. It's a good death. It's a death with his name on the right side of it, the death he'd have written for himself if the writing were his to do, and the cold piece of him that's kept the books all night looks at it and sees that it's not only good. It's *clean.* A man who drowns freeing the line is a man nobody audits. A man who goes up the wire alive is a man who sits in a warm room afterward and answers questions about the deck-load and the gust warning and the release he didn't trip, and there is no version of those questions where the warm room ends warm for Cole Vantine.

The water has his secrets. The boat's been burying them all night, the chart locker and the engine flat and the route log going down under the dog-leg degree by degree, and the last thing the water can bury is the man who held the course — bury him clean, bury him a hero, bury him the way you'd pull a sheet over a face, and leave nobody up top with both halves of the truth.

Except.

Ren shifts in the pinch to set herself for the swim, getting a knee under her against the surge, and as she braces her arm comes off her ribs for the first time in hours to take a grip on the frame — and the thing she's been holding pressed flat under her oilskin all night swings out clear of her body into the swinging lamp-light.

Flat. Hard. Sealed. A bag, a ruggedized case, the size of a thing you'd carry off a boat that you carried out of the drowned forward end where the boat keeps her instruments and her papers and her dead.

And Cole, who has spent the whole night sure the water had it, sure the chart locker buried it, sure the boat was on his side — Cole sees the bag come clear of her ribs in the cold light and feels the whole load of the night shift under him at once, the way a deck shifts when the lashing you trusted goes, and the deck-loading part of him that never stops weighing weighs this, fast, in the half-second before she pulls it back against her chest, and gives back the answer that has been sitting in the box with him for hours breathing slow against him in the dark.

She went down for the EPIRB. She came up and said *nothing, couldn't reach it, too fouled,* and her voice had a thing in it he couldn't place. She didn't come up with nothing. She came up with that. She's been carrying it against her ribs since, through the settle, through the dark, through every hour he spent congratulating the sea for burying his secrets — carrying the one thing he couldn't dive for, the one thing the water didn't get, and in a minute she is going to carry it up the wire into the light where the warm rooms and the questions are.

She has the truth. The water never had it. She'll take it up with her, the truth and her own dirty hands both, and Cole goes very still in the rising black with his arm hooked in the frame and the sea at his throat, and the last piece of the long night's arithmetic drops into place, cold and clean and final, the way the panel dropped on its one held corner.

He can't out-useful that bag. There's no job left whose doing makes that bag say a kinder thing. But there is one thing left he can still do, the same thing it's always been: go down and not come up, make his drowning indistinguishable from his courage, die the skipper-last-off and leave her alone in the warm with the bag and her own guilt and a dead man's mercy. He can't take the truth from her. But he can make himself a thing she has to weigh against it — a hero in the water, a man who saved them, a name his family ashore has nothing but — and let the weighing be hers, and let the silence look like the kindest thing a tired woman could choose.

The strobe on the frame strikes its small white pulse into the dark, once, again, painting the wedge in stutters — the climbing water, the folded panel, the case pressed flat to Ren's ribs, the one arm Cole has hooked in the frame above the flood. She looks at him through the stutter with the thing in her face that knows him, and Cole holds the high corner and looks back, and does not let her see the cold piece of him close its books.

She had the truth. And he, who had buried everything else the water would take, knew at last the one thing left to bury was himself.


---


# Chapter Twenty-Three

## Name the One

*POV: Ren — T+24:00*

The basket goes up with Sal in it and the box comes down to take the rest.

Ren watches the cage rise into the cut the swimmer made — a ragged mouth of grey daylight where there was steel an hour ago, the first true light she's seen since the lights went out, and it is the wrong color, it is sky-color, it is the color of up and it is pouring water like a broken dam. Sal goes through it on his back, strapped, the bad leg out straight, his big ruined face turned toward her in the strobe-flash, mouthing something she can't hear over the surge and the rotor-beat that comes and goes as the helo holds its station above a hull that is folding under it. Then the cut swallows him. Then it's just the hole, and the water coming through the hole, and her.

And Cole. To her left, lower, where the wedge pinches down to the black. She finds him by his breath the way she has found everyone all night, the slow even metronome that has never once panicked, going now in the cold like a man pacing himself for work.

The water's at her neck.

She marks it the way she marks everything, on her own body, the only instrument she has left — it was at her chest when Sal went into the basket and it is at the soft hollow under her jaw now, and the climb between those two marks took less time than it should have, took *minutes,* because the swimmer's cut and the surge through it have stopped the box pretending to be a box. There is no float left to measure in hours. There is the rate the water is rising and the rate it is rising is a thing she can feel against her throat, cold and patient and faster every breath, and she does the only math the night has left her, which is the math of how many breaths of air are left between the waterline and the wedge of steel a hand above her crown, and the number is small, and the number is dropping, and a number that drops faster than you can spend it is not a clock anymore. It's a verdict counting itself down.

*The basket comes once more,* the swimmer told them, before he went up with Sal on the line to work the hoist. *Once more for one, and then I have to look at the weather and the fuel and the boat, and if she goes before I'm back you go with her. Name the one.*

He'd looked at her when he said it. They'd both looked at her, the swimmer and Cole, because she's the medic, because triage is the thing she does, because in a box where the question is *who lives* she is the one whose whole ruined life has been built around answering it.

She has not named the one.

---

The water finds the tablet first. She feels it before she feels it on her skin — a cold flat shift against her ribs where the sealed brick of Dee's truth has ridden all night under her oilskin, the messenger beside it, two small honest machines that have kept her warm the way an ulcer keeps you warm, and the rising water sluices in under her collar and pries at them, wants them, wants to take them down to where it took the chart locker and the EPIRB and Dee herself. She gets a forearm up across her chest and pins them there. It is the first decision she makes and she makes it without thinking, which tells her something about herself she doesn't have time to look at: that her arm went to the evidence before it went to her own throat.

"Ren." Cole, in the dark, level. "You have to get to the cut. When the basket comes you have to be under the cut and ready. You can't be down here in the pinch when it drops — you'll never get clear of the gear in time."

He's right. He's always right about the boat; that's the horror she's been living inside for ten hours. The cut is up the slope, on the high side, where the swimmer breached her — and between Ren and the cut is the throat of the old passage and the tangle of shed gear and a current pulsing through the new wound in the hull that wants to suck her *down* it, toward the drowned forward end, toward the place she dove for the truth and came up knowing.

"Then you come too," she says. "We both stage under the cut. Basket comes, one goes, the other—" She stops. The other waits in a box that has minutes. They both know how the sentence ends. She doesn't end it.

"One of us goes under the cut," Cole says. "Move, Ren. While there's air to move in."

---

She moves.

It is the worst water she has been in all night and she has been in killing water three times now — the roll, the dive for the suits she gave away, the dive for the tablet — and this is worse than all of them because this water is *inside the room,* this is the room becoming the sea, and there is no surfacing from it, there is only getting to the one shrinking pocket of air before it closes.

She goes on her side, one arm clamped over the machines, the other hauling. The cold takes her the way it takes her every time, a fist closing, but the cold is old news, the cold is the thing she stopped being afraid of around hour three; what's new is the current. It pulses with the hull's slow working, a suck and a surge, and on the suck it tries to pour her down the throat of the passage toward the drowned forward end, and she has to time her pulls to the surge, haul when the water heaves her up the slope, lock down when it drags her back, and her hands — her hands have been gone for hours, hooks she hangs her will on, and she hangs it now, gets a hook around a frame and holds against the suck and feels her shoulder take the whole weight of a body the water wants.

"Surge—" Cole's voice, from below and behind. "Ride the surge, don't fight the back of it—"

She rides the surge. It throws her up the slope a body-length and she takes it and gets a knee onto something, gear, grating, the inhabited ground of the night gone underwater, and then the suck comes back and she is not braced for this one, this one comes harder, the hull working through a long groan, and the water that was at her neck closes over her face.

Black. Instant. The same black as the first minute of the book, the same black as the dives, total and close and full of diesel, and her body does the thing it did in the first minute, the gulp, the cold-shock reflex going for air where there is no air, and she clamps her own throat shut against it with the last clean discipline she has because a breath here is the sea and the sea is the end, and she holds, and she hauls for where the air was.

Her free hand finds gear. The wrong gear. A crab-pot's edge, the steel mesh, and her fingers — her useless hooked fingers — go *through* the mesh on the suck and the surge slams the pot down and her hand is in it, pinned, the mesh closed over her wrist like a trap, and the water has her by the hand and is pulling the rest of her down the slope into the drowned dark, and she is under, and she is held, and the math runs itself in the cold sorting room at the base of her with no inflection at all: *cold-shock buys ninety seconds before the involuntary gasp wins. The wrist is jammed past the joint. You cannot free a jammed joint with hooks for hands in under ninety seconds. This is the part where the body lets you push past the point where you should turn back, and there is no turning back, and—*

A hand closes on her collar.

She knows the hand. She has been pulled out of the water by this hand before — in the first hour, off the rack when Pavel died, the hand that hauled her up choking while a man drowned eight feet away. The same fist in the same collar, and she feels the whole strength of him come down the arm and into her, the slow broad deceptive strength of a man who dove salvage as a boy and has the lungs and the back for exactly this, and Cole gets his other hand down to her trapped wrist in the black and works it — not pulling, he doesn't pull, a pull would tear the hand off at the wrist, he *works* it, finds the pot's edge by feel and lifts the steel that has her and turns her hand the one way it will come, the diver's patience even now, even drowning, even with the air going — and her hand comes free and his fist in her collar hauls her up the slope against the suck and her head breaks into the air.

She gasps. The gasp is real air. Foul, thin, copper-thick, the last of it, but air, and she takes it in great tearing pulls with her freed hand clamped back over the machines and her whole body shaking in the way that isn't only cold, and Cole's arm is a bar across her, holding her against the steel in the high pinch under the cut, the way he held her and Sal through the settle, the way he has held this whole room together with these hands all night.

"I've got you. I've got you. Breathe. There's air right here, breathe it." His voice in her ear, low and warm, the hand on the tiller. "There she is. There you are. You're up."

She's up. She's up because of him. The arithmetic of the night completes itself one more time and it completes the way it has completed since the first hour: the guilty man's hands saved her, again, the most useful hands in the drowning room, and she owes him her life again, a fresh chain on top of all the others, and she hates it with a clarity that is almost warmth, and she cannot make the hatred matter against the simple fact of the breath in her chest that he gave her.

---

The cut is a yard above them now and the water is at their chins and the basket is not there.

She cranes up at the ragged grey mouth and the daylight and the water pouring through it and she can see, in flashes, the line — the cable, taut, running up out of sight into the rotor-wash, no cage on the end of it. Empty wire. The cycle takes time the way the swimmer warned them it would, and they do not have the time; the water is at their chins and the wedge of air is a foot deep and shrinking and the helo cannot make the steel hold still.

"Strobe," Cole says. "Keep the strobe up. He sights on the strobe."

She'd forgotten she had it. It's in her trapped-then-freed hand, the strobe Cole fished off Marcus's dead hip in the settle, the one thing that paints them to the men above, and she gets it up out of the water over her head where the cut can see it, and it flashes its hard white pulse up into the grey, and somewhere up there a man on a wire sees a small white light flashing from inside a sinking boat and knows there is still someone alive in the dark to come back for.

One. The basket comes back for one.

She does the math she has been refusing to do all night, and now there is no refusing it, now the water is at her chin and the box is closing and the basket is one basket for one body and there are two living souls in the pinch under the cut, and the night has finally asked the question her whole life has been about, the question she ran to the Bering Sea to stop being asked: *who lives.*

The medical-correct call is herself. She knows it cold, she ran it an hour ago when she was still pretending there'd be a second cycle: Sal first, immobile, broken, gone if left — done, Sal's up. Then the medic, because a medic on a wire keeps a hypothermic patient alive on the flight and a fisherman with bad knees does not, because she is the one who can hold Sal's airway and his traction through the violent rewarming that kills more hypothermics than the water ever does, because by every number she is the one who should go and Cole is the one who should wait. By every number. She is very good at the numbers. The numbers killed a child in London and she has spent ten years being unable to stop trusting them and they say *go.*

And under the numbers, the other thing, the thing Cole put on her tongue an hour ago in front of the swimmer, gentle, devastating, never a threat: *you're the one who stopped working the boy. If there's an inquiry into whose hands did what in this hull, yours are in it. The cleanest report for both of us is the one where the weather rolled us and nobody looks too hard at anybody's hands.* He'd made sure she knew that he knew. He'd handed her her own guilt and let her feel the weight of it, and the weight of it is this: if she goes up now, she goes up owing him silence, and if she stays, she dies in a box with the man whose hands she still needs.

She opens her mouth to say her own name. The medical-correct call. The one she can defend to any clipboard in any warm room for the rest of her life.

And the hull answers for her.

---

It comes as the long iron sigh, the one from the first minute, the one she'd know in her sleep for the rest of her life however long that is — the deep structural groan of steel deciding which way is up and choosing, finally, all the way down. The wedge over their heads tips and *keeps* tipping and the air in it crushes out through the cut in a belch of foul gas and the water leaps up their faces and the float that was measured in minutes is measured now in this, in the next breath, in the thing happening to the steel above her crown.

And the basket drops through the cut.

It comes down out of the grey on its cable, the high-walled cage, swinging, slamming the lip of the cut, water sheeting off it, and it drops to the surface of the rising water beside them and the swimmer's voice comes down the wire with it, torn ragged by the rotor, one word, *GO,* and there is no time to name anyone, there is no time for the numbers or the report or the chain or the owed silence, there is only the cage and the water at her mouth now and the steel coming down.

Cole's hands take her.

Not her collar this time. Her whole body. The slow broad strength that worked her wrist free in the dark gets under her and *lifts,* lifts her up out of the closing water and toward the cage, and she understands what he's doing in the half-second it takes him to do it, understands it the way she understood the inversion in the first minute, all of it at once and too late to stop it: he is putting her in the basket. He is choosing to be the one who stays.

"Cole—*no*—" She gets a hook of a hand on his arm. "No, you—the numbers, you're the diver, you—get in, get *in,* I'll—"

"You're the medic." His face in the strobe, very close, calm, managing even this, the warm laid careful over the cold one last time. "Sal needs you on the flight. I'd kill him on the wire and you'd keep him alive. That's the call. You'd make the same call." A beat, and the thing under the warm rises into his eyes where she can see it, where he lets her see it, *wants* her to see it. "And somebody's got to be last off his boat, Ren. The skipper goes last. That's how the report reads. That's the clean version. Take the clean version."

And she sees it, all of it, in the half-second their eyes hold over the rising black water — that this is not the noble thing it will look like from above, the captain last off his boat, the hero's exit. It is the cleanest report he can write with his own body. The water over the inquiry, the drowning that drowns the question, his death made to look like honor so that the only honor anybody ever has to remember is his. He is leaving her the basket and the burden in the same lift of his arms — the tablet against her ribs and her own thirty seconds with her back turned, the sole witness, the compromised one, the one whose hands aren't clean enough to point. He wants her to understand it here, now, so that when she's warm and dry in a room with a clipboard she'll know silence is the merciful thing: that he died for them and she let a boy go cold, and the dead don't need the truth and neither do the living, and he has made the lie easy for her on purpose, with his own life, his last and most terrible act of management.

He gives her the basket. He gives her the silence. He gives her his hands one last time, lifting.

"*GO,*" the swimmer screams down the wire, and the water takes the wedge, and Cole's hands shove her up and into the cage and her body falls into the high steel walls of it and the cable goes taut and the basket leaps off the surface of the flooding box.

She fights it. The medic in her fights it the way she fought to stay over Marcus, the reflex she could not triage out of herself, *don't leave a savable patient, don't lift the rope, there is margin, there is always margin*—she gets her hooked hands over the rim of the basket and tries to climb back down out of it, back into the box, back to the man in the water, and a fist closes on her—the swimmer's, leaning down the wire, hard and certain and kind—and pins her into the cage, holds her down in it, says something in her ear that is not words, that is just the sound a man makes when he is keeping you alive against your own hands, and she cannot get past him, she cannot get back down, the cage is rising and the box is shrinking under her and the water is closing the cut.

The last she sees of Cole is his face in the strobe she's still clutching, tipped up at her from the black water, the cold rising past his chin, calm, managing even this, his mouth moving in something that isn't quite words—she'll spend the rest of her life trying to read it, in every warm room after, his lips making a shape she can't hear over the rotor, a shape that might be *go,* that might be *I've got you,* that might be the thing he most wanted her to carry up—and then the water takes the strobe-light off his face the way the water took the lights in the first minute of the wreck, instant and total, and he is gone into the black under her, and she is rising through the grey toward a sky that is finally, after twenty-four hours, up where it belongs.

She rises out of the cut into the rotor-wash and the spray and the unbearable open grey of the world, the tablet pressed to her ribs by a hooked hand that will not open, the strobe still flashing in the other into a sky that doesn't need it anymore, and below her the *Adeline Marsh* folds her last fold and the cut goes under and the box that promised four people an hour is a closing dark seam on a grey sea, and somewhere inside it a man who saved her life four times is choosing the water over the room he can no longer manage, last off his boat, exactly as the clean version will say, exactly as he made sure it would say.

The basket turns slowly on the wire as it lifts.

She does not look down again. She has counted heads by their breathing for twenty-four hours and she counts them one last time, by reflex, by the law of the night: Sal, up, in the helo, breathing. Herself, in the cage, breathing. And the silence where a third breath was, the slow even metronome that never once panicked, gone quiet under the water like the boy's breath went quiet, like Pavel's, like all the breaths the night has stopped, and the count comes out two, and she holds the two, and she knows with a cold that has nothing to do with the air that she is going to spend the rest of her life as the only living person who can say what the third breath cost, and what it bought, and which one of those it meant to do.

She can feel the cold still working at the freed hand, the hooked one, the wrist the pot trapped and Cole worked loose — going from hook toward dead, the medic's hand, the useful one, the one she'll carry up into every warm room she ever stands in, and open over every clipboard, and never quite be able to make say what it knows.

The sky is up where it belongs. She rises toward it and does not look down.


---


# Chapter Twenty-Four

## The Last Man

*POV: Cole — T+24:40*

Now the box is his.

The basket's gone up the cut with Ren in it and the hull's let go of her like it never wanted her, and Cole is alone in the box for the first time in twenty-four hours. Not alone — the swimmer's still here, the orange of him bobbing in the breach, harnessed to a tag-line, working. But the swimmer's a man passing through, a man with a helicopter over his head and a clock in his ear, and he doesn't count the way the dead count, the way a drowned house full of people you knew counts. The box is empty of everyone Cole was managing. Sal's up. Ren's up — he'd put her there himself, his own hands, the gear had her by the leg and she'd gone under and he'd gotten a fist in her collar the way he'd gotten a fist in everybody's collar all night, and brought her up choking, and set her in the basket and told the swimmer *her, take her,* before she could say her own name, before she could say his. She'd fought to climb back down to him — medic's reflex, *don't leave a patient* — and the swimmer had held her in, and her face in the strobe had been the last thing, white and furious and afraid, mouthing something Cole didn't let himself read. He'd mouthed back the shape of *go,* and he doesn't know if she got it.

The water's at his chest and rising and he doesn't have a number for how fast anymore, the way he doesn't have a number for the air, the way Ren took the numbers up the line with her in her frozen head. He has the feel of it instead, which is all he's ever really run a boat on under the arithmetic — the feel of a hull that's done floating. She's been done for ten minutes. The breach did it. The swimmer cut a panel and ran a line to get Sal's broken leg out through the throat and the sea came in through the cut like it had been leaning on the door, and the float-time Ren measured in hours fell to one hour and then the one hour fell to nothing you could measure, just *soon,* just *now,* just the steady drop of the air-roof toward the rising water, the wedge closing like a fist.

"Skipper." The swimmer's voice, close, flattened by the steel the way every voice gets flattened in here. He's got a hand on the lip of the breach and his mask up and he's looking at Cole across the black water with the calm professional hardness of a man who has already decided he is not going to die for this. "Skipper, you're it. Last one. Basket's coming back down on this cycle and you're getting in it. You hear me?"

"I hear you."

"This bird's bingo on fuel in — " the swimmer doesn't finish it, glances up at the light, recalculates, doesn't like the number. "Soon. We do not have a second cycle in us, you understand? You get in the basket on this pass or we are not having a good day. Both of us."

"Understood."

The swimmer reads him for a half-second longer, the way Cole's been reading people all night, and Cole gives him nothing to read but a steady man — the steadiest man in the box, the only man in the box, the man who knows the box. *We're alright,* his face says, the useful lie his face has been saying for twenty-four hours, and the swimmer takes it, the way they all took it, because a drowning room takes the calm man at his word; it doesn't have time to audit him.

"Stand by for the basket," the swimmer says, and ducks back out through the breach into the wash, gone up his tag-line toward the light, and Cole is alone again in the dark with the water climbing him and the thing he came down here twenty-four hours ago not knowing he'd been waiting for.

---

He'd have got in the basket.

That's the part he turns over in the last of his own private dark, the part he wants to be true and is even mostly true. He'd have got in it. He'd have gone up the way Sal went up and Ren went up, and he'd have come out into the rotor-wash and the cold open air with the sky finally up where the sky goes, and they'd have wrapped him in something and flown him to a warm bright room, and the swimmer would have written in his report *recovered four, lost three, skipper assisted extraction, last man off,* and that report would have walked into every room Cole walked into for the rest of his life ahead of him like a thrown line, and he'd have held it the way you hold a thrown line, and it would have held.

He'd have got in the basket. And then the warm bright room. And then the other men in it, the ones with the clipboards, the ones who do the audit a drowning room never has time for. *Walk us through the night, Captain Vantine. From the top of the watch.* And he is a man who can walk a room through anything, he's spent his life walking rooms through things, and he'd have walked them through the weather and the gust and the roll, *forty seconds, nothing to be done, she went over like she'd been waiting all season to do it* — and most of it would have held, because most of it is true, the useful kind, and the part that isn't true is underwater.

He'd believed that an hour ago. Two. The clock's gone soft. He'd believed the boat was burying her own secrets — the chart locker gone under for good in the settle, the drowned engine flat with Dee in it, the route log and the heading and the time he held her course, all of it where no diver alive could reach it. He'd felt the settle's cold mercy and been ashamed of how much he wanted it and wanted it anyway. *The water's on my side.* He'd leaned his back on that in the dark.

And then he'd seen the thing under Ren's oilskin.

In the surge, hauling her up out of the gear that had her by the leg, his hand had gone where his hands go, finding the body to save it, and it had found the flat hard sealed shape tucked against her ribs under the wet orange of her coat, the shape she'd kept one hand on for hours in the dark, the shape he'd half-registered against his chest in the settle and told himself was nothing, was her medical kit, was the strobe. It isn't the strobe. The strobe was in his own fist when he found it on the dead kid's hip. He'd had the strobe. So what's the flat hard thing she's been holding to her ribs since she came up off the second dive gone strange and slowed and turned inward —

He knows what it is. He's known for the length of one surge, which is about the length of time it takes a man to understand the thing he's spent all night not understanding.

She didn't surface from that dive with nothing. *Couldn't reach it, too fouled* — she'd said that, spitting seawater, and her voice had had a thing in it he couldn't place, and he's a man who can place anything in a voice, and the not-placing had sat in him for hours like the headache. She came up with something. She came up with the chart locker's secret in her two hands and she'd carried it against her ribs through the settle and the breach and the surge that nearly drowned her, and she'd fought him to keep it when the gear had her, and she'd gone up the line just now with both fists locked on the rails of the basket and one of them, he sees it now, the way you see a sea you should have seen ten seconds ago, one of them locked on the lump under her coat.

The water has the chart locker. The water does not have the thing she carried up.

She's taking it into the warm bright room with the clipboards. She is the one thing he never managed, leaning slow against his chest in the dark all the way through the worst of it, counted in the three, holding the only thing in the world that can damn him, and he'd been congratulating the sea.

He stands in the rising black water in the empty box and feels the old reflex come up through him, the management, the *handle it, ease it, find the angle* — and finds there's no angle. There's no room left to walk through. There's a woman going up a wire into the light with the truth pressed to her ribs and there is no warm steady sentence in the world that pulls her back down.

There's only one card left. And the water's been holding it for him the whole time, and he just hadn't turned it over.

---

He hears the line before he understands what he's hearing.

A grinding, off in the breach, under the slop of the water — the basket's haul-line, or the swimmer's tag-line, one of the lines that run up through the cut into the rotor-light, dragging hard against the lip of the panel they cut, fouling on the bent steel and the gear that's slumped down toward the throat as the hull goes. He's heard a thousand lines foul on a thousand snags. He knows the sound the way he knows his own name. A line under load, hung up on steel, and above it a helicopter bingo on fuel and a basket that has to come down one more time and go up one more time with the last man in it, and a line that won't run clean through the cut will jam the davit, jam the hoist, hang the basket halfway in a folding hull while the bird burns the last of itself over the water.

Somebody has to clear it.

The thought arrives complete and Cole holds very still around it, the way he held still around the boat's first warning of the settle, the way you hold still around a thing that's true. The line's fouled in the breach, down where the water's deepest, where the cut steel and the slumped gear have come together in the dark. A man would have to go down into it. A man would have to put his head under the rising water in the throat of the box and work the line off the snag by feel, in the cold, with the air gone, and a man who went down there to do that, with the hull folding and the water taking the void —

He looks at it the way he's looked at every cost-benefit all night. This is the part of him that has never learned to lie, the part that weighs. The line genuinely needs clearing. That's the first true thing, and he holds onto it. The basket won't run if the line's fouled; the swimmer can't get the last man up through a jammed hoist; somebody has to go down and free it or the whole extraction hangs and the bird leaves with the line still down here and a dead boat under it. That's true. He doesn't have to lie to himself about that. It's the only kind he's ever needed.

And the second thing, the thing under the first, the thing he steers his mind around the way he steered the kid off the chart table and steered Ren away from the locker and steered himself, all night, around the one trip of the one lever he didn't pull — he lets this one come up only at the edge, only as much of it as his hands need:

Down there is a job only he can do. Up there is the basket, the wire, the rotor-light, the warm bright room, the men with the clipboards, and the woman with the truth against her ribs already going up to meet them. Down there is a line that needs a diver. Up there is a verdict.

He is a free-diver. He came up out of salvage work with these lungs; it's why he was the one who could run the passage, the one the room couldn't spare, the most useful hands in the drowning. He is the only diver left in the box. The swimmer can't do it — the swimmer's harnessed, the swimmer's a man with a helo over his head who has already decided not to die here. There is exactly one man left who can put his head under the cold and clear that line.

He doesn't finish the sentence about why he's glad of it. He's careful. He's been careful all night. He makes the first sentence, the true one, the one his hands can stand on: *the line's fouled and somebody has to clear it and I'm the only one who can.* And he lets the rest stay under the water with everything else the water's keeping for him.

"Swimmer." He says it up toward the breach, toward the light, and his voice comes out the way it's come out all night, level, easy, the hand on the tiller. "Your haul-line's fouled in the cut. Down on the low side. It'll jam your hoist."

A pause from the light. "Say again, skipper — get to the breach, we're sending the basket—"

"Line's fouled." He's already moving, already taking the long breath, the breath you take from the belly, the diver's breath, the cold gas-reflex clawing at it and Cole holding it down the way he's held everything down. "I'll clear it. I know the box. I'll have it in a minute. Stand by your basket."

"Skipper, *negative,* do not — we do not have time for you to—"

"You don't have time for a jammed hoist either," Cole says, reasonable, warm, the voice that's never failed him where anyone could see. "Thirty seconds. I clear the line, you drop the basket clean, I'm in it. Stand by."

And it's even true, the useful kind of true, every word of it load-bearing and the lie nowhere a man could point to it — the lie is only in the place he won't look, the place under the water where the air won't last for the return and Cole knows it and lets himself not finish knowing it.

He fills his lungs in the foul thin air, all the way, the last good breath the box has to give, and the air is bad and copper and used and it doesn't matter now, and he goes down into the black water in the throat of the box, into the cold that's been waiting for him for twenty-four hours, into the part of the boat that's already drowned.

---

The cold takes him the way it takes everything, like a hand closing, like it's been leaning on the door.

He goes down the slope on the feel of it, head-first into the throat, his hands out ahead reading the dark — the bent lip of the cut panel, the slumped gear, the slick weeping steel that used to be a wall and is a floor and is nothing now, just the inside of a closing thing. The water's deeper than he's ever been in the box and getting deeper while he's in it. He can feel the hull working around him, the long iron sigh of it coming up through the water into his chest, the boat laying herself down the last degree, the wedge of air shrinking above and behind him to a crack, to a smear, to gone.

His hand finds the line.

It's the haul-line, half-inch, hard under load, run up through the cut to the davit and dragging across the bent steel of the panel where the slumped gear has pinched it. It's fouled exactly the way he heard it — a turn of it dragged down and around a crab-pot rail that's slid into the throat, the same gear, some part of him notes, the same un-dumped gear, the pots that should have gone over the rail a day ago and didn't because he didn't trip the release, the pots that fouled the EPIRB and the pots that fouled this, the whole boat strung up on the one thing he wouldn't let go of, top to bottom, the deck-load that's been killing them since before the roll. His own sin laid out under his hands in the dark like a wiring diagram. He'd laugh if he had the air.

He works it. The useful hands, in the cold, in the black, working the line off the snag the way they've worked everything all night — gripping, freeing, the cold already turning his fingers slow, the slow he knows, the ten-minute clock that's really three minutes for hands this cold this deep, and he doesn't have three minutes of air anyway, so it doesn't matter, the arithmetic comes out the same from every direction now and he's grateful for that, he's been doing arithmetic all night and here at last is a sum that won't argue with him.

A turn off the rail. Two. The line jumps in his grip as the load comes onto it, fairer now, running cleaner. Almost.
