One more turn, dragged down under the bent steel, and his fingers find it and work it, slow, slower, the cold up to his wrists now the way it was up to her wrists in the first minute of the first hour, the body shunting the edges away, deciding the hands are expendable, and Cole lets it decide, lets the hands be expendable, they were only ever good for two things and one of them was saving lives in a bad night and the other was holding a course he should have turned off of, and they're doing the first one now, here at the end, freeing the line that lifts the last man, and he holds onto that, he leans his whole cold weight on that the way he's leaned it on the steel all night.

The line comes free in his grip.

He feels it go — the last turn slips the rail and the haul-line runs clean up through the cut, hard and true, the load coming smooth onto the davit, and somewhere up in the rotor-light above the water the basket starts to rise, and Cole, with his head under the drowned throat of the box and the last of the air a memory in his chest and the cold up past his wrists into the place where the hands stop being hands, feels the line go taut and clean and lift, and knows that it lifts. The extraction holds. The last man goes up.

Except the last man is him, and he is down here, and he is not going up.

He has air for the surface. He knows that with the cold flat certainty Ren would know it with — there's a sip of it left in him, enough for the kick back up the slope into the smear of bad air that's still maybe a box, enough to put his face into the last crack of it and breathe the copper poison one more time and be in the basket on the next cycle, the cycle the swimmer said there wasn't, and maybe there is and maybe there isn't, and maybe the bird's gone bingo while he was under and maybe it hasn't, and a man could go up and find out.

He doesn't go up.

He hangs in the cold black water with the freed line running true above him and the boat laying down the last of the way around him, and he feels the old comfort settle over him in the cold, the warmest thing he's felt all night, the fatal one, the one he's leaned on his whole life — *he had done a lot of good in a bad night. That was a thing worth remembering about a man.* He freed the line. He got Sal out and Ren out and he freed the line that lifts them clear, the most useful hands in a drowning room right down to the last turn off the rail, and a man who did all that, a man whose hands did all that, the ledger on a man like that —

He doesn't finish it. He's careful, even here, even now, even with no one left to manage and nothing left to protect and the water taking the light. He lets the comfort have him instead of the sentence. The comfort is enough. It's always been enough.

The line runs true above him, lifting.

And Cole stops reaching for the surface.


---


# Chapter Twenty-Five

## Up Where It Belongs

*POV: Ren — T+25:00*

The basket comes up out of the breach and the world turns right-side up so fast it makes her sick.

For twenty-five hours down has been the sky. She has soldered that into herself, rebuilt it every time the cold tried to take her wits — *down is the sky, up is the bilge, don't reach the wrong way* — and now the cage swings free of the hull and the rotor light hits her face and gravity stands up out of its grave and points where it's supposed to point, and her whole body lurches against the change like a passenger thrown by a stop she didn't see coming. She grabs the rails. Her hands close late, a half-second behind the order, the way they've been closing late for hours. She makes the count by feel because she can't trust her eyes in the wash. Four fingers on the left rail. Three that answer on the right. The thumb's gone somewhere she can't find.

The cage spins. Below her the sea is a black floor lit grey by the helo and torn white where the rotor downwash flattens it, and in the middle of it is the long dark back of the *Adeline Marsh*, what's left of her above water, keel-up and lower than she was an hour ago and lower than that even while Ren watches, a whale sounding slow, and Ren is being lifted off the back of a thing that is going under, and somewhere inside it a man is not coming up.

She knows it the way she knows a flat line. Not as news. As arithmetic already done.

The wire jerks her higher. The cold that lives in open Aleutian air at this hour comes through the wet of her and finds the places the water hadn't bothered with yet — the back of her neck, the strip of scalp where her hair's cropped, the soaked seam of her collar — and it is a different cold than the box's cold, faster, meaner, a cold with a wind behind it, and her chest does the thing she's been fighting since the first second of all this, the gulp reflex, the cold-shock gasp that drowns more people than the sea does. She rides it down. *Not now. You did not survive the water to drown on dry air.* She breathes through her teeth, thin, controlled, a paramedic's breath, the one she's taught to a hundred panicking strangers in another life. The wind takes it apart as fast as she makes it.

Above her the open belly of the helo is a square of yellow light and a man's head and shoulders black against it, leaning out, hands working the hoist, and his mouth is moving and there is no chance on earth she can hear him. The rotors are the whole world. They are inside her skull. She watches his hand instead — a flat palm, *steady, steady,* then a curl of fingers, *come on, come to me* — and she answers the only way that matters, which is to keep her grip and not fight the wire and let herself be a weight that behaves.

The flat hard shape is still against her ribs.

She checks it the way she's checked it for fourteen hours, with the inside of her arm, pressing her elbow to her side, and feels the corner of Dee's tablet in its bag dig into the soft place under her short ribs, sealed, dead-cold, dry inside its dry-bag inside her oilskin, the one thing she carried up out of the engine flat and never put down. Her arm clamps it without her deciding to. Some animal part of her has decided the tablet is a vital sign and won't let go of it any more than it would let go of a pulse.

The cage swings hard once on the wire and the door swings with it, the whole open square of yellow light wheeling, and for the length of a breath she is hanging over nothing again, the way she hung over nothing in the first forty seconds of all this, a body in a barrel going over a falls — and then the hoistman times the swing the way you time a wave and gives the wire a haul on the in-stroke and the cage comes to the door instead of past it. She feels how much skill is in that, the same skill the boat ran on under the arithmetic: a man reading a thing that won't hold still and timing his pull to its rhythm instead of his own. Then his hands reach down and her shoulders reach the lip of the door and there are other hands, gloved, certain, fisting her harness and her collar and hauling, and her knee bangs the door-sill and she can't feel which knee, and the helo's floor comes up under her and takes her weight and it does not move, it does not slosh, it does not tip a degree further into the only position the water will allow — it is a floor, an actual floor, level and loud and bolted to something that flies, and the wrongness of a thing being level after a day of nothing being level is so total that for a second she can't make her body trust it. She lies on a floor that holds still and her hands keep bracing against a roll that doesn't come.

"You're aboard. You're aboard, you hear me, you're up, you're safe—"

The hoistman, close to her ear now, shouting it into the side of her head where she can almost hear it under the rotors, and the word *safe* goes through her like cold water, because safe is a word for people who don't know what she knows, and she is not going to be safe again, she understands this lying on the good level floor, she has carried up out of the dark a thing that ends safe for everyone it touches.

She gets her hands under her. They tell her *no* and she does it anyway.

---

Sal is across the cabin floor strapped into a litter, and even before she's all the way up she's reading him, the reflex firing before the rest of her catches up, the eye going down the body the way it always goes — airway, color, the leg, the leg, the leg.

The leg is wrong.

She knew it would be. She splinted it herself, hours and a lifetime ago, gaff handles and cut line and a pad of neoprene cut off a dead suit, with Cole's hands pulling the counter-traction because his were the only hands strong enough — and she has watched the cold do to that leg over the day exactly what cold does, which is nothing kind. Now there's a corpsman crouched over it, a young Coast Guard rescue tech with his sleeves shoved up and a trauma shears in his fist, and he's got the wet sleeve of Sal's pant split to the hip and his gloved fingers walking the swelling, and his face when he glances up at the leg and then away from it is a face Ren has worn. It's the face you make when you've already added a column nobody's asked you to add yet.

She gets across the floor on her knees because her feet won't do it. The cabin is a bright clattering box — a real box, she thinks, a box that flies, full of webbing and red bags and the smell of jet fuel and the heat blasting from somewhere that her skin can feel and her core can't, not yet, the warmth standing on the outside of her like a coat she can't get into — and she crawls through it to Sal and gets a hand on his jaw and turns his face to her.

His eyes find her. They're slow, but they find her.

"Ren." His mouth makes it. The rotors eat the sound but she's reading lips now, she's been reading lips for an hour. "Ren. You're up." And then the next thing, the thing she watches form on his cracked mouth and would give a great deal not to read, his head moving a half-inch side to side, his eyes going past her to the open door and the wire and the black water below it. *Where's—*

She doesn't let him finish it. She puts two fingers on his lips, which is not a medical act, which is the most unmedical thing she's done in twenty-five hours, and she holds his question shut and shakes her head once, small, *not now, don't,* and Sal — Sal who has known Cole twenty years, Sal who heard the engine note hold after the gust, Sal who signed his own silent contract in the dark of the box and hates the math of it the way she hates hers — Sal closes his eyes and lets the question go back down. A tear runs sideways off the bridge of his nose, pulled flat by the cabin wind. He knows. He's known since the basket came up with only her in it. The two of them are going to be the people who know, and the knowing has already started its work, dividing them off from the bright loud living cabin, two cold people in a warm room.

The corpsman is shouting at her. She catches the back half. "—you hurt? Ma'am, are you injured, where are you hurt—" and she shakes her head, *no,* which is a lie of the useful kind, and she gets her mouth close to his ear.

"His leg. Compartment's been building for hours — eighteen, twenty — closed mid-shaft femur, the cold's slowed the bleed but the pressure's up, you palpate that thigh it's a drum. Pulse below it?"

The corpsman blinks at her. A deckhand in a torn survival suit reciting him a trauma handover in the language he speaks. "I — pedal pulse is thready—"

"It's been thready six hours. Don't release that splint to look at it, you'll lose your tamponade and he'll bleed into the cabin. Mark the time you can't feel a pulse and tell the surgeon you marked it. He's going to lose the leg." She says it flat, the way you say a thing that's already true, and she watches the young man flinch from it, and she has no warmth to spare him for the flinch. "He's going to lose the leg and he's going to live, and those are the only two facts that matter, so let's keep the second one."

The corpsman nods. Something in him reorganizes around her voice the way rooms have been reorganizing around her voice all day, and she feels it happen and is too tired to want it. She does not want to be the one who knows. She has wanted, for six years, since London, to do the work without holding the verdict, and here she is again with both hands full of verdict.

---

There's a thing left to do, the last hull job, the one her hands won't want.

Sal's airway. The litter's at a bad angle for the flight and Sal's head has lolled and his tongue's gone back the way a half-conscious tongue does, and the corpsman's busy at the leg, and the hoistman's gone back to the door and the wire because there's another body to come up — *Cole,* her mind says, and corrects, brutal, *no, not Cole, there is no other body to come up, the wire is going down for nobody* — and so Sal's airway is hers, the way everything's been hers.

She braces a boot against a litter strut and gets behind his head, and the cabin pitches in a gust the way the cabin does and she rides it without thinking, deck-legs, the one thing the cold hasn't taken yet. She has done this ten thousand times. Two fingers under the angle of the jaw, lift, draw the chin forward, open the throat. A first-day skill. The most basic useful thing one human can do for another, the gift of an open airway, and her hands will not do it.

She watches them try. The left one — the burn-scarred one, the one she never explains, the one that's been a hook since hour twelve — sets two fingers under Sal's jaw and they're the wrong fingers, they're cold meat, they don't grade their pressure, they're either nothing or too much, and she can't feel the bone she's lifting against. The right is a half-second better and a half-second is the difference between an airway and a face you've shoved. She makes the lift by watching, not by feeling — she lifts until Sal's chest, which she can see, starts to fill easier, and then she stops, eyes on the rise and fall, doing by sight what the hands used to do by touch, and it works, his chest deepens, the slur of his breathing smooths, and she holds the jaw in that position with hands she cannot feel and understands that this is what's left of her, this is the medic she's going to be from now on: a woman who keeps an airway open by looking at it because the hands have stopped reporting in.

*The body shunts the edges away first.* She taught that to greenhorns in a heated London classroom with a model arm. *The wrists, then the fingers, then the slowing.* She knew the math of cold the way she knew the math of blood loss. She did not know, in the warm classroom with the model arm, that the shunt doesn't always switch all the way back. That some of what the cold takes to save your heart, the heart keeps. That you can come up out of the water with your life and find the cold has decided to stay in your left hand and live there, a tenant the body let in to survive the night and now can't evict.

She holds Sal's airway by sight and watches the door where no second basket is coming, and below the helo the back of the *Adeline Marsh* slides under without any fuss at all, the last of her air letting go in one long exhale that Ren can't hear over the rotors but can feel, somehow, in the soles of her feet through the bolted floor, the same long iron sigh that started all of this, the hull deciding which way was up one final time and choosing the bottom — and then the sea where the boat was is just sea, grey-black and indifferent and closing over the place like a mouth, and the box that held her for a day and a night and most of another day is gone, the ledger run out, float zero, and the man who stayed in it is part of the seabed now, and Ren keeps her dead hand under Sal's jaw and counts the breaths she can see.

One head. Two, with Sal. The count she carries up is two.

It's the wrong number. There should be a third breath in this cabin, slow and even and managing itself, the metronome breath she clocked in the first dark minute and filed as a strange vital sign — *somebody's not panicking* — and went on not understanding for twenty-five hours. That breath is underwater now, where it put itself. She listens for it out of habit and gets rotors and Sal and her own ragged saw and the empty space where the even one used to be, and she thinks, with the flat cruelty that is the only honesty she has left: *he stopped managing his breathing at last. It only took drowning.*

---

The flight is long and loud and she spends it on her knees holding a jaw she can't feel, and somewhere in it the heat finally finds a way past the cold standing guard on her skin and starts, in tiny vicious increments, to reach her core, and the rewarming hurts the way it's supposed to hurt — afterdrop, the cold blood from the limbs coming home to the heart, the deep ache and the shudder that isn't a shiver but the body's whole frame trying to come back online at once. She knows every step of it. Knowing it doesn't make it cost less. She shakes against the bolted floor and keeps the airway and bears it and thinks of nothing, which is the only mercy on offer, the loud rotor nothing that lets her be a pair of hands and not a person who knows things.

Then the man comes back from the door and crouches beside her, and she knows him by the cut of him before she sees his face. The swimmer. Hartline. The one who came up the flooded throat into the wedge with the first outside light the box had seen in half a day, the green-white lamp that painted the strobe and the cold faces and the rising water; the one who said *two now* and made the math a guillotine; the one who held her in the basket when she fought to climb back down. He's out of the water now, wrapped in something, but the cold's on him too, she can see it — the colorless lips, the shake he's working to hide, a man who spent his own ten-minute clock keeping them alive and is paying for it. They spent the same currency tonight. She feels a kinship with him she doesn't want, the kinship of people who did the arithmetic out loud and made the cut.

He gets his head next to hers, under the rotors, into the small space where words can pass.

"You okay?" he says. "You're okay. We got you. We got all of you we could get."

*All of you we could get.* She hears the seam in it, the place where his own arithmetic snagged, and she knows that seam, she's lived in it for six years. *I got the two I could reach. The third one I couldn't.* He's carrying his own cut out of this hull the way she carried Marcus out of London. She wants to tell him it doesn't get lighter and she doesn't, because that's not a thing you tell a man on the worst flight of his life, that's a thing you let him find out, the way they let her find out.

He looks at Sal, at the leg, at the corpsman, and back at her, and she watches him decide to give her the thing he thinks she needs, the thing every report needs, the warm true-enough thing that lets a room stand up under what just happened to it. She watches it form on his exhausted face the way she watched Sal's question form, and she can no more stop it than she could stop the tide.

"Your skipper," Hartline says, and his voice does the thing voices do around the word, drops and steadies, the flat reverent register people use for the dead they're about to make a story of. "I want you to know. He's the reason there's any of you. He talked me into the hull — nobody finds that pocket without him, nobody. And that line at the end, the haul-line fouled, the basket would've hung—" the swimmer's jaw works; he believes every word, she can see he believes it, it has the awful weight of a true thing. "He went back down and cleared it himself. With the box going under. He freed the line that lifted you. Last man off his boat." He puts a cold hand on her shoulder, a benediction, the start of the official truth being written across her in the warm bright cabin while the hull settles on the seabed below. "He was a hero, ma'am. You came out because of him. You hold onto that."

Ren says nothing.

She holds Sal's jaw open with a hand she cannot feel, and she feels the swimmer's story settle over the cabin like the heat she still can't get into — soft, total, indistinguishable from comfort — and she understands, kneeling in the loud warm light with the truth dry against her ribs and her left hand a stranger's where it lifts the bone, that the hull was the easy problem, and that the hard one is only now, in the warm, beginning.


---


# Chapter Twenty-Six

## The Water Over the Report

*POV: Cole — T+25:20 (concurrent with Ch 25)*

The line comes free in his grip, and above him, faint and silver through forty feet of black going green, the basket starts to rise.

That's the job done. He feels it the way he's felt every job done in a long life of them — a small click in the chest, the satisfaction of a thing that was fouled now running clear. The haul-line had wrapped a turn and a half around a crab-pot rail that slumped out of the dark when the hull laid down its last degree, and a wrapped haul-line is a dead basket, and a dead basket is Sal hanging in the cut with the water climbing his litter and the bird burning fuel it doesn't have. So Cole went down to it. One more job that only the diver left in the box could do. He found the rail by feel, found the wrap by feel, walked his fingers along the line to the bight and lifted it off the steel the way you free any fouled line, patient, no jerking, let the strain do the work — and it slipped, and now the line is straight and singing up through the murk and the basket lifts with Ren in it, going up where it belongs.

He could go up after it.

There is, even now, a little air. Not in the box — the box is gone, the wedge folded to a smear and then to nothing while he was working, the last of it gone out the cut in a long burp he heard more than felt — but in him. He came down on a breath and he hasn't spent all of it. A man with his lungs has a margin even at the bottom of a thing like this, and the surface is twenty-some feet of black water away, straight up, where the strobe still pulses in Ren's fist somewhere in the cage going up, a small white heartbeat climbing out of the dark, and all he'd have to do is kick.

He doesn't kick.

He notices that he doesn't, the way you notice a tool you've set down. His legs hang in the cold. The cold is up past his wrists now and into the place where the hands stop being hands — that's Ren's phrase, he heard her use it on the kid, the wrists then the fingers then the slowing, the math of cold she runs on everybody like a tide table — and his hands have stopped reporting in, and he lets them stop. He holds the freed line a moment longer than he needs to, feeling the tremble of the basket's weight come down it from above, the small live vibration of people being saved, and then he opens his fingers, which he can't feel opening, and lets even that go.

There's nobody to manage down here. That's the strange part. He keeps reaching for the next thing to handle — the next voice to steady, the next pair of hands to put a job in, the next sentence to lay over the room like a tarp over a deck-load — and there's no room. There's no deck-load. There's just him and the cold and the green going black, and for the first time in a day and a night and most of another day there is nobody whose face he has to read, nobody to keep busy so they don't ask where the chart table went, no witness left to be the steady one for, and the quiet of that is so total it's almost a kind of warmth.

He always was the steady one. People will say so.

---

Let himself, then. Just here. Just this once, with the surface a thing he's decided against and no audience but the water, let himself walk the deck he's been not-walking for twenty-five hours.

It started long before the gust. That's the part he never lets himself put first, because if you put it first the rest follows like pots off a rail, and a man can't work a bad night with all of it laid out. He puts it first now. The *Sara Lynn* — his own boat, his name on her, his life's whole climb finished and floating in a slip in Anacortes with a balloon payment on her that came due in a season that didn't come in. Money is a quiet thing until it isn't. He'd carried it all winter like a stone in the boot, that number, and the *Adeline Marsh*'s berth had been the only soft place to set the foot down: relief skipper, decent share, Hale's boat, Hale who made him, Hale who'd ask no questions because Hale was running her heavy too and they both knew it and neither of them said it because saying it was the thing you couldn't take back.

He co-signed her stability paper. A favor. A signature for an old man who taught you everything, what's a signature between men like that. And then all season the pots went on the deck stacked a tier high, a tier and a half on the good days, the center of her riding up and up where you could feel it in the way she came back lazy off a sea, a half-beat slow, a boat carrying her weight too tall — and every time he felt it he told himself *she takes it, she's always taken it, twenty years she's taken it,* which is the oldest lie the sea ever sold a man, the lie that what held yesterday is a promise about tonight.

He sees it plain now. Down here where there's nothing to do with the seeing. The cold has worked up into his forearms while he's been walking the deck, the long muscles going slow and stupid the way the hands already went, and he notes it the way you note the tide turning — not alarm, just the water coming on at its own rate, the body paying out its line.

The gust came up off the island the way they do — a williwaw, a fist of wind dropped straight down off the cold high ground and out across the water flat and sudden, no slope to it, no warning in the glass, just the radio chatter from the boats inshore saying *it's blowing the chop off the tops up here* and then the *Adeline Marsh*'s own anemometer climbing in a way that wasn't weather building, it was weather arriving. He'd had Ren off the bridge an hour by then. He puts that next, because it goes next, because the order is the truth and he's done lying about the order.

He sent her below.

He's told himself the warm version so many times it has the smooth worn feel of a thing handled too much — *she was froze through, she was green-handed in a building sea, any skipper'd send a cold deckhand down to thaw, it was a kindness, it was the right call at the time* — and every word of it is true and not one word of it is why. The why is meaner and smaller and he knows the shape of it the way you know a tooth with your tongue. Ren keeps things. She counts. She's the kind of crew who'd glance at the load and glance at the heading and the throttle and file it, file it the way she files everything, in that flat counting way of hers, and a man running a boat over her marks in a building sea does not want a clear-eyed counter on the bridge logging how heavy and how late. He wanted no second pair of eyes. He told her she was cold. He put his hand on her shoulder and he was even gentle about it, and the gentleness was the cover and he half-knew it was the cover even while his hand was warm on her shoulder, and that — that's the one that won't wash off, the tenderness with the calculation living inside it like a hook inside bait.

He removed the witness, and then he held the course.

---

That's the act. The held course. Not a thing he did — a thing he didn't do, which is worse, because a thing you didn't do has no moment you can point to, no lever you pulled, no smoking deed. Just a deck-load release sitting there under his hand, the thing you trip to dump the pots and let the boat stand back up, and a throttle up to make the next pot-line before the weather closed, and a choice that was no choice because choosing meant admitting.

If he'd dumped the deck the boat would have lived. He knows that the way Ren knows a flat line — not as news, as arithmetic long done. Dump the rail, she sheds the topside weight, the center comes down, she takes the knockdown and stands up cursing and everybody goes home cold. But you cannot dump a deck-load you weren't supposed to be carrying without every man on the water and the Coast Guard after and the board after that asking the one question — *how'd you come to be stacked that high, Cole, who signed off on her trim* — and the answer to that question was his own name on a paper, and the *Sara Lynn* in her slip, and the whole climb of a life undone in a hearing room. So his hand stayed off the release. He held her into it loaded, throttle up, betting she'd take it the way she'd always taken it. The high pots caught the gust like a sail. She went past her righting arm — he felt the exact moment, the half-beat where she should have come back and didn't, the long horrible lean past every angle he had a name for — and she rolled.

Forty seconds.

He's run those forty seconds backward a thousand times in the dark of the box, looking for the place where it wasn't his, and there is no place. The squall was real and the squall was a coin-flip and a hundred good skippers might have called it his way and ridden it and made the pot-line and bought the drinks — but he knows, down here, with the lie gone quiet, that he didn't hold the course because it was a defensible call in a hard sea. He held it because letting go of the deck-load meant letting go of the *Sara Lynn*, and he could not make his hand do it. The release that wasn't tripped. A small fast deniable nothing of a non-act, and Hale died in his bunk and Dee died at her station and Pavel under the pots and a green boy of warmth he didn't have and a dog and now the boat herself, all of it hanging off the one bight of line he wouldn't lift off the rail.

He sees it. He doesn't reach for the warm version. There's no version of it down here that he could use, and he was always a man who reached for the version he could use, and there's nothing to do with this one but hold it, the way the water's holding him, and it's heavier than the water.

---

So. The report.

He'd known, the second the Herc droned over and marked them and the *sixty* came in behind it, that rescue was the start of the report and not the end of the night. Everyone else in the box heard an aircraft and heard *saved.* He heard an aircraft and heard *a room with a long table in it and his name at the top of a document and a slow procedural voice asking him to walk them through the loading, the heading, the time of the gust warning, the actuation of the deck-load release* — and he'd started, right then, gentle and warm in the swimmer's lamp, laying the tarp over it. *Weather rolled us, fast, nothing to be done.* He'd said it to Hartline, the one official ear on scene, said it in front of Ren, and Ren had handed it back to him flat, with no weight under it — *Nothing to be done* — not selling it, not killing it, just setting it down between them where they could both look at it, and in that flat handoff he'd understood she knew. He'd suspected since the second dive. He'd been almost sure when she came up spitting seawater and told him *nothing, couldn't reach it, too fouled* in a voice that tasted of the comfortable lies he tells, the lie of a person who has just decided to triage the truth.

And then in the breach, hauling her out of the surge with the mesh on her wrist, his hand had found the flat hard sealed thing under her oilskin and he'd known it cold and complete: she came up from the chart locker with something. She's had it against her ribs all night. The boat didn't bury her own secrets after all — Ren dug one up and carried it through the whole long dark, keeping a guilty man's leg in traction with one hand and his ruin pressed to her side with the other. Dee's tablet, most like. The deck-load figure and the heading and the throttle and the timestamp and the release that never fired, all of it logged in a brick of waterproof memory that the cold can't rot and the sea can't drown and a man's warm voice cannot manage, because you cannot read a face on a machine, and a machine does not need to lean on you, and need was the only court that ever acquitted anybody and the tablet has no need.

His heart is doing something he can hear now, a slow heavy knock up under the breastbone, too far apart, each one landing like a fist on a hull from a long way off. He counts two of them in the time one used to take. That's the cold getting down into the pump itself, down past the fingers and the forearms into the deep machinery, and he knows what it means the way Ren would know it, and he doesn't mind the knowing.

He couldn't out-useful the tablet.

That was the thing he finally understood with the water at his chest and the basket coming for one more — that the whole long beautiful machine of it, the dives and the splint and the warmed boy and the talked-in swimmer, all the good his hands had done in a bad night, the ledger he'd been so sure he was balancing — none of it touched the tablet. He'd believed, his whole life he'd believed, that if your hands save enough the held course is forgiven, that being needed and being good were the same coin spent two ways. He'd watched a room full of dying people lean on him and felt the leaning as a kind of absolution, every body that breathed because of him a small clean entry against the big dirty one. He'd been wrong the way you're wrong about a thing you needed too badly to test. A drowning room doesn't audit its rescuers — but a board does, and a tablet does, and a flat-voiced woman with a verdict in her arms does.

So there was one card left. He saw it form in the rising black. There was only ever the one.

---

If he goes up, he goes up into the report. Into the table and the slow voice and Dee's tablet sliding across it to indict him point by point, the deck-load over her marks, the course held after the warning, the release un-actuated, his name on the trim paper — all of it, every link, no warm version that survives a timestamp. And Hale's widow in the back of the room learning what her husband's protégé did to her husband's boat. And the *Sara Lynn* gone anyway, sold off the dock to pay the lawyers, the climb undone exactly the way he'd held the course to keep it from being undone, so that the held course bought him nothing in the end but a few more hours and six dead. A man could not stand up under that. He'd spent his last reserve of management already and there was none left over for that table.

But if he goes *down.*

If the box takes him — the skipper who talked the swimmer in, who freed the fouled line with the hull going under, last man off his boat, the captain who stayed — then there's no man at the table. There's a hero and a tragedy and a clean grief and a name his sister and his nieces ashore can say at the back of the church without flinching. Hale's widow gets her husband's boat rolled by weather and her husband's good young skipper drowned trying to save the crew, which is a thing you can live next to. The board has a casualty and not a culprit. And the tablet — the tablet's just data then, isn't it, just a machine's cold record with no living mouth to give it weight, and who reads a dead man's overload off a brick of memory and calls it murder when the man drowned saving the survivors? You don't speak ill of the man who freed the line that lifted you. It would be obscene. The story closes itself over the data the way the water's closing over him, soft and total, and the truth lies down on the seabed in a steel box where it belongs.

And Ren.

This is the last management. He'd be lying if he called it anything else, and he's done lying down here, so: this is the last room he handles, and he handles it from the bottom of the sea, and it's the cruelest thing he's ever done and he does it on purpose with what's left of the breath in his chest.

Ren came up out of the box owing nobody but herself a truth — and carrying her own. He saw her tag the boy. He doesn't know the why of it, whatever it is she carries that he was never let near; he only knows what he saw, which was a medic put two fingers to a kid's throat and let her face go to the place a skipper's face goes when he calls a man overboard lost, and stop working, and turn to the water. He'd trusted the call because she was the medical one the way they'd all trusted his because he was the steady one, and the boy died, and then the boy's little machine surfaced still pinging, and Cole had watched something break behind Ren's eyes that he recognized, because he'd been breaking the same way for twenty-five hours: the look of a person who's just learned the body had margin they didn't spend.

Her hands aren't clean either. That's the chain, and he's holding it, and by drowning he draws it tight and walks away and leaves her holding both ends.

Because now she has to choose. She can let him be the hero — and that's the easy door, the kind door, the door that protects the kid's family and Hale's widow and his nieces and, not least, *her,* because if nobody examines his hands then nobody examines hers, and the boy who died on her watch stays a tragedy of the cold and not a column in a report with her name above it. He's made silence the merciful choice. He's made it the *clean* one. The dead don't need the truth, he tells himself — Hale doesn't need it, Pavel doesn't, the boy doesn't — and the living need it even less, and Ren most of all, because the truth costs her too. He's drowning to give her the mercy of not having to. He's almost sure that's why he does it. The justification slides up warm out of long habit, the smooth worn shape of it — *you're doing her a kindness, Cole, you're taking it off her, the last useful thing, one more thing only you can do* —

— and then it fails him.

It just fails. Down here at the bottom with the cold in the place where the hands stop being hands, the grammar he's run his whole life on, the conditional clauses and the *anybody-would-have* and the *right-call-at-the-time,* the whole warm machinery of around — it runs out of slack like a line paid to its bitter end, and there's nothing on the other side of it but the plain thing, and the plain thing is: he isn't drowning to spare Ren the choice. He's drowning to make the choice for her, the way he made the choice to send her below, the way he made the choice to hold the course — by removing what he can't control. She's the last witness. The water is the last release he won't trip, except this time the deck-load is himself, and he's letting it go over the side to save the boat, and the boat is his name.

He sees it. For one held second, with no version left to reach for, he sees exactly what he is.

And then the cold offers the warm version one more time, the way it does, the way Ren said it does — *the dangerous warmth, the urge to stop* — and he is so tired, and the seeing is so heavy, and the old comfort comes up through him like the false heat through the dying kid: *you did a lot of good in a bad night. Whatever else. That's a thing worth remembering about a man.* It is not true the way he needs it to be true. He knows that now, knows it clean, and he reaches for it anyway, because a man reaches for the version he can use, and it's the last thing in the box, and he takes it down with him the way Ren took the tablet up.

He stops thinking about the report. He stops thinking about the table and the widow and the chain in Ren's two hands. There's a great deal of dark and the strobe is gone up out of it, a white heartbeat climbing away toward a sky that's finally up where it belongs, and his chest has begun to argue with him, the old burn at the bottom of a held breath, the body letting you push past the point where you should turn back, the body's worst kindness — and he doesn't turn back. He keeps the breath because spending it would mean kicking and kicking would mean the surface and the surface would mean the report. He holds it the way he held the course. It is the one consistent thing he's done all his life: held on to the wrong thing because letting go meant admitting.

The burn climbs. The strobe is a memory of a light now, a green ghost behind his eyes. Somewhere far above, the basket lifts a woman with the truth on her body up into the air, and the truth is his now too, finally, completely, the way it was never his on the surface — and he gets to keep it for the length of one drowning, which is the only honest thing he has ever owned.

The cold gives you longer than it feels and less than you hoped. He'd hoped for absolution. He got the water, which was almost the same, and would have to do.

The light goes the way it went the first minute of all of this, and Cole Vantine lets the breath out, and the sea closes over the place like a mouth, and the last steady breath in the *Adeline Marsh* stops managing itself at last.


---


# Chapter Twenty-Seven

## The Cold That Stays

*POV: Ren — T+25:40 / aboard the cutter*

The helo puts them down on a deck that pitches, and for one bad second Ren reads it as the box again — the floor leaning, the world choosing a side — before she understands it is only a ship, a real ship, the cutter *Sorrel* taking the swell on her quarter the honest way a hull takes a swell when it is right-side up and means to stay there.

By the time they get her inside, off the open deck and through a watertight door her boot does not clear, into the heat and the diesel hum and the fluorescent flatness of the cutter's sick bay, Sal is already on the table and the handover is already done. She gave it on the helo floor on her knees — the femur, the compartment, the hours, the pulse she marked, the leg they were going to lose — gave it to a corpsman who took it in the language she still speaks, and the chief here has it secondhand off the radio already, she can tell by the way he works: no questions, just the ship's own traction rig going on over the lashing she tied, good steady hands that have seen a lot of cold men through this door. He doesn't waste a glance asking her anything. The handover crossed over with the patient. There's nothing left for her to tell him about the leg that he doesn't already have.

What he hands her instead is a job, which is the kindest thing anyone has done for her since the basket. He nods her behind Sal's head without breaking the rhythm of his hands. *Keep me an airway. Can you do that.*

*Can you do that.* She looks down at her hands.

The left one closes a half-second after she tells it to and stops where it wants to, not where she put it. The right is better by a half-second and a half-second is the whole distance between an open airway and a face you've shoved. She gets behind Sal's head anyway, because the chief asked, because the job is there, because the work is the rope you hold when the hands have stopped reporting in. Two fingers under the jaw, lift, draw the chin — by sight, the way she learned it on the helo floor, the feel gone, the bone under her fingertips a rumor, reading his chest fill off his ribs because she cannot read it off her fingers anymore. The slur goes out of his breath. She holds the jaw exactly there and does not look away, because the second she stops watching, the hands will let it slip and not tell her.

The door dogs shut behind the last of the litter crew and seals the wind out, and the sudden quiet after the rotors is so total it rings. The cutter takes the next swell the honest way. And in the warm bright held-still room, with Sal breathing under hands she cannot trust, the thing Ren has been outrunning since the basket finally catches up to her, which is not the leg and not the cold and not her own ruined fingers. It is the count.

Sal's eyes open while she's holding his jaw. They're slow, but they find her, the way they found her in the cabin.

His mouth moves around the airway she's keeping. *Ren.* And then his eyes go past her — not to the door this time, there's no door he thinks the answer is behind, just up and to the side, the way a man looks when he's checking a count he already knows the answer to — and he stops, and his face does a thing she has to look away from, because it's the same thing her own face must be doing. He's counting heads. He's getting two and the table makes three with the chief and the corpsman makes four and there's a whole bright loud room full of breathing now, more breath than she's heard in a day, and exactly one breath missing from it, the slow even managed one, the metronome she clocked in the first dark minute and filed as a strange vital sign and went on not understanding for twenty-five hours.

"Don't," she tells him, the same word she gave him in the cabin, and Sal closes his eyes and lets the count go back down, and a tear runs flat across the bridge of his nose and gets lost in the seawater already there. He knows. He's known since the basket came up with only her in it. Two cold people in a warm room, dividing off from the living the way the dead divide you off, and neither of them has said a single true word out loud and both of them have said everything.

Somebody peels the cut suit off her shoulders without asking and packs hot blankets against her, and the rewarming starts its slow vicious climb up into her core, the deep ache she knows every step of and that knowing it doesn't make cost less. She keeps the airway and bears it and watches Sal breathe, and somewhere under the ache she registers that the chief has stopped working the leg and is standing very still over it, and she knows that stillness, she has worn that stillness, it's the stillness of a man who has finished adding a column nobody asked him to add.

"It'll come off above the knee," the chief says quietly, to the corpsman, not to her, not to Sal. "When we make Dutch. Tell the surgeon now so they're spun up." He says it the flat way you say a thing that's already true, and Ren says nothing. She said the same figure an hour ago on the helo floor and being right about it bought her exactly nothing, the way being right about all of it has bought her exactly nothing.

She holds the jaw. She watches the chest. The leg is the easy arithmetic — it has only the two facts in it, lose the limb and keep the man, and you keep the man. The hard sum is the one nobody on this ship has started yet, and she is the only one aboard who can do it, and she has carried it up out of the water dry against her ribs.

---

The story starts setting up in the warm room while she kneels there, and she watches it happen the way she watched the tide come up the weld seam — slow, patient, nobody choosing it, the level simply rising.

It comes in through the door with the swimmer.

Hartline's been seen to — dry coveralls, a mug somebody's wrapped his hands around, the colorless lips coming back to a human color — but the cold he spent is still on him, the small managed shake of a man who pushed his own ten-minute clock past where it wanted to go. He's giving his account to a lieutenant with a clipboard, low, professional, the way you debrief while it's fresh, and Ren is not trying to hear it and hears it anyway, the small bright room carrying every word.

"—made entry up the flooded companionway, the skipper talked me through the geometry, I'd never have found the pocket without him, it's inverted in there, nothing's where you'd—" The clipboard makes a note. "Three living when I got in. Cook with the femur, the deckhand here, and the skipper. I took the cook first, litter case, then her—" a tip of the mug toward Ren without his eyes leaving the lieutenant— "and the skipper stayed to work the haul-line when it fouled. Cleared it himself with the box going under. That's the only reason the second hoist made. Last man off." The shake in his hands when he lifts the mug. "He went back down for us. You can put that in plain."

*You can put that in plain.* The lieutenant puts it in plain. The pen moves and the words go onto the clipboard and the clipboard goes into a file and the file goes up the chain, and Ren kneels there holding a dead-handed grip on a stranger's jaw and watches the comfortable version of the night become, in real time, in good faith, by people who would never lie, the institutional truth. Nobody in this room is lying. That's the thing nobody warned her about, in London or here — that the lie doesn't need liars. It only needs a roomful of decent tired people who want the dead to have died for something, and a man who knew that about people, who built his whole drowning around knowing it. Cole isn't here to tell it. He doesn't have to be. He set it going and stepped off the side and it tells itself, soft and total, the way the water closed over the place he was, and every honest mouth in the room is doing his work for him without knowing whose work it is.

She could let it. The thought arrives clean and reasonable and she does not flinch from it, because she has stopped, somewhere in the last hour, being able to lie to herself about her own arithmetic.

She could let it stand. Cole is dead — he can't be punished, only remembered, and the version on the clipboard is a kind version, a version his sister and his nieces ashore can say at the back of a church without their faces coming apart. Hale Ferro's widow gets a husband rolled by weather and a good young skipper drowned trying to save the crew, which is a thing a person can build a life next to. Sal gets to keep his twenty years of Cole clean. And Ren — Ren gets the one thing she has wanted for six years, which is to do the work and not hold the verdict, to set the column down and walk away from the sum. If nobody examines Cole's hands, then nobody examines hers. The boy who died on her watch stays a tragedy of the cold and not a line in a report with her name above it. *He had margin and I tagged him and went to the leg.* If she says nothing, that sentence dies in her and nowhere else. Cole made silence the kind choice. He made it, she sees now, the *clean* one — he did it on purpose, with the last breath in his chest, the cruelest gift she's ever been handed: a door marked *mercy* that is really marked *complicity*, and no way to tell from the inside which side of it she'd be standing on.

It would be so easy. That's the part that frightens her. Not that the lie is hard to tell but that it isn't, that it's already told, that all she has to do to tell it is keep holding this jaw and keep her mouth shut and let the warm room do the rest.

The flat hard shape is still against her ribs.

---

She feels for it the way she's felt for it for fifteen hours, the inside of her arm pressing her elbow to her side, and the corner of Dee's tablet is there in its dry-bag inside the suit they're peeling off her, and the suit is going, the corpsman has it half off her arms, and the bag with it.

"Wait—" Her voice comes up rough. The corpsman has the cut suit balled in one fist and is reaching for the wet gear bag at her feet, the pile of it, the torn neoprene and the stripped buckles and the dead-cold brick in its bag, all of it ruined, all of it the kind of thing you cut off a hypothermic and throw in the gash bin because it's done its job and it's contaminated and there are forty cleaner things to do.

"It's all wet, ma'am, we'll get you dry stuff—"

"Not that." She can't point with the left hand; it won't make the shape. She points with her chin, with her whole face, the way she pointed for Sal. "The bag. The dry-bag in the pile. That one stays with me."

He pauses, the way people pause around her voice. "It's just—"

"It's evidence." The word lands in the bright room harder than she means it to. The lieutenant with the clipboard looks over. Hartline looks over. The chief's hands keep working the leg but his eyes come up. She has said a word that changes the weather in a room and she feels every face turn the new way the weather's blowing. "It's off the engine flat. It's logging gear. It does not go in the bin and it does not leave my sight, and if you need a chain-of-custody on it then start one now and write my name on it, but that bag stays where I can see it."

The corpsman looks at the lieutenant. The lieutenant looks at Ren — a longer look this time, the file getting reopened, *upright, talking, not the worst thing in the room, and now also: this one knows something* — and then he nods, and the corpsman fishes the dry-bag out of the wet ruin and sets it on the deck against the bulkhead where Ren can see it, and somebody finds a ziplock and a marker and the small dumb ritual of custody begins around the one cold object in the room that will not lie. Dee's tablet. Sealed. Dead-cold. Unread — she still can't power it wet, can't open it here, doesn't need to. She knows what's on it the way she knows the leg is gone: not because she's looked but because the arithmetic is already done. Deck-load over the marks. Heading held after the gust. Throttle up. The release that never fired. The timestamps the cold can't rot and the sea can't drown and a warm reverent roomful of decent people can't talk over, because you cannot read a face on a machine, and a machine does not need anyone to lean on it, and need was the only court that ever acquitted anybody.

She keeps one eye on the bag and one on Sal's chest and holds the airway with hands that have stopped being hands, and she understands that she has just done a small irreversible thing. She has put the truth in the room on the record. Not told it — only refused to let it be thrown away. The smallest possible act. A bag on a deck with a name on it. And it is the first thing she has done since the surface that Cole did not plan for, the first place where his drowning didn't reach, the first crack in the soft total telling — because the story on the clipboard has no room in it for a logging brick off the engine flat, and now the brick is in the room, named, witnessed, refusing the bin, and the story is going to have to grow a seam to fit around it.

She has not decided anything. She wants that to be true and makes herself look at whether it's true. She has not decided to speak. She has only declined to let the evidence go in the gash bin, which is the cowardly half of brave, the keeping-the-option-open that costs nothing yet. The deciding is still ahead of her, in some warm dry room without rotors in it, and she already knows the shape of it the way she knew the shape of Sal's leg in the dark — she knew it was gone hours before anyone said amputation; she's known the shape of the deciding since the basket came up with only her in it. She just hasn't said the figure out loud, even to herself. *A bad figure spoken in the dark is a weight, not a tool.* That was hour one. She is not in hour one anymore. The dark is gone and the room is bright and there is no one left in it who needs her to hold the count steady, no roomful of dying strangers to splint the truth for, and that's the thing the warm light makes plain that the dark let her hide: down here in the bright, the lie isn't a rope anymore. There's nothing left to hold over. It's just a lie. It's the comfortable kind, and she has been told the comfortable kind all her life and told it to others all her life, and she is so tired of the weight of it she could lie down on the bolted deck and not get up.

Sal's chest rises and falls under her eyes. The bag sits against the bulkhead with her name on it. The cutter takes the next swell the honest way.

---

A man comes through the watertight door who is not a corpsman and not a swimmer.

She clocks him before he reaches her — the way she clocks everything, the file building itself unasked — and what she files is: dry, warm, rested, no salt on him, a soft jacket over the blue, a man who came to this from a desk and not from the water. He's older, careful in the way he moves around the patient and the working hands, and he waits, which the others didn't, he waits at the edge of the bright room until the chief looks up and gives him the small nod that means *she can be spoken to,* and only then does he come the rest of the way and lower himself to one knee beside her so his voice doesn't have to cross the room, so it stays between the two of them, the courtesy of a man who has done this before and knows how it lands.

"Ms. Okafor." Her name in his mouth, gentle, already knowing it, already written somewhere on a form. "I'm sorry to be near you this soon. I'm not going to keep you." He has a Coast Guard investigator's voice, she'll learn the title later, the marine-casualty office, the people who come to the cutter to meet the survivors before the cold's all the way out of them because the first account is the truest one and they know it; but right now he is just a warm careful voice at her shoulder, the most dangerous and the most welcome thing that's happened to her since the door dogged shut. "We're going to need your account. Not now. When you're warm, when the chief releases you, when you've slept. There's no rush on it tonight." He lets that sit, kind, and then he says the thing he came to say, the small routine sentence that is the whole of his job and the whole of her life from here, and he says it so gently she almost misses how much it weighs. "Just what happened. In your own words. However you remember it."

*In your own words.*

She holds Sal's jaw and watches his chest fill and looks past the investigator's kind face to the bag on the deck, the dead-cold brick with her name on the ziplock, the one thing in the room that has no own-words because it has only the timestamps; and then she looks down at her own left hand where it lies on the table edge, the burn-scarred one, the medic's hand, the useful one, curled half-shut in a grip she didn't fully make and can't fully feel, the cold moved into it for good now, a tenant the body let in to survive the night and can't evict.

*In your own words.* That's the whole question. That's the only question there's ever going to be. Not the tablet — the tablet will say what it says whether she speaks or not, the machine has no own-words to triage. Hers do. She gets to choose which cold she carries up out of this — the one that's already moved into her hand, that one's not up to her, the lease is signed; or the other one, the one in her mouth, the one she can still decide whether to spend.

"Thank you," she says, which is not an answer, which is the most paramedic thing she's said all day, the flat certain non-thing you give a frightened room while you go on holding the airway.

The investigator nods, and stands, and leaves his card in the gear pile by the bag — she sees him do it, sees him choose to set it by the bag and not in her ruined-handed grip, a small read of his own — and goes back out through the watertight door into the wind, and the door seals, and the warm room rings.

Ren holds Sal's airway open with a hand she cannot feel, and watches the bag on the deck, and understands that *in your own words* is the entire weight of it, and that she gets to choose which cold she carries — the one already living in her hand, or the one she has not yet let out of her mouth — and that the first one was the cold's to take, but the second one is hers.


---


# Chapter Twenty-Eight

## What Sal Heard

*POV: Ren — +four days / Anchorage*

They have her in a step-down room on the fourth floor with a window that looks at a parking structure and a heater under the sill that runs all night, and she still cannot get the left hand warm.

The right one came most of the way back. The cold borrowed it and is paying it off in installments — pins and aches, a thumb that overshoots a button by a hair, the gross grip there and the fine grip arriving late — and she clocks its recovery the way she'd chart a man's color coming up off the floor, hour by hour, a thing trending toward the line. The left one is a different patient. The burn-scarred one. The medic's hand. Two fingertips on it went the color of a dead man's lips on the first night and the surgeon used the word *demarcation* the way the cutter's chief used the word *amputation* over Sal's leg, flat, already true before he said it, and what it means is that the cold signed a lease and intends to keep a deposit. Not the whole hand. A fingertip, maybe two. They are watching the line move. She watches it too, because watching is a thing she knows how to do with her eyes when her hands have stopped reporting in.

The hands are wrapped. White mitts of gauze and silver dressing to the wrist, fastened with tape she can't pick at, which is the point. A woman in scrubs comes twice a shift to do the dressings and Ren lies still and lets her, a weight that behaves, and watches her own fingers get unwound and reassessed and wound again, and does not look at the two on the left longer than the nurse does, because she has read that face before from the other side and she knows exactly what file it is building and exactly what that file is worth.

She has not touched the tablet in four days.

It is in the locker by the door, in its evidence bag, with her name on the chain-of-custody tag and a Coast Guard property sticker over that, and a young petty officer signs it out and in when the investigator's office wants eyes on it and signs it back, and the small dumb ritual goes on around the one cold object in the building that will not lie. They have not powered it. They are waiting for a lab in Seattle and a warrant of their own internal kind and the forensic protocol that keeps a dead engineer's last log admissible, and that is fine, that is more than fine, because Ren could not power it if they handed it to her this minute. She has two hands wrapped to the wrist. She could not push the small recessed button on the side, the one she'd have to hold three seconds, the one her thumb went looking for in the dark of the engine flat and found by feel four days and a lifetime ago. The smallest possible physical task — press a button, hold it three seconds, watch a screen come alive — and the cold has taken even that off her, has made the thing she carried up out of the sea something she now has to hand to someone else to open.

She thinks about that more than she means to. That she got it up the basket and onto the helo floor and into the bright loud room and refused to let a corpsman bin it, fought a whole warm institution to keep it on her body — and the last twelve inches of it, the button, the screen, the looking, those are not hers anymore. She gave them to the cold somewhere around hour twelve and the cold is not giving them back. *I carried the truth a mile through black water and froze the hand that could open it.* It is the kind of line that wants to mean something and she makes herself not let it. It only means her thumb doesn't work. The truth on the tablet is the same whether her thumb works or not. That is the whole mercy of a machine: it has no own-words to triage, no hand that fails, no court of need. It will say what it says when a dry warm stranger in Seattle presses the button she can't.

---

Sal is two floors down, in surgery for the third time in four days, and they have run out of leg to take below the knee.

She knew it on the cutter. She said it on the cutter, to a chief who was right behind her, and being right about it has bought her exactly what being right has bought her all the way through this — nothing, a column added correctly to a sum nobody wanted. The femur was the start of it and the cold was the rest. The thigh that went board-tight in the dark, the compartment that filled while she splinted it under traction with Cole's hands as the counterweight, the eighteen, twenty hours of a leg starved of its own blood at thirty-four degrees — there is a point past which a limb is a thing you are keeping warm for the sake of the man it's attached to, and Sal's leg crossed that point somewhere around the second tide, and she knew it, and she held the traction anyway because the traction was keeping the bleed off his deck and the man on his shelf. Useful hands. You can save a body without saving the part. You can do the right thing and have it come out as a thing they cut off you in pieces over four days in a building you didn't choose.

She is in the chair by his bed when they bring him back up from the third one, and she watches them transfer him off the gurney the way she watches everything now, the eye going down him — color, the line in his neck, the cradle where the leg was — and the cradle is shorter than it was this morning, and the rest of him is the gray of a man who has been opened a third time, and she does the arithmetic on his fluids and his output off the bag and the monitor without deciding to, because that is the language she has and the only one that lets her sit here without her own face coming apart.

He surfaces slow. The anesthetic lets a man go the way the cold let Marcus go, reluctant, by stages, and she watches Sal's breathing for the slur the same way she watched it on the cutter, two fingers' width of attention she can't switch off. His chest deepens. The slur goes out of it. His eyes find the ceiling, then the window, then her, and they are slow but they are his, the way they found her in the cabin, the way they found her on the cutter, and his mouth works around a dry tongue.

"You're still here," he says. The voice is wrecked, a galley king's bark sanded down to a whisper, but the deflection is intact, the reflex to make a small joke land in front of the big thing — and then it thins out, the way it thinned out in the hull when the pain and the cold won, and he doesn't finish whatever the joke was going to be.

"Where else." She doesn't move to take his hand. She can't; hers are mitts; and even if they weren't she has learned, in four days, that touch is a thing you owe people and a thing you have stopped being able to pay. She gets a cup of chipped ice off the tray instead, fits it against her wrapped palm, and feeds him a chip the way you'd feed a man too weak to take it himself, the smallest useful thing one human can do for another. He takes it. His eyes close at the cold of it, the good cold, the kind that is only cold.

"They took it above the knee," he says, when he can. Not a question. He says it the flat way the chief said it, the way you say a thing that is already true, and she understands he has been doing the same arithmetic she has, lying here, a man who has cooked forty years and knows exactly what a body does with what you take from it.

"This morning. The knee wouldn't hold. It's clean now. They got ahead of it."

"Clean." He tries the word like a sauce that's gone off. "That's a hell of a thing to call it."

"It's the right thing to call it. You're going to live. You're going to be a pain in the ass to a prosthetist for a year and then you're going to be a pain in the ass on two feet, one of them yours." The gallows reflex, the flat dark thing she reaches for at the worst moment, a paramedic's tic she never grew out of, and it lands on him the way it lands on the worst-off — it gives him something to be besides afraid, a small hard thing to hold.

He almost laughs. It costs him and he pays it. Then the laugh goes out and the room gets very quiet, just the heater and the monitor and the parking structure beyond the glass, and Sal lies looking at the ceiling and Ren feeds him another chip and waits, because she knows this quiet, she has worn this quiet, it is the quiet of a man finishing a column nobody asked him to add.

---

"Ren." Different now. Lower. The deflection gone out of it entirely, the way his breath went even in the dark and she filed it wrong. "I have to tell you a thing and I need you to not be a medic at me while I do it."

She sets the ice down. "Okay."

"He ran her heavy." Sal says it to the ceiling, not to her. His breath is short; the third surgery is still in him, riding under every word, and he spends the air in small lots. "All season. Cole. The deck."

He stops. Breathes. The monitor counts him.

"You came on in December. I cooked that boat all fall, I watched it load. Pots two, three high. Past where Ferro'd have — " A swallow. "Stood for it. Ten years ago."

"Sal — "

"Let me." His hand lifts off the blanket an inch and drops. "While I've got the — while I'm up for it." A breath. "I'm not making myself clean. I ate the same money. But she rode heavy. You feel it in how she comes back up off a sea." His good hand turns over, slow, palm to ceiling — a boat righting. "Slow. Like a fat man out of a low chair. She didn't snap up."

Ren doesn't say anything. She has learned, in four days, that the worst thing you can do to a man telling you the truth is help him.

His eyes close. For a second she thinks the anesthetic has him back. Then.

"The night." His throat works. "I'm in the galley. You'd gone below, he'd sent you. I heard the gust." A breath, longer, dredged. "You don't hear a williwaw. People get that wrong. You don't hear it coming. You hear what the boat does."

The heater ticks. His chest lifts, holds, lets down.

"She heeled. And the engine note — " He stops. His hand, the one she isn't holding, makes a small flat motion in the air, a man turning a throttle that isn't there. The hand is shaking, and not from the cold. "The note held."

He has to rest on it. She watches the number on the monitor and waits.

"We're going over. Deck's under green. And the note — held. He had the wheel and the squall on him and he kept her at the pot-line. Didn't back her down. Didn't turn up into it." Each clause a separate breath now, the sentence rationed out across the cost of saying it. "And he didn't dump the deck. I was — waiting for it. Twenty years you wait for that sound. A skipper dumping the rail when she's past her marks. Loudest thing on a boat."

The pause goes long. The monitor idles, eighty-eight, eighty-seven.

"It never came," Sal says. "She went over loaded. Last thing I heard before the water was that note. Holding course. Like nothing was wrong."

He turns his head on the pillow, slow, and finds her, and his eyes are wet but his voice is dry, scraped flat and certain. "I knew it in the hull. First hour. Before you did." His breath catches and he rides it down. "I heard him breathing easy in the dark while the rest of us were drowning out our own names. That's a man who already knows what happened."

She has stopped breathing herself. She makes herself start again.

"And I said nothing." His hand comes flat down on the blanket where the leg isn't, and his face changes at the contact, at finding the flat place his hand expected a thigh and met a void, and she sees it cost him something separate from the talking, the body filing its own amputation again, fresh, mid-sentence. "Twenty years I've known him. I lay in that water and watched him drive a cleat into a frame and rig this leg" — the hand, on the flat blanket, on the nothing — "in traction with his own hands. Held the counter-pull an hour till his arms shook. And I knew." A breath that has a wreck in it. "I kept my mouth shut. Because his were the only hands that could keep the leg. And I wanted the leg."

The hand presses down on the absence, as if it could still feel for what isn't there.

"I traded the one for the other and I knew I was doing it." His voice doesn't break. That's the worst of it. He has had four days to practice not letting it break. "And they took it anyway. Three times. In pieces. So I sold my silence for a leg that's in a hospital bin, and got to keep the breath, and that's — " He stops. Tries to find air for the last of it and can't, quite, and lets it come out thin. "That's the man you're looking at. They can have the leg. It's just meat. The part I'd give up first if I had the choosing — that's the part I kept, and it's worth less than the leg."

---

She gets up. She doesn't decide to; the body does it, the way it always does the thing the mouth can't. She goes to the window and stands with her wrapped hands held a little away from her sides, useless, and looks at the parking structure and the gray sky over it that is the right way up, sky up, ground down, and the rightness of it is still its own kind of wrong four days on.

"You didn't kill anybody," she says to the glass.

"No." Sal's voice behind her. "He did. And then he saved you out of it with the same hands, and that's the part I can't — " He stops. Starts again, softer. "Come here. I'm not done and I don't want to say the rest of it to your back."

She comes back. She sits.

"You're going to give a report," Sal says. "The man in the soft jacket's been by twice asking after you, they'll have you in a room with a recorder inside the week. And you've got Dee's box in that locker by the door, I'm not stupid, I watched you fight a corpsman for a wet bag on the cutter floor like it was your own child — so I know what you've got, and I know what's on it, near enough. And I'm going to tell you a thing now and I want you to know I'm telling you the *opposite* of what's good for me, so you'll know it's clean."

She waits.

"You could let it lie."

The heater ticks. Down the hall a cart rolls past on a bad wheel.

"He's dead," Sal says. "You can't punish a dead man. You can only hang him. And a hanged man doesn't feel the rope — only the people under it do." It is the one aphorism he spends, and he spends it slow, the way the surgery makes him spend everything now. "His sister. His nieces. That wife who'll come up here to bury him anyway, because that's what people do."

He breathes. The monitor counts.

"You put Dee's box on a table, and they don't get a skipper who died saving his crew. They get a man who ran a boat into the sea for a fish ticket. And drowned outrunning the bill." A swallow. "That's a different funeral. That's a thing his people carry the rest of their lives."

"Sal." But she doesn't stop him. She can't; her face won't let her.

"And he did save you." His good hand lifts, falls. "That's the part that isn't a lie. His hands put you in the basket and stayed behind. Whatever was in his head — I don't know it, you don't either, not for sure." A breath, dredged. "Both true. They don't cancel. And nobody up here in the warm can hold the two of them at once. They'll pick one. People always pick one."

He stops to breathe. She doesn't help him.

"And it's not just his people." He gestures, a small flat helpless thing, and the gesture runs out of air. "The kid. You stopped a few minutes early. I was there. I heard you call it."

She goes very still.

"Eight minutes of CPR on a nineteen-year-old. Hands frozen to hooks. Then you called it, and I'm telling you as a man who watched — you did everything a person could do." His eyes hold hers. "But you know what you know. And you've got a face, Ren. I've watched it four days. The face of a person carrying a thing."

A long breath. The hand on the flat blanket.

"You put Cole's box on the table, you put your own hands on the table next to it. They don't examine his without examining yours. And there's no room where you come out of that clean. Not for him. Not for the kid. Not for you."

He says it gently. That's what makes it unbearable. He says it the way you'd say a true thing to someone you loved, and he is the one man alive with the best right in the world to want Cole damned — the man whose leg paid for Cole's silence, the man who lay in the water knowing — and he is sitting here, freshly opened a third time, his hand pressed to the place a leg used to be, making the case for letting it lie, *from inside* the strongest possible reason to want it told. He's not protecting Cole. He's protecting her. He's offering her the comfortable door and standing in it on one leg so she can see it isn't a trap, so she'll know that if she walks through it, a good man held it open for her and meant her well.

"You could let it lie," Sal says again, quieter, the air nearly gone out of him. "I'd never say a word against you. I'd back the weather story to my grave. The box goes in a drawer in Seattle, the sea keeps its secrets, and we all go home and try to get warm." A breath. "You're allowed to choose that. I want you to know you're allowed. That's the only thing I dragged this body up out of the sea to tell you."

---

She sits with it.

The math runs whether she wants it to or not — it always has, it ran on a child in London while her hands were inside another patient, it ran on Marcus in the dark while she turned away to bail. She runs it on this. On one side of the line: Cole's sister, his nieces at the back of a church, a wife who can say *he died a hero* and build a life next to it; Sal's twenty years kept clean; her own hands never on a table; Marcus a tragedy of the cold and not a line in a report under her name. A whole roomful of warm decent people who want the dead to have died for something, and would help her, in good faith, never lying, lay the comfortable thing down soft and total over the cold one. *He had margin and I tagged him and went to the leg.* If she lets it lie, that sentence dies in her and nowhere else. The cold has already taken the hand. She could keep the mouth.

On the other side: the timestamps. Dee's box in the locker by the door, dead-cold, sealed, that she cannot even power, that has no face to read and no court of need to plead before, that will say *deck-load over the marks, heading held after the gust, throttle up, the release that never fired* whether or not a single human mouth in any warm room ever opens. The truth doesn't need her. That's the thing she keeps circling back to and can't get past. The truth is already in the locker, already on its way to a lab, already going to come out of a dry warm stranger's hands in Seattle no matter what she does. So this was never a choice between the truth and the lie. The box took that choice off the table the minute she carried it up. This is only a choice about whether *she* will say it in her own words, or let the institution find it without her and let her own silence be one more decent mouth doing Cole's work for him.

*In your own words.* That's still the whole question. The only one there's ever going to be.

"Sal," she says. "Thank you."

He hears the shape of it. His face does a thing she has to look away from, because it's the same thing his face did on the cutter when he counted the breaths in the room and got the wrong number, the same thing her own face must be doing.

"You're not going to let it lie," he says. Not a question.

"I don't know yet what I'm going to say." Which is true, and which is the most paramedic thing she has said all day — the flat certain non-thing you give a frightened man while you go on holding the airway. "I know I'm going to be in the room. I know they're going to power the box whether I'm there or not. I haven't decided what comes out of my mouth." She looks at her wrapped hands in her lap, the mitts, the two fingertips on the left that the cold means to keep. "But I'm done deciding whether to *look*. That part I'm done with. I did enough not-looking in the dark to last me."

Sal closes his eyes. A tear runs flat across the bridge of his nose toward the pillow, the way it did on the cutter, and gets lost in nothing, because there's no seawater on him anymore, only this, only the warm and the loss and the leg that's gone and the man he kept quiet for who's at the bottom of the Bering Sea managing them both from down there, even now, even dead.

"He'd have talked you out of it," Sal says, eyes shut. "If he was here.
