He'd have had three good reasons and a warm voice and he'd have made you feel like the truth was the cruel choice and the silence was the kind one, and he'd have had you halfway believing it before he was done, because that's what he was, that was the whole of him, the best hands and the worst — " He stops. "He's not here. That's the one thing he can't manage. He's not in the room."

But he is, Ren thinks. He's in the locker by the door. He's in the timestamps and the un-tripped release and the engine note that held, and he's in the case Sal just made for him without meaning to, the case for silence laid out by the one man with the most right to want it told. He's not in the room. He doesn't have to be. He set it going and stepped off the side, and it goes on running without him, warmly, in other people's mouths.

She gets up. She fits another chip of ice against his lips with a hand she can't feel and he takes it and his throat works, and she goes to the door and stands by the locker where the bag sits with her name on the chain-of-custody and a property sticker over it, the one cold thing in the building that won't lie, and she doesn't open it, can't open it, wouldn't open it here if she could.

In the corridor a nurse is coming with the cart for the four o'clock dressings, and beyond her, by the elevators, a man in a soft jacket is standing with a folder under his arm, not pushing, waiting the way he waited on the cutter, at the edge of the bright room until someone gives him the nod — and he sees her see him, and he doesn't come forward, only lifts the folder a half-inch, a question, *when you're able*, and lets it down again.

Ren looks at the man, and at the locker, and at her own wrapped hands that carried a mile of truth up out of the dark and can no longer push a button.

"Set me the date," she says, loud enough to cross the corridor, in the flat certain voice that changes the weather in a room. "The formal interview. Set me the date."

The man in the soft jacket nods, once, and writes something in his folder, and the small machinery of the thing she has not yet decided how to say begins, quietly, to turn — and behind her in the warm room Sal lets out a breath that has a wreck in it, and the heater ticks, and the cold sits in her two left fingertips taking up its lease.

What she has decided is small, and it is only the first thing. She will be in the room. She will let them power the box. She has stopped triaging whether to look — has stopped writing off the inconvenient thing the way she wrote off the boy in the dark. That much she is done arguing with.

The rest of it she carries out of the room undecided, the way you carry a patient whose number you haven't called yet — what her own mouth will do, in a bright room, with a recorder running and Cole's whole warm case for silence already laid out for her by the one man who had the most right to break it. She does not know yet. She tells herself she does not know. But the not-knowing has a shape to it already, low and cold and certain underneath, the way Sal's leg had a shape under her hands in the dark before she let herself name it — and she goes down the corridor away from the locker carrying the shape and not yet the word for it, and she will have, she knows, exactly until the day they set her to decide.



---


# Chapter Twenty-Nine

## The Case for Silence

*POV: Ren — the recovered voice (Cole's voicemail and log) — +eleven days / a borrowed cabin, Unalaska*

The personal effects come to her in a Coast Guard property carton the size of a bread box, taped, initialed, a manifest stapled to the lid, and Ren signs for it with a hand she still cannot feel and carries it to the cabin a marine-casualty clerk found her because the hospital wanted the bed and she would not get on a plane south until after the interview, and the interview is the day after tomorrow.

She should not have it. She knows that — a witness does not take custody of the dead man's belongings the night before she testifies about him; there is a chain for this, a next of kin, a sister in Anacortes who has not answered the office's calls. But the clerk was young and tired and the wife is named as alternate contact on the old crew card and the wife is in Dutch Harbor right now, came up on the ferry to claim a body the sea has not given back, and somebody decided the simplest thing was to let the survivor who knew him hand the wife the box in the morning, person to person, the decent way. So it sits on the cabin table under a lamp, and the heater under the window runs the way every heater has run for eleven days, and Ren cannot get warm, and inside the box is what is left of Cole Vantine that the swimmer cut off him in the water and a corpsman bagged and a clerk inventoried, and she is alone with it, and she opens it because she is going to be asked, in thirty-some hours, to say what he was, and she does not want to say it without having looked.

*This is the last thing he does to her, and he did it on purpose, and he is going to do it through the things in this box.*

---

The manifest, first, because the manifest is a list and a list is a place to stand.

One wallet, leather, sodden, dried stiff. One wristwatch, dive, still running. One marriage band, gold, worn thin on the inside curve. One folding knife. One handheld VHF, dead. One waterproof notebook, spiral, the skipper's working log. One mobile telephone, recovered in a dry pocket of the survival suit, *device powered, charge 4%, see attached.* And a yellow page torn from the manifest pad where the clerk has written, in a careful hand, *NOK contact unable — spouse (est.) en route Dutch — release pending — survivor Okafor to convey.*

She lays them out on the table the way she'd lay out a kit. The watch is the thing that undoes her first and she does not expect it. It is still running. It kept time at the bottom of the Bering Sea inside a drowned man's sleeve and it is running now on the cabin table, the second hand going round, faithful and stupid and indifferent, keeping a count nobody is reading. She turns it face-down so she does not have to watch it count.

The notebook is swollen to twice its width, the pages a gray pulp, but the cold and the wet have done the kind thing the cold sometimes does, which is to fix the ink instead of running it, and the working pages hold. It is a skipper's log and it is exactly what a skipper's log is — pot counts, soak times, set positions in his own shorthand, weights, the small arithmetic of a working deck. She reads it the way she reads a chart, and what she is reading for is the load, and the load is there, page after page, in his own hand, not hidden, not coded, because a man does not hide a thing from his own working log: *deck 110 — 118 — full racks two high fwd — held 122 stack aft —* numbers she does not need Dee's tablet to translate, numbers that are over the marks on their face, written down plainly by the man who carried them because carrying them was just the work, was just the season, was just what everybody aft of the galley was doing for a payment on something. The log is not a confession. That is the terrible thing about it. It is a man doing his job and writing down that he did it, and the doing was the crime, and he wrote it like weather.

Near the back, the last working page, the night of it. A set position. A soak. And then, in a hand that is the same hand but pressed harder, the pen dug in: *blowing inshore — boats reporting williwaw off the high ground — closing weather — one more line before it shuts.* And then nothing. The pen stops there. Whatever he did next he did with his hands and not his pen, and the page after it is blank, swollen, gray, the rest of the night unwritten because the night took the writing of it, and she sits looking at *one more line before it shuts* in the lamplight and understands she is looking at the last sentence he let himself finish before the thing he would not write.

She closes the log. Her hands are cold. The room is warm. Her hands are cold.

---

The phone has four percent and the clerk has plugged it to a wall cord in the carton, a small mercy or a small cruelty, she can't tell which, and when she wakes the screen with the side of her wrong thumb it opens with no code, the way a working man's phone opens, no wall around it because who would, out here, and the screen is the screen of any man's phone — a weather app, a tide app, a marine forecast, a photo of a boat that is not the *Adeline Marsh*, a smaller boat, older, beautiful, the *Sara Lynn* hand-lettered on her transom, his whole life's climb floating in a slip in flat water — and a call log, and a voicemail.

One voicemail, in the outbox. Not received. Recorded and not sent.

She should not. She knows the shape of the should-not exactly, the way she knew not to take the box, the way she knew the leg was gone — a thing already true that she is going to do anyway. It is a message to his wife. It is the most private thing a man owns, a message to the woman who left him, recorded and never sent, and Ren is the wrong person in the world to hear it, a witness the night before she damns him, and she presses it with the side of her thumb because she is going to stand in a bright room in thirty hours and say what Cole Vantine was, and she will not say it having flinched from the one place he is still allowed to speak.

The phone says the date. Eleven days ago. The afternoon before the boat went over.

And then there is his voice in the warm cabin, low and easy and alive, and the back of her neck goes to ice.

---

"Hey. It's me."

A breath. Boat noise behind it — the hum of a main, the slap of a wake, a man on a bridge with a phone to his ear and a watch to keep.

"It's — I know. I know you said don't, and I'm not gonna make it weird, I just." A small laugh, the warm kind, the kind he used on the kid, on the green sea, on the dark. "Pots are good. We had a good string today. I keep doing the math on it, what it'd take, the payment, the back end of it, and it's — it's close, Jeanie. It's closer than it's been. One more decent trip and the *Lynn*'s clear and I can — "

The voice stops. Recalibrates. The management surfacing even here, even alone on a bridge talking to a woman who isn't there: he hears himself selling her the boat again, the climb again, the *one more trip* again, the thing that ended the marriage, and he hears it, and he stops himself, and the stopping is the most honest sound on the tape.

"I'm doing it again. Sorry. I'm — that's the thing you said, right, that I'd be on my deathbed telling you about a good string of pots. You're not wrong. You were never wrong about that." Another breath. The main humming under him. "I just wanted to say it's blowing up a little out here and I always think about you when it blows. Don't know why. You'd hate that. You'd say *don't think about me when it's dangerous, Cole, think about me when it's Tuesday and nothing's happening* — " and the laugh again, smaller, with a wreck already in it that he doesn't know is coming — "and you'd be right about that too. So. It's Tuesday. Nothing's happening. I'm thinking about you. There. I did it right for once."

A long pause. The wake. The hum.

"Be good to know you were rooting for me down there. That's all. Whatever I — whatever it costs to get her clear. Be good to know somebody thought it was worth it." A breath. "I'll call you Sunday. We'll be in by Sunday. Okay. Bye. Love — "

And the recording ends, cut off, *love* with no object, because he didn't send it, because he listened back and decided the *love* was too much or not enough or a door he wasn't allowed to open, and he saved it to the outbox to think about and the sea did his thinking for him. *Be good to know somebody thought it was worth it.* The man asking, the day before, into a phone, into a silence, whether the held thing was worth the holding. And then the next day he held the course anyway, with that question still unanswered in his pocket, and six people died for the answer he never got.

Ren sits very still. The heater ticks. Her hands are cold.

It is a true thing and it is a tender thing and it is a real man, and she had let herself, in eleven days, file him as a shape — *the guilty one, the one who didn't trip the release, the one who managed the dark* — and the tape will not let the shape hold. The tape is a man on a bridge missing his wife and doing bad math about a boat, the way ten thousand men are doing bad math about a boat tonight, the way Sal did the same money's bidding from the galley, the way the whole fleet does, and out of all of them the sea picked this one to make an example, and the example is sitting on a cabin table with the second hand still going round.

*This is the case for silence,* she thinks. *This is him making it. He recorded it eleven days ago and he never sent it and it is making his case anyway, from the outbox, the way the swimmer made it from the clipboard and Sal made it from the bed. He doesn't even have to be in the room. He set it going and stepped off the side.*

---

She knows the case now. She has heard it from every direction — the swimmer made it from the clipboard, Sal made it from the bed, and the box on the table is only the last and most intimate mouth saying it — and she does not need to itemize it again, because the log and the tape have just made it for her without a single word she could underline. The man on the bridge missing his wife. The numbers written down like weather. The hands that freed the line. She has only to sit in the lamplight and let the two things she has just held in her own wrapped hands go on being true at the same time, the way they have been true at the same time since the first dark minute in the hull: the deck-load over the marks in his own plain shorthand, and the warm voice asking a silence whether the held thing was worth holding. Both real. Both his. The log that damns him and the voicemail that breaks her heart, lying side by side on a cabin table under one lamp, and no warm room on earth able to hold the two of them at once.

That is the whole horror of the man, and she lets it stand at its full height because she has learned the one true thing about a temptation, which is that you cannot refuse it honestly until you have let it be as strong as it really is. He is not a lie she can catch. He is a true thing she has to refuse anyway. He died getting them out — that is the part that isn't a lie at any angle, the thing the timestamps will never be able to touch, his hands in the basket, his body staying behind — and you do not put a man on a table for an overload he carried like weather when he drowned saving the people the overload nearly killed. Sal said that. Sal, who has the most right in the world to want him hung, said *you'd be lighting a fire in your own house to prove the neighbor's an arsonist.* And Sal was right.

And her own hands aren't clean, and that is the last weight in the case, the one Cole built to fit her exactly, the one he set in her arms with the last breath in his chest, knowing she'd be standing here holding it. She stopped working a boy who might have lived. If she puts Cole's hands on a table, she puts her own hands on the table beside them; there is no room where his are examined and hers are not. *He had margin and I tagged him and went to the leg.* If she lets it lie, that sentence dies in her and nowhere else.

*We can both be buried clean,* the box says, in his low easy voice, the voice that changed the weather in a dark room. *Nobody examines anybody's hands. Your boy stays a tragedy of the cold. My nieces get a skipper who died saving his crew. Jeanie gets a husband she can stand next to in a church. The tablet's just a brick in a drawer in Seattle with no living mouth to give it weight. You and me, Ren. We go home and we try to get warm. That's the kind door. I held it open for you on the way down. Last useful thing I ever did.*

She hears it clearly. She wants to be honest that she hears it, wants to be the one witness who admits the door was real and the dead man's hand was warm on it.

And then she does the small physical thing that is the whole chapter, the only task there is to do in a warm cabin the night before, which is: she turns off the phone.

---

She holds the side button with the side of her wrong thumb, three seconds, the same hold she could not give Dee's tablet, and this one she can manage because turning a thing off needs less than turning it on, the cold lets you stop a machine when it won't let you start one, and the screen with the *Sara Lynn* on it goes dark, and his voice that is not on it anymore but might as well be goes quiet, and the cabin has only the heater in it now, ticking.

She puts the watch face-down back in the box. The notebook. The dead VHF. The folding knife. She does not put the ring back yet; she holds it a moment, the gold worn thin on the inside curve from twenty years of a hand that wore it after she left, and she sets it in last, on top, where the wife will see it first when she opens the box in the morning, because that much she can manage, the staging of one small mercy for a woman who is owed a great deal more than a survivor can give her. Then she folds the lid down, and tapes it, and the tape is loud in the room.

The dead man stops talking.

That is the thing she sits with after — that he has stopped, that the box is shut, that for the first time since the basket came up with only her in it there is no Cole voice in the room making the case, and the silence where his voice was is the same silence as the breath that went missing from the count, the metronome that quit, the slow even managed breathing she clocked in the first dark minute and filed as a strange vital sign. He is not in the room. Sal was right about that, in the hospital, eyes shut: *he's not in the room, that's the one thing he can't manage.*

But Sal was also wrong, and she sees the wrongness now with the box taped shut under the lamp. Cole is not in the room and Cole does not need to be in the room. That was always the trick of him, the whole of him, the best hands and the worst: he understood that the surest way to bury a thing is to make yourself the only one who can dig — and then he died, and made *her* the only one with the shovel, and walked off the side of the boat trusting the room to tell itself, the swimmer to tell it, Sal to tell it, the box on the table to tell it, the comfortable true thing to lay itself down soft and total over the cold true thing the way the water laid itself over him. He is managing the room from the bottom of the Bering Sea. He is managing it right now, through a sealed carton with his ring on top. The case for silence is the last and best room he ever ran, and he ran it the way he ran the dark — warmly, plausibly, with every word of it true, leaning on the one thing a drowning room and a grieving fleet and a guilty witness all have in common, which is that they would all so much rather not look.

*He's still managing me,* she thinks. *From down there. With his hands gone and his name clean and his ring on the box, he is still the steady one, still laying the tarp over the deck-load, still telling me it's nothing, it's the kind choice, you'd be doing everyone a kindness, Ren, one more thing only you can do.*

She gets up. She does not decide to; the body does it, the way it always does the thing the mouth can't yet. She goes to the window where the heater runs and looks out at the gravel road and the fuel dock and the gray channel beyond it lying flat and indifferent and the right way up, sky up, water down, and the rightness of it is still its own wrong eleven days on. Somewhere out there the cutter is making for the open water and somewhere south the lab in Seattle has Dee's brick of timestamps that will say what it says whether or not a single human mouth ever opens, and somewhere in Dutch a woman named Jeanie is in a room she didn't choose waiting for a body the sea won't give back, and tomorrow Ren will put a taped box in that woman's hands and not be able to tell her what is in it or what she is going to do about it the day after.

She decides the one thing she can decide tonight, which is small and is the whole of it: she will not be managed.

Not because she is brave. She is too cold to be brave; bravery is a warm-handed thing and her hands have stopped reporting in. Not because the case is wrong — the case is right, the case is the best case there ever was, every word of it true, that is exactly why refusing it costs. She will not be managed because she has spent her whole life on the comfortable side of the count — tagging the child, tagging the boy, telling the dark *nobody dies in the next hour* and meaning it as a rope and knowing it as a lie — and she is so tired of the weight of the comfortable thing that she would rather carry the other weight, the cold one, the one that gives you longer than it feels and less than you hoped and does not care whether the price is fair. Cole carried the wrong thing his whole life because letting go of it meant admitting. She watched him do it. She watched him hold the course and hold the breath and hold the warm version all the way to the bottom, the most consistent man she ever met, consistent in the one ruinous way: he never once let go of the thing he should have let go of.

She is going to trip the release. That is the only way she has left to say it to herself, in the grammar the boat taught her. The deck-load is heavy and the gust is on her and the thing she has to do is the loudest thing on a boat, the gear going over the side, and every reason in the world says hold the course and ride it loaded and make the comfortable pot-line — and she is going to dump the rail anyway, in a bright room, in her own words, with a hand that won't close, and she is going to put her own broken grip on the table next to his, because she will not launder her boy under his boat, and there is no version of that room where she comes out clean, and she is going to go into it anyway.

The box sits taped on the table with the ring on top. The watch inside it goes on counting in the dark where she can't see it. The heater ticks. Her hands are cold, and the cold is in them for good now, she knows that, the lease is signed; but the other cold, the one in her mouth, the one she has not yet let out — that one is still hers to spend, and the day after tomorrow she is going to spend it, all of it, and let the warm room hate her for it, and she will not get warm again afterward and she knows it, and she is going to say it anyway.

She turns off the lamp. The dead man is quiet. For the first time in eleven days, she does not reach to make sure the box is still there, the way you stop reaching for a thing you have decided to let go of, and in the dark cabin with the heater running she finds she cannot tell anymore which of them she is no longer holding on to — the man at the bottom of the sea, or the comfortable version of herself that he spent his last breath handing her, gift-wrapped, warm, the kindest cruelest thing, the door marked mercy with complicity on the other side of it.

She goes to bed cold. The interview is the day after tomorrow. She already knows the shape of what she will say, the way she knew the shape of Sal's leg in the dark, and she hates it, and she is done being managed, and that is the only thing she has decided, and it is enough to keep her from sleeping, and it is enough.


---


# Chapter Thirty

## In Your Own Words

*POV: Ren — +thirteen days / a federal building, Anchorage*

The room is too warm and she still can't feel the left hand, and those two facts sit in her like a pulse and a deficit, the warmth she can't use and the hand that won't report, and she walks into the interview carrying both of them the way she carried the tablet up the basket — clipped to her, not held, because holding takes fingers that close.

They have given the room a long table and bad light and a carafe of coffee sweating onto a cork mat, and the carafe is the first thing she clocks because it is the warmest object in the room and her body wants it before her mind has a vote. Three of them on the far side. A marine-casualty investigator in the soft jacket she knows from the cutter and the hospital corridor, a recorder set square in front of him with one red eye lit. A second man, older, a captain's bars worn down to a shine, who does not introduce himself past a rank she doesn't catch. And a woman with a laptop and a yellow pad who will, she understands, be the one who actually writes down what is said, the human transcriber under the machine one, two memories overlapping the way she counted heads by breathing in the first dark minute — the recorder for the words and the woman for the weight.

She does not sit until they tell her to. Two seasons on a deck and six years in a uniform have taught her hands where to be in a room she doesn't own, and the hands go to her lap, the wrapped one and the gloved one, the right that came back and the left that didn't, and she folds them the way you fold a thing you don't want to look at.

"Ms. Okafor." The soft-jacket man. He has a folder and he does not open it; it is a prop and they both know it. "Thank you for coming in. I know it's been a hard few weeks."

"It has."

"We'll keep it as short as the law lets us. This is the formal casualty interview for the loss of the *Adeline Marsh* — six souls, plus the vessel. You're under no obligation to characterize anyone. We want the facts as you observed them. Your account, in your own words."

*In your own words.* The phrase lands the way it landed in the hospital, the way it has landed in her every night for thirteen nights, the only question there has ever been. She nods. The red eye watches.

"Before we begin." She says it before he can start, because there is a thing on the table that has to go on the table, and she is done not-looking. "I have an item to enter into the record. It came up with me out of the hull. It's been in Coast Guard custody since the cutter. I'd like it logged before I say anything, so what I say can be checked against it instead of taken on my word."

The two men look at each other. The woman's pen stops.

"The engineer's tablet," the soft-jacket man says. Not a question. They know she has it; they have had it; the small dumb ritual of the evidence bag and the chain-of-custody tag has gone on around it for thirteen days. "The engineer, Ms. Okonkwo's device. It's in the lab in Seattle. We've imaged it."

So they have already pressed the button she couldn't. A dry warm stranger in a clean room has held the side button three seconds with a hand that closes and watched the screen come alive, the last twelve inches of the truth she carried a mile through black water and could not finish. She feels it land somewhere under the deficit hand and does not let it move her face.

"Then it'll say what it says whether I'm in this room or not," she says. "I know that. I want it in the record anyway, under my name, that I'm the one who went down for it. Not because it needs me. Because I don't want my silence to be one more decent mouth doing the easy thing."

The older man, the captain, speaks for the first time. "You want to put it in evidence," he says slowly, "and you want it on the record that *you* put it there." He is not unkind. He is a man who has sat at a great many of these tables and has learned the shape of a witness who is about to make her own life harder. "You understand the device implicates the master who relieved you. Mr. Vantine. Who is deceased. Who, by every account we have so far — the swimmer, the cook, the crew of the *sixty* — died extracting you and Mr. Brandt from the hull."

"I understand it."

"Then I have to ask you the question I'd ask anybody." He folds his hands; his close on each other, both of them, and she watches them do it the way she watches everything now. "Are you sure you want to do this."

---

She has been asked this before. Sal asked it from a bed with one leg, freshly opened a third time, the man with the best right in the world to want Cole damned, holding the door open so she'd know it wasn't a trap. The box on the cabin table asked it in Cole's own warm voice, low and easy, *we can both be buried clean.* The cold has asked it every night for thirteen nights, ticking under the window where the heater runs. *You could let it lie. The cold already took the hand. You could keep the mouth.*

She is sure. She has been sure since a taped carton under a lamp eleven days ago, since she turned off a phone with the side of a thumb she couldn't feel and the dead man stopped talking. But sure is not the same as easy, and she does not want the captain to mistake the one for the other, because a witness who finds this easy is a witness who hasn't understood what she's doing, and she has understood it down to the bone.

"No," she says. "I'm not sure I want to do it. I want it done. Those aren't the same. Log the tablet."

The woman with the pad writes something. The soft-jacket man says the words into the machine — item entered, chain-of-custody number, the survivor's request — and there is a small physical task in it after all, the only one this room holds, and it is the task the whole book of her hands has been building toward: she has to sign.

He slides a single sheet across the cork mat, the custody acknowledgment, a line at the bottom and a date beside it. He sets a pen on it, a cheap government pen, capped.

She picks it up with the right hand, which mostly works now, the cold paying it back in installments. And then she stops, because the form is laid out for a right-handed person and the line runs the wrong way for her wrist, and the honest thing — the thing she came here to be — is to do this with the hand that did the work, and the hand that did the work is the left one, the burn-scarred one, the medic's hand, the one that held the tablet against her ribs for a mile of black water and froze for it, the one she cannot feel two fingertips of and will not feel again.

She moves the pen to the left hand. The glove comes off; the nurse fits it loose now so she can. The fingertips are the color the surgeon used the word *demarcation* about, a color she has seen on the lips of men she pronounced, and she wraps them around the pen and they do not close so much as approximate, the gross grip there and the fine grip gone for good, and she presses the nib to the line and writes her name.

It comes out wrong. The R is hers and the rest is a child's, the letters not flowing because the hand that makes them flow is at the bottom of the Bering Sea with everything else the cold took, and she watches her own name come out of her own dead fingers in a shape she doesn't recognize and thinks, with the flat clinical part of her that never stops, *that's what the truth costs to sign — a hand that won't make your own name look like yours.* She does not soften it. She lets the ugly signature stand. She caps the pen.

"Renata Okafor," the soft-jacket man reads off it, gently, for the machine. "Thank you."

"It's mine," she says. "It doesn't look like it. It's mine."

---

Then she gives the account, and she gives it the way she'd give a report at a handover — flat, sequenced, the way you'd hand a patient across to the next crew, no editorial, just the observations and the times, because the observations and the times are the cruelest possible weapon and she has learned this whole long cold mile that you do not need to swing a thing that heavy; you only need to set it down where the light is.

She walks them through the deck. She came aboard in December, she tells them; she was not aboard for the first trips of the season, but she stood on that deck for two trips and she has stood on enough decks to know how a boat sits, and the *Adeline Marsh* ran heavy. The pots were stacked two and three high past where she'd have expected, and the boat came back up off a sea slow, the way a loaded boat comes up slow, the lazy roll, *like a fat man getting out of a low chair* — Sal's words, she does not use them, she uses her own, *she didn't snap up the way a right boat snaps up* — and she says it without weight, the way you'd note an output off a bag, and lets the captain with the worn bars do his own arithmetic on what a man co-signs when he co-signs a stability certification and then loads a boat past it.

She does not say *Cole loaded her over the marks to make a payment on his own boat.* She does not know that from the deck; she knows it from the log and the tablet and a voicemail she had no right to hear, and that is hearsay and grief and not hers to swing. She gives them the deck she stood on. She gives them the slow roll. She lets the tablet say the figures.

She walks them through the night. She was on watch in a building sea, she tells them, and an hour, maybe ninety minutes, before the roll, the relief skipper sent her below — *to thaw out,* he said, she was cold-handed and green in a rising sea, it was a kindness, it read as a kindness — and she went, grateful, and she was peeling her wet gloves in the galley passage when the boat lay down. She does not say *he took the only clear-eyed witness off the bridge.* She did not know that on the night. She knows it now the way you know a thing in the rearview, the tenderness rereading as cover, and she will not put a motive in the record she only assembled afterward in a borrowed cabin with a dead man's effects spread on a table. She gives them the time she was relieved and the reason she was given and lets them lay it next to the timestamps and decide for themselves why a man clears his bridge of the one person who keeps details.

"You're being very careful," the captain says, "not to tell us what he did."

"I'm telling you what I saw." She keeps her voice level, the flat certain voice that changes the weather in a room, the only inheritance she trusts. "What he did is on the tablet, and the tablet doesn't need me to say it for it. The deck-load figures are his own, in his own log and in the engineer's data — he wrote them down because to him they were just the work. The heading after the gust warning is logged. The throttle position is logged. The deck-load release —" and here she stops, one beat, because this is the center of it, the synecdoche of the whole sin, the thing she has carried in her chest since a black dive fourteen days and a lifetime ago — "the deck-load release was never actuated. The gear that should have gone over the side to save the boat stayed lashed. I found the float-free beacon fouled in that same un-dumped gear, three turns of its line around a pot rail, when I dove the passage. The thing that would have called for help was strangled by the thing he wouldn't let go of. That I saw. That's mine to say. The rest is timestamps."

The woman writes. The recorder's eye is steady and red. The soft-jacket man has gone very still, and she knows the stillness; it is the stillness of a man who came in expecting to coax a hero-story out of a traumatized deckhand and is instead being handed a sounded, sequenced casualty by a witness who is doing his job for him and hating it.

"He claimed a beacon," the captain says. "The manual bridge unit. Did it transmit?"

"No," she says. "Nothing he did called for help. The float-free unit never rose — it was fouled, I told you how. If he keyed the bridge unit I never saw the confirmation and the tablet doesn't show a signal. Help came because the kid had a personal satellite messenger zipped in his bib against the rules, and it pinged our position for hours while he was dying. The rescue you ran came off a nineteen-year-old's contraband. Not off anything the skipper did." She lets that sit, because it is true and because it is the cruelest true thing in the file and because a casualty board should have the cruelest true thing. "Mark that. The man who's about to be buried a hero didn't summon you. The boy nobody was supposed to listen to did."

---

She could stop there.

She is aware of it the way she was aware, in the first dark minute, of the gulp reflex that drowns people on their first gasp — a thing the body wants to do that she has to decide about. She could stop here. The tablet is in. The deck is sounded. The held course and the un-tripped release are in the record under her ugly signature, and Cole Vantine is indicted by his own numbers without one editorial word from her, and she has done the thing she came to do, refused the comfortable lie, given the costly truth, ruined a dead man's name with nothing but facts and let the data do the hanging she would not do with her mouth. She could stop, and walk out, and the cold would be in her hand but not in the rest of her, and she would be, by every measure this room can take, the brave witness who told the truth about a skipper.

And it would be a lie. Not in what she said. In what she left.

Because there is a hand in this file that is hers, and it is not the one that signed.

"There's one more thing for the record," she says.

The captain's worn hands open. The soft-jacket man's eyes come up. "You don't have to—"

"I do." Flat. Certain. "You're going to investigate every set of hands in that hull. That's what this is. You'll have his, off the tablet. You should have mine. I'm not going to put his on the table and keep mine in my pocket. That's the one thing I came here not to do."

She tells them about Marcus.

She tells it the way she told the deck — flat, sequenced, the times — because the cold clinical exactness is the only way she can get the words out and because Marcus deserves the same precision she gave the boat, no less and no softer. Nineteen, green, owner's nephew. Mild hypothermia by hour seven, moderate by hour nine, the shivering slowing and then stopping — the body giving up the argument, the paradoxical warmth, the *I'm okay now* that means the opposite. She splinted the cook's leg under traction in those hours, she tells them, a job that took both her hands and the only other strong pair in the hull, and to do it she triaged her attention off the boy. She let him drift to *stable enough.* She tagged him, in her mind, the one she couldn't change.

"And then I dove the passage," she says, "and I came up knowing what I told you I came up knowing — about the deck, about the beacon, about the skipper — and it was in my head, all of it, taking up the room a medic needs. And when the kid crashed, I worked him, and then I stopped working him." She makes herself say the number, the way she made herself sign with the dead hand. "I gave him eight minutes of CPR in the dark with my hands frozen to hooks, and then I called it, and I was certain when I called it. I'd tagged him already. I'd written him off before he was gone."

The room is very quiet. The heater. The red eye.

"After he died," she says, "I stripped the suit off his body for the cook, and I found the satellite messenger in his bib. Still powered. Still sending. And the log on it showed it had been acquiring and reporting position for hours — which meant he'd been calling for help, quietly, the whole time, with a machine he wasn't supposed to have, while I had two fingers on his throat deciding he was already gone. He had margin." She has to stop. She starts again. "I don't know how much. I'll never know how much. But the body that's pinging for hours isn't the body that's beyond reach. He had margin, and I tagged him expectant, and I went to the leg, and a few minutes one way or the other in a thing like that is sometimes the whole of it. I stopped too early. He might have lived. I want that in the record next to the skipper's deck-load. Under the same name. Mine."

She hears the woman's pen stop, and start, and stop again.

"Ms. Okafor," the soft-jacket man says, very carefully, "you were a casualty yourself. Hypothermic, injured, in the dark, performing CPR with—"

"I know what I was." She does not let him give it to her; she came here precisely to refuse the gift of it. "I know all the reasons. A review board in London gave me all the same reasons once, about a different child on a different board, and cleared me, and the reasons were all true and the child was still dead and the reasons never once helped. You can write the reasons down. Write them down. But you'll write them down *next to what I did*, not instead of it. I'm not going to launder my hands under his boat. He ran a careless boat into the sea, and I let a boy go a few minutes early, and both of those are true, and they don't cancel, and I'll be damned if I'll let his larger wrong be the warm water I wash mine in. Both. On the record. That's the whole reason I came."

---

The captain looks at her for a long moment, and there is something in the worn face she can't read and doesn't try to, because she has learned this whole cold mile that you cannot manage what's in another person's face and the trying is its own small surrender.

"You understand," he says at last, "that there will be people who use this. The deck-load finding will be on the master, and the master died saving you, and now the survivor who carried up the evidence has also entered her own clinical failure into the same record. You understand how that gets told. That it muddies your account of him. That it gives anyone who wants the weather-story a way to call you the deckhand who sank a dead man to get out from under her own — "

"Yes." She has done this arithmetic. She did it in a borrowed cabin under a lamp. On one side of the line: Cole's sister, his nieces at the back of a church, the wife named Jeanie who came up on a ferry to claim a body the sea won't give back, all of whom get a hero if she keeps her mouth shut; her own name kept clean; Marcus a tragedy of the cold and not a line in a federal record under her hand. On the other side: an account she can live inside. The math has never come out in her favor. She is here anyway. "I understand exactly how it gets told. His family will hate me. The fleet will have a name for me by the time I'm off the island. I'll be the one who told. I've thought about all of it. I'm telling you anyway, because the alternative is to do to the truth what I did to the boy — tag the inconvenient part of it expectant, write it off because spending myself on it costs too much, and walk away clean. I've done that once already in my life. I did it twice in that hull. I'm not doing it a third time in a warm room with a recorder running. That's all. That's the whole of it."

The soft-jacket man reaches out and stops the recorder. The red eye goes dark. He looks tired, and human, and for a moment not an investigator at all, just a man who has been handed something heavier than he wanted.

"It's a brave account," he says quietly.

"No," she says. "Brave is a warm-handed word. My hands have stopped reporting in. It's not brave. It's just the only one I can live inside." She gathers herself to stand, the wrapped left and the working right. "Are we done?"

"We're done. Unless there's anything else."

There isn't. There is only the deficit and the warmth she still can't use, and the ugly signature drying on the form, and somewhere south a lab with the tablet she carried a mile and could not open, already saying everything she just said and more, in figures no grief can argue with — so that the whole of what she did in this room, in the end, was insist on saying it *first*, in her own words, with the dead hand, before the machine could say it for her and let her off.

She stands.

---

In the corridor someone catches up to her — the woman with the pad, off the record now, no pen — and says, kindly, the way people say the kind useless things, that there's coffee in the room, that Ren should take a minute, take something warm before she goes back out into the cold.

So she goes back in, alone, and pours the coffee with the right hand because the left can't manage the carafe, and she wraps both hands around the paper cup the way you're supposed to, the way every poster in every break room she ever clocked into told you to warm cold hands, both palms cupped to the heat.

The right hand takes it. The warmth comes up through the right palm, real and ordinary and good, the heat of a thing that is only a thing.

The left hand holds the cup and feels nothing.

She stands there a long moment with the cup between her two hands, one of them drinking the warmth and one of them dead to it, and she understands that this is how it is going to be now, that this is the lease the cold signed in a black mile of water and intends to keep — not the whole of her, just enough, just the part that did the work, the medic's hand, the useful one, the one she'd give up first if anyone were doing the choosing, kept by the cold as its deposit. She did not save the truth and her peace. She did not even save the truth and her hand. They were all of them divisible, every last one, competence from care and the warm from the cold and the account from the absolution, divisible the way Cole's good hands were divisible from the boat he drowned, and she walked into the dividing knowing it and did it anyway and there is no warm room on earth that will undo it.

She finishes the coffee that only half of her can feel.

Outside, the channel lies flat and gray and the right way up, sky over water the way it's supposed to be, thirteen days on, and the rightness of it is still its own wrong, and somewhere out there the *Adeline Marsh* is on the bottom with a man in her who managed everyone he ever met and is managing them still, from down there, with his name about to be ruined by the witness he made and the data he couldn't drown. Ren stands in the doorway between the warm room and the cold day with a dead hand and an account she can live inside, and she does the last arithmetic, the one the hull spent thirty hours and six lives teaching her, the math of cold gone all the way over into the math of everything else: it gives you longer than it feels and less than you hoped, and it does not care, it has never once cared, whether the price was fair.

She steps out into the cold, which is the same temperature as her hand, and goes to find out what's left to be warm in.


---
